# Acts Interpretation Act 1931 (Tas) — Ordinary-meaning principle
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Acts Interpretation Act 1931 (Tas)] [SECTION: s 8B(1)(c), s 8B(3)] [CITATION: Acts Interpretation Act 1931 (Tas)] [TYPE: interpretive principle] [TOPIC: ordinary meaning in statutory interpretation] [CONCEPTS: ordinary meaning, context, purpose, Acts Interpretation Act, s8B, dictionary meaning, statutory construction, interpretive aids, purposive approach]

The ordinary-meaning principle under the Acts Interpretation Act 1931 (Tas) requires that, in the absence of a statutory definition, a word in an Act be given its ordinary meaning as conveyed by the provision having regard to its context and the purpose or object of the Act. Section 8B(3) directs attention to the contextual and purposive reading of words, and s 8B(1)(c) permits recourse to dictionary meanings to confirm the ordinary meaning conveyed by the provision. Practically, this means courts first ascertain the ordinary meaning consistent with the statute’s purpose and context, and only then consult extrinsic aids such as dictionary definitions to confirm that reading. This principle shapes interpretation across Tasmanian criminal statutes where no express definitions are provided.

# Acts Interpretation Act 1931 (Tas) — Reckoning of time (exclusion of first day)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Acts Interpretation Act 1931 (Tas)] [SECTION: s 29] [CITATION: Acts Interpretation Act 1931 (Tas) s 29] [TYPE: interpretive principle] [TOPIC: reckoning of time, exclusion of first day] [CONCEPTS: reckoning time, exclude first day, s29, sentence commencement, Acts Interpretation Act, calculation of periods, inclusive/exclusive counting]

Section 29 of the Acts Interpretation Act 1931 (Tas) prescribes that when a period is dated or reckoned from a given day the period “shall be reckoned exclusively of that day”, so the first day is excluded from the calculation unless the statute expressly indicates otherwise. In sentencing and statutory time calculations, this can affect the start date of orders and time-limited rights. Where a court expressly orders a sentence “commencing on” a date, that wording can make the date inclusive; by contrast, an order expressed as “from” a date will follow the Act and be exclusive of that date. The rule interacts with common-law principles about divisions of a day and credit for parts of days served.

# Reckoning of time — Case law on fractions of a day (Prowse; Forster)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: Prowse v McIntyre (1961) Tas SR 9; Forster v Jododex Australia Pty Ltd [1972] HCA 61] [TOPIC: treatment of fractions of a day in law] [CONCEPTS: day fractions, exclusion of first day, credit for part-day, sentencing credit, Prowse v McIntyre, Forster v Jododex]

The law generally disregards divisions of a day for many legal calculations. In Prowse v McIntyre (1961) (Tas SR 9), authorities have applied the exclusion of the first day unless the statute indicates otherwise. Forster v Jododex Australia Pty Ltd [1972] HCA 61 (127 CLR 421) at 446 (Gibbs J) confirms that fractions of a day are ordinarily disregarded in legal reckoning, referencing earlier authorities (including Lester v Garland). These authorities support the statutory rule in s 29 of the Acts Interpretation Act 1931 (Tas) and inform sentencing practice such as awarding credit for parts of days actually served.

# Attorney General v Barnes [TASSC 2023] — subordinate legislation and interpretation
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: Attorney General v Barnes (TASSC 2023)] [TOPIC: limits on consulting subordinate legislation when interpreting parent Act] [CONCEPTS: subordinate legislation, interpretive aids, delegation, Acts Interpretation, judicial caution, antecedent materials, statutory context]

Attorney General v Barnes (TASSC 2023) is cited in the source as a caution that subordinate legislation should not be consulted to interpret the empowering parent legislation. The principle is that interpretation of primary legislation should not depend on the content of subordinate instruments in a manner that would reverse the intended hierarchy of statutory materials. Where relied upon, subordinate instruments may be admissible only as extrinsic material in the narrow ways permitted by interpretive law — they cannot supplant or redefine primary statutory meaning.

# Oaths Act (Tas) — Commissioner for declarations signature requirement (s 13)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Oaths Act (Tas)] [SECTION: s 13] [CITATION: Oaths Act (Tas) s 13] [TYPE: statutory definition / procedural rule] [TOPIC: commissioner for declarations signature requirement] [CONCEPTS: commissioner for declarations, Oaths Act, statutory declaration, signature block, police as commissioners, legal practitioners, formality, attestation]

Section 13 of the Oaths Act (Tas) prescribes what a commissioner for declarations must add after signing a statutory declaration. A commissioner signing “in that capacity” must add (a) the words “commissioner for declarations”; and (b) his or her title where qualified under section 12(2). The Act defines commissioners for declarations to include police and legal practitioners (as provided in the Act). Compliance with s 13 is necessary for the correct execution and admissibility of statutory declarations used as evidence or proof in criminal procedures.

# Failure to provide name and address — s 55A of the POA (as cited)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: offence] [TOPIC: failure to provide name and address to police] [CONCEPTS: name and address, offense to refuse details, arrest without warrant, s55A, police requests, reasonable grounds, procedural compliance]

Failure to provide details when requested by an officer where the person has committed, or is committing, an offence (or where the officer has reasonable grounds to believe so) is an offense referenced in the source as being punishable under “s 55A of the POA”. The source notes that such a refusal can justify arrest without warrant pursuant to that provision. The exact title of the POA is not supplied in the source; the chunk records the rule as presented: refusal to provide name and address on reasonable request may constitute an arrestable offence under s 55A of the POA (as cited).

# Executing arrest warrants — duty to have warrant, give copy, and reasonable belief (Criminal Code)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Criminal Code Act 1924 (Tas)] [SECTION: s 301] [CITATION: Criminal Code Act 1924 (Tas) s 301] [TYPE: procedural rule] [TOPIC: execution of arrest warrants] [CONCEPTS: arrest warrant, execution, copy to defendant, reasonable belief, Criminal Code s301, warrant availability, lawful arrest]

Execution of warrants of arrest is governed by Criminal Code Act 1924 (Tas) s 301. The officer must have reasonable belief in the warrant’s validity and, where practicable, provide a copy of the warrant to the defendant as soon as practicable. The source notes that if the warrant exists and the above conditions are complied with, no further issue arises; it is desirable to have the warrant available at the time of arrest but the duty to act on a reasonable belief in its existence is central.

# Duties on arrest — Criminal Code Act 1924 (Tas) ss 301–303 (duty to give reasons)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Criminal Code Act 1924 (Tas)] [SECTION: ss 301–303] [CITATION: Criminal Code Act 1924 (Tas) ss 301–303] [TYPE: procedural rule] [TOPIC: duties on arrest, informing arrestee] [CONCEPTS: duty to give reasons, s301(2), process or warrant, practical impossibility, arrest explanation, lawful arrest, custody procedures]

Criminal Code Act 1924 (Tas) ss 301–303 impose duties on arresting persons, including s 301(2) which makes it the duty of a person arresting another, whether with or without warrant, to give notice, if practicable, of the process or warrant under which he is acting or of the cause of the arrest. The duty is qualified by practicability — where it is impracticable to inform the arrestee (e.g. arrestee incapacitated), the duty does not apply. This statutory duty complements the common-law obligation to inform an arrestee of reasons for arrest.

# Power of arrest without warrant — statutory sources cited
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: procedural rule] [TOPIC: powers to arrest without warrant] [CONCEPTS: arrest without warrant, Criminal Code s27, Family Violence Act s11, POA s55, MOD Act s29, reasonable grounds, found offending, summary arrest powers]

The source cites multiple statutory bases for arrest without warrant in Tasmania: Criminal Code Act 1924 (Tas) s 27 (general power to arrest), Family Violence Act s 11 (power in family violence contexts), s 55 of the POA (as cited in source), and s 29 of the MOD Act (as cited). Each statute supplies context-specific grounds; the common requirement is that an officer have lawful authority (statutory power) and, where applicable, a reasonable belief that the person is committing an offence. The specific statutory text should be consulted for scope and limitations in each context.

# Duty to state reason for arrest — statutory and common-law duty; exceptions
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Criminal Code Act 1924 (Tas)] [SECTION: s 301(2)] [CITATION: Criminal Code Act 1924 (Tas) s 301(2)] [TYPE: procedural rule / evidentiary principle] [TOPIC: duty to inform arrestee of reasons for arrest] [CONCEPTS: duty to inform, s301(2), common law Christie v Leachinsky, impracticability exception, summary offences, opportunity to respond, justification for liberty deprivation]

The duty to inform an arrestee of the reason for arrest exists both at common law (Christie v Leachinsky [1947] AC 573) and statutorily under Criminal Code Act 1924 (Tas) s 301(2). The common-law justification emphasises the gravity of liberty deprivation and the arrestee’s chance to offer explanations that may secure immediate release. The statutory duty is subject to the qualification “if practicable”: examples of impracticability include arrestee being violently intoxicated, deaf and unable to lip-read, or immediately fleeing. A separate common-law exception is when the reason is obvious (e.g. caught in the act); that exception is not explicitly mirrored in s 301(2).

# Johnson v Reeves [2004] TASSC 110 — impracticability exception to s 301(2)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: Johnson v Reeves [2004] TASSC 110] [TOPIC: when it is not practicable to inform arrestee of reason for arrest] [CONCEPTS: impracticability, s301(2), duty to inform, police duty, arrestee incapacitated, high standard for non-disclosure]

Johnson v Reeves [2004] TASSC 110 recognises an impracticability exception to the s 301(2) duty to inform. The case holds that if it is pointless to inform or attempt to inform an arrested person of the cause (for example due to incapacity or inability to hear), it is not “practicable” to do so within the meaning of s 301(2). Courts apply a high standard before finding non-practicability justified.

# Wilkie v Patterson [2016] TASMC 6 — limited scope for threats/volatile situations to excuse explanation
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: Wilkie v Patterson [2016] TASMC 6] [TOPIC: duty to state reason despite threatening/volatile situations] [CONCEPTS: duty to inform, threat to safety, practicability, police explanation, high standard, arrest procedure]

Wilkie v Patterson [2016] TASMC 6 emphasises that volatile situations or threats to police safety do not automatically justify failing to explain reasons for arrest. The decision reflects a high threshold before the duty in s 301(2) can be excused on safety or volatility grounds; officers should make reasonable efforts to communicate reasons unless clearly impracticable.

# Christie v Leachinsky [1947] AC 573 — common-law duty to inform reason for arrest
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: Christie v Leachinsky [1947] AC 573] [TOPIC: historical source of duty to inform arrested person] [CONCEPTS: duty to inform, common law, liberty protection, opportunity to explain, summary offences, legal justification]

Christie v Leachinsky [1947] AC 573 is the principal common-law authority explaining the duty to inform an arrested person of the reason for arrest. The case sets out two rationales: (1) the substantial interference with liberty requires justification to be communicated; and (2) informing the arrestee permits immediate explanation and potential release. This common-law duty underpins statutory provisions such as Criminal Code s 301(2) and remains influential in procedural fairness assessments.

# "Found offending" and power to arrest — Kim Hibble v Phegan et al [1997] TASSC 52 (principle)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: Kim Hibble v Jody William Phegan; Kim Hibble v Dean Mark Jenkins; David John Brooks v Leigh Andrew Phegan; Kim Hibble v Kim Maree Ogden; David John Brooks v Andrew David Hulse Files [1997] TASSC 52] [TOPIC: meaning of "found offending" and reasonable belief test] [CONCEPTS: found offending, reasonable belief, flagrante delicto, immediate pursuit, arrest without warrant, Lynch v Hargrave, Trebeck v Croudace]

Kim Hibble v Phegan et al [1997] TASSC 52 explains that the statutory power to arrest a person “committing” an offence or “found offending” is exercisable where the arresting officer believes on reasonable grounds that the person is committing the offence. The Court adopted the approach in Lynch v Hargrave and Trebeck v Croudace: whether the person was in fact offending is for subsequent judicial determination; the officer’s reasonable belief at the time is sufficient to justify arrest. The decision describes three archetypal bases for arrest: (1) person taken flagrante delicto; (2) officer believes on reasonable grounds that person is flagrante delicto; (3) immediate/fresh pursuit after either circumstance.

# Lynch v Hargrave [1971] VR 99 — subjective reasonable-belief approach to "found offending"
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: Lynch v Hargrave [1971] VR 99] [TOPIC: interpretation of "found offending" and officer’s knowledge] [CONCEPTS: found offending, reasonable grounds, subjective belief, arrest law, Trebeck v Croudace, immediate pursuit, officer’s facts known]

Lynch v Hargrave [1971] VR 99 held that the power to arrest a person “found offending” is to be exercised according to the facts as known to the arresting officer: a reasonable belief based on those facts suffices. Whether the person was actually offending is a matter for the courts to determine later; the exercise of arrest power focuses on the officer’s reasonable grounds and the contemporaneous circumstances.

# Trebeck v Croudace [1918] 1 KB 158 — authority on "found offending"
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: Trebeck v Croudace [1918] 1 KB 158] [TOPIC: historical authority for arrest where officer believes on reasonable grounds] [CONCEPTS: found offending, reasonable grounds, arrest, historical precedent, officer perception]

Trebeck v Croudace [1918] 1 KB 158 is an historical authority relied upon in later decisions (including Lynch v Hargrave) to support the principle that an arrest may be lawful where the arresting officer reasonably believes the person is offending on the facts known at the time. The case underpins the subjective reasonable-belief test for “found offending” arrests.

# Additional historical authorities on "committing" or "found offending" (Simmons; Hanway; R v Smith; Lester)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: Simmons v Millingen (1846) 2 CB 524; Hanway v Boultbee (1830); R v Smith (1876) 14 SCR (NSW) 419; Lester v Garland (1808)] [TOPIC: historical precedents on meaning of "committing" offences] [CONCEPTS: committing offence, historical cases, interpretation, flagrante delicto, immediate pursuit, common-law roots]

The source cites multiple historical English and colonial authorities (Simmons v Millingen (1846); Hanway v Boultbee (1830); R v Smith (1876); Lester v Garland (1808)) as part of the doctrinal background to the meaning of “committing” and “found offending”. These cases collectively trace the common-law understanding that arrest powers in flagrante delicto situations depend on the facts known to the arresting officer and immediate circumstances such as fresh pursuit.

# Criminal Law (Detention and Interrogation) Act 1995 (Tas) — definition of "in custody" (s 3)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Criminal Law (Detention and Interrogation) Act 1995 (Tas)] [SECTION: s 3] [CITATION: Criminal Law (Detention and Interrogation) Act 1995 (Tas) s 3] [TYPE: statutory definition] [TOPIC: definition of "in custody"] [CONCEPTS: in custody, lawful arrest by warrant, Criminal Code s27 arrest, s3(2), detention, arrestee protections, scope of Act]

Section 3(2) of the Criminal Law (Detention and Interrogation) Act 1995 (Tas) defines when a person is “in custody” for the purposes of the Act: notably where the person is (a) under lawful arrest by warrant, or (b) under lawful arrest under section 27 of the Criminal Code or a provision of any other Act. This statutory definition establishes the threshold for entitlement to the Act’s protections (e.g. rights to silence, communication, interpreter and reasonable questioning time).

# Protections under Criminal Law (Detention and Interrogation) Act 1995 (Tas) — ss 4–6, 15–16 (overview)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Criminal Law (Detention and Interrogation) Act 1995 (Tas)] [CITATION: Criminal Law (Detention and Interrogation) Act 1995 (Tas) ss 4–6, 15–16] [TYPE: statutory procedural protections] [TOPIC: rights and protections while in custody] [CONCEPTS: right to silence, right to legal communication, interpreter, reasonable questioning time, custody officer duties, s4, s5, s6, s15, s16]

The Criminal Law (Detention and Interrogation) Act 1995 (Tas) provides statutory protections for persons “in custody”, including: (a) requirement that the arrestee be taken before a magistrate or justice as soon as practicable (s 4(1)); (b) requirement that the arrestee be informed of the right to silence prior to questioning (s 4(5)); (c) right to communicate with a friend/relative and a legal practitioner prior to questioning (s 6(1)), and police duties to defer questioning to enable that communication (s 6(2)); (d) provision of an interpreter where needed (s 5); (e) questioning only for a reasonable time with factors set out in s 4(4); and (f) custody officer duties (ss 15–16) including receiving the arrestee and ensuring treatment in accordance with the Act.

# Custodial status categories — volunteers, lawful arrest, custody without formal arrest (Sammak; Reid; Swan)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: custodial status categories and effect on Act protections] [CONCEPTS: volunteers assisting police, informal arrest, lawful arrest, Sammak, Reid, Swan, entitlement to detention protections, freedom to leave]

The law distinguishes three practical custodial categories: (1) volunteers assisting police — not in custody and free to leave so statutory detention protections in ss 4–6 were not intended to apply; (2) persons under lawful arrest — not free to leave and clearly entitled to statutory protections; (3) persons in police custody though not formally arrested — decisions including Sammak (Tas CCA) and subsequent cases (Reid, Swan) raise doubt whether the Act’s protections apply to informal custody. Determining custodial status depends on whether a reasonable person would consider themselves free to leave given police conduct and communications.

# Forensic procedure orders — police application and public-interest considerations
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: procedural rule] [TOPIC: forensic procedure order applications and public interest considerations] [CONCEPTS: forensic procedure order, police application, s14 application, s17 public interest, child victim considerations, custody pending order, ss22, s23, evidentiary need]

The source records that forensic procedure orders (applications by police) may be brought under s 14 of the relevant Act (unnamed in the source). Courts must consider public-interest factors under s 17, including the evidentiary value of images or material — for example, pictures of child victims that assist explanation of their account. The court may order detention pursuant to ss 22 or 23 where necessary to effect the order. The source emphasises that public-interest balancing and child-protection considerations are key in these applications.

# Power of entry to effect arrest — implied licence to approach dwelling (Halliday v Nevill)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Criminal Code Act 1924 (Tas)] [SECTION: s 26A] [CITATION: Criminal Code Act 1924 (Tas) s 26A] [TYPE: procedural rule / interpretive principle] [TOPIC: implied licence to approach house, power to enter to effect arrest] [CONCEPTS: implied licence, path or driveway, Halliday v Nevill, s26A, entry to curtilage, police approach, no signs, lawful entry]

Criminal Code Act 1924 (Tas) s 26A (as cited) governs power to enter premises to effect an arrest; the common-law implied-licence doctrine allows members of the public (including police acting lawfully) to use unobstructed paths or driveways to approach a house for legitimate purposes where no prohibitory signs exist. Halliday v Nevill [1984] HCA 80 established that where access ways to a suburban dwelling are unobstructed and no notice forbids entry, the law implies a licence to use them for legitimate visits that do not interfere with possession. This implied licence supports lawful approaches to effect contact or arrest but does not in itself authorise entry into the dwelling.

# Tasmania v Lee [2005] TASSC 117 — limits of implied licence (no implied right to enter house)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: Tasmania v Lee [2005] TASSC 117] [TOPIC: implied licence does not authorise entry into dwelling] [CONCEPTS: implied licence, driveway access, trespass, police entry, Halliday distinction, dwelling entry, privacy limits]

Tasmania v Lee [2005] TASSC 117 clarifies that the implied licence to use paths or driveways does not extend to an implied licence to enter the house itself. The decision holds that, absent invitation or other lawful authority, entry into the dwelling beyond the access path would amount to trespass. Thus police may lawfully approach to the door under implied licence but require separate lawful authority (warrant, consent, exigent circumstances) to enter the house.

# Power to seize third-party property as potential evidence — Ghani v Jones principle
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: police seizure of property from third parties and reasonableness test] [CONCEPTS: seizure from third parties, Ghani v Jones, reasonableness of refusal, mobile phones, evidence preservation, police powers, Lord Denning test]

Ghani v Jones [1970] 1 QB 693 articulates a common-law framework under which police may examine or, in some cases, seize property from third parties when the refusal to cooperate is “quite unreasonable” given investigative necessity. Lord Denning gave examples where a third party’s refusal to permit police inspection would be clearly unreasonable (e.g. abandoned stolen car). The test emphasises the reasonableness of the third party’s refusal in the circumstances and the seriousness of the suspected offence, balanced against privacy and property interests. This principle has been applied in later local cases concerning seizure of bystanders’ mobile phones containing potential evidence.

# Police v Ronald Sines / Police v Andrew Love (NSW Local Court, 7 July 2017) — application of Ghani
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: Police v Ronald Sines; Police v Andrew Love (NSW Local Court, 7 July 2017)] [TOPIC: seizure of mobile phone from bystander recording incident] [CONCEPTS: mobile phone seizure, bystander evidence, Ghani v Jones, reasonable grounds, retention for download, fourth-party privacy]

In Police v Ronald Sines; Police v Andrew Love (NSW Local Court, 7 July 2017), a local magistrate applied Ghani v Jones to hold that police had power to seize a bystander’s mobile phone that apparently recorded an alleged assault on police. The decision reasoned that: (a) police had reasonable grounds to believe a serious offence had occurred; (b) the phone likely contained evidence; (c) the bystander’s refusal appeared unreasonable in the circumstances; and (d) retention was to be limited to time reasonably necessary to download footage. The magistrate did not exhaustively justify why the refusal was unreasonable but applied Lord Denning’s framework in the local factual matrix.

# Criminal Code Act 1924 (Tas) — lawful custody definition reference (s 106)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Criminal Code Act 1924 (Tas)] [SECTION: s 106] [CITATION: Criminal Code Act 1924 (Tas) s 106] [TYPE: statutory definition] [TOPIC: lawful custody, custody definitions] [CONCEPTS: lawful custody, s106, detention, custody vs arrest, Criminal Code, custody for interrogation purposes]

Criminal Code Act 1924 (Tas) s 106 provides statutory treatment of “lawful custody” relevant to detention and interrogation contexts. The source references s 106 in defining lawful custody and in outlining interplay with the Criminal Law (Detention and Interrogation) Act 1995 (Tas) protections which depend on whether a person is in lawful custody as defined under the Code.

# Justices Act 1959 and Justices Rules/regulations (list)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Justices Act 1959 (Tas)] [CITATION: Justices Act 1959 (Tas); Justices Rules 2003 and listed amendments] [TYPE: statutory reference] [TOPIC: Justices Act and subordinate rules] [CONCEPTS: Justices Act 1959, Justices Rules 2003, subordinate legislation, victim impact rules, family violence rules, review rules, regulations, procedural law]

The source lists the Justices Act 1959 (Tas) and numerous subordinate instruments (Justices Rules 2003; Justices (Review) Rules 2004; Justices Amendment Rules series; Justices Regulations 2013; Justices (Restraint Orders) Rules 2013, etc.). These instruments govern magistrates’ procedures and associated procedural matters. The list serves as a reference compendium rather than doctrinal exposition in the source part.

# Magistrates Court (Criminal and General Division) Act 2019; Restraint Orders Act 2019 — new legislation references
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: statutory reference] [TOPIC: new Tasmanian legislation referenced in source] [CONCEPTS: Magistrates Court Act 2019, Restraint Orders Act 2019, reform, procedural changes, new statutes, Tasmania 2019 legislative reforms]

The source notes two pieces of “new legislation”: the Magistrates Court (Criminal and General Division) Act 2019 and the Restraint Orders Act 2019. The text records these as recent legislative developments without further doctrinal analysis in this part.

# Sammak; Reid; Swan decisions — custodial-status uncertainty (reference)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: cases affecting whether informal custody attracts Act protections] [CONCEPTS: Sammak, Reid, Swan, informal custody, custody without arrest, Tasmanian CCA, Slicer J, entitlement to Act protections] [REVIEW]

The source refers to Sammak (Tasmanian Court of Criminal Appeal) and the decisions Reid and Swan (Slicer J) as authorities creating doubt whether the protections of the Criminal Law (Detention and Interrogation) Act 1995 apply to persons in police custody who are not formally arrested. The source provides no full citations for these cases; this chunk is flagged [REVIEW] pending full citation and confirmation before final ingestion.

# Christie v Leachinsky; Gow v Davies — common-law duty applied to summary offences
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: Christie v Leachinsky [1947] AC 573; Gow v Davies [1992] 1 Tas R 1] [TOPIC: application of common-law duty to summary offences] [CONCEPTS: duty to inform, summary offences, common law, local Tasmanian application, procedural fairness]

Christie v Leachinsky [1947] AC 573 sets out the common-law duty to inform an arrested person of the reason for arrest; Gow v Davies [1992] 1 Tas R 1 is cited in the source as establishing that the common-law duty also applies to summary offences in Tasmania. Together these authorities support the proposition that the duty to inform is not confined to indictable offences and is part of procedural fairness.

# Halliday v Nevill [1984] HCA 80 — implied licence doctrine
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: Halliday v Nevill [1984] HCA 80] [TOPIC: implied licence to approach dwelling via unobstructed path] [CONCEPTS: implied licence, driveway access, unobstructed path, notice forbidding entry, legitimate purpose, civil trespass limits]

Halliday v Nevill [1984] HCA 80 held that where a path or driveway to a suburban dwelling is unobstructed and no sign or other indication forbids entry, the law implies a licence in favour of members of the public to use the path or driveway for legitimate purposes that involve no interference with occupation or property. This implied licence supports lawful approaches to the front door and is conceptually distinct from any right to enter the dwelling.

# Tasmania v Lee [2005] TASSC 117 — implied licence does not authorise entry to house (authority)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: Tasmania v Lee [2005] TASSC 117] [TOPIC: limits on implied licence re entry into home] [CONCEPTS: implied licence, no house entry, trespass, police conduct, driveway vs dwelling]

Tasmania v Lee [2005] TASSC 117 confirmed that an implied licence to approach the dwelling’s entrance does not extend to entry into the home itself without invitation or independent lawful authority; doing so would be trespass. The case distinguishes approach to the door (per Halliday) from entry into the residence.

# Ghani v Jones [1970] 1 QB 693 — third-party property seizure principle
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: Ghani v Jones [1970] 1 QB 693] [TOPIC: power to examine/seize third-party property where refusal unreasonable] [CONCEPTS: seizure third-party property, Lord Denning, reasonableness test, investigative necessity, police examination, private property rights]

Ghani v Jones [1970] 1 QB 693 (Lord Denning) held that police may be justified in examining or seizing property from a non-suspect third party in circumstances where the third party’s refusal would be “quite unreasonable” given the investigative necessity. The decision supplies hypothetical situations where inspection would be reasonable (e.g. abandoned stolen vehicle) and sets a balancing test between investigative need and property/ privacy rights.

# Police v Ronald Sines; Police v Andrew Love (NSW Local Court, 7 July 2017) — application note
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: Police v Ronald Sines; Police v Andrew Love (NSW Local Court, 7 July 2017)] [TOPIC: local application of Ghani to phone seizure] [CONCEPTS: phone seizure, recording of police action, evidence preservation, reasonable grounds, retention period, download footage]

A NSW Local Court decision (Police v Ronald Sines; Police v Andrew Love, 7 July 2017) applied Ghani v Jones to justify seizure of a bystander’s mobile phone that recorded an alleged assault on an officer. The court found police had reasonable grounds to suspect the phone contained evidence of a serious offence and that the refusal to hand over the phone was unreasonable, so temporary seizure and limited retention for downloading footage were lawful in that instance.

No doctrinal units were listed under [UNPROCESSED] in the prior coverage report. Therefore there are no additional formatted chunks to produce for this part.

### Chunk 4 — Interpretation: Ordinary meaning (Acts Interpretation Act 1931)

This chunk covers the principle of giving words their ordinary meaning under the Acts Interpretation Act 1931.

In the absence of a statutory definition it is necessary to give the word its ordinary meaning, and that means "the ordinary meaning conveyed by a provision having regard to its context in the Act and to the purpose or object of the Act (Acts interpretation Act 1931, 8B(3). Recourse may be made to the dictionary meaning "to confirm the interpretation conveyed by the ordinary meaning of the provision" (8B(1)(c) Acts interp Act.

### Chunk 5 — Reckoning of time (Acts Interpretation Act 1931 s29 and cases)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Acts Interpretation Act 1931 (Tas)] [SECTION: s29] [CITATION: Acts Interpretation Act 1931 s29 - Reckoning of Time] [CATEGORY: legal doctrine] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Reckoning of time - exclusion of first day and sentencing commencements] [CONCEPTS: reckoning of time, exclusion of first day, s29, sentencing commencement, Acts interp, Prowse v McIntyre, Forster v Jododex]

This chunk covers the reckoning of time rules under Tasmanian statutory interpretation and supporting authority.

Tasmanian statutory interpretation requires you to *exclude* the day the period is dated from when you reckon a period "dated or reckoned from a given day". *Section 29 of the Acts Interpretation Act 1931 (Tas)* states that a period reckoned from a given day "shall be reckoned exclusively of that day" --- so the first day is excluded from the calculation.

NOTE sentencing orders usually say "commencing on" so it would be inclusive of the same day, as it is express by the court. If it were to say "from x date" then it would be the next day. Confusing. Look at the Acts interp Act.

And Def's are usually given full credit for parts of days served.

Supported by consistent authority in the reckoning of time cases: *Prowse v McIntyre* \[1961\] Tas SR 9 (re exclusion of first day unless statute indicates otherwise).

General principle of law that the law is not concerned with divisions of a day, and fractions of a day are disregarded: *Prowse v McIntyre *[\[1961\] HCA 79](https://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/viewdoc/au/cases/cth/HCA/1961/79.html); [(1961) 111 CLR 264](https://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/LawCite?cit=%281961%29%20111%20CLR%20264); *Forster v Jododex Australia Pty Ltd *[\[1972\] HCA 61](https://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/viewdoc/au/cases/cth/HCA/1972/61.html); [(1972) 127 CLR 421](https://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/LawCite?cit=%281972%29%20127%20CLR%20421) at 446, per Gibbs J. In *Forster*,* *Gibbs J referred to Sir William Grant MR in *Lester v Garland *(1808) 15 VES Jun, at 257 (33 ER, at 752):

### Chunk 11 — Need to state reason (Criminal Code s301(2) and cases)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Criminal Code Act 1924 (Tas)] [SECTION: 301(2)] [CITATION: Criminal Code s301(2) - Duty to Give Notice of Process or Cause of Arrest] [CATEGORY: legal doctrine] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Duty to state reason for arrest and exceptions] [CONCEPTS: duty to state reason, s301(2), practicable, Johnson v Reeves, Wilkie v Patterson, common law exceptions]

This chunk covers the statutory duty to inform an arrested person of the cause of arrest, the practicability exception, and leading cases.

Need to state reason

 The *Criminal Code, *s301(2), provides as follows:

"(2) It is the duty of a person arresting another, whether with or without warrant, to give notice, if practicable, of the process or warrant under which he is acting, or of the cause of the arrest."

if it is pointless (i.e. they would not be able to hear the cops) to inform or attempt to inform an arrested person of the cause of the arrest, it is not "practicable" to do so within the meaning of s301(2) **Johnson v Reeves \[2004\] TASSC 110 (24 September 2004)**

Very hi standard!!!!! Even the threat of violence against police, or a volitaile sitiaution does not warrant the failure of an explanation for not stating the reasons see **Wilkie v Patterson \[2016\] TASMC 6**

### Chunk 12 — "Found offending" (Kim Hibble v ... [1997] TASSC 52) — case authority
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Kim Hibble v Jody William Phegan; Kim Hibble v Dean Mark Jenkins; David John Brooks v Leigh Andrew Phegan; Kim Hibble v Kim Maree Ogden; David John Brooks v Andrew David Hulse Files \[1997\] TASSC 52 (20 May 1997)] [CITATION: Kim Hibble v Phegan & others [1997] TASSC 52 - Found Offending and Arrest Power] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: "Found offending" construed; police belief on reasonable grounds; exercise of s34B(3) powers] [CONCEPTS: found offending, s34B(3), arrest without warrant, reasonable grounds, Lynch v Hargrave, Trebeck v Croudace, Simmons v Millingen, exercising arrest power]

This chunk covers the judicial reasoning in Kim Hibble v Phegan & others concerning "found offending" and the lawfulness of arrests made on reasonable belief.

"Found offending"

**Kim Hibble v Jody William Phegan, Kim Hibble v Dean Mark Jenkins David John Brooks v Leigh Andrew Phegan, Kim Hibble v Kim Maree Ogden, David John Brooks v Andrew David Hulse Files \[1997\] TASSC 52 (20 May 1997)**

*48. The Act, s34B(3) provides the authority for a police officer to arrest without warrant a person committing certain offences, including the offences of resisting and assaulting a police officer in the execution of his duty. Some suggestion was made during the course of argument that this power of arrest is governed by the provisions of the Act, s55. I do not accept that submission because s34B is not a section in respect of which it is "specially provided" that a police officer may arrest without warrant a person found offending against that provision. What is provided for by s34B(3) is that a police officer may arrest without warrant a person "committing" an offence.*

*49. In the course of argument, considerable reference was made to Lynch v Hargrave [\[1971\] VicRp 11](http://www.austlii.edu.au/au/cases/vic/VicRp/1971/11.html); [\[1971\] VR 99.](http://classic.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/LawCite?cit=%5b1971%5d%20VR%2099?stem=0&synonyms=0&query=poa1935140%20s13) Relevantly, that case concerned the expression "found offending", and, in particular, whether, before it could be said that a person was "found offending", it needed to be established objectively that that person in fact was offending or whether it was sufficient that the person finding the alleged offender believed on reasonable grounds that the person was offending. Relying on Trebeck v Croudace [\[1918\] 1 KB 158](http://classic.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/LawCite?cit=%5b1918%5d%201%20KB%20158?stem=0&synonyms=0&query=poa1935140%20s13), McInerney J held at 109 - 110:*

*"Accordingly, I am of the view the power of arrest of a person 'found offending' is to be regarded as exercisable according to the facts as then known to the member of the police force. Whether or not the person arrested was in fact and in law offending in the relevant time is a matter for subsequent determination of the court."*

*50. There is no reason why the same principle should not be applied to the word "offending". I do apply it, so that the power conferred by s34B(3) is exercisable whenever a police officer believes, on reasonable grounds, that the relevant person is committing a relevant offence created by s34B. It follows that the lawfulness of the arrests of Leigh Phegan and Hulse is not determined by whether they actually assaulted Lee. The arrests may have been lawful and, accordingly, Rowlands may have been acting in the execution of his duty at relevant times even if the obstruction of Lee was not established.*

*51. Applying Lynch v Hargrave (supra) and the well-established meaning of "committing" (see Simmons v Millingen [\[1846\] EngR 275](http://www.worldlii.org/int/cases/EngR/1846/275.html); [(1846) 2 CB 524](http://classic.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/LawCite?cit=%281846%29%202%20CB%20524?stem=0&synonyms=0&query=poa1935140%20s13); Hanway v Boultbee [\[1830\] EngR 888](http://www.worldlii.org/int/cases/EngR/1830/888.html); [(1830) 1 M & Rob 15](http://classic.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/LawCite?cit=%281830%29%201%20Macl%20%26%20R%2015?stem=0&synonyms=0&query=poa1935140%20s13), [174 ER 6](http://classic.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/LawCite?cit=174%20ER%206?stem=0&synonyms=0&query=poa1935140%20s13); R v Smith [(1876) 14 SCR (NSW) 419)](http://classic.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/LawCite?cit=%281876%29%2014%20SCR%20%28NSW%29%20419?stem=0&synonyms=0&query=poa1935140%20s13) I construe s34B(3) as conferring a power of arrest in those cases:*

*1 where a person is taken flagrente delicto doing the prohibited act;*

*2 where the arresting officer believes on reasonable grounds that a person taken is taken flagrente delicto doing the prohibited act; and*

*3 where a person is taken immediately and in fresh pursuit after being found in either of those circumstances.*

*52. A police officer's power of arrest is to be exercised by him or her personally and on the facts known to him or her. The mere direction of a senior officer provides no lawful authority for the making of an arrest by the police officer to whom the direction is given. An arrest is a serious interference with personal liberty. That liberty may not be infringed except by lawful authority. The direction of a senior officer does not provide that lawful authority. Any officer who complied with the direction given by Lee, did so at his peril unless there was some other basis which authorised that officer to make the arrest.*

---

### Chunk 13 — Custody defined (Criminal Law (Detention and Interrogation) Act 1995 s3)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Criminal Law (Detention and Interrogation) Act 1995 (Tas)] [SECTION: 3] [CITATION: Criminal Law (Detention and Interrogation) Act 1995 s3 - Definition of Custody] [CATEGORY: legal doctrine] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Definition of 'in custody' for the purposes of the Act] [CONCEPTS: in custody, lawful arrest, warrant, Criminal Code s27, detention and interrogation, custody officer]

This chunk covers the statutory definition of "in custody" under the Criminal Law (Detention and Interrogation) Act 1995.

Custody defined:

Criminal Law (Detention and Interrogation) Act 1995

3.   Interpretation

(1)  In this Act, unless the contrary intention appears --

(2)  For the purposes of this Act, a person is in custody if he or she is --

(a) under lawful arrest by warrant; or

(b) under lawful arrest under [section 27 of the ](https://www.legislation.tas.gov.au/view/html/inforce/2015-10-13/act-1924-069#GS27@EN)[Criminal Code](https://www.legislation.tas.gov.au/view/html/inforce/2015-10-13/act-1924-069) or a provision of any other Act.

### Chunk 14 — The duty to give reasons for arrest (common law and statutory discussion)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Criminal Code Act 1924 (Tas)] [SECTION: s301(2)] [CITATION: Duty to Give Reasons for Arrest - Common Law and s301(2)] [CATEGORY: legal doctrine] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Duty to inform arrested person of reasons; common law authorities and exceptions] [CONCEPTS: duty to inform, Christie v Leachinsky, common law, practicable, exceptions, right to explanation]

This chunk covers the common law and statutory duty to inform a person of the reasons for arrest, plus the rationale and exceptions.

The duty to give reasons for arrest 

- Except when not practicable see **Johnson v Reeves \[2004\] TASSC 110 for a discussion on this**

When making an arrest a police officer has a duty to inform the person being arrested of the reasons for that arrest. This duty exists at both common law (Christie v Leachinsky \[1947\] AC 573) (which applies to summary offences ((Gow v Davies \[1992\] 1 Tas R 1; Criminal Code Act s 4(3).

))28) and under s 301(2) of the Criminal Code (which applies to indictable offences). This section of the Code provides: (2) It is the duty of a person arresting another, whether with or without warrant, to give notice, if practicable, of the process or warrant under which he is acting or the cause of the arrest.

As can be seen from s 301(2), where it is not 'practicable', there is no duty to inform of the reasons for arrest. Examples of when it might be impracticable to give the reasons for arrest are if the arrestee is blind drunk, if the arrestee is deaf and cannot lip-read or if the arrestee responds with an immediate counterattack or by running away.

Another exception to the rule under the common law is that where the reason for the arrest is obvious, for example if a robber is caught red-handed, it may not be necessary to give the reason for arrest (Christie v Leachinsky \[1947\] AC 573, at 587) . This exception is not mentioned in the Code provision.

Christie v Leachinsky is the principal common law authority explaining this duty. This case discusses the history of the duty, dating it back to at least 1755, and details the two justifications for the duty. The first justification is that being arrested is such a substantial interference with a person's liberty, that it must be justified and that the person being arrested should be informed of that justification. Secondly, informing the arrestee of the reasons for the arrest gives the person the opportunity to offer an explanation to clear themselves and be released as soon as possible.

---

### Chunk 15 — The problem defined: Protections provided by the Act (checklist)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Criminal Law (Detention and Interrogation) Act 1995 (Tas)] [SECTION: ss4-6, 15-16] [CITATION: Criminal Law (Detention and Interrogation) Act 1995 - Protections Checklist] [CATEGORY: checklist] [TYPE: checklist] [TOPIC: Statutory protections for arrested persons under the Act] [CONCEPTS: right to magistrate, right to silence, right to contact, interpreter, reasonable questioning time, custody officer duties]

This chunk covers the statutory protections afforded to persons in custody under the Criminal Law (Detention and Interrogation) Act 1995, presented as a checklist.

Tactical checklist: Protections provided by the Act

The rights of citizens who have been arrested are safeguarded by a number of statutory and common law protections. In Tasmania, the majority of these protections are contained in the Criminal Law (Detention and Interrogation) Act 1995 (Tas). They include:

- The requirement that the arrestee be taken before a magistrate or justice as soon as practicable after arrest unless released unconditionally or released on police bail: s 4(1);
- The requirement that the arrestee be informed of the right to silence prior to questioning: s 4(5);
- The requirement that the arrestee be informed of the right to communicate with a friend or relative and a legal practitioner prior to questioning: s 6(1);
- The requirement (subject to s 6(3) and (6)) that the police defer any questioning and investigation to enable the arrestee to make or attempt to make the communication: s 6(2);
- The requirement (subject to s 6(3) and (6)) that the police afford the arrestee reasonable facilities to make the communication: s 6(7);
- The provision of an interpreter when needed: s 5;
- The requirement that the arrestee be questioned only for a reasonable time: s 4(2)(a); reasonableness in this context is determined by the considerations set out in s 4(4);
- The requirement that the arrestee be taken before a custody officer without delay and placed in the custody of the custody officer: s 15(1);
- The requirement that the custody officer perform the duties in relation to the arrestee imposed by s 15(2) and (4); and
- The requirement that the custody officer ensure that the arrestee is treated in accordance with the Act: s 16 (1).

---

### Chunk 16 — To whom do the Act's protections apply? (Volunteers; Under lawful arrest)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Criminal Law (Detention and Interrogation) Act 1995 (Tas)] [CITATION: Application of Detention and Interrogation Act - Volunteers vs Arrested Persons] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Whether protections apply to volunteers, arrested persons, and those in custody though not formally arrested] [CONCEPTS: volunteers, in custody, informal arrest, Sammak, Reid, Swan, protections applicability]

This chunk covers who is entitled to the protections of the Criminal Law (Detention and Interrogation) Act: volunteers, those under lawful arrest, and those in police custody without formal arrest.

To whom do the Act's protections apply?

'In custody' under the Criminal Law (Detention and Interrogation) Act

The Criminal Law (Detention and Interrogation) Act affords the protections contained in sections 4-6, 15 and 16 to people who are 'in custody'. Section 3(2) provides that a person is 'in custody' for the purposes of the Act if he or she is:

(a) under lawful arrest by warrant; or

(b) under lawful arrest under section 27 of the Criminal Code or a provision of any other Act.

Because the application of the Act depends upon whether a person is under lawful arrest, it is critically important to determine what exactly is meant by the term, 'under lawful arrest'. The term 'arrest' is not defined in the Act. In practice, it can often be difficult to ascertain a suspect's precise custodial status. The case law has distinguished three major custodial categories: (a) persons who are "voluntarily assisting the police with their enquiries", (b) persons who are under arrest and, (c) in Tasmania, since the decision of the Tasmanian Court of Criminal Appeal in Sammak (followed in Reid and Swan) persons who are in the custody of the police though not under arrest.

1. Volunteers

A person who is "voluntarily assisting the police with their inquiries" is not under arrest, either at common law or under the Act. In fact, such a person is, in the eyes of the law, not detained at all and is at liberty to leave. Accordingly, it was felt, at the time that the Act was enacted, that volunteers did not need the protections afforded by sections 4-6 of the Act and, therefore, those protections were not applied to them.

2. Under lawful arrest

In contrast, a person who is under arrest is not free to leave and, where the arrest is lawful, must submit to the authority of the arrestor. For an arrest to be lawful there must be a power to arrest and an arrest in fact. Essentially, the latter requires that the police make it clear to the suspect that he or she is no longer free. There is no set formula for what must be said and done to effect a lawful arrest. Any form of words may be used which, in the circumstances of the case, make it clear to the arrestee that he or she is under compulsion. Where there has been a formal, lawful arrest the protections provided by the Act will clearly apply to the arrestee. However, at common law, a person may also be found to be under arrest, even if there has been no formal arrest, if the police behave in such a way as to give him or her reasonable grounds for believing that he or she is not free to leave. It is not clear whether the protections provided by the Act apply to a person who is under informal arrest in this sense although since the decisions of the Court of Criminal Appeal in Sammak and of Slicer J in Reid and Swan, there is real doubt that they do.

---

### Chunk 18 — Power of entry to effect arrest (s26A Criminal Code) and implied licence
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Criminal Code Act 1924 (Tas)] [SECTION: s26A] [CITATION: Criminal Code s26A - Power to Enter Premises to Effect Arrest; Implied Licence Cases] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Power to enter premises to effect arrest and implied licence doctrine] [CONCEPTS: power of entry, implied licence, Halliday v Nevill, Tasmania v Lee, driveway entry, trespass, police entry, s26A]

This chunk covers the statutory power to enter premises to effect arrest and the limits of implied licence to approach a dwelling.

Power to enter premises to effect power of arrest (s26A CODE)

Implied licence 

Implied licence to enter driveway or path if no signs to contrary

*Halliday v Nevill*, [\[1984\] HCA 80](http://www8.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/viewdoc/au/cases/cth/HCA/1984/80.html); the majority of the High Court held, and I read from the head note:

"If the path or driveway leading to the entrance of a suburban dwelling-house is left unobstructed and with entrance gate unlocked and there is no notice or other indication that entry by visitors generally or particularly designated visitors is forbidden or unauthorized, the law will imply a licence in favour of any member of the public to go upon the path or driveway for any legitimate purpose that in itself involves no interference with the occupier's possession or injury to the occupier, his guests or his or their property."

Implied licence limited though!

However, in **Tasmania v Lee \[2005\] TASSC 117** it was clarified the It was held that there was an implied licence on the part of the police officer to go onto the driveway or path leading up to the door of the house, and it is implicit from that that there was no power to enter the house itself. To do so without invitation would amount to a trespass.

---

### Chunk 19 — Power to seize (common law and cases; Ghani v Jones; NSW example)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Power to Seize Evidence - Ghani v Jones and subsequent examples] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Common law power to seize property (including from third parties); tests and examples] [CONCEPTS: seizure, third party seizure, Ghani v Jones, unreasonable refusal, mobile phone, police seizure, evidence preservation, Magistrate Swain decision]

This chunk covers the common law foundations for seizure of property (including from third parties), illustrative guidance from Ghani v Jones and an application in a NSW Local Court decision.

Potentially a common law power to seize evidence or potential sources of evidence of 3rd parties English case of Ghani v Jones \[1970\] 1 QB 693

Power to seize property from third parties It has become common practice for police to seize mobile phones from bystanders who are recording incidents in which they are not criminally involved. Phones are seized (and sometimes retained for long periods) for the purpose of preserving and downloading the footage for use as evidence. The personal inconvenience to the phone's owner, not to mention the potential interference with their privacy, can be significant.

In Ghani v Jones, Lord Denning held that seizure of property from non-suspects may be justified in some circumstances. However, in order to enliven this power, the person's refusal to hand over the property "must be quite unreasonable". He provided (at pp 1704 and 1705) some hypothetical examples of situations in which a third party would be unreasonable in refusing to allow the police to examine their property. For example, some bank robbers "borrow" a private car and then abandon it by the roadside. Police wish to examine the car for fingerprints; the owner of the car would be unreasonable in refusing to allow them to do so. The seizure of a phone from a bystander was recently considered by Her Honour Magistrate Swain in the NSW Local Court in Police v Ronald Sines; Police v Andrew Love (date of decision 7 July 2017; not published on NSW Caselaw). Mr Love was using his mobile phone to record an altercation between his friend Mr Sines and a police officer. Police asked him to hand over his phone, which he refused to do. Police then tried to seize the phone, and Mr Love threw it on the ground, from where it was retrieved by police. Applying Ghani v Jones, her Honour held (at paras 118-119 of her judgment) that the police officer had the power to seize the phone because:
• police had reasonable grounds to believe that the offence of assaulting police had been committed;
• assaulting police in the execution of their duty is a serious offence;
• "the police had reasonable grounds for believing that the phone contained material which could prove the serious offence alleged", as Mr Love appeared to have filmed the incident;
• although Mr Love was not criminally involved in the assault police, "the police had reasonable grounds to believe that Mr Love's refusal was unreasonable"; and
• the phone would not be kept for any longer than reasonably necessary, as the police officer told Mr Love that his phone would be returned after any footage was downloaded. Her Honour did not elaborate on why Mr Love's refusal to hand over his phone may have been unreasonable. It is also questionable whether she correctly applied the test set down by Lord Denning. Needless

# Search and Seizure — Tasmanian Criminal Law

No unprocessed doctrinal units were identified in the previous coverage report; there are therefore no additional chunks to format.

### Chunk 1 — Tasmania v Hodges (case notes on consent and lawful entry)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Tasmania v Hodges [2022] TASSC 45] [CITATION: Tasmania v Hodges (2022) - Consent and Lawful Entry] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Not consenting/not resisting does not equal consent; lawful entry precondition] [CONCEPTS: search consent, lawful entry, hotel room, private premises, reasonable belief, hotel occupancy, Hodges, warrant requirement, s6 Search Warrants Act]

This chunk covers: Tasmania v Hodges observations on consent and pre-conditions to search powers.

Not resisting or not saying no to a search does not amount to consent. Tasmania v Hodges [2022] TASSC 45 (26 July 2022)

Pre-condition to search powers on reasonable belief under MOD is "lawful" entry to private premise. Must have warrant etc. Private premises also held to include hotel room under occupancy in certain circumstances. Tasmania v Hodges [2022] TASSC 45 (26 July 2022)

### Chunk 4 — Search warrants: phone as "document" under Evidence Act; flight mode practicalities
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Evidence Act 2001 (Tas)] [CITATION: Evidence Act 2001 (Tas) - Phone as document / seizure practicalities] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Phone considered a "document"; practical limits on examining phone contents; steps to prevent destruction] [CONCEPTS: phone search, document definition, Evidence Act, seizure, flight mode, remote wiping, forensic access, external apps, passwords]

This chunk covers: Phone as evidence, what police can examine and actions to prevent destruction.

Re: Phone -- Phone falls within *Evidence Act* definition of a "document." Can examine contents, but that likely wouldn't extend to external applications. General distinction is what can be examined while phone is in "flight mode." Not sure about changing passwords, but Police can take steps to prevent destruction of evidence (namely putting the phone in flight mode to prevent remote wiping).

---

### Chunk 5 — Police powers of search and seizure: scope of warrant; Crowley v Murphy; Ghani v Jones tests; Search Warrants Act s6(e)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Search Warrants Act 1997 (Tas) s 6] [CITATION: Search Warrants Act 1997 (Tas) s6 - Scope of Search Warrant and Ghani Tests] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Limitations on scope of warrant, Crowley v Murphy, Ghani v Jones fivefold test, s6(e) seizure powers] [CONCEPTS: scope of warrant, Crowley v Murphy, Ghani v Jones, s6 Search Warrants Act, evidential material, fruit of crime, seizure limits, reasonable grounds]

This chunk covers: Scope of search warrants and when police can seize items not particularised.

POLICE POWERS OF SEARCH AND SEIZURE

Scope of the search warrant (i.e. seizing items not particularised in the warrant).

Police can't use a warrant to go on a fishing exercise: Crowley v Murphy.

However, whether they can lawfully seize items not mentioned in the warrant will depend on whether the conditions from Ghani v Jones, which are now embodied (mostly) in s. 6 of the Search Warrants Act (tas), can be satisfied:

1. Must relate to a serious offence;

2. Property must be the fruity of the crime or the means by which it was committed, or material evidence of its commission; and

3. That the person in possession was involved in the commission of the crime either as a principal or accessory.

In addition:

4. Police must keep them for no longer that necessary to complete investigations or to preserve as evidence; and

5. The lawfulness of the police conduct must be judged at the time of the seizure, not by what happens later (hindsight or subsequent events cannot justify the seizure).

Under s6(e) of the Search Warrants Act the executing officer can seize any material found on the premises that they believe on reasonable grounds to be either:

- Evidential material in relation to the offence which the warrant relates; or

- Evidential material in relation to another offence.

### Chunk 6 — Common law rights re search and seizure (Coco v R and commentary)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Coco v R (1994) - Common law rights and trespass] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Common law protections against unauthorised entry and search; Coco v R citation] [CONCEPTS: common law, trespass, Coco v R, property rights, entry without warrant, exclusion of others, human rights, search authorization]

This chunk covers: Common law rights as limiting searches and seizures.

3.3 Common law rights

The only time that search and seizure is permitted is when pursuant to arrest; pursuant to a warrant or with consent.

Coco v R (1994) 179 CLR 427 at pp 435-6 per Mason CJ, Brennan, Gaudron and McHugh JJ

"Every unauthorized entry upon private property is a trespass, the right of a person in possession or entitled to possession of premises to exclude others from those premises being a fundamental common law right." (emphasis added) @ 435

Chapters 7 & 8 in Gans et al, Criminal Process and Human Rights (Federation Press, 2011)

---

### Chunk 7 — When can you search at common law? R v Jefferies example
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: R v Jefferies [1994] 1 NZLR 290 - Roadstop vehicle search] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Roadstop vehicle search and interaction with statute/BOR; common law powers] [CONCEPTS: roadstop, vehicle search, common law, R v Jefferies, reasonable suspicion, statutory interaction, NZ case]

This chunk covers: R v Jefferies factual example of a vehicle stop and boot search.

When can you search at common law?

R v Jefferies [1994] 1 NZLR 290 at 300-301

A police constable in an unmarked police car heard over the police radio that a light blue car had left the scene of an armed robbery. It was the escape vehicle, and believed to be heading in the P.C's direction. Presently, along came a light blue vehicle, which the PC stopped. Conversation between the four men and the PC made it clear that they were being stopped in relation to the armed robbery.

Without asking for consent the PC searched the boot of the car and found $30,000 worth of marijuana. None of the men were involved in the armed robbery.

Was this search lawful under statute? How did the statute interact with the NZ Bill of Rights? What about at CL powers of search?

Section 60 of the Arms Act gave the PC grounds to search on the reasonable suspicion of weapons being on site. There was a procedural responsibility which was not adhered to -- namely identifying himself. Despite this is it was still held to be lawfully effected.

---

### Chunk 8 — Search and seizure: common law and statute (doctrinal discussion)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Search and seizure - common law and statute doctrinal notes] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Definitions of search and seizure; limits of common law search powers; search incident to arrest] [CONCEPTS: definition of search, seizure, arrest, common law limits, Barnett & Grant v Campbell, Lindley v Rutter, personal search, Police Act s57A, conversion, trespass]

This chunk covers: The doctrinal distinction between search and seizure and the limits of common law powers.

Search and seizure: common law and statute

A search is an examination of a person or property and a seizure is a taking of what is discovered. Seizure under the Fourth Amendment to the United States Constitution also extends to seizure of persons which in New Zealand might more readily [*33] be dealt with under the arbitrary arrest and detention provisions of s 22.

The common law does not recognise a right to search anyone in the absence of an arrest. Nor does it recognise a general right of search on arrest. As an incident to an arrest a police officer may search an accused and take possession of any property tending to show that person's guilt (Barnett & Grant v Campbell (1902) 21 NZLR 484). As well as searching for such property the police may search for objects with which a person might do himself or herself or any others injury, or which might be employed to facilitate escape. As Donaldson LJ observed in Lindley v Rutter (1980) 72 Cr App R 1, 6:

"It is the duty of any constable who lawfully has a prisoner in his charge to take all reasonable measures to ensure that the prisoner does not escape or assist others to do so, does not injure himself or others, does not destroy or dispose of evidence and does not commit further crime such, for example, as malicious damage to property. This list is not exhaustive, but it is sufficient for present purposes. What measures are reasonable in the discharge of this duty will depend upon the likelihood that the particular [*34] prisoner will do any of these things unless prevented. That in turn will involve the constable in considering the known or apparent disposition and sobriety of the prisoner. What can never be justified is the adoption of any particular measures without regard to all the circumstances of the particular case."

A personal search is a restraint on freedom and an affront to human dignity. It is exercisable at common law only for good reason. The Courts will examine any claimed justification with great care and generally speaking no one should be required to submit to that restraint unless he or she knows in substance the reason for the search (Brazil v Chief Constable of Surrey [1983] 1 WLR 1155, 1162).

Special statutes authorise personal including body searches in particular cases and s 57A of the Police Act 1958 adds to the common law right of search on arrest. It allows for the search and seizure of all money and all or any other property found on or in the possession of a person taken into lawful custody and at a police station or in any other premises or vehicle used for police purposes.

The common law did not allow any entry and search of private property by officers of the [*35] state without permission of the owner or occupier except under a warrant issued by a justice acting on evidence that there was strong cause to believe that stolen goods were concealed there.

---

### Chunk 9 — Judicial/legislative criteria for issuing search warrants (warrant grounds)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Warrant issuing criteria - District Court / justice / Registrar] [CATEGORY: legal doctrine] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Grounds a judge must be satisfied of to issue a search warrant] [CONCEPTS: search warrant grounds, issuance criteria, judge satisfaction, evidence of offences, intended use, property as evidence]

This chunk covers: Criteria for issuing a search warrant as set out by the judiciary.

When can you search at common law?

A District Court Judge, justice or Registrar may issue a search warrant if satisfied that there is reasonable ground for believing that there is present there:

(a) Any thing upon or in respect of which any offence punishable by imprisonment has been or is suspected of having been committed; or

(b) Any thing which there is reasonable ground to believe will be evidence as to the commission of any such offence; or

(c) Any thing which there is reasonable ground to believe is intended to be used for the purpose of committing any such offence . . .

Under other specific statutes conditions under which warrants may be issued or warrantless searches undertaken are expressed in quite different ways and there is no single test or criterion running through the legislation.

---

### Chunk 10 — When is a search "unreasonable"
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Unreasonableness of a search - doctrinal note] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Tests for when a search is unreasonable; s21 and balancing interests] [CONCEPTS: unreasonable search, s21, privacy, Entick v Carrington, balancing test, government restraint, search manner]

This chunk covers: When a search will be "unreasonable" under doctrine.

When is a search "unreasonable"

A search will be unreasonable if the circumstances giving rise to the search make the search itself unreasonable or if an otherwise initially reasonable search is carried out in an unreasonable manner.

Section 21 is a negative provision in that it is a restraint on governmental action and confers no powers on the government. In particular it does not empower a "reasonable" search.

The test of unreasonableness requires consideration of the values underlying the right and a balancing of the relevant values and public interests involved. As apparent from Entick v Carrington, where the action was for trespass to property, the common law concern was for the protections of property rights: entry on to private property was a trespass and the seizure of property was a conversion. However both in America and Canada privacy values have become an accepted basis for the constitutional protection.

---

### Chunk 11 — Grounds of the Search: reasonable grounds to suspect or believe — Lindley v Rutter
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Lindley v Rutter [1981] QB 128 - lawful purpose and subjective basis] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Requirement for lawful purpose and officer's state of mind for searches] [CONCEPTS: Lindley v Rutter, reasonable grounds, lawful purpose, standing order, subjective mindset, body search, bra removal]

This chunk covers: Lindley v Rutter on the need for an officer to have a lawful purpose and to apply her mind.

Lindley v Rutter [1981] QB 128

A drunken woman was taken to a police station where her bra was taken off. She was arrested -- resisted search -- two officers searched her believing they were under a standing order to search every female prisoner and remove the bra for their own protection.

There is a duty of an officer to have a lawful purpose for search -- a standing order from a senior officer is not enough. There has to be a basis.

Held: If they officer's mind did not apply itself to having a lawful purpose for the search, than the search was unlawful (subjective mindset of officer required level of analysis). There was no legal basis to search her -- standing order not sufficient.

---

### Chunk 12 — George v Rockett — reasonable suspicion vs belief
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: George v Rockett (1990) 170 CLR 104 - Reasonable suspicion definition] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Definition of reasonable suspicion; distinction between suspicion and belief; need for factual basis] [CONCEPTS: George v Rockett, reasonable suspicion, reasonable belief, suspicion vs belief, specificity, warrant particularity]

This chunk covers: The High Court's statements on reasonable suspicion and its limits.

George v Rockett (1990) 170 CLR 104

- Reasonable suspicion is: the existence of facts which are sufficient to induce that state of mind in a reasonable person.' @ 112

- Suspicion is 'a state of conjecture or surmise where proof is lacking' @ 115

- 'The facts which can reasonably ground a suspicion may be quite insufficient reasonably to ground a belief, yet some factual basis for the suspicion must be shown.' @ 115. (George v Rockett) -- so suspicion is less than a belief.

See 111 of judgement. Reference and description of the search is the basis of est the boundaries the search powers. There must be specificity in the warrant -- the type of evidence soiught must be detailed

---

### Chunk 13 — Nguyen v Elliott (unreported) — unlawful search and resistance
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Nguyen v Elliott (Supreme Court, Vic, Hedigan J, No 7540 of 1994) - Unreported] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Unlawful search, resistance justified, police not in course of duty] [CONCEPTS: Nguyen v Elliott, unlawful search, resistance justified, reasonable basis, police duty, evidence exclusion, Victorian unreported]

This chunk covers: Nguyen v Elliott facts and the legal consequence that resistance to an unlawful search was justified.

Nguyen v Elliott (unreported, Supreme Court, Victoria, Hedigan J, No 7540 of 1994, 6 February 1995)

(Note Tasmanian has a higher standard of reasonable belief than this Vic example)

The act of a suspect kicking out and contacting a police officer , is not out of proportion to the threat of being searched against ones will, and that any bruising which may follow is justified in the circumstances where the it is an unwarranted search.

The subsequent charge for actions following an arrest on this basis is not grounds upon which the def can be held accountable. One cannot be guilty of resiting officer in course of duty when an unwarranted search is clearly not in the course of an officer's duty.

F. Police watching group of Asian youths in park (were known to have been involved in other offences). When they saw the police they behaved suspiciously (i.e. looking around etc). Eventually approached one of the boys and said they wanted to search. He objected. Police persisted and eventually he kicked a police officer (bit one during scuffle). Continued to resist and was then handcuffed and charged him with numerous offences relating to the resistance. No drugs found on him.

H. Accused defended the charges on the grounds that the police were not in the execution of their duty. They had no reasonable basis for the search. TJ and appeal judge agreed. Held that the police had no right to search the young men as there was no reasonable basis for the belief that the youths were in possession of drugs. Accused could thus not be prosecuted for the offences.

But, the only reason the Court of Criminal Appeal upheld the decision was that they said the TJ had a better basis to judge the demeanour of the witnesses. Clear they were reluctant to do this. The whole tenor of the decision is that they wanted to agree with the police.

---

### Chunk 14 — MacLeod; Beneficial Finance — issuing officer standards and obligations
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: MacLeod (1991) 61 A Crim R 465 & Beneficial Finance Corp v AFP (1991) 31 FCR 523 - Issuing officer obligations] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Subjective standard for issuing officer; obligation to clear ambiguities] [CONCEPTS: issuing officer, subjective satisfaction, MacLeod, Beneficial Finance, Jackson v Mijovich, warrant clarity, enquiries, information under oath]

This chunk covers: The standard and obligations of the issuing officer when granting warrants.

MacLeod (1991) 61 A Crim R 465

Issuing officer must be satisfied on info given to them under oath that there is reasonable grounds - subjective standard of the actual officer. Not enough that someone else is satisfied that there is such a suspicion.

Held. George v Rockett was applied by Slicer J here. See para 8-9 for a summary of the law in this area:

• it is the state of mind of the issuing officer that is relevant.

• issuing officer must satisfy himself or herself that the necessary criteria are present

• issuing officer cannot rely merely on the suspicion of the informant

• need for particularity in relation to object and the offence; mere reference to the relevant section is not enough.

Beneficial Finance Corp v AFP (1991) 31 FCR 523

H. The issuing officer doesn't have an obligation to go behind the warrant and investigate further (even if incomplete). However, there is an obligation to clear up ambiguities or make enquiries if they have concerns. Also see Jackson v Mijovich (1991) 23 ALD 470.

---

### Chunk 15 — Sniffer dogs: reliability and legislative differences
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Sniffer dogs - practical note and literature] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Drug detection dogs as basis for searches; jurisdictional differences and reliability] [CONCEPTS: sniffer dogs, drug detection, reasonable grounds, Tasmanian law, accuracy rates, reliability, Gans et al, Criminal Process and Human Rights]

This chunk covers: How sniffer dog indications interact with reasonable grounds and the empirical reliability issue.

Sniffer Dogs: The Right to Security of the Person Chapter 7 in Gans et al, Criminal Process and Human Rights (Federation Press, 2011) pp 268 -- 270; 273 - 275

In Qld, NT and SA, legislation expressly provides that an indication by a drug detection dog of the presence of a drug provides police with a reasonable ground for a search (@ 268)

- Tas doesn't have legislation on this

- Interesting statistics: some dogs have a 90-92% accuracy rate (Chevy, from Canada), whilst others, such as NSW dogs have a reliability rate of 26%.

- However, some behaviours + sniffer dogs will undoubtedly constitute a reasonable ground for a search: consider a man, who upon seeing police with a sniffer dog starts muttering 'fuck fuck fuck' to himself. Reasonable suspicion? (@ 275)

### Chunk 16 — Statutory powers of search — checklist of relevant statutes
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Statutory powers of search - Tasmanian and Commonwealth statutes checklist] [CATEGORY: checklist] [TYPE: checklist] [TOPIC: Statutes that create or modify search powers in Tasmania and Commonwealth] [CONCEPTS: statutory powers, Crimes Act 1914, Police Offences Act, Search Warrants Act, Family Violence Act, Poisons Act, Police Powers (Public Safety) Act, Misuse of Drugs Act]

This chunk covers: Key statutes to check for search powers.

This is a checklist of statutes that may authorise searches:

- Crimes Act 1914 (Cth) ss 3ZE -- 3ZI

- Police Offences Act 1935 (Tas), ss 57, 58, 58A, 58B

- Poisons Act 1971 (Tas), ss 90A(1)(b), 90B & 90C

- Search Warrants Act 1997 (Tas) s 6(2)(a)

- Family Violence Act 2004 (Tas), s 10(3) (Where family violence in affect, search powers exist.)

- Police Powers (Public Safety) Act 2005, ss 18, 19, 22, 23

- Misuse of Drugs Act 2001 (Tas), ss 29, 30 (Reasonable belief).

- (Also note: special statutes authorise searches for poisons, liquor, etc; accused persons in custody incidental to arrest)
