### Chunk 17 — Personal Search (reiteration of Lindley and Donaldson LJ quote)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Personal Search - Lindley v Rutter and Donaldson LJ quote] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Personal searches as affront to dignity and officer obligations] [CONCEPTS: personal search, dignity, Lindley v Rutter, Donaldson LJ, reasonable measures, prisoner safety, statutory searches, s57A Police Act]

This chunk covers: Personal searches and judicial commentary on their gravity.

Personal Search

Lindley v Rutter [1981] QB 128

Standing orders to search any female person arrested and placed in a cell, and to remove their brassiere.

Was the PC acting in execution of her duty when she searched the defendant, and removed the defendant's brassiere?

Because she formed no state of mind -- no purpose in performing the search, it was unlawful

"So far as searches are concerned, he should appreciate that they involve an affront to the dignity and privacy of the individual. Furthermore, there are degrees of affront involved in such a search. Clearly going through someone's pockets or handbag is less of an affron than a body search. In every case a police officer ordering a search or depriving a prisoner of property should have a very good reason for doing so." Donaldson LJ @ 135

Nguyen v Elliott (see earlier) — one officer 'merely curious' as to whether an Asian youth had drugs on him, rather than having reasonable suspicion.

Unlawful search meant that resistance was permitted, and subsequent arrest was unlawful and resistance was permitted.

---

### Chunk 18 — Search of premises: Entick v Carrington; Dillon v O'Brien & Davies; Ghani v Jones fivefold test
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Entick v Carrington (1765); Dillon v O'Brien & Davies (1887); Ghani v Jones [1970] 1 QB 693 - Search of premises and Ghani fivefold test] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Historic and common law authorities on searches of premises and the Ghani fivefold seizure test] [CONCEPTS: Entick v Carrington, Dillon v O'Brien, Ghani v Jones, search premises, trespass, seizure tests, Lord Denning, material evidence, lawful arrest search]

This chunk covers: Historic common law authorities and the Ghani fivefold test for seizure on premises.

Search of Premises

Entick v Carrington (1765) 2 Wilson KB 275; (1765) 19 St.T 129

"The poorest man may in his cottage bid Defiance to all the forces of the crown. It May be frail -- its roof may shake -- the Wind may blow through it -- the storm may Enter -- the rain may enter -- but the king of England cannot enter -- all his force Dares not cross the threshold of the Ruined tenement. So be it -- unless he has Justification by law."

There is a conflict of authority between the UK and Australia

Dillon v O'Brien & Davies (1887) 16 Cox CC 245 -- warrant for arrest

Can search property where person is arrested:

"... it is clear, and beyond doubt, that, at least in cases of treason and felony, constables ... are entitled, upon a lawful arrest by them of one charged ... to take and detain property found in his possession which will form material evidence in his prosecution for that crime...' @ 249

Applied to misdemeanours as well

Ghani v Jones [1970] 1 QB 693

This case represents the 'high water mark'

Held. Per Lord Denning: it is settled law that where the police enter premises pursuant to search warrant or pursuant to power of arrest in relation to serious offence; they are entitled to search for and take any goods in relation to that offence. If during the search they find other goods relating to other events they are entitled to seize them as well provided they behave reasonably and detain the goods for no longer than is necessary.

Fivefold test of Lord Denning MR:

Can seize item when legally on the premises, without the consent of the owner if:

1. Reasonable grounds for believing serious offence has been committed

2. Reasonable grounds for believing the article in question is either the fruit of the crime, the instrument by which the crime is committed, or is material evidence

3. Reasonable grounds to believe that the person in possession of it has himself committed the crime, or is implicated, or is accessory or at any rate his refusal must be quite unreasonable

4. Must not keep the article nor prevents its removal for any longer than is reasonably necessary to complete their investigations or to preserve it for evidence. If a copy will suffice, a copy.

5. The lawfulness of the conduct of the police will be judged at the time and not by what happens afterwards

---

### Chunk 19 — Jeffrey v Black — sandwich case principle
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Jeffrey v Black [1977] 3 WLR 895 - Sandwich case] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Limits on searching premises of an arrestee for unrelated matters] [CONCEPTS: Jeffrey v Black, sandwich case, scope of search, arrest-related search, consent silence, serious crime exception]

This chunk covers: Jeffrey v Black and the principle that police may not search premises of an arrestee for unrelated matters without warrant or consent.

Jeffrey v Black [1977] 3 WLR 895

Sandwich case: Police officers may not search premises of an arrestee without either a warrant or the consent of the accused, at least for evidence of matters unrelated to the reason for the previous arrest of the accused. There must be a connection with the evidence gathered/ search and the initial charge.

If related the reason for the previous arrest -- tickety boo.

So if search granted, they can search for and take things relating to that, but are not authorised to search for evidence which is unrelated to the offence.

Silent is not consent either.

Case also stands for the principle that where there is a serious crime; the suspect can have their premises searched -- not for misdemeanours.

They ignored the right of the sandwich thief to refuse entry.

---

### Chunk 20 — R (Rottman) v Commissioner of Police of the Metropolis — arrest warrant and search implications
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: R (on the application of Rottman) v Commissioner of Police of the Metropolis [2002] 2 All ER 865; [2002] 2 WLR 1315] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Extradition arrest warrants and search of premises related to arrest on site] [CONCEPTS: Rottman, extradition arrest, warrant, search premises, Dillon extension, arrest on site, search lawfulness]

This chunk covers: Rottman and the permissibility of searching premises where an individual is arrested.

R (on the application of Rottman) v Commissioner of Police of the Metropolis [2002] 2 All ER 865; [2002] 2 WLR 1315 - arrest warrant for extra-jurisdictional offence.

Applies to extradition arrest warrants.

Dissent very interesting

Procedurally sound to search premises where individual arrested. This means that if they were arrested on site, it does not make sense that another search warrant is needed.

Factually: includes building on premises even where person arrested outside building structure -- another extension: extending Dillon by not being in the immediate vicinity of the arrest

---

### Chunk 21 — Australian divergence: Challenge Plastics; Laurens v Willers
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Challenge Plastics (1993) 115 ALR 149; Laurens v Willers (2002) 131 A Crim R 281 - Diverging authorities] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Australian cases questioning Ghani's scope; common law limits to seizure] [CONCEPTS: Challenge Plastics, Laurens v Willers, common law divergence, seizure limits, exclusive possession, caravan park, stolen goods]

This chunk covers: Australian authority that narrows Ghani's high-water mark.

Challenge Plastics Pty Ltd v Collector of Customs (1993) 115 ALR 149

Ghani and Lord Denning went too far.Common law powers of search are restricted to: Dillon v O'Brien - being in the possession of a person at the time of his arrest either for a felony or a misdemeanour

Laurens v Willers (2002) 131 A Crim R 281 per Heenan J at [44]

Seizure under a search warrant

Basis of common law rights with search: action of trespass based in exclusive possession rights

Here: applicants were in a caravan park to which they had no exclusive possession

Moreover, second common law power of P.O: to seize stolen goods if without violence or harm to the goods of the applicant

- Line of authority away from Ghani as a principle in Aus.

---

### Chunk 22 — Tasmania v Woodberry — compelled search and exclusion of evidence
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Tasmania v Woodberry [2012] TASSC 89] [CITATION: Tasmania v Woodberry (2012) - Compelled search, evidence excluded] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Police compelled search of premises without warrant/consent; exclusion of search evidence] [CONCEPTS: Woodberry, compelled search, consent refusal, exclusionary rule, search warrant, arrest, evidence excluded, alternative means]

This chunk covers: Woodberry facts and the court's exclusion of evidence where police compelled search without lawful power.

Tasmania v Woodberry [2012] TASSC 89

Serious offence where woman stole $30,000 from business she worked for. Visited by 2 police officers. Not cautioned or warned that she could speak to a lawyer so there was an arrest.

She was taken from her place of business and was told that she would need to accompany them to the house -- she claimed that she was compelled to comply with the search.

The court noted that there was no power of the police to compel her to submit to a search of her house -- in so doing, the police officer disregarded her right to refuse entry. All evidence of the search was excluded. There were other means available to the officers to initiate the search such as consent or getting a search warrant.

### Chunk 23 — Spyke & Alexander (Tas unreported) — application of Ghani and Search Warrants Act
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Spyke & Alexander Tas Unreported Serial No 2 of 1981 BC8171022 - Ghani and Search Warrants Act application] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Application of Ghani and Tas Search Warrants Act provisions on seizure of other offences] [CONCEPTS: Spyke & Alexander, Ghani application, Search Warrants Act s5/s6, forged checks, typewriter, Tas unreported case]

This chunk covers: Tasmanian unreported case applying Ghani principles and Search Warrants Act.

[Spyke & Alexander] Tas Unreported Serial No 2 of 1981 BC8171022

Forged checks on typewriter.

Applied Ghani and also in s5 and s6 of Tas Search Warrant Acts (the aspect of allowing police to sieze items of other offences when entering pursuant to a search warrant under that Act) -- not a great deal of reason why they are applying Ghani (cf. Challenge Plastics which considered the law carefully)

<!-- block_003 master -->

# Search Warrants Act 1997 (Tas) — When warrants can be issued (s 5)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Search Warrants Act 1997 (Tas)] [SECTION: s 5(1)] [CITATION: Search Warrants Act 1997 (Tas) s 5(1)] [TYPE: statutory provision] [TOPIC: issuance of search warrants] [CONCEPTS: reasonable grounds, reasonable suspicion, evidential material, issuing officer, search warrant application, judicial oversight, 72 hours, premises search, statutory test]
The issuing officer may issue a warrant to search premises only if satisfied by information on oath that there are reasonable grounds for suspecting that there is, or there will be within the next 72 hours, any evidential material at the premises. The first sentence of this chunk states the rule. This statutory provision imposes an objective threshold — the issuing officer must be satisfied on sworn information that evidential material is present or imminently likely to be present. The requirement of oathed information evidences the Act’s emphasis on an independent judicial marker between state power and occupier privacy. The section operates alongside s 4 of the Act, which preserves other statutory powers of search and seizure. The 72‑hour temporal window confines the prospective reach of the warrant to relevant imminent occurrences and is an express statutory limitation on the issuing officer’s power.

# Search Warrants Act 1997 (Tas) — Non-exclusion of other laws (s 4)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Search Warrants Act 1997 (Tas)] [SECTION: s 4] [CITATION: Search Warrants Act 1997 (Tas) s 4] [TYPE: statutory provision] [TOPIC: interaction with other search powers] [CONCEPTS: non-exclusion clause, statutory interaction, other search powers, seizure, preservation of powers, concurrent authority, legislative overlap]
Section 4 of the Search Warrants Act 1997 (Tas) provides that the Act does not limit or exclude the operation of any other law purporting to grant powers of search, seizure, arrest or detention. The rule is that the Search Warrants Act coexists with other statutory or common‑law authorities for search and seizure; police and agencies may rely upon alternative enabling provisions where applicable. Practically, this means that agents must identify the correct enabling power (the Act or another law) and comply with that law’s distinct thresholds and safeguards when seeking or executing search powers.

# Common law purpose of a search warrant — control device and rejection of general warrants
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: purpose of search warrants at common law] [CONCEPTS: judicial oversight, control device, general warrants, Star Chamber, occupier privacy, trespass, independent judicial officer, conservative issue of warrants, safeguard]
At common law a search warrant operates as a control device that interposes an independent judicial officer between the executive (police) and the citizen; this judicial interposition protects occupier privacy and limits arbitrary entries. The doctrine explains why common law courts historically rejected general warrants — those permitting unspecified searches at will — because general warrants were instruments of arbitrary oppression (Star Chamber abuses). The common‑law rule also requires that goods seized during a search must be covered by the warrant’s scope; otherwise the searcher may be liable for trespass to property.

# Duties of the issuing officer — independent mind and satisfaction
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: issuing officer’s duties when granting warrants] [CONCEPTS: independent mind, reasonable grounds, objective sufficiency, rubber-stamping, magistrate’s state of mind, oath, affidavit, judicial satisfaction, George v Rockett, MacLeod]
An issuing officer must bring an independent mind to the warrant application and be personally satisfied that the statutory conditions for issuing a warrant are met. The rule is that it is the state of mind of the issuing judicial officer (not merely the applicant) that must satisfy the statutory test (for example, reasonable grounds to suspect evidential material). Applications are ordinarily supported by sworn information or affidavit and must provide sufficient evidence to induce the requisite suspicion or belief in a reasonable person. The issuing officer cannot simply “rubber stamp” the applicant’s assertions; where the material before the officer is inadequate, the warrant may be invalid.

# Duty of disclosure by warrant applicant — competing authorities (Karina Fisheries v Mitson; Lego Australia v Paraggio)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: applicant’s duty of disclosure in warrant proceedings] [CONCEPTS: full disclosure, misrepresentation, fraud, material facts, informant duties, warrant invalidation, Karina Fisheries, Lego Australia, best practice]
There is conflicting authority on the applicant’s duty of disclosure when seeking a warrant. One line (Karina Fisheries v Mitson (1990) 26 FCR 473) articulates a duty of full disclosure of all material facts by the informant that might affect the issuing officer’s decision, grounded in protection of privacy against arbitrary entry. The contrary authority (Lego Australia v Paraggio (1994) 52 FCR 542) holds that nondisclosure will invalidate a warrant only where there has been fraud, misrepresentation, or a half‑truth. Best practice for prosecutors and applicants, notwithstanding the conflict, is to disclose all relevant information to the issuing officer to avoid later challenge.

# Particularity and wording required in a warrant — nature of offence & premises
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: particularity and sufficiency of description in warrants] [CONCEPTS: particularity, nature of offence, boundaries of search, substantial compliance, NSW v Corbett, Winston Dean v White, certainty of premises, misstatement of offence, repealed provisions]
A warrant must describe sufficiently the nature of the offence and the object of the search so that those subject to the warrant can ascertain what is being searched for and the boundaries of the search. The High Court in New South Wales v Corbett (2007) 230 CLR 606 held that the statement of the offence in a warrant need not match indictment‑level precision; substantial clarity about the nature of the offence is required so police are not misled as to their search power. Conversely, Winston Dean v Graeme Ross White [1996] TASSC 22 illustrates that a warrant may be invalid if the description of premises is too broad or uncertain so that it is unclear which premises are covered.

# Extent of power under a warrant — seizure of non-specified things and s 6(1)(e)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Search Warrants Act 1997 (Tas)] [SECTION: s 6(1)(e)] [CITATION: Search Warrants Act 1997 (Tas) s 6(1)(e)] [TYPE: statutory provision] [TOPIC: scope of seizure under warrants] [CONCEPTS: seizure scope, evidential material, other offences, reasonable grounds, statutory power, s6, Challenge Plastics, non-specified items]
Executing officers under s 6(1)(e) of the Search Warrants Act 1997 (Tas) can seize material found on premises that they believe on reasonable grounds to be evidential material in relation to the offence specified in the warrant or to another offence. The statutory rule authorizes seizing non‑specified items as evidential material where reasonable grounds exist; this statutory allowance codifies and restricts the broader common‑law debates about “fishing” searches. The seizure must be assessed on reasonable grounds present at the time of seizure (not hindsight), and retention must be no longer than necessary to preserve evidence or complete investigations.

# Scope of search and prohibition on "fishing exercises" — Crowley v Murphy
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: prohibition on exploratory/fishing searches] [CONCEPTS: fishing exercise, scope limit, Crowley v Murphy, proper purpose, strict adherence, warrant directions, fair exercise, good faith]
Police are prohibited from using a warrant as a pretext to conduct a general “fishing exercise” for documents or items when they do not know what they are searching for. Crowley v Murphy (1981) 34 ALR 496 articulated that execution of a warrant must be in good faith, for the statutory purpose, fairly and with regard to affected persons’ rights, and strictly within the directions of the warrant. Officers must not exceed the scope or authority conferred by the warrant.

# Legal professional privilege and search warrants — Baker v Campbell and s 22
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Search Warrants Act 1997 (Tas)] [SECTION: s 22] [CITATION: Search Warrants Act 1997 (Tas) s 22] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: legal professional privilege and search warrants] [CONCEPTS: legal professional privilege, fundamental common law right, Baker v Campbell, exclusion from warrants, documents, privilege protection, statutory override]
Legal professional privilege (LPP) is a fundamental common‑law right and search warrants do not authorise seizure of documents protected by LPP unless legislation makes a clear and express override. Baker v Campbell (1983) 153 CLR 52 confirmed that LPP is not merely an evidentiary rule but a fundamental common‑law right such that warrants ordinarily cannot validly authorise seizure of privileged documents. Section 22 of the Search Warrants Act 1997 (Tas) addresses privilege procedures; any legislative override must be clear and specific.

# Execution formalities — announcement, showing warrant and completeness (s 19; s 8)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Search Warrants Act 1997 (Tas)] [SECTION: s 19; s 8] [CITATION: Search Warrants Act 1997 (Tas) ss 8, 19] [TYPE: procedural rule] [TOPIC: execution formalities for searches] [CONCEPTS: announcement requirement, show particulars, s19, s8, occupier rights, authenticity, incomplete warrant, Oke v Commissioner AFP, draft warrant]
When executing a warrant police must make required announcements and provide particulars of the warrant to the occupier (s 19 and s 8 of the Search Warrants Act 1997 (Tas)). The warrant, when shown, must be sufficiently complete and authentic so that the occupier can ascertain its validity and the authority of the executing officers. In Oke v Commissioner Australian Federal Police (2007) 168 A Crim R 503 the warrant handed over was incomplete/draft and failed to identify the officer or date properly; the court held execution invalid. The formalities serve to protect occupants and to ensure lawful exercise of coercive powers.

# Temporary cessation of search — s 9(2) Search Warrants Act 1997 (Tas)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Search Warrants Act 1997 (Tas)] [SECTION: s 9(2)] [CITATION: Search Warrants Act 1997 (Tas) s 9(2)] [TYPE: statutory provision] [TOPIC: temporary cessation and resumption of search] [CONCEPTS: temporary cessation, break, return to search, time limit, occupier consent, statutory limitation, resumption period]
Section 9(2) of the Search Warrants Act 1997 (Tas) permits a temporary cessation of the search and a return to searching under the warrant for a limited period (for example, a statutory one‑hour limit referenced in notes). The statutory rule allows brief interruptions to deal with practical matters (meal breaks etc.) but restricts prolonged deprivation of access to a dwelling unless occupier consent or other lawful authority exists. Execution must remain within the timeframe and purpose authorized by the warrant.

# Valid entry to premises — consent, warrant, or lawful arrest; common-law starting point
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: lawful entry to premises] [CONCEPTS: consent, lawful arrest, search warrant, Coco v R, Kuru v NSW, trespass, authorisation, statute required]
The common‑law rule is that police have no special power to gain lawful entry to premises beyond consent, statutory authorisation, or circumstances implied by law (for example, to prevent breach of the peace or to perform a lawful arrest). Coco v R (1994) 179 CLR 427 and Kuru v New South Wales indicate that without clear authorising statute or lawful exception, unauthorized entry is trespass and may attract remedies. Consequently, to lawfully enter for search purposes police generally require consent or a search warrant unless another recognised common‑law exception operates.

# Search of premises incidental to arrest — scope and limits (Dillon v O'Brien; Jeffrey v Black)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: incidental searches on arrest] [CONCEPTS: incidental search, immediate vicinity, arrestee, Dillon v O'Brien, Jeffrey v Black, limitations, need for warrant, home search restrictions]
When police lawfully enter premises to effect an arrest, they may search the arrestee and the immediate area surrounding the arrestee (e.g. table, desk) for items relevant to the arrest (weapons, evidence, means of escape), but they may not, as a general rule, remove the arrestee to another location (for example, their home) and there conduct a search without a warrant (Jeffrey v Black [1977] 3 WLR 895). Dillon v O'Brien & Davies (1887) 16 Cox CC 245 illustrates the limited scope of searches incidental to arrest: the search must be closely related in time and place to the arrest and purposively limited.

# Ghani v Jones factors for seizing material relating to other offences (common-law test)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: conditions for seizing items relating to other offences] [CONCEPTS: Ghani v Jones, serious offence, fruit of crime, means of commission, material evidence, involvement of possessor, reasonable retention, contemporaneous assessment, no hindsight]
Ghani v Jones [1970] 1 QB 693 established five common‑law factors that may justify police seizing property found during the course of a lawful entry or search when it relates to another offence: (1) the offence is serious; (2) the property is the fruit of the crime, the means of its commission, or material evidence of its commission; (3) the possessor of the property was involved in the offence as principal or accessory; (4) police retain the property no longer than necessary to preserve evidence or complete investigations; and (5) lawfulness is judged at the time of seizure (no hindsight). Elements of this approach have been codified or reflected in statutory provisions such as s 6 of the Search Warrants Act 1997 (Tas), but its application in Australia has been contested (see Challenge Plastics).

# Challenge Plastics v Collector of Customs — critique of Ghani in Australian context
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Challenge Plastics Pty Ltd v Collector of Customs (1993) 115 ALR 149] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: statutory safeguard and limits on Ghani extension] [CONCEPTS: Challenge Plastics, Ghani critique, statutory safeguard, warrant requirement, judicial scrutiny, arbitrary interference, specificity, Heerey J]
Challenge Plastics Pty Ltd v Collector of Customs (1993) 115 ALR 149 declined to extend Ghani v Jones’ broad common‑law search powers into the Australian statutory context where the statute prescribes warrant safeguards. Heerey J emphasised that the requirement of a warrant is a practical safeguard against arbitrary interference with property, requiring oath and particularity; Ghani’s unconfined expansion would undermine statutory protections. The case argues for strict scrutiny of warrants and against relying on common‑law extensions that render warrants redundant.

# Baker v Campbell — legal professional privilege and limits of search warrants
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Baker v Campbell (1983) 153 CLR 52] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: legal professional privilege as a fundamental right] [CONCEPTS: legal professional privilege, fundamental right, privileged documents, search warrants, limits, HCA]
Baker v Campbell (1983) 153 CLR 52 holds that legal professional privilege is a fundamental common‑law right and not merely an evidentiary rule; as such, search warrants ordinarily do not authorise seizure of privileged documents. The High Court treated LPP as a serious protective doctrine, requiring special legislative clarity to displace it. Warrants executed in a manner that seize privileged material will attract strict judicial scrutiny and may be held invalid as exceeding lawful authority.

# Crowley v Murphy — limits and principles in execution of warrants
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Crowley v Murphy (1981) 34 ALR 496] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: execution conduct standards for warrants] [CONCEPTS: Crowley v Murphy, good faith, proper purpose, fairness, strict adherence, no fishing, directions of warrant, officer conduct]
Crowley v Murphy (1981) 34 ALR 496 summarises essential principles governing the conduct of officers executing warrants: they must act in good faith; exercise powers only for conferred purposes, not for ulterior motives; act fairly in the circumstances; have regard to persons affected including their rights; and strictly follow the warrant’s directions without exceeding authority. The decision reinforces the prohibition on exploratory or fishing searches.

# Oke v Commissioner Australian Federal Police — completeness and authenticity of warrant copies
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Oke v Commissioner Australian Federal Police (2007) 168 A Crim R 503] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: requirements when showing warrant to occupier] [CONCEPTS: Oke, incomplete warrant, draft warrant, authenticity, occupier protection, copy requirements, invalid execution]
Oke v Commissioner Australian Federal Police (2007) 168 A Crim R 503 held that supplying an incomplete or draft copy of a search warrant that lacked identifying particulars (date, officer name, guidance on privilege claims) did not satisfy statutory requirements and rendered the execution invalid. The case emphasises that when the occupier is shown the warrant the document must be sufficiently authentic and complete to enable the person to ascertain its legal status and content.

# Larsson v Commissioner of Police for NSW — deprivation of home and limits of warrant powers
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Larsson v Commissioner of Police for NSW (1988) 16 NSWLR 173] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: unlawful deprivation and limits of search powers] [CONCEPTS: Larsson, deprivation of access, home, unlawful prevention, excessive exercise, personal rights, occupier access]
Larsson v Commissioner of Police for NSW (1988) 16 NSWLR 173 holds that warrants do not permit deprivation of an occupier’s right to access or to prevent a person entering their home absent lawful authority. In Larsson police prevented the plaintiff from entering his basement and detained items; the action exceeded warrant authority. The case highlights limits on executing officers and protects occupier access/possession of premises.

# NSW v Corbett — degree of precision required in describing the offence
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: New South Wales v Corbett (2007) 230 CLR 606] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: sufficiency of offence description in warrants] [CONCEPTS: NSW v Corbett, offence description, substantial clarity, indictment precision, repealed provision, nature of offence, boundaries of search]
New South Wales v Corbett (2007) 230 CLR 606 confirmed that a warrant’s statement of the offence need not achieve indictment‑level precision; the description must be sufficient to convey the nature of the offence and the object of the search so police and occupiers can understand the warrant’s scope. Minor errors or reference to repealed but substantially similar provisions will not invalidate a warrant where no misapprehension as to police power could arise.

# Winston Dean v Graeme Ross White — specificity of premises
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Winston Dean v Graeme Ross White [1996] TASSC 22] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: certainty of premises in warrants] [CONCEPTS: Winston Dean, TASSC, premises specificity, invalid warrant, description too broad, Tasmania authority]
Winston Dean v Graeme Ross White [1996] TASSC 22 found a warrant invalid where the description of premises to be searched was too broad and uncertain such that multiple premises might satisfy the description and it was unclear which premises were intended. The decision stresses the requirement of adequate specificity as to place to prevent arbitrary or overbroad intrusions.

# Search execution and legal professional privilege procedure — Oke practical illustration
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: practical problems where privilege arises during execution] [CONCEPTS: privilege claims, third-party review, bundling, challenge process, Oke, procedural safeguards, seizure return]
When privilege issues arise during execution, practical mechanisms (for example, bundling documents and third‑party or judicial review to determine privilege) have been developed in practice; however, Oke demonstrates these procedures can fail where the warrant execution and documentation are defective. The rule is that occupiers asserting privilege must be given procedural avenues to protect documents, and executing officers must respect those procedural safeguards or risk invalidating the search.

# Search Warrants — effect of invalid warrant on admissibility (s 138 Evidence Act)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: admissibility of evidence from unlawful searches] [CONCEPTS: s138 Evidence Act, improperly obtained evidence, exclusion discretion, unlawful entry, judge’s discretion, remedy, evidence exclusion]
Evidence obtained by an invalid warrant or by unlawful entry is subject to the court’s discretion to exclude it under s 138 of the Evidence Act (uniform evidence law). The legal rule is that improperly obtained evidence may be excluded where its admission would have an adverse effect on the fairness of the proceedings or the administration of justice; the invalidity of the warrant is a primary ground for such discretion.

# Personal search common-law rule — only by consent, warrant, or incident to arrest
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: lawful bases for personal searches] [CONCEPTS: consent, warrant, incident to arrest, bodily search, personal search, CL exception, Lindley v Rutter, protections]
At common law personal searches may be conducted only with the person’s consent, pursuant to a valid search warrant, or incident to a lawful arrest. The common‑law rule emphasises bodily integrity and privacy: absent one of these bases a personal search is trespass or unlawful. The scope of an incidental search (when arrest is the basis) is further limited by necessity — to discover weapons, evidence relating to the offence, or to prevent escape or ensure safety.

# Police Offences Act 1935 (Tas) s 58B — search of accused person in custody
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Police Offences Act 1935 (Tas)] [SECTION: s 58B] [CITATION: Police Offences Act 1935 (Tas) s 58B] [TYPE: statutory provision] [TOPIC: searching persons in lawful custody] [CONCEPTS: s58B, person in custody, reasonable grounds, weapons, evidential material, removal of clothing, safe keeping, force, seizure powers]
Section 58B of the Police Offences Act 1935 (Tas) authorises a police officer who believes on reasonable grounds it is necessary to search a person in lawful custody to do so for specified purposes: to ascertain concealed weapons or articles, to remove articles for safekeeping, or to obtain evidence relating to an offence or prevent loss or destruction of evidence. The section limits removal of clothing unless reasonable grounds exist and permits reasonable force. The statute thus codifies and clarifies permissible custodial searches in Tasmania.

# Commonwealth Crimes Act — searches in arrest context: ss 3ZE–3ZI (strip/frisk/ordinary search safeguards)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Crimes Act 1914 (Cth)] [SECTION: ss 3ZE–3ZI] [CITATION: Crimes Act 1914 (Cth) ss 3ZE–3ZI] [TYPE: statutory provision] [TOPIC: search types and safeguards under Commonwealth law] [CONCEPTS: strip search, frisk search, ordinary search, safeguards, police station, authorisation, same-sex officer, superintendent authorisation, privacy]
The Commonwealth Crimes Act 1914 (Cth) ss 3ZE–3ZI distinguish frisk, ordinary, and strip searches and impose safeguards for invasive searches. Strip searches generally must be carried out at a police station, be authorised by a superintendent or higher, and comply with privacy and same‑sex officer requirements and other procedural protections. These provisions reflect higher intrusiveness and therefore stricter procedural and supervisory controls compared with some state provisions.

# Tasmanian statutory extensions permitting non-arrest personal searches
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: statutory provision] [TOPIC: Tasmanian statutes authorising searches without arrest] [CONCEPTS: Police Offences Act ss58/58A, Search Warrants Act s6(2)(a), Family Violence Act s10(3), Poisons Act ss90A–90C, Public Safety Act ss18(2), Misuse of Drugs ss29–30, non-arrest search powers]
Multiple Tasmanian statutes extend police powers to conduct personal searches in non‑arrest contexts where specified thresholds are met (for example, reasonable grounds to suspect an offence). These include provisions in the Police Offences Act 1935 (Tas) (ss 58, 58A), Search Warrants Act 1997 (Tas) s 6(2)(a), Family Violence Act 2004 s 10(3), Poisons Act 1971 (Tas) ss 90A–90C, Police Powers (Public Safety) Act 2005 ss 18(2) and 22, and Misuse of Drugs Act 2001 (Tas) ss 29–30. Each provision imposes its own tests and safeguards; officers must identify and comply with the correct authorising clause.

# Definitions and differences: 'reasonable suspicion' vs 'reasonable belief' (George v Rockett)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: George v Rockett (1990) 170 CLR 104] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: meaning of suspicion and belief] [CONCEPTS: George v Rockett, reasonable suspicion, reasonable belief, Hussein, objective circumstances, state of mind, threshold, proof]
George v Rockett (1990) 170 CLR 104 explains that 'suspicion' and 'belief' represent different mental states: suspicion is a state of conjecture or surmise supported by objective facts that arouse it; belief connotes a stronger inclination towards acceptance and requires clearer objective circumstances though not proof. The distinction affects statutory tests that use either term and the quantum of objective material necessary to satisfy an issuing officer or executing officer.

# Nguyen v Elliott — application of personal search standards (unreported)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Nguyen v Elliott (unreported, Supreme Court Victoria, Hedigan J, No 7540 of 1994, 6 February 1995)] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: personal search legality and resistance to unlawful search] [CONCEPTS: personal search, lawful authority, reasonable suspicion, resistance, arrest, use of force, admissibility]
In Nguyen v Elliott (unreported, Hedigan J) the court considered whether police had lawful authority to search a person sitting on a bench. The magistrate found police lacked reasonable suspicion to justify the search, rendering it unlawful; the accused’s resistance was therefore held to be lawful. The case illustrates that personal searches require objectively grounded suspicion or belief and that unlawful searches may justify resistance as a defence to related offences.

# Sniffer dogs — legal reliability and human-rights concerns
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: sniffer dog alerts as grounds for search] [CONCEPTS: sniffer dogs, dog accuracy, reasonable suspicion, statutory designation, human rights, privacy, reliability statistics, legislative allowance]
Legislation in some jurisdictions treats sniffer‑dog behaviour as sufficient grounds to prompt a search, but dog alerts have significant reliability concerns (reported hit rates as low as 26% in some studies). The doctrinal point is that reliance on inherently unreliable indicators raises human‑rights issues (privacy, arbitrary searches). Courts and policymakers must weigh statutory provisions enabling dog‑prompted searches against accuracy data and proportionality principles.

# Coco v R and privacy limits on surveillance / intrusive measures
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Coco v R (1994) 179 CLR 427] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: requirement for clear statutory authorisation for intrusion] [CONCEPTS: Coco v R, listening devices, warrantless interception, trespass, clear statutory authorisation, right to exclude, privacy]
Coco v R (1994) 179 CLR 427 held that intrusive measures such as installing listening devices require clear and unambiguous statutory authorisation; absent such clarity, the law does not permit encroachment on territorial privacy. The case underlines that significant intrusions on privacy require explicit legislative mandate and cannot be implied from general statutory language.

# Dillon v O'Brien & Davies — searches incidental to arrest (historical rule)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Dillon v O'Brien & Davies (1887) 16 Cox CC 245] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: historical rule on searches incidental to arrest] [CONCEPTS: Dillon v O'Brien, incidental search, immediate vicinity, arrestee, historical common law, purpose limitation]
Dillon v O'Brien & Davies (1887) 16 Cox CC 245 is an early common‑law authority establishing that searches incidental to arrest are limited to the arrestee and the immediate area to locate evidence related to the arrest or prevent escape or danger. The decision informed later delineations of what constitutes an incidental search and its permissible scope.

# Ghani v Jones — common-law expansion for searches when entry lawful
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Ghani v Jones [1970] 1 QB 693] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: search/seizure beyond initial purpose; five-factor test] [CONCEPTS: Ghani v Jones, seizure for other offences, five factors, serious offence, fruit of crime, possessor involvement, retention period, contemporaneous assessment]
Ghani v Jones [1970] 1 QB 693 permitted, in the UK context, a wider search and seizure of material for offences other than the one prompting entry provided the five‑factor test (seriousness; fruit/means/evidence; possessor’s involvement; limited retention; assessment at time) is satisfied. The case significantly broadened police search powers in the UK but its application in Australia has been controversial and limited by statutory frameworks.

# Challenge Plastics — statutory safeguard preferred to common-law extension (reiterated)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Challenge Plastics Pty Ltd v Collector of Customs (1993) 115 ALR 149] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: statutory safeguards vs Ghani expansion] [CONCEPTS: Challenge Plastics, statutory safeguard, warrant requirement, Heerey J, reject Ghani extension, Australia context]
Challenge Plastics (1993) 115 ALR 149 (already chunked above) emphasised that where statute prescribes warrant safeguards, courts should not import expansive common‑law powers like Ghani that circumvent those safeguards. The authority insists on strict statutory compliance and particularity when state officials interfere with property.

# Rottman and extraterritorial/arrest-warrant context
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: R (on the application of Rottman) v Commissioner of Police of the Metropolis [2002] 2 All ER 865; [2002] 2 WLR 1315] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Ghani principles applied to extra-jurisdictional offences] [CONCEPTS: Rottman, extraterritorial offence, Ghani application, arrest warrant, jurisdictional issue]
R (on the application of Rottman) v Commissioner of Police of the Metropolis [2002] related to arrest warrants for extra‑jurisdictional offences and indicated that Ghani‑type powers may still be considered in that context in UK jurisprudence; its relevance in Australia is circumscribed by differing statutory frameworks.

# Laurens v Willers — confining Ghani-type powers in Australia
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Laurens v Willers (2002) 131 A Crim R 281] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: limiting Ghani in Australian common law] [CONCEPTS: Laurens v Willers, confine search power, Australian approach, Heenan J, limit to immediate vicinity, statutory compliance]
Laurens v Willers (2002) 131 A Crim R 281 (Heenan J) criticised Ghani’s unconfined powers and preferred confining search powers in Australia to material related to the offence prompting entry and its immediate vicinity, reinforcing the value of statutory safeguards and warrant requirements.

# Execution conduct standards — practical checklist (announcement, show warrant, scope, privilege)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: procedural rule] [TOPIC: practical checklist for lawful execution of warrants] [CONCEPTS: announcement, opportunity to allow entry, s19 exceptions, show warrant, s8 particulars, LPP handling, scope limits, no fishing, documentation]
When executing a warrant police must: (1) make a proper announcement of authority and opportunity to allow entry unless immediate entry is justified by reasonable grounds (s 19); (2) show particulars and a copy of the warrant to the occupier if present (s 8); (3) identify and respect claims to legal professional privilege and follow procedures for contested documents; (4) limit the search to items described or properly seized under statutory tests (no fishing); and (5) document actions to support later judicial review on lawfulness of execution.

# Effect of invalid warrant or unlawful detention on evidence — exclusion doctrines
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: consequences of invalid process for admissibility] [CONCEPTS: unlawfully obtained evidence, s138 Evidence Act, judges discretion, unfairness, s90 UEL, exclusion, illegality consequences]
If a warrant is invalid or detention is unlawful, evidence obtained thereby may be excluded under s 138 of the Evidence Act (or equivalent uniform evidence law provisions) where its admission would have an adverse effect on the fairness of proceedings. The court will consider the circumstances and may exclude evidence to preserve fairness and the administration of justice.

# Right to silence — common-law bundle and international instrument reference
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: right to silence and its components] [CONCEPTS: right to silence, ICCPR Art 14(3)(g), bundle of rights, trial immunity, Miranda-type protections, R v Director of Serious Fraud Office ex parte Smith, historical origin, Star Chamber]
The right to silence comprises a bundle of immunities (as noted in R v Director of Serious Fraud Office ex parte Smith [1993] AC 1): general immunity from compulsion to answer questions on pain of punishment; immunity from compelled self‑incrimination; specific protections during police interviews; trial immunities from adverse inferences, and protections against compelled testimony at trial. The right is grounded in ICCPR Art 14(3)(g) and common‑law concerns about coercion (Star Chamber abuses). Different components apply in differing procedural contexts.

# Statutory abrogations and limitations on right to silence — selected statutes
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: statutory provision] [TOPIC: statutory provisions limiting silence] [CONCEPTS: abrogation, Crimes Act ss3UC/3V, Police Offences Act s55A, Misuse of Drugs Act s31, ASIO Act s34L, compelled information, penalties, narrow scope]
Several statutes partially abrogate or limit the right to silence by imposing obligations to provide identity or other information in narrow circumstances: Crimes Act 1914 (Cth) ss 3UC & 3V (identity/proof of ID), Police Offences Act 1935 (Tas) s 55A (compel name/address when reasonably suspected), Misuse of Drugs Act 2001 (Tas) s 31 (supplier identification), and ASIO Act 1979 (Cth) s 34L (compulsory questioning under a warrant, with severe penalties). These statutory powers are typically narrow, context‑specific, and must be carefully construed.

# Detention for interrogation — Williams and statutory frameworks (Tas & Cth)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: lawfulness of detention for questioning and statutory time limits] [CONCEPTS: Williams (1986), detention for interrogation, Criminal Law (Detention & Interrogation) Act 1995 (Tas), reasonable time, Crimes Act 1914 (Cth) time limits, 2/4 hour limits, procedural safeguards]
At common law, arrest for the sole purpose of interrogation is not authorised (Williams (1986) 161 CLR 278). To address policing needs, jurisdictions enacted statutory regimes permitting detention for a reasonable time for interrogation: Tasmania’s Criminal Law (Detention & Interrogation) Act 1995 (Tas) permits detention for a reasonable time; Commonwealth provisions in the Crimes Act 1914 (Cth) set express time limits (for example, two hours for children/indigenous persons and four hours for others, subject to reasonableness). “Reasonable time” is determined by case‑specific factors (transport, intoxication, forensic procedures). Detention beyond statutory limits is unlawful and may render evidence inadmissible.

# Protections for detainees — caution, communication, interpreter, recording
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: detainee protections during interrogation] [CONCEPTS: Judges' Rules, caution, s139 UEL, right to communicate, s23G Crimes Act, interview friend, s23N interpreter, video-recording, R v Challender, R v Kelly]
Detainees are entitled to procedural safeguards: the right to be cautioned (administrative Judges’ Rules and statutory recognition in s139 UEL), the right to communicate with a friend/relative and a lawyer (Crimes Act 1914 (Cth) s 23G; Tas provisions provide similar protections in practice), the right to an interpreter (s 23N Cth for vulnerable groups), and in serious‑offence interviews a requirement that interviews be video‑recorded (s 85A UEL) for admissibility. Cases such as R v Challender and R v Kelly illustrate application and limits of these protections.

# Video-recording requirements and admissibility — s85A UEL, R v Kelly, R v Browne
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: recording interviews and admissibility] [CONCEPTS: s85A UEL, video-recording, official questioning, Kelly, Browne, practicability exception, exceptional circumstances, recording start/stop]
Section 85A of the uniform evidence law requires video‑recording of interviews where an admission in relation to a serious offence is made for the admission to be controlled. R v Kelly considered what constitutes the period of official questioning. R v Browne emphasised that “not practicable” is a narrow exception and police should reasonably carry recording equipment. Courts are slow to accept “exceptional circumstances” to admit unrecorded admissions absent clear justification.

# R v Challender — unrecorded informal questioning and unfairness
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: R v Challender] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: informal pre-interview chats and fairness] [CONCEPTS: informal questioning, official questioning, recording, unfairness, s90 UEL, admission, tape recording, R v Challender application]
In R v Challender the accused made admissions during an informal chat before the formal recorded interview. The court held the early chat did not constitute “official questioning” for s85A purposes (per Kelly) but used s90 (unfairness) to exclude admissions, finding the police deliberately elicited statements to catch the accused unawares undermining fairness. The case illustrates how admissibility may be challenged on fairness even where recording rules do not strictly apply.

# R v Kelly — when questioning is 'official' for recording obligation
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: R v Kelly] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: definition of official questioning for recording requirements] [CONCEPTS: R v Kelly, official questioning, period of questioning, admissibility, recording cessation, narrow concept]
R v Kelly explored whether off‑the‑record statements made outside the interview room were part of the “official questioning” period requiring recording; the High Court framed the period of official questioning narrowly — the requirement attaches to the time when questioning actually begins and ceases. The decision limits the recording obligation to discrete periods of interrogation and has given rise to debate and dissenting views about the breadth of application.

# R v Browne — practicability and exceptional circumstances exceptions to recording
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: R v Browne] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: exceptions to video-recording requirement] [CONCEPTS: R v Browne, practicability, exceptional circumstances, recording equipment, police responsibility, narrow exception]
R v Browne held that “not practicable” is a strict and narrow exception to the s85A recording requirement; police are expected to carry recording equipment or otherwise ensure the practicality of recording admissions in serious‑offence contexts. The court rejected routine or convenience‑based excuses and warned against expansive use of “exceptional circumstances” to admit unrecorded confessions.

# Carr v Western Australia — consent to recording unnecessary where surveillance captures admission
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Carr v Western Australia] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: recording admissions without consent] [CONCEPTS: Carr v WA, surveillance, recording consent, admissibility, implied recording, no requirement of consent]
Carr v Western Australia held that there is no implied requirement that an accused must consent to be recorded for the recording to be admissible; admissions captured on surveillance recordings may be admissible even without consent. The decision supports prosecutorial reliance on non‑consensual recordings where lawfully obtained.

# Right to silence cases — Collins; R v Stillman; Jalloh v Germany; Collins reasoning
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: extensions and applications of right to silence] [CONCEPTS: Collins, R v Stillman, Jalloh v Germany, compelled physical evidence, expanded right, bodily privacy, coerced extraction]
Cases such as Collins (Canadian), R v Stillman (forcible dental impressions), and Jalloh v Germany (forced vomiting) extend right‑to‑silence concepts into bodily integrity contexts, holding that forcible extraction of physical evidence (pressing, dental impressions, forced ingestion) can infringe rights and render evidence problematic. These authorities illustrate the reach of rights protecting persons from coercive investigatory techniques.

# Judges’ Rules and common‑law right to silence (Rule 1)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Judges' Rules and their role] [CONCEPTS: Judges' Rules, Rule 1, not law but yardstick, interview conduct, s138 evidence challenge, s90 unfairness]
The Judges’ Rules articulate administrative guidelines governing police questioning and the right to silence at common law. Though not binding law, they serve as a benchmark: evidence obtained in breach can be challenged under evidence exclusion provisions (s 138 Evidence Act) or doctrines of unfairness (s 90 UEL). They inform expectations about cautioning and conduct during interviews.

# Rice v Connelly — refusal to assist police not an offence (no legal duty to answer)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Rice v Connelly] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: refusing to cooperate is not criminal obstruction] [CONCEPTS: Rice v Connelly, refusal to answer, no legal duty, obstruction, moral vs legal duty, police interactions]
Rice v Connelly held a person’s refusal to answer police questions (for example, name or purpose) is not, by itself, an offence of obstruction; there is no general legal duty to assist police even if a social or moral obligation exists. The rule protects individual autonomy in interactions with law enforcement.

# Statutory provisions compelling identity or submission — selected Tasmanian & Commonwealth provisions
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: statutory provision] [TOPIC: statutory powers to compel information or submissions] [CONCEPTS: Crimes Act ss3UC/3V, Police Offences s55A, Misuse of Drugs s31, Road Safety breath tests, ASIO s34L, compelled evidence, scope]
Several statutory provisions allow police to compel identity, proof of identity or specific information and in some contexts physical submissions (e.g., breath tests): Crimes Act 1914 (Cth) ss 3UC & 3V; Police Offences Act 1935 (Tas) s 55A; Misuse of Drugs Act 2001 (Tas) s 31; Road Safety (Alcohol & Drugs) Act 1970 (Tas) s 15(1); and ASIO Act 1979 (Cth) s 34L. These provisions contain their own penalties and are narrowly framed — officers must apply and interpret them carefully.

# Detention time-limits and 'reasonable time' guidance
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: acceptable detention duration for interrogation] [CONCEPTS: reasonable time, transport delay, intoxication, forensic procedures, statutory maxima, release obligations, police bail, bring before justice]
Detention for interrogation must comply with statutory time limits or be a reasonable time under enabling legislation. Relevant factors include transport time to a station, the need to conduct identification procedures, intoxication, or participation in forensic processes. Once the reasonable period ends, the accused must be released or brought before a justice; excessive detention renders subsequent evidence vulnerable to exclusion.

# Protections for vulnerable interviewees — s23N (interview friend) and special rules
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Crimes Act 1914 (Cth)] [SECTION: s 23N] [CITATION: Crimes Act 1914 (Cth) s23N] [TYPE: statutory provision] [TOPIC: interview friend and protections for minors/Indigenous persons] [CONCEPTS: interview friend, minors, Indigenous suspects, presence during questioning, private consultation, reasonable exception, vulnerability safeguards]
Crimes Act 1914 (Cth) s 23N and related provisions require that interviewing vulnerable persons (children, Aboriginal and Torres Strait Islanders) not be conducted without an appropriate 'interview friend' present, and that suspects be permitted private consultation. Police may only proceed without such protections where they reasonably consider the person’s understanding is adequate. These provisions are protective and restrict admissibility where breached.

# Personal searches — strip search safeguards and concerns (state vs federal differences)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: strip search law and practical concerns] [CONCEPTS: strip search, safeguard differences, police abuse concerns, s58B limitations, Commonwealth 3Z series, privacy, same-sex officer]
Strip searches raise heightened privacy and dignity concerns; Commonwealth and Tasmanian statutes differ in their safeguards. Commonwealth ss 3ZE–3ZI impose location, authorisation and privacy safeguards; Tasmanian s 58B allows custodial searches including removal of clothing where reasonable grounds exist but lacks some Commonwealth procedural restrictions, giving rise to contemporary concerns about potential abuse.

# Human-rights framing — privacy, bodily and territorial privacy (Pohoretsky; Jefferies)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: human-rights perspective on searches] [CONCEPTS: privacy rights, Article 8 ECHR, Article 17 ICCPR, bodily privacy, territorial privacy, Pohoretsky, Jefferies, dignity, proportionality]
Human‑rights analysis frames search and seizure law in terms of privacy, dignity and proportionality. Bodily privacy (searches of the person) is traditionally regarded as more intrusive than territorial privacy (premises searches) in some jurisdictions (see Pohoretsky and Jefferies). Rights under Article 8 ECHR and Article 17 ICCPR inform proportionality and necessity assessments in intrusive police actions.

# Case authority — George v Rockett (1990) 170 CLR 104
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: George v Rockett (1990) 170 CLR 104] [TOPIC: issuing officer’s independent mind; meaning of suspicion and belief] [CONCEPTS: independent judicial mind, reasonable suspicion, reasonable belief, objective facts, applicant vs magistrate state of mind, warrant issue]
George v Rockett (1990) 170 CLR 104 is a leading High Court authority on warrants: it emphasises that an issuing judicial officer must exercise an independent judgment when deciding to issue a warrant and clarifies differences between 'suspicion' and 'belief' as objective states of mind. The case confirmed the requirement that sufficient material be placed before the judicial officer to allow formation of a rational suspicion or belief and that the judge or magistrate must not simply accept the applicant’s assertion without their own evaluative process.

# Case authority — MacLeod (1991) 61 A Crim R 465
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: MacLeod (1991) 61 A Crim R 465] [TOPIC: applicant’s assertions vs issuing officer’s mind; insufficiency of evidence before JP] [CONCEPTS: MacLeod, issuing authority mind, rubber stamping, insufficiency, JP notebook, affidavit, independent assessment]
MacLeod (1991) 61 A Crim R 465 applied George v Rockett and confirmed that validity of a warrant depends upon the issuing authority’s own mental formation of suspicion/belief; courts will scrutinise whether material placed before the issuing officer was sufficient to induce that state. The case found a warrant invalid where the court could not be satisfied that the issuing JP had sufficient evidence before him to form the necessary belief despite absence of a technical defect.

# Case authority — Karina Fisheries v Mitson (1990) 26 FCR 473
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: Karina Fisheries v Mitson (1990) 26 FCR 473] [TOPIC: full disclosure duty of informant] [CONCEPTS: full disclosure, informant duty, privacy protection, material facts, warrant issue, FCR authority]
Karina Fisheries v Mitson (1990) 26 FCR 473 held that an informant seeking a search warrant has a duty of full disclosure of all material facts that might affect the issuing officer’s decision, grounded in protecting privacy and preventing arbitrary entry. The decision emphasises the high duty of candour expected of informants when invoking coercive state powers.

# Case authority — Lego Australia v Paraggio (1994) 52 FCR 542
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: Lego Australia v Paraggio (1994) 52 FCR 542] [TOPIC: non-disclosure only invalidating warrants in fraud/misrepresentation] [CONCEPTS: Lego Australia, limited duty, misrepresentation, fraud, sufficiency on face, s10(1) Crimes Act, conflicting authority]
Lego Australia v Paraggio (1994) 52 FCR 542 rejected Karina’s broad duty-of-disclosure approach, holding that a warrant will be set aside for non‑disclosure only where there is fraud or material misrepresentation by the applicant. The court focused on whether the information supplied was, on its face, sufficient for statutory purposes (s 10(1) Crimes Act referenced in the source). This authority represents the narrower approach adopted in many prosecutions.

# Case authority — Winston Dean v Graeme Ross White [1996] TASSC 22
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Winston Dean v Graeme Ross White [1996] TASSC 22] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: specificity of premises] [CONCEPTS: Winston Dean, Tasmanian Supreme Court, premises uncertainty, invalidation, warrant description]
Winston Dean v Graeme Ross White [1996] TASSC 22 emphasised that a warrant which fails to identify premises with adequate certainty is invalid; the case is an important Tasmanian authority on the necessary specificity of place descriptions in warrants.

# Case authority — Jeffrey v Black [1977] 3 WLR 895
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Jeffrey v Black [1977] 3 WLR 895] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: limits on taking accused to home for search] [CONCEPTS: Jeffrey v Black, home search, incidental to arrest, need for warrant, timing and place limits]
Jeffrey v Black [1977] 3 WLR 895 held that taking an arrestee to their home for the purpose of conducting a search could not be justified as a search incidental to arrest; police should have obtained a separate warrant. The decision delineates the temporal and spatial limits of incidental searches.

# Case authority — R v Director of Serious Fraud Office ex parte Smith [1993] AC 1
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: R v Director of Serious Fraud Office, ex parte Smith [1993] AC 1] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: conceptualisation of right to silence as bundle] [CONCEPTS: bundle of rights, right to silence components, Lord Mustill, historical sources, immunities]
R v Director of Serious Fraud Office, ex parte Smith [1993] AC 1 explained that the right to silence comprises multiple immunities with different historical bases and policy rationales. The decision provides a useful taxonomy for analysing which aspect of the right to silence applies in particular contexts.

# Case authority — Williams (1986) 161 CLR 278
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Williams (1986) 161 CLR 278] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: arrest for interrogation not authorised at common law] [CONCEPTS: Williams, detention for interrogation, common law prohibition, statutory response, need for legislative authorisation]
Williams (1986) 161 CLR 278 held that there is no common‑law authorisation to arrest a person solely for interrogation; jurisdictions responded by enacting statutory regimes permitting limited detention for questioning under specified safeguards.

# Case authority — Lindley v Rutter
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Lindley v Rutter] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: scope of searches incident to arrest] [CONCEPTS: Lindley v Rutter, reasonable grounds, search constraints, evidential items, safety and prevention of escape]
Lindley v Rutter addresses constraints on searches incident to arrest: the police must have reasonable grounds to conduct the personal search, and the search is limited to finding items related to the offence, preventing escape, or ensuring safety.

### Chunk 1: Search Warrants - Common Law purpose and George v Rockett reference
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Purpose of search warrants at common law and George v Rockett] [CONCEPTS: search warrant purpose, control design, judicial officer, general warrants, George v Rockett]

Content:
Tactical note: Purpose of a search warrant at common law and the High Court's explanation in George v Rockett (1990).

Search Warrants

**Common Law**

Purpose of a search warrant: Search warrant operates as a control design --imposes an independent judicial officer between official and citizen. That's why at CL you see the initial conservative approach to issue search warrant (and the jealously the CL protects rights of occupiers)

At CL: need to ensure that goods seized during the search were covered by the search warrant (if not, could be sued for trespass to private property)

CL rejected the idea of general warrants (i.e. unspecified warrants to search anytime/anywhere) --because they had been used by the Star Chamber in a very oppressive way.

***George v Rockett*** (1990) 170 CLR 104

HC explaining the purpose of a search warrant

Statutory extensions

Have expanded to any offence at all (s5 SW Act TAS), not just re: stolen property.

***Search Warrants Act 1997*** (Tas)

***Crimes Act 1914*** (Cth), s 3E

***Police Offences Act*** 1935 (Tas), s 60

General warrant provision, but confined to searches for stolen property (issued to all PO by the commissioner of police, not issued by an independent judicial officer). They are issued for 6months (and allow police to enter any premises where they have a reasonable believe there may be stolen property).

\*Not HR's compliant (as no safeguard of judicial oversight)

General warrant, couple with Spyke gives PO a pretty free hand at searching shit in Tas. All they need is reasonable belief

### Chunk 3: Granting a warrant — George v Rockett and MacLeod
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: George v Rockett (1990) 170 CLR 104] [CASE: MacLeod (1991) 61 A Crim R 465] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: What is required for a valid warrant; issuing officer must form independent belief] [CONCEPTS: independent mind, issuing authority, reasonable suspicion, affidavit, George v Rockett, MacLeod]

Content:
Tactical workflow for deciding whether a judicial officer should issue a warrant and what is required in the application.

***George v Rockett (1990) 170 CLR 104***

F: PO had given an application for a warrant to a Qld Magistrate. The application just stated that reasonable grounds for believe that there were documents on premises re: ex-premier.

HC held: this was completely legit. Just need sufficient evidence placed before the magistrate or JP so that they themselves have a RB/Suspicion that the items were on the property.

Purpose of a search warrant: Search warrant operates as a control design --imposes an independent judicial officer between official and citizen. That's why at CL you see the initial conservative approach to issue search warrant (and the jealously the CL protects rights of occupiers)

This gives an indication of what a JO should do when decided whether to issue a warrant.

HC said warrants can only work effectively if the JO brings an independent mind to the application

***\*MacLeod (1991) 61 A Crim R 465***

Applied George v Rockett (Slicer J)

What is required for a valid warrant: not the mind of the applicant, but the mind of the issuing authority. Justice cannot rely on the suspicion/believe of the applicant, must form it themselves.

Applications are usually made on oath, or by affidavit, and must put before the authority sufficient evidence to enable them to form a reasonable believe/suspicion

This case is an example where warrant had no technical defect, however court could not be satisfied on the evidence it had (JPs notebook), that the JP actually did have sufficient evidence before him to form reasonable suspicion/belief.

### Chunk 4: Disclosure — duty of disclosure for warrant applicants (Karina Fisheries v Mitson; Lego Australia v Paraggio)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Karina Fisheries v Mitson (1990) 26 FCR 473] [CASE: Lego Australia v Paraggio (1994) 52 FCR 542] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Conflicting authority on disclosure obligations of warrant applicants] [CONCEPTS: disclosure duty, Karina Fisheries, Lego Australia, fraud, misrepresentation, best practice]

Content:
What the chunk covers: conflicting authority and practical advice on disclosure obligations when applying for a search warrant.

#### 3.8.2 Disclosure -- what is required of the party seeking a warrant?

There is conflicting authority as to the duty of disclosure on the part of the applicant for the search warrant***. Karina Fisheries v Mitson*** (1990) 26 FCR 473 held that all matters must be disclosed that would affect the issuing of the warrant. However, in ***Lego Australia v Paraggio*** 25/8/94 it was held that only where there has been fraud or misrepresentation will a warrant be struck out for non-disclosure. Most prosecutors believe though that everything relevant should be disclosed in the interests of best practice and conservativeness.

***Karina Fisheries v Mitson*** (1990) 26 FCR 473

Full disclosure by the applicant is required that might effect the making of a decision

**This case focuses on greater privacy**

*"There is a duty of full disclosure of all material facts by the informant seeking a search warrant. This duty stems from the common law tradition of protecting the privacy of individuals against the arbitrary use of the power of entry and search\...." @ 473*

(emphasis added)

***Lego Australia v Paraggio*** (1994) 52 FCR 542

Only fraud or misrepresentation will invalidate a warrant (sooo mixed authority on this point)

This case spec rejects Katrina above --a warrant can be set aside because of fraud and an informant must act in good faith in making an application. The statemnets made must be sufficient to est statute -- the critical q. is not whether the ...

This case is more popular.

There is no general duty of disclosure in making an application for the grant of a search warrant' @ 542

Instead, the Court asked whether the material in the information was sufficient on its face for the purposes of s 10(1) of the Crimes Act?

Contrast approach Karina

Contrast facts ***George v Rockett***

### Chunk 5: Wording of a warrant (NSW v Corbett; Winston Dean v White)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: NSW v Corbett (2007) 230 CLR 606] [CASE: Winston Dean v Graeme Ross White [1996] TASSC 22] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Degree of precision required in warrant wording; specificity of premises] [CONCEPTS: warrant wording, nature of offence, particularity, NSW v Corbett, premises specificity, Winston Dean]

Content:
This chunk covers the necessary precision and particularity required in the wording of a search warrant.

#### 3.8.3 Wording of a Warrant

***NSW v Corbett*** (2007) 230 CLR 606

Sooo....don't need to be technical, but people need to know what the PO are searching for, and that they do have the power to search (which they did in that case)

Degree of precision required in identifying the offence

F. Warrant issued to search for firearms. The particular provision relied upon in the warrant had been repealed though. At first instance it was held that this misstatement of the offence invalidated the warrant. Upheld by the Court of Appeal (NSW) then appealed to HCA.

H. Appeal allowed. Statement of offence in search warrants does not have to have the precision that is required in an indictment. Only require a statement of the nature of the offence so that you can clearly see what the search is for and establish the boundaries of the search.

Here the warrant was clear in stating the nature of the offence and all other details. Repealed provision had been replaced by a very similar provision so you couldn't say that this statement could possibly have given rise to any misapprehension of the police power to search for firearms. So here the similarity of the provisions was crucial.

Faield suciiced of a police officer -- note said police will die -- taken as a threat and a warrant was issued.

The warrant was granted under repeal;ed legislation (valid when made but law reprealed before the warrant executed -- howver, new law had similar wording -- warrant was still ok because there was no error in the application or the process by which it was granted.

Nature of offernce and the object of the se3arch were both described appropriately in the warrant, so it was substantially OK.

***Winston Dean v Graeme Ross White*** \[1996\] TASSC 22

Also relevant for disclosure requirements in addition to wording.

Court found lack of specificity in the premises to be searched -- too broad in its description. The warrant was invalid because it was not limited to one premises and not certain as to which premises answered that description in the warrant,

### Chunk 6: Extent of power under a warrant (Challenge Plastics; s6(1)(e) Search Warrants Act 1997 (Tas))
