### Chunk 7: Execution of a warrant — Baker v Campbell; Crowley v Murphy; Oke v AFP; Larsson v Commr NSW; s9(2) Search Warrants Act 1997 (Tas)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Baker v Campbell (1983) 153 CLR 52] [CASE: Crowley v Murphy (1981) 34 ALR 496] [CASE: Oke v Commr Australian Federal Police (2007) 168 A Crim R 503] [CASE: Larsson v Commr of Police for NSW (1988) 16 NSWLR 173] [ACT: Search Warrants Act 1997 (Tas)] [SECTION: s9(2)] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Execution of search warrants and limits (LPP, strict adherence, announcement, invalid execution)] [CONCEPTS: legal professional privilege, strict compliance, announcement, Oke, Crowley, Larsson, s9(2)]

Content:
This chunk covers execution rules for warrants, legal professional privilege, strict adherence to warrant terms, announcement and delivery of warrant particulars, and limits on deprivation of premises.

***Baker v Campbell*** (1983) 153 CLR 52

Re. Legal pro privileged not restricted to judicial proceedings.

App were a firm of lawyers -- warrant said enter anytime and take any docs relating to 5 businesses. The terms of the warrant must be strictly adhered to. Authorised only to do no more than absolutely necessary to satisfy the warrant.

Search warrants and their application to documents protected by legal professional privilege. F. D (police) attempted to seize documents protected by privilege.

H. HCA held that the search warrant did not cover the documents as they were covered by LPP. Legal professional privilege is not simply a rule of evidence; rather it is a fundamental common law human right. So the HCA treat LPP in a very important fashion. Search warrants thus cannot apply to documents covered by LPP. This causes some significant problems in practice.

***Crowley v Murphy*** (1981) 34 ALR 496

Warrant to seize implies warrant to search (phrasing of particular legislative provision)

Lockhart J:

1. good faith;

2. power exercised for the purposes for which they're conferred; not ulterior purpose;

3. fairly in regard to the circumstances;

4. exercised having regard to the persons affected by warrant's exercise, including their rights

5. officer must strictly follow the directions of the warrant and not exceed the limit of its authority (@ 521)

H. Police cannot go on a **'fishing exercise'** looking for documents when they don't really know what they are looking for.

***Oke v Commr Australian Federal Police*** (2007) 168 A Crim R 503

***\***
AFP searching a house and the issue was whether they gave an incomplete copy of the search warrant. There were parts missing -- name of the officer, guidleines as to the treatment of claims for legal pro privilege. Did not satisfy statutory requirements so the execution was invalid.

2 princples:

*When handing a warrant over it must be sufficient to satisdy the searchee so that they can acertain its authenticity -- what was supplied was only a draft one -- could not identify the date or anytone, or anything on oits surface to indicate that it was legal.*

Demonstrates the difficulties with the above process.

F. Oke contacted solicitor about police going through documents (including client/solicitor information). Solicitor then spent the rest of the day trying to negotiate with police to not look at documents he alleged were covered by privilege. Persuaded the police to desist in search and entered into written agreement. This solicitor really stood his ground. AFP hadn't exercised the warrant properly and tried to get out of the whole thing by returning the documents. He refused so they dumped them on his doorstep.

H. The warrant was invalid.

***Larsson v Commr of Police for NSW*** (1988) 16 NSWLR 173

Denial of pltf right to enter own basement. Lrg number of tems found in the basement some possibly related to drug use -- others that were not.

They prevented im form going down and locked it to sto\[p him. But they had no authorioty to do so. Warrqant doesn't permit the deprivation of ones home

s 9(2) ***Search Warrants Act 1997*** (Tas).

Does permit temporary cessation and return to searching under a warrant (but only allows one hour-so basically a lunch break), unless they have consent of the occupier

---

### Chunk 8: Search and seizure — Valid entry to premises and common law protections (Search Warrant Question Template)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: checklist] [TYPE: procedure] [TOPIC: Valid entry to premises for purpose of search and seizure and when police need a warrant] [CONCEPTS: lawful entry, consent, arrest, search warrant, Kuru v NSW, Coco v R, trespass]

Content:
Tactical checklist: Valid entry to premises for purpose of search and seizure.

Tactical anchor: Valid entry to premises for purpose of search and Seizure

Common law protection of property is extensive. Fundamental right to private property -- and to exclude others from it -- see for example HCA in *Coco v R*.

According to *Kuru v NSW*, the police have no special powers to gain lawful entry to premises beyond authorisation in the common law or statute. To lawfully enter premises they will require either:

- Consent; or

- A licence or lawful authorisation.

Further, the common law authorises the police to enter premises;

- to prevent a breach of the peace; or

- Make a lawful arrest and then enter and seize items in the possession of the arrestee.

To otherwise gain lawful entry; the police require a ***search warrant***.

---

### Chunk 9: Application for a search warrant — s5(1) Search Warrants Act 1997 (Tas) and duties of issuing officer
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Search Warrants Act 1997 (Tas)] [SECTION: s5(1)] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Requirements to issue a warrant under s5(1) and duties of issuing officer (including George v Rockett and MacLeod)] [CONCEPTS: s5(1), reasonable grounds, issuing officer, independent mind, affidavit, Beneficial Finance, Jackson v Mijovich]

Content:
What the chunk covers: Application process under s5(1) and the duties of the issuing officer including case law guidance.

**Search warrants**

**Application**

An application for a search warrant should be made in writing by the applicant (but this is not required by the *Search Warrants Act* in Tasmania). It must then be considered by a judicial officer (usually Justice of the Peace or Magistrate).

The general provision most commonly relied upon for an application for a search warrant is contained in s5(1) of the Search Warrants Act:

**5. When search warrants can be issued**

**(1)** An issuing officer may issue a warrant to search premises if the officer is satisfied by information on oath that ***there are reasonable grounds for suspecting that there is, or there will be within the next 72 hours, any evidential material at the premises***.

But, the *Search Warrants* *Act* does not limit or exclude the operation of any other law purporting to grant powers of search, seizure, arrest or detention (s4).

***Duties of issuing officer***

- A warrant can only be issued if the conditions in s5 (above) are satisfied; or the conditions imposed in any other enabling legislation being relied upon.

- Usually require ***reasonable grounds for suspecting*** that the property will be on the premises.

- The nature of the search warrant process is to ***interpose a judicial officer*** between the executive and citizens to act as a control on excessive executive action; the judicial officer acts as a 'fundamental safeguard': *George v Rocket*.

- To make this process effective; the judicial officer is therefore required to ***bring an independent mind to bear on the process***: *George v Rocket*.

- The ***judicial officer*** must therefore ***be satisfied*** that there are ***grounds for issuing the warrant***:

- *MacLeod* and *George v Rocket* confirm that this requires :

  - there be ***objectively reasonable grounds*** for the belief.

  - I.e. was the information ***sufficient to induce the requisite suspicion*** in a ***reasonable person***?

- A suspicion is more than a mere idle wondering (*George v Rocket)*.

- It is clear that the judicial officer cannot simply 'rubber stamp' the application by simply relying on the suspicions of the applicant: *MacLeod*.

- So importantly, it is the ***state of mind of the issuing officer*** which will be the Court's focus: *MacLeod*.

- However, judicial officer is not required to go 'behind the application' to investigate further or seek more information unless there are clear ambiguities or other questions raised by the application: *Beneficial Finance Corp v AFP* and *Jackson v Mijovich*.

---

### Chunk 10: Duties of applicant — extent of disclosure required (Karina Fisheries v Mitson vs Lego Australia v Paraggio)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Karina Fisheries v Mitson (1990) 26 FCR 473] [CASE: Lego Australia v Paraggio (1994) 52 FCR 542] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Disclosure obligations of applicant; competing authorities and best practice] [CONCEPTS: disclosure, fraud, misrepresentation, best practice, Karina Fisheries, Lego Australia]

Content:
What the chunk covers: competing authority on whether applicants must make full disclosure or only avoid fraud/misrepresentation.

***Duties of applicant -- extent of disclosure required***

- Competing authority on this point; argue both ways.

- Compare *Karina Fisheries* and *Lego Australia*.

- The applicant for a warrant should disclose all matters that would affect the issuing of the warrant otherwise it may be invalid: *Karina Fisheries.*

- The search warrant will only be invalid for non-disclosure where there has been some fraud, half-truth or misrepresentation on the part of the applicant: *Lego Australia.*

- But practically, it is good practice to disclose all information.

---

### Chunk 11: Particularity in warrant — need for sufficient particularity; NSW v Corbett guidance
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: NSW v Corbett (2007) 230 CLR 606] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Particularity requirements in warrants — place, offence, boundaries of search] [CONCEPTS: particularity, nature of offence, boundaries, s5, NSW v Corbett, minor errors]

Content:
What the chunk covers: need for sufficient particularity in the warrant and errors that will be tolerated.

***Particularity in warrant***

There is a need for sufficient particularity in the application; state the place, name of applicant etc. Also need to describe the nature of the offence so that it can be established what the search is for and to establish boundaries for the search. Some minor errors will be fine though provided the nature of the offence is clear: *NSW v Corbet*.

Come to preliminary conclusion on whether warrant would be struck out as a result of the application.

---

### Chunk 12: Execution of the warrant — announcement requirements, s19 and s8 Search Warrants Act
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Search Warrants Act 1997 (Tas)] [SECTION: s19, s8] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Announcement obligations and showing warrant particulars upon execution] [CONCEPTS: s19, s8, announcement, Lippl, O'Neil, show warrant, occupier rights]

Content:
This chunk covers the requirements for a proper announcement and providing particulars of the warrant during execution.

**Execution of the warrant**

***Has a proper Announcement been made?***

The requirements to make a 'proper announcement' as contained in the arrest cases of *Lippl* and *O'Neil* have been embodied in s19 of the *Search Warrants Act*. As a result;

(1) The police must announce that they are authorised to enter the premises, and give opportunity for person to allow entry.

However, not required to give such an announcement if the police believe on reasonable grounds (see cases in relation to arrest *Rockett*) that immediate entry is required to ensure the safety of any person or the effective execution of the warrant (for instance if they believe evidence is about to be destroyed).

Also note, s8 of the *Search Warrants Act*; requirement to show particulars of the warrant to the occupier of the premises (if present).

---

### Chunk 13: Scope of the search warrant — fishing, Ghani v Jones conditions, s6(e) Search Warrants Act (evidential material for other offences)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Search Warrants Act 1997 (Tas)] [SECTION: s6, s6(e)] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: When police can seize items not particularised in the warrant; Ghani v Jones conditions and statutory embodiment] [CONCEPTS: fishing exercise, Ghani v Jones, s6, s6(e), evidential material, serious offence, retention time]

Content:
What the chunk covers: legal tests for seizing items not specified in a warrant and statutory counterpart.

***Scope of the search warrant (i.e. seizing items not particularised in the warrant).***

It is clear that the police cannot use a warrant to go on a fishing exercise: *Crowley v Murphy*.

However, whether they can lawfully seize items not mentioned in the warrant will depend on whether the conditions from *Ghani v Jones*, which are now embodied (mostly) in s6 of the *Search Warrants Act*, can be satisfied:

(1) Must relate to a serious offence;

(2) Property must be the fruity of the crime or the means by which it was committed, or material evidence of its commission; and

(3) That the person in possession was involved in the commission of the crime either as a principal or accessory.

In addition:

(4) Police must keep them for no longer that necessary to complete investigations or to preserve as evidence; and

(5) The lawfulness of the police conduct must be judged at the time of the seizure, not by what happens later (hindsight or subsequent events cannot justify the seizure).

Under s6(e) of the *Search Warrants Act* the executing officer can seize any material found on the premises that they believe on reasonable grounds to be either:

- Evidential material in relation to the offence which the warrant relates; or

- Evidential material in relation to another offence.

---

### Chunk 14: Application to legal professional privilege — Baker v Campbell; s22 Search Warrants Act; Oke v AFP
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Baker v Campbell (1983) 153 CLR 52] [CASE: Oke v Commr Australian Federal Police (2007) 168 A Crim R 503] [ACT: Search Warrants Act 1997 (Tas)] [SECTION: s22] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Legal professional privilege and search warrants; statutory procedure for challenges] [CONCEPTS: legal professional privilege, Baker v Campbell, s22, privilege procedure, Oke, AFP, bundle to third party]

Content:
What the chunk covers: interaction of search warrants and legal professional privilege and the statutory procedures developed in practice.

***Application to legal professional privilege***

Legal professional privilege is a fundamental common law right; therefore search warrants to not apply to documents covered by LPP (see *Baker v Campbell* and s22 *Search Warrants Act*). To override this; must be made ***very clear*** by legislation.

NB practical approach AFP and Law Reform Commission developed; documents bundled up and given to third party to determine if they are, in fact, privileged. Person alleging privileged has four days to establish privileged status. But see *Oke v Commr. AFP* for practical illustration of the difficulties with this process.

---

### Chunk 15: Conclusion — invalid warrant consequences (s138 Evidence Act)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Consequences of invalid search warrants for admissibility of evidence] [CONCEPTS: invalid warrant, unlawful entry, s138 Evidence Act, exclusion discretion, improperly obtained evidence]

Content:
What the chunk covers: consequences when a warrant is invalid and evidence obtained may be excluded.

**Conclusion**

If warrant is invalid, then the entry is unlawful and all evidence obtained will be at the judge's discretion to exclude it on the basis it was improperly or illegally obtained evidence (s138 of *Evidence Act*).

---

### Chunk 16: CCP Part Three — INTERROGATION: Right to Silence overview (ICCPR Art 14(3)(g); R v Director of Serious Fraud Office ex parte Smith)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Right to Silence — sources, rationales and components] [CONCEPTS: right to silence, ICCPR Art 14(3)(g), R v Director of Serious Fraud Office, bundle of rights, presumption of innocence, Star Chamber]

Content:
What the chunk covers: foundational description of the Right to Silence and its component immunities.

**[INTERROGATION]{.underline}**

**Everyone as a basic right not to assist the State in investigation of an offence against them**

**1. Right to Silence**

Art 14(3)(g) ICCPR

- right of a person not to be compelled to testify himself or herself or to confess guilt

- [One rationale:]{.underline} it acts as a curb upon the excesses of law enforcement agencies

- Thought to derive in CL from the excesses of the Star Chamber in UK which had the power to 'press' (torture) confessions out of people

- Linked to presumption of innocence and onus of proof BRD on the Crown

- [Scope:]{.underline} Considered in R v Director of Serious Fraud Office, ex parte Smith \[1993\] AC 1 at 30 by Lord Mustal: Right to silence does not cover a single right, rather it consists of a 'bundle of rights' which have a number of different historical sources and motivations:

- 1. General immunity of everyone to be free from being compelled to answer questions on pain of punishment

- 2. General immunity of everyone from being compelled to answer question on pain of punishment *that may incriminate them*.

- 3. Specific immunity possessed by suspects --when being interviewed by law enforcement officials to not answer questions

- 4. General immunity from being questioned at all when charged with a criminal offence (i.e. once charged, agents should not question them further at all)

- 5. Trial immunity: from being compelled to answer on pain of punishment at trial

- 6. Trial immunity for accused: from having adverse inferences drawn against them from refusing to answer questions on trial

- Based on the notion that we all have a right to tell other people to 'mind their own business'

- These 6 instances of Right to Silence are very rigid: only allow for circumstances of oral evidence, thus may not extend to other types of evidence (such as ID parade, photos etc)

---

### Chunk 17: Other immunities / expansion of right to silence (Collins, R v Stillman, Jalloh v Germany)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Cases expanding the right to silence and protection against intrusive evidence extraction] [CONCEPTS: bodily privacy, coercive extraction, Collins, R v Stillman, Jalloh v Germany, inadmissible evidence]

Content:
What the chunk covers: examples where courts treated invasive evidence extraction as infringing right to silence.

[Other immunities derived from people subject to investigation:]{.underline}

- Derive from CL dislike of the behavior of the Star Chamber (and 'pressing' confessions)

- Adherence to the idea that any evidence obtained by pressure is inherently unreliable

- Expanded to include notions of fair play and a fair trial

- And also desire to minimize risk of unfair trial

[Ground for arguing for the extension of the right to silence in non-oral areas:]{.underline}

*(Collins)*

F: PO expelled drugs from suspects mouth by squeezing his throat.

H: Canadian Court adopted an expanded view of the right to silence --said it infringed right to silence to do this.

*(R v Stillman)*

F: PO forcibly taken an imprint of suspects teeth

H: Conduct was held to infringe the Right to Silence

*(Jalloh v Germany)*

F:

H: Infrigement to force the suspect to swallow a pill to trigger vomiting to prove they have swallowed drugs

---

### Chunk 18: Right to Silence at Common Law (Judge's Rule) and Rice v Connelly
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Rice v Connelly] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Common law right to silence; Judges' Rules as administrative guidance and limits on arrests for refusal to cooperate] [CONCEPTS: right to silence CL, Judges Rules, police questioning, Rice v Connelly, refusal to cooperate]

Content:
What the chunk covers: the common law position on police questioning and a key authority on refusal to provide information.

**[Right to Silence at Common Law (Rule 1 of the Judges Rule)]{.underline}**

Police have the power to question anyone (whether suspected of committing an offence or not) in relation to an offence. However, anyone who is questioned has the right not to answer

- As this is embodied in Judges Rules, it does not have the force of law. However it is a 'yard stick' by which to judge conduct. Thus any evidence obtained in breach of the Judges Rules can be challenged under s138 or s90 of the UEL

*(Rice v Connelly)*

F: A seen lurking suspiciously in area where many burglary's had occurred. He refused to tell them name, what he was doing etc. They asked him to accompany his to a Station, he refused. Then they arrested him on willful obstruction.

H: not an offence for a person to refuse to cooperate with police in their investigations (i.e. not giving name and address) --does not constitute the offence of obstruction. People probably have a moral or social duty to assist police, but no legal duty.

---

### Chunk 19: Statutory abrogations of the Right to Silence — list of Commonwealth and Tas provisions
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: checklist] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Statutory provisions that compel information or abrogate aspects of the right to silence] [CONCEPTS: statutory abrogation, Crimes Act ss3UC,3V, Police Offences Act s55A, Misuse of Drugs Act s31, breath test, ASIO s34L]

Content:
This chunk covers the various statutory provisions that limit or compel information from individuals and must be read carefully for scope.

[Statutory Abrogations of the Right to Silence]{.underline}

*\*Need to read the provisions carefully to work out what their scope is (and what they allow the PO to compel a person to provide --i.e. an offence not to provide information in some circumstances). Usually, it is very narrow, and can only be compelled in narrow circumstances*

*All of the provisions below give police the power to demand name and address (sometimes with extra bits, or in different circumstances)*

*Crimes Act* *1914* (Cth), ss 3UC & 3V

In Cth place, give reason for being there, and proof of ID

*Police Offences Act 1935* (Tas), s 55A (only allows police to compel name and address when PO reasonably suspected of committing an offence under the POA. If refuse or give false, they are obstructing course of judges)

*Misuse of Drugs Act 2001* (Tas), s 31

*Road Safety (Alcohol & Drugs) Act 1970* (Tas), s15(1)

*Australian Security Intelligence Organisation Act 1979* (Cth), s 34L Unlimited provision. Requires people to provide info to proscribed authorities when answering question, pursuant to a questioning warrant. Don't need to be a suspect, and it is an offence not to supply info (max sentence, 5 years)

Some provisions allow police to demand other information/instructions:

*Road Safety (Alcohol & Drugs) Act 1979* (Tas), ss 7A & 8 (submit to a breath test)

*Firearms Act 1996* (Tas), s 134 (if found with a firearm in a public place, they can compel you to hand it over so they can inspect it)

*Misuse of Drugs Act 2001* (Tas), s 31(2) Self incrimination provision: if they find any drugs on your premises, they can demand you supply to them the name of your supplier (and an offence not to do so)

---

### Chunk 20: Detention for Interrogation — Williams; Tas Detention & Interrogation Act 1995; Crimes Act (Cth) time limits
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Williams (1986) 161 CLR 278] [ACT: Criminal Law (Detention & Interrogation) Act 1995 (Tas)] [ACT: Crimes Act 1914 (Cth)] [SECTION: ss 23C, CA, D & DA] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Lawfulness of detention for interrogation, statutory time limits and reasonable time concept] [CONCEPTS: detention for interrogation, reasonable time, Williams, Tas D&I Act, Crimes Act time limits, arrested person]

Content:
What the chunk covers: the common law prohibition on arrest for interrogation and statutory frameworks permitting detention for questioning within reasonable time limits.

**`. Detention for Interrogation**

*Williams* (1986) 161 CLR 278: no authorization of arrest for the purpose of interrogation. This has been problematic for police, and therefore each jurisdiction has enabled legislation which ensure that although not lawful at CL detention post arrest may be lawful under the *Detention and Interrogation Act Tas or Crimes Act (Cth)*.

Following this, each Australian jurisdiction enabled PO to detain lawfully arrested persons for a reasonable time for questioning

Tas provision is the ***Criminal Law (Detention & Interrogation) Act* *1995* (Tas), ss 4(2) & (9).** Gives noime limit, only a reasonable time (guidance as to what this is for relevant case)

***Crimes Act 1914* (Cth), ss 23C, CA, D & DA**

Sets time limit (2 hrs for Children and A &TS, 4 hrs for everyone else). But can only detain for a reasonable period of time, with 4 hours being the max.

*Reasonable time*: judged according to the facts of the case and the guidance of the legislation.

E.g. how long taken to transport the arrestee to police HQs, ID Parade time allowance, allowance to deal with intoxication of the suspect, for participations in forensic procedures.

Once the reasonable time has expired, the obligation to release on police bail, or bring before a justice re emerges.

If suspect is obtained beyond the reasonable time, the detention will be unlawful --thus any evidence obtained during unlawful detention will be deemed invalid (*Williams*) will then apply.

Interrogation to be valid:

1. Need a properly grounded, lawful arrest

---

**[2. PROTECTIONS FOR DETAINEES:]{.underline}**

### Chunk 22: Caution — Carr authority re right to caution at common law vs administrative Judges Rules

What the chunk covers: legal status of the caution requirement at common law and the administrative Judges Rules.

**Caution**

*CARR* tells us that a right to caution does not exist as such, at CL. However the administrative guidelines of the judges rules require police to do so.

### Chunk 23: Right to Communicate — R v Challender; s23G Crimes Act 1914 (Cth); interpreter protections
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: R v Challender] [ACT: Crimes Act 1914 (Cth)] [SECTION: s23G, s23N] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Right to communicate with friend or lawyer and protections for vulnerable groups] [CONCEPTS: right to communicate, interview friend, s23G, Challender, interpreter, vulnerable suspects]

Content:
What the chunk covers: obligations to permit communication with a friend or lawyer and interpreter safeguards.

*R v Challender*

F: Suspect had requested to talk to relative and lawyer and was refused.

H: Info provided after the arrest was prohibited by the act.

*Crimes Act* *1914* (Cth), s 23G -- P

Friend or lawyer may be present in the interview (s23G) --but no equivalent allowance in Tas CL D&I Act

Interpreter

S23N (Cth)

Must not interview a A or TS I without an 'interview friend' present, and must allow suspect time to talk in private with the friend (caveat is something H....if police reasonably of the view that the persons level of education and understanding would mean that they would not be disadvantaged to be interviewed without them)

### Chunk 24: Video-recording of interviews — s85A UEL (R v Kelly), Carr v WA, R v Challender, R v Browne
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: R v Kelly] [CASE: R v Challender] [CASE: R v Browne] [CASE: Carr v WA] [ACT: Evidence Act / UEL s85A (referenced)] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Video-recording requirements for serious offence interviews and exceptions] [CONCEPTS: s85A, video-recording, admissibility, Kelly, Browne, exceptional circumstances]

Content:
What the chunk covers: rules and cases on video-recording admissions and interviews and the narrowness/breadth of "interview."

**Video-Recording**

Any admission of a serious offence must be recorded to be admissible

S85A --considered in *R v Kelly*

Issue: whether the police should have video statements made by the A (after he made off the cuff statements outside the interview room).

Were those statements made to the police during 'official questioning'

HC: (still applies in Tas) It is the period of time when the questioning commences and ceases. Relatively narrow concept in the sense that it only applies to confessions or admissions made during official questions, not to admissions made otherwise. Relatively broad as the requirement to record applies even if not in custody or under arrest. In this case, the questioning ceased when the Sgt said 'we will conclude the interview' and turned off the recording device.

Kirby J dissent: this interpretive approach enabled the police to undermine the purpose of the legislation

CF. *Carr v WA*

Considering the term 'interview'

H: any conversation between a member of the PO and a suspect, was covered by the term 'interview' when in relation to an offence. It didn't matter if the conversation was informal, or initiated by the suspect.

Said the legislation tries to prevent 'verballing' by the police, and this warranted a broad interpretation of 'interview'

*R v Challender*

F: A arrested early in morning, and interviewed late afternoon. Before interview, PO took her outside for smoke and a chat, and told her the types of things they would ask her in the interview. The A made a number of comments about the offences being investigated, and later on tape, the PO asked her about the things she had said earlier, she admitted making the comments

H: The early chat did not constitute 'official questioning' due to *Kelly* thus could not exclude the admissions on the basis they had not been recorded. But, applied s90 of the UEL instead, and said what the PO had done was unfair to the A (as the A may have thought that what she was saying was 'off the record'), and she may not have been aware of her right to silence, and not in a good position to decide whether to exercise the right to silence. Further, said the whole point of the chat was to try and catch the A unaware, and thus undermined her rights.

S85A UEL Exceptions (s85A b-d)

Considered in *R v Browne*

F: PO had not recorded the admissions of the A, but argued they had a reasonable explanation for this as it was not practicable to do so (as at the time the admissions were made, they didn't have a video recorded with them). PO were at A's house for the purpose of detaining him

H: CJ --that's not a reasonable excuse, you should be carrying recording equipment with you because there is a strong chance they will say something.

PO also tried to argue that 'exceptional circumstances' should apply on basis that the making of the admissions was not in dispute, and therefore should be accepted.

CJ: that is not an exceptional circumstance. The court should be very slow to apply the 'exceptional circumstances' exception, and there must be something in the case that takes it out of the ordinary, and that was not the case here.

\*Consider: is it a requirement that the A consent to being recorded??

*Carr*: HC held no implied requirement of consent to the recording. (A caught on surveillance camera making admissions). A had challenged the admissibility of the admissions on basis he had not consented to being recorded.

---

### Chunk 25: Police powers of search and seizure — Common law and Human Rights (Coco v R)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Coco v R] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Common law protections for property and privacy; human rights overview] [CONCEPTS: Coco v R, trespass, right to privacy, Article 8 ECHR, Article 9 ICCPR, territorial privacy]

Content:
What the chunk covers: common law protection of property, human rights framing and differences.

**[3. POLICE POWERS OF SEARCH AND SEIZURE]{.underline}**

**The Common Law and Human Rights**

CL is informed by decisions like *Coco*

*HC:* every unauthorized entry upon private property is a trespass, the right of a person in possession or entitled to possession of premises to exclude other from those premises being a fundamental common law right.

Thus these rights are considered in relation to the fundamental right of people to exclude others from their property

[Human Rights]{.underline}

**Right to Privacy** (Art 8 ECHR), (Art 17 ICCPR)

- Broad HR, and dynamic/evolving.

- Art 17: meaning of privacy generally is unclear

- Many people treat it as a bundle of different rights that people have:

- Right to control entry onto private premises (territorial privacy)

- Freedom from interference from your personal space (bodily privacy)

- Controlling personal info

- Freedom from surveillance and from interception of one' communications (privacy of communications)

- However, still remains a very contentious HR

- *R v Pohoretsky* \[1987\] 1 SCR 945 per Lamer J at \[5\]: bodily privacy --a violation of the sanctity of a persons body is much more serious than that of his office or home

- *R v Jefferies* \[1994\] 1 NZLR 290 Richardson J: personal search is a restraint on freedom, and an affront to human dignity.

---

### Chunk 26: Personal search — circumstances and extent at common law (consent, warrant, incident to lawful arrest; Lindley v Rutter)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Lindley v Rutter] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: When police can conduct personal searches and limits of those searches] [CONCEPTS: personal search, consent, warrant, incident to arrest, Lindley v Rutter, POA 58B]

Content:
What the chunk covers: the narrow circumstances in which personal searches are permitted at common law and the purposes of such searches.

**Personal Search**

***Circumstances and extent of search and seizure: common law and statute***

[Common Law]{.underline}

**Right to Privacy**

- Fundamental right

- CL tend to treat territorial privacy as more important than personal (unlike HR, other way around)

- Delineate only 3 circumstances where police can conduct personal search:

- With the consent of the person

- Pursuant to a warrant

- As an incident to a lawful arrest

[Common Law]{.underline}

Personal search is restricted to:

- With consent

- Pursuant to a warrant

- As an incident of a lawful arrest

[Arrest]{.underline}

- *Lindley v Rutter*

- *POA 58B*

- *Crimes Act (Cth) ss3ZE-3ZI*

*[Lindley v Rutter]{.underline}*

> F: Extent of search and what can they search for and seize?
>
> H: Restricted search and seizure pursuant to an arrest to situations where the police required reasonable grounds to conduct the search (e.g. dangerous driving, hard to establish why a personal search would be needed)

- **To take items relating to the commission of the offence for which arrested;**

- **To prevent escape;**

- **To protect the safety of the accused prisoner or anyone else, such as the arrestor.**

### Chunk 27: Police Offences Act s58B — statutory text and discussion (Tas)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Police Offences Act 1935 (Tas)] [SECTION: s58B] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Statutory power to search a person in lawful custody under s58B] [CONCEPTS: s58B, search in custody, remove clothing, weapons, retain articles, reasonable grounds, police powers]

Content:
This chunk covers the statutory provision s58B allowing police to search persons in lawful custody and the statutory particulars and safeguards.

*Police Offences Act* 1935 (Tas) s 58B

*How invasive can it be under s58B?*

Covers frisk and strip searching (if reasonable grounds to believe that removal of clothing would assist in finding an offence)

**58B. Search of accused person in custody**

      (1) If a police officer **believes on reasonable grounds** that it is necessary to search a person who is in lawful custody, a police officer may search that person --

(a) for the purpose of ascertaining whether there is concealed on that person or in that person's clothing a weapon or other article capable of being used to inflict injury or to assist that person to escape from custody; or

(ab) for the purpose of removing into safe keeping any other articles belonging to, or in the possession of, the person; or

(b) for the purpose of obtaining evidence relating to the commission of the offence or preventing the loss or destruction of such evidence.

      (2) Subsection (1)(b) does not authorise a police officer to require a person to remove any clothing unless there are reasonable grounds for believing that the removal may provide evidence of the commission of an offence.

      (3) A police officer may take and retain --

(a) any weapon or article found as a result of the search under subsection (1)(a); and

(b) any article of clothing removed under subsection (2).

      (4) A police officer may use such force as is reasonably necessary for the purposes of exercising powers under this section.

      (5) Nothing in this section prevents a search of a person in lawful custody in any circumstances where it is otherwise lawful to search the person.

Note s58B(1)(ab): for removing into safe keeping any other articles belonging to, or in the possession of the person

---

### Chunk 28: Crimes Act (Cth) ss3ZE-3ZI — frisk, ordinary and strip search regimes and safeguards; contrast with s58B
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Crimes Act 1914 (Cth)] [SECTION: ss3ZE-3ZI] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Commonwealth strip search regime, safeguards and differences with Tas s58B] [CONCEPTS: strip search, frisk search, 3ZH, 3ZI, safeguards, police station, authorising officer, privacy]

Content:
What the chunk covers: the Commonwealth detailed rules for conducting strip searches and the safeguards required under ss3ZE-3ZI.

[*Crimes Act (Cth)* 3ZE-3ZI:]{.underline}

outlines for people under arrest.

Includes:

- frisk search,

- ordinary search (the person and any accessory with them, incl. removal of outer garmets), (if RB carrying evidential material or ceaseable items)

- strip search (required to remove all of clothing, not cavity search) --SS can only be carried out when under arrest, and at a police station and only when reasonable grounds to establish they have on them a 'ceasable item' or evidential material

s3ZH: sets out guidelines for a strip search and 3ZI the rules for doing one:

Safe guards re: strip searches:

- Under arrest

- At police station

- Authorised by Superintendent or higer

- And comply with requirement's under 3ZI (e.g. privacy, same sex PO)

S58B does not have the same restrictions on strip searches as the Cth Crimes Act. Concerns in recent times re: police abuse of power to conduct strip searches.

### Chunk 30: Grounds of the search — reasonable grounds to suspect or believe; George v Rockett distinctions; Nguyen v Elliott; sniffer dogs
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: George v Rockett (1990) 170 CLR 104] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Definitions/application of 'suspicion' and 'belief' and evidentiary thresholds; sniffer dog reliability note] [CONCEPTS: reasonable suspicion, reasonable belief, George v Rockett, Nguyen v Elliott, sniffer dogs, objective circumstances]

Content:
What the chunk covers: how courts distinguish 'suspicion' from 'belief', practical application to personal searches, and reliability concerns (e.g., sniffer dogs).

**Grounds of the Search: Reasonable Grounds to Suspect or Believe**

- Most require a 'reasonable grounds for belief' that a search is necessary. Some terminology is 'reasonable suspicion'.

*Lindley v Rutter* \[1981\] QB 128

- where a person is under arrest then the CL tells us that there are a number of requirements that must be fulfilled.

- 1\) Must be on reasonable grounds to conduct a personal search

- 2\) PO can only take items from you that relate to an offence OR narrower, search for items in relation only to the offence that you have been arrested for

- 3\) PO can take items that may assist in escaping or may injure the arrestee or arrestor

*George v Rockett* (1990) 170 CLR 104

H: 'suspcion' and 'believe' refer to quite different states of mind. Applied UK *Hussein*, where suspicion 'refers to a state of mind that is one of conjecture or surmise, 'I suspect, but cannot prove', But need to point to facts that sufficiently arouse the suspicion. The objective circumstances must be sufficient to ground the suspicion.

'Believe' needs the obj circumstances must point to it even more clearly, but don't need to reach the level of proof.

'an inclination of the mind towards acsenting to, rather than rejecting, a proposition.

*Nguyen v Elliott* (unreported, Supreme Court, Victoria, Hedigan J, No 7540 of 1994, 6 February 1995)

*How the definitions have been applied*

Court considered how they are applied in the context of a personal search

F: PO observing accussed sitting on park bench, and suspected he was in possession of drugs. Many people approached and talked to A, and he was looking suspicious. PO approached him and ask questions, A responded abusively that he was sick of them. PO then invited him to be searched in a private alley. A refused and resisted the search (assaulted the PO trying to search him, and bit the other PO when he came to PO1's aid).

I: was resistance by the A lawful in these circumstances:

Trial Judge: PO did not have a lawful authority to search him --only approached him out of curiosity, no reasonable suspicion or belief. Therefore, the search was unlawful, and his resistance was lawful (thus successfully defended charge of assault)

\*Evidence needed to establish reasonable suspicion or believe:

- Entirely dependent on the facts of each case, but for interest re: Sniffer Dogs:

Sniffer Dogs: The Right to Security of the Person Chapter 7 in Gans et al, *Criminal Process and Human Rights* (Federation Press, 2011) pp 268 -- 270; 273 - 275

- Legislation provides that drug dog behavior is a sufficient ground for suspicion to prompt a search

- Problem: drug dogs are inherently unreliable. At most, accuracy is only 26% of all cases where sniffer dog behavior has prompted search

- For this reason, legislation that allows this could be a breach of HR

- Can depend on the type of dog (reknowned dog in Canada with an almost perfect hit rate)

---

### Chunk 31: Search of premises — common law starting point and key authorities (Coco v R, Ibbett, Dillon v O'Brien)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Coco v R (1994) 179 CLR 427] [CASE: New South Wales v Ibbett (2006) 229 CLR 638] [CASE: Kuru v New South Wales (2008) 246 ALR 260] [CASE: Dillon v O'Brien & Davies (1887) 16 Cox CC 245] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Common law limits on searching premises and legislative necessity for intrusive powers] [CONCEPTS: trespass, install listening device, legislative authorization, Coco v R, Dillon v O'Brien, Ibbett, Kuru]

Content:
What the chunk covers: common law limits on entering and searching premises and relevant case law.

**Search of Premises**

GR: an occupier may deny access to anyone. There must clear legislative authority to allow someone to override that.

CL Starting point: **(narrow)**

search of premises only permitted when police enter on to premises pursuant to an arrest, or with the consent of owner/occupier, pursuant to a search warrant. Originally at CL, SW only permitted in order to retrieve stolen property.

Extent of search permissible at CL: Dillon and O'Brien

At Genreally extends power s to search premises pursuant to a warrant or pre or post arrest.

*Coco v R* (1994) 179 CLR 427 at pp 435-6 (see also *New South Wales v Ibbett* (2006) 229 CLR 638 *and Kuru v New South Wales* (2008) 246 ALR 260 at \[40\]-\[55\])

No clear unambiguous authorization for police to go on to private premises and install a listening device, therefore the legislation did not allow them to do so.

*Ibbett*: if police do trespass onto private premises, aggravated damages are unacceptable.

*Dillon v O'Brien & Davies* (1887) 16 Cox CC 245 -- warrant for arrest

Where police enter premises to arrest, can search an arrestee and the area immediately around the arrestee (e.g. table/desk) for the same purpose (to find evidence of the offence, secure any items that may be used to effect an escape or cause danger to themselves or someone else)

---

### Chunk 32: Search incidental to arrest — Dillon v O'Brien & Davies; Jeffrey v Black limits on moving arrestee to home to search
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Dillon v O'Brien & Davies (1887) 16 Cox CC 245] [CASE: Jeffrey v Black [1977] 3 WLR 895] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Limits of searches incidental to arrest and prohibition on moving arrestee to home to search] [CONCEPTS: incidental search, immediate vicinity, arrest, Jeffrey v Black, home search]

Content:
What the chunk covers: limits on scope of searches incidental to arrest and authority not to move arrestee to home to expand search.

*Dillon v O'Brien & Davies* (1887) 16 Cox CC 245 -- warrant for arrest

Where police enter premises to arrest, can search an arrestee and the area immediately around the arrestee (e.g. table/desk) for the same purpose (to find evidence of the offence, secure any items that may be used to effect an escape or cause danger to themselves or someone else)

*Jeffrey v Black* \[1977\] 3 WLR 895

F: police arrest accussed outside own premises, can they then take the accussed to his home to search?

H: That search could not be considered to be a search 'incidental' to an arrest (they should have obtained a search warrant)

So...cant arrest in one location and then take to their home to search it (not considered a search incidental to an arrest)

---

### Chunk 33: Ghani v Jones — expansion when consent or lawful entry; five pre-conditions
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Ghani v Jones [1970] 1 QB 693] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: When police entering lawfully can search for evidentiary material beyond immediate offence — Ghani five pre-conditions] [CONCEPTS: Ghani v Jones, consent, serious offence, fruits of crime, accessory, retention, lawfulness at time]

Content:
What the chunk covers: Ghani v Jones judgment allowing broader seizure beyond immediate offence subject to five conditions and its implications.

*Ghani v Jones* \[1970\] 1 QB 693

Vastly increased police power of search from *Dillon v O'Brien* when effecting an arrest or under a search warrant. Why? Probs, parliament was being very slow to expand police investigative tools in this area. Very different to that in Australia (which has always had quite extensive search warrants and search provisions).

Court of Appeal: If the police enter premises lawfully in order to affect an arrest or pursuant to a search warrant, they may search the premises to find evidentiary material in relation to the offence that they are arresting for/search warrant for. If during the course of the search find material about another offence, they may cease those items as well, provided they behave reasonably and don't retain the items ceased for an unreasonable period of time. (confirmed in *Rottman)*

In *Ghani* the PO had entered with the consent of the occupier. H: they could search in this circumstances, not only to the offence they were investigating, but any other items they reasonably believed to afford evidence to another arrest. But court imposed 5 pre-conditions on the search and procedure:

1. RB it is a serious offence evidence

2. Property was the fruit of the crime , or the means of its commission, or material evidence of its commission

3. That the person in possession was involved in the crimes as a principle or accessory

4. The police must keep it for no longer than necessary

5. The lawfulness of the police conduct is to be judged at the time they conducted the search (not with the value of hindsight)

> (and see *Spyke* but cf with *Challenge Plastics*)

---

### Chunk 34: Declined application of Ghani — Challenge Plastics; Laurens v Willers; emphasis on statutory safeguards
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Challenge Plastics Pty Ltd v Collector of Customs (1993) 115 ALR 149] [CASE: Laurens v Willers (2002) 131 A Crim R 281] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Judicial rejection/limitation of Ghani expansion and statutory safeguard emphasis] [CONCEPTS: Challenge Plastics, reject Ghani, statutory safeguard, warrant specificity, Laurens v Willers]

Content:
What the chunk covers: judicial criticism of Ghani's unconfined expansion and preference for statutory safeguards.

**Declined *Ghani v Jones***

*Challenge Plastics Pty Ltd v Collector of Customs* (1993) 115 ALR 149 per Heerey J at 157-157

Good analysis of the CL (tried to argue the dissent in *Rottman*)

Heery J: police search did not comply with the statutory provision (went beyond this). Then argued that *Ghani* applied to extend powers beyond the statute.

Declined to apply *Ghani* and gave good analysis of CL in UK and Aust and difference between the 2 jurisdictions.

Re Ghani: No search warrant, arrest warrant, arrest, did not involve stolen goods, the extention of the norm made by Ghani is perhaps even more suprising given denning in some other case. The requirement of a warrant is a statutory safeguard to avoid arbitrary interference with personal property. The State wishing to cease property must swear on oath of the need to cease it (protective safeguard between investigators and citizens). Requires a degree of specificity --and courts will scrutinize warrants very carefully to ensure warrants have been complied with. *Ghani* really makes the process of obtaining a search warrant pretty irrelevant, and will no longer apply the safeguards they were designed to do. If *Ghani* is right, state officials are better off without a warrant at all.

*Laurens v Willers* (2002) 131 A Crim R 281 per Heenan J at \[44\]

H: Ghani v Jones giving far too unconfined powers to the police to conduct search, thus preferred to confine the power of search at CL to that of article in relation to the offence for which the police had entered, and the immediate vicinity (CL approach basically)

Consider the *Search Warrant Act* carefully

---

# Australian Postal Corporation Act 1989 (Cth)

# Criminal Law (Detention and Interrogation) Act 1995 (Tas)

### Chunk 1 — APCA s 90N: Non-Negotiables (Hard Rules) re: receiving vs opening mail
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Australian Postal Corporation Act 1989] [SECTION: s 90N] [CITATION: Australian Postal Corporation Act 1989 s 90N - Mail search workflow] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: procedure] [TOPIC: Non-negotiable rules for handling suspicious mail under APCA s 90N] [CONCEPTS: mail seizure, Australia Post, opening mail, warrant requirement, search warrants, drug mail, prescribed belief]

Tactical workflow for dealing with suspicious mail under APCA s 90N and related rules.

Police can RECEIVE mail without a warrant --- but not open it

- Australia Post can lawfully **hand over** suspicious mail if it's evidence of an offence.

- **Taking possession ≠ searching or opening.**

- Opening **always** requires a warrant (Search Warrants Act s 5).

Illicit drugs are NOT "dangerous or injurious" for postal law

- You **cannot** rely on APCA s 90Z to open mail just because it contains drugs.

- Courts have been explicit: drugs ≠ explosives / immediate danger.

- This is a **repeat failure point** in prosecutions.

You must form YOUR OWN prescribed belief

- A Post employee's suspicion **is not enough**.

- You must personally meet **s 29 Misuse of Drugs Act** threshold.

- "Prescribed belief" = more than suspicion, less than proof (George v Rockett).

- Common lawful method: **drug dog indication** you personally observe.

Even with prescribed belief --- you STILL need a warrant to open

- s 29 MDA allows **seizure**, not opening.

- Opening without a warrant = unlawful search, high exclusion risk.

---

### Chunk 2 — What Australia Post CAN and CAN'T Do (Practical permissions and prohibitions)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Australian Postal Corporation Act 1989] [SECTION: s 90N/s 90Z context] [CITATION: Australian Postal Corporation Act 1989 - Australia Post powers and limits] [CATEGORY: checklist] [TYPE: checklist] [TOPIC: Australia Post permitted and prohibited actions regarding suspicious items] [CONCEPTS: x-ray, sniff, hand over, opening mail, disclosure, post employee authority, chain of custody]

What this chunk covers: what Australia Post can and cannot do when handling suspicious mail.

They can:

- X-ray, sniff, scan (non-intrusive examination).

- Disclose the article to police if it's suspected evidence.

- Act under APCA protections if done lawfully and in good faith.

They cannot:

- Open mail unless very specific exceptions apply (undeliverable, quarantine, etc.).

- Create lawful grounds for police just by saying "we suspect drugs".

---

### Chunk 3 — Practical Workflow (Defensible Path) for police handling suspicious mail
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Australian Postal Corporation Act 1989] [SECTION: s 90N; Misuse of Drugs Act 1967 (Tas) s 29 referenced] [CITATION: APCA s 90N - Practical Workflow (Defensible Path)] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: procedure] [TOPIC: step-by-step defensible workflow for seizure and search of mail] [CONCEPTS: seizure steps, warrant, prescribed belief, chain of custody, drug dog, telephone warrant]

Tactical workflow for proceeding with seizure and opening of suspicious mail.

1.  **Confirm permitted person**

    - Verify the AusPost employee is lawfully authorised.

2.  **Document how the parcel came to attention**

    - Random scan vs targeted activity matters (Melick issue).

3.  **Independently form prescribed belief**

    - Drug dog, leakage, weight inconsistencies, smell, etc.

    - You must be able to articulate this **personally**.

4.  **Seize but do not open**

    - Secure chain of custody.

5.  **Obtain a warrant**

    - Telephone warrant acceptable.

    - Can rely on AusPost info + your own observations.

6.  **Open only after warrant**

    - Anything earlier risks exclusion.

---

### Chunk 4 — Key Risk Triggers (Red Flags) and Bottom Line policy
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: red flags and summary policy for mail-related drug prosecutions] [CONCEPTS: red flags, exclusion risk, conservative policy, opening authority, dangerous goods misconception, post employee overreach]

This chunk covers the main red flags and the practitioner's bottom line advice on mail seizure matters.

Key Risk Triggers (Red Flags)

- Opening mail "because Post said so"

- Treating drugs as "dangerous goods"

- No record of *how* belief was formed

- Post employees exceeding their authority (x-ray misuse, disclosures)

- Assuming urgency removes warrant requirements

Bottom Line

- **Mail is one of the easiest ways to lose an otherwise strong drug matter.**

- If you shortcut belief formation or opening authority, **the evidence dies**.

- This policy is conservative by design --- follow it and prosecutions survive.

---

### Chunk 5 — Search Warrants: Common Law overview and purpose
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Common law purpose of search warrants and protections] [CONCEPTS: search warrant purpose, judicial oversight, control design, general warrants, trespass, occupier rights]

This chunk covers the common law purpose of a search warrant and related practitioner commentary.

Purpose of a search warrant: Search warrant operates as a control design --imposes an independent judicial officer between official and citizen. That's why at CL you see the initial conservative approach to issue search warrant (and the jealously the CL protects rights of occupiers)

At CL: need to ensure that goods ceased during the search were covered by the search warrant (if not, could be sued for trespass to private property)

CL rejected the idea of general warrants (i.e. unspecified warrants to search anytime/anywhere) --because they had been used by the Star Chamber in a very oppressive way.

---

### Chunk 6 — Statutory extensions and listed statutes
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Search Warrants Act 1997 (Tas); Crimes Act 1914 (Cth); Police Offences Act 1935 (Tas)] [SECTION: Search Warrants Act 1997 (Tas) general; Crimes Act 1914 (Cth) s 3E; Police Offences Act 1935 (Tas) s 60] [CITATION: Statutory extensions - Search Warrants Act 1997 (Tas) and related statutes] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: statutory expansion of warrant purposes and relevant Tasmanian/Cth provisions] [CONCEPTS: s5 SWA, s3E Crimes Act, s60 Police Offences Act, statutory warrants, expansion of powers]

This chunk covers statutory extensions to warrant scope and identified statutes.

Have expanded to any offence at all (s5 SWA), not just re: stolen property.

*George v Rockett* (1990) 170 CLR 104

HC explaining the purpose of a search warrant

*Search Warrants Act 1997* (Tas)

*Crimes Act 1914* (Cth), s 3E

*Police Offences Act* 1935 (Tas), s 60

General warrant provision, but confined to searches for stolen property (issued to all PO by the commissioner of police, not issued by an independent judicial officer). They are issued for 6months (and allow police to enter any premises where they have a reasonable believe there may be stolen property).

\*Not HR's compliant (as no safeguard of judicial oversight)

General warrant, couple with *Spyke* gives PO a pretty free hand at searching shit in Tas. All they need is reasonable belief

### Chunk 7 — Granting a warrant and George v Rockett (1990) 170 CLR 104
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: George v Rockett (1990) 170 CLR 104] [CITATION: George v Rockett (1990) 170 CLR 104 - Granting a warrant discussion] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: George v Rockett on judicial officer's role in issuing warrants] [CONCEPTS: reasonable suspicion, issuing authority mind, independent judicial officer, warrant validity, Rockett]

This chunk covers the facts and holdings of George v Rockett and practitioner notes on grant of warrants.

***George v Rockett* (1990) 170 CLR 104**

F: PO had given an application for a warrant to a Qld Magistrate. The application just stated that reasonable grounds for believe that there were documents on premises re: ex-premier.

HC held: this was completely legit. Just need sufficient evidence placed before the magistrate or JP so that they themselves have a RB/Suspicion that the items were on the property.

Purpose of a search warrant: Search warrant operates as a control design --imposes an independent judicial officer between official and citizen. That's why at CL you see the initial conservative approach to issue search warrant (and the jealously the CL protects rights of occupiers)

This gives an indication of what a JO should do when decided whether to issue a warrant.

HC said warrants can only work effectively if the JO brings an independent mind to the application

---

### Chunk 8 — MacLeod (1991) 61 A.Crim.R. 465 — mind of issuing authority
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: MacLeod (1991) 61 A.Crim.R. 465] [CITATION: MacLeod (1991) 61 A.Crim.R. 465 - issuing authority mind requirement] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: requirement that issuing authority form reasonable belief independently] [CONCEPTS: applicant evidence, JP notebook, affidavit, independent mind, warrant sufficiency]

This chunk covers MacLeod and its application of George v Rockett.

***MacLeod (1991) 61 A.Crim.R. 465***

Applied George v Rockett (Slicer J)

What is required for a valid warrant: not the mind of the applicant, but the mind of the issuing authority. Justice cannot rely on the suspicion/believe of the applicant, must form it themselves.

Applications are usually made on oath, or by affidavit, and must put before the authority sufficient evidence to enable them to form a reasonable believe/suspicion

This case is an example where warrant had no technical defect, however court could not be satisfied on the evidence it had (JPs notebook), that the JP actually did have sufficient evidence before him to form reasonable suspicion/belief.

---

### Chunk 9 — Disclosure obligations for warrant applicants; Karina Fisheries v Mitson and Lego Australia v Paraggio
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Karina Fisheries v Mitson (1990) 26 FCR 473; Lego Australia v Paraggio (1994) 52 FCR 542] [CITATION: Disclosure obligations - Karina Fisheries and Lego Australia] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: disclosure duties and misrepresentation impact on warrants] [CONCEPTS: full disclosure, non-disclosure, fraud, misrepresentation, warrant invalidation]

This chunk covers what disclosure is required of the party seeking a warrant.

***Disclosure -- what is required of the party seeking a warrant?***

*Karina Fisheries v Mitson* (1990) 26 FCR 473

Full disclosure by the applicant is required that might effect the making of a decision

*Lego Australia v Paraggio* (1994) 52 FCR 542

Only fraud or misrepresentation will invalidate a warrant (sooo mixed authority on this point)

Probs best if PO do disclose all material knowledge they have to the issuing authority, and then if it is issued, unlikely to be challenged in future for non disclosure

### Chunk 10 — Wording of a warrant; NSW v Corbett (2007) 230 CLR 606
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: New South Wales v Corbett (2007) 230 CLR 606] [CITATION: NSW v Corbett (2007) 230 CLR 606 - wording of warrant technical defects] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: effect of incorrect statutory references in warrant wording] [CONCEPTS: warrant wording, technical defects, clarity of search purpose, statutory reference errors, power to search]

This chunk covers NSW v Corbett and practitioner notes on the wording of warrants.

***NSW v Corbett* (2007) 230 CLR 606**

F: Warrant made reference to a section in legislation that had been repealed at the time it had been applied for

HC: The fact the warrant referred to a section that had been repealed didn't matter, as it was quite clear on the warrant what the PO were searching for (unlawfully possessed firearms), didn't matter that the wrong provision had been listed in the warrant.

Sooo....don't need to be technical, but people need to know what the PO are searching for, and that they do have the power to search (which they did in that case)

### Chunk 11 — Extent of power under a warrant; Challenge Plastics and local application
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Challenge Plastics v Collector of Customs (1993) 115 ALR 149; Ghani v Jones (local application); Spyk/Spyke reference] [CITATION: Challenge Plastics (1993) - extent of search warrant powers] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: scope of conduct authorized by warrant and Tasmanian application] [CONCEPTS: scope of warrant, statutory scrutiny, reasonable conduct, s6(1)(e) SWA Tas, Ghani v Jones, Spyk]

Scrutinize legislation to establish what conduct it authorizes.

In Tasmania, we have applied *Ghani v Jones* and *Spyk*

s 6(1)(e) *Search Warrants Act 1997* (Tas)

Must behave reasonably

### Chunk 12 — Legal Professional Privilege (LPP) and search warrants (doctrine and Baker v Campbell)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Baker v Campbell (1983) 153 CLR 52] [CITATION: LPP and search warrants - Baker v Campbell (1983) 153 CLR 52] [CATEGORY: legal doctrine] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: priority of legal professional privilege over search warrants] [CONCEPTS: legal professional privilege, privilege trumping warrant, pre-hearing LPP, sequestration, Oke rules]

This chunk covers LPP as a fundamental common law right and its interaction with search warrants.

Kirby J describes it as a fundamental CL right. And thus it should not be the subject of forced disclosure at the cost of anything. Thus where does it stand when police have a warrant (can they seize items subject to LPP?)

HC authority suggests they can't, and thus LPP trumps a search warrant. Thus, police unable to seize documents covered by LPP from a firm or home. LPP applies pre hearing and at hearing.

Thus a quandary when someone claims LPP in relation to items the police wish to seize. If police look at them, they breach LPP (which is unlawful). This in *Oke*, a number of formal rules were set down for when someone claims LPP:

The rules provide that the document should be bundled up and sent to 3^rd^ party for safe keeping (police not to look). Then the person claiming LPP has a specified time to establish LPP on them on interlocutory proceedings before a court (i.e. proceeding before the proceeding).

*Baker v Campbell* (1983) 153 CLR 52

HC decision: legal professional privilege trumps the search warrant (unless the legislation under which the search warrant derives allows it) --courts don't like legislation to trump LPP

---

### Chunk 13 — Execution of a warrant; Crowley v Murphy and Oke
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Crowley v Murphy (1981) 34 ALR 496; Oke v Commr Australian Federal Police (2007) 168 A Crim R 503] [CITATION: Execution of warrant - Crowley, Oke, Larsson references] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: principles for executing warrants, fishing expeditions, LPP handling rules] [CONCEPTS: execution of warrant, fishing expedition, privacy, reasonable identification, LPP procedure, interlocutory proceedings]

This chunk covers principles and cases concerning execution of warrants and LPP handling.

*Crowley v Murphy* (1981) 34 ALR 496

Set out the principles behind search warrants, and relies heavily on the principle of legality and the right to privacy. Search warrant can't be used by police to go on a fishing expedition. Thus can't search whole filing cabinet to cease items in it --must be sensible about identifying items that are covered by the warrant. Or 'wreck revenge on people'

*Oke v Commr Australian Federal Police* (2007) 168 A Crim R 503

See notes directly above re: LPP rules/guidelines

Therese reckons this case shows the bravery of lawyers standing up to police

### Chunk 14 — Larsson v Commr of Police for NSW (1988) 16 NSWLR 173 and s 9(2) SWA Tas — single-hit execution and return
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Larsson v Commr of Police for NSW (1988) 16 NSWLR 173] [ACT: Search Warrants Act 1997 (Tas)] [SECTION: s 9(2)] [CITATION: Larsson (1988) - single-hit execution and s9(2) SWA Tas temporary cessation] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: when and how a warrant is executed, power to return to premises, temporary cessation rules] [CONCEPTS: single-hit execution, return to premises, temporary cessation, consent of occupier, s9(2) SWA Tas]

This chunk covers Larsson and the Tasmanian statutory provision on temporary cessation.

*Larsson v Commr of Police for NSW* (1988) 16 NSWLR 173

Warrant can only be executed in one hit, unless legislation gives them the power to return (however may have separate searches under general warrant)

s 9(2) *Search Warrants Act 1997* (Tas).

Does permit temporary cessation and return to searching under a warrant (but only allows one hour-so basically a lunch break), unless they have consent of the occupier

### Chunk 16 — Interrogation: Williams (1986) and detention powers
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Williams (1986) 161 CLR 278] [ACT: Criminal Law (Detention and Interrogation) Act 1995 (Tas)] [SECTION: s 4 referenced] [CITATION: Williams (1986) 161 CLR 278 - interrogation and detention limits] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: arrest for investigative purposes and detention limits] [CONCEPTS: arrest purpose, interrogation, detention power, s4 Detention and Interrogation Act, right to silence]

This chunk covers the principle that police cannot arrest simply for furthering investigation and notes Tasmanian statutory detention power.

*Williams* (1986) 161 CLR 278 (**CR**)

**H**. Police cannot arrest for the purposes of furthering the investigation or simply asking questions.

However, note the above discussion regarding s4 of the police the *Criminal Law (Detention and Interrogation) Act 1995* (Tas) regarding the power to detain people for reasonable time after lawful arrest.

### Chunk 17 — Other Protections for Detainees: Video recording requirement s85A Evidence Act 2001 (Tas)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Evidence Act 2001 (Tas)] [SECTION: s85A; s85 exception referenced] [CITATION: Evidence Act 2001 (Tas) s85A - video recording protections] [CATEGORY: legal doctrine] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: video recording of admissions and admissibility consequences] [CONCEPTS: s85A, video recording, admissions, inadmissibility, official questioning, exceptions]

This chunk covers the statutory video-recording protection for detainees in Tasmania.

This chunk covers video recording protections for detainees.

s85A of the *Evidence Act* *2001* (Tas) requires video recording of admissions; if not complied with evidence will be inadmissible (unless exceptions apply). Designed as a protective mechanism to prevent 'verballing'.

---

### Chunk 18 — R v Kelly (2004) 205 ALR 274 — videotaping and "official questioning"
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: R v Kelly (2004) 205 ALR 274] [CITATION: R v Kelly (2004) 205 ALR 274 - videotaping admissions and "official questioning"] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Kelly on what constitutes "official questioning" for s85A Evidence Act] [CONCEPTS: verbal admissions, video recording requirement, official questioning, narrow textual approach, exceptions]

This chunk covers facts and holding in R v Kelly about video recording.

*R v Kelly* (2004) 205 ALR 274 (**CR**)

**F**. Kelly didn't want to participate in video recording and instead made admissions on paper. He then made a video recording stating that the admission was only made because the police were harassing him. He then made another statement saying that he was sorry for the video and his own performance while being video taped (stating that he was 'playing the game').

Admissions had to be taped if they were in the course of 'official questioning'. So what is official questioning? Accused argued that it was during official questioning therefore admission not admissible.

**H**. HCA held that this was not official questioning. Majority took a very narrow, textual and specific analysis of the provision while the minority took a far more purposive approach.

Kelly still stands as the law, though we have had differently constituted High Court's interpret different (yet similar provisions) much broader (i.e. *Nichols v Coates*).

---

### Chunk 19 — R v Challender [2007] TASSC 58 — "chat" vs official questioning and fairness exclusion
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: R v Challender [2007] TASSC 58] [CITATION: R v Challender [2007] TASSC 58 - chat vs official questioning] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: application of Kelly; informal pre-interview chat leading to exclusion on fairness grounds] [CONCEPTS: pre-interview chat, official questioning, exclusionary discretion, fairness, Blow J]

This chunk covers R v Challender and its critique of police technique.

*R v Challender* \[2007\] TASSC 58

**F**. Accused arrested early in the morning and was still in detention in the afternoon. Before interview, officer took her outside for chat and smoke. Told her what was going to happen in the interview (i.e. kinds of questions etc) and that she was going to be charged with drug offences.

She said during the chat that all she had was a couple of pills. When he took her up to the interview room he asked her what she had said earlier and asked her to confirm it. Accused became confused and reluctant and wanted to speak to lawyer. Then confirmed she had done it. So the question was, was the earlier chat 'official questioning'.

**H**. Applying Kelly, the court held it was not official questioning. Blow J held that this was not a good approach though. Had complied with the letter of the law but not the spirit. Here they were confirming the previous admission (but it wasn't a very ethical approach -- i.e. be friendly to start with to get them to confess and then trap them). So Blow J held unfair, and excluded the evidence on that basis.

---

### Chunk 20 — Exceptions to video recording (s85 Evidence Act) and R v Browne [2000] TASSC 105
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Evidence Act 2001 (Tas)] [SECTION: s85] [CITATION: s85 Evidence Act exceptions and R v Browne] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: exceptional circumstances exception to video recording requirement] [CONCEPTS: exceptional circumstances, evidence exception, police equipment, true exception, Cleary J, Browne]

This chunk covers the exceptional circumstances exception and R v Browne.

Exceptions to the video recording provisions are in s85 of Evidence Act. May be unreasonable in some situations to require recording. See for example Cox CJ in *Browne*.

*R v Browne* \[2000\] TASSC 105 (**CR**)

**F**. Accused charged with 3 counts of sexual assault. Reasonable mistake as to age was raised as defence. Case considered the meaning of the exceptional circumstances exception in s85 of the *Evidence Act*.

**H**. Cleary lays down the proposition that a court will only find the circumstantial exceptional if the circumstances are ***truly exceptional***. The police should be able to take equipment with them when they know that they are going to make an arrest. Not a basis to excuse the making of the video tape that the police didn't have video equipment with them when they knew they were going to be making a video.

There is also an exception where the accused doesn't consent to the taping (e.g. *Kelly* and also *Nicholls v Coates*). This may take it out of the required area for video taping. Does this mean that the video must be consented to? What about video taping without knowledge? These questions were both considered in *Carr*.

---

### Chunk 21 — Carr v Western Australia (2007) 239 ALR 415 — consent and meaning of interview
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Carr v Western Australia (2007) 239 ALR 415] [CITATION: Carr v Western Australia (2007) 239 ALR 415 - consent and interview meaning for videotaping] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: HCA purposive approach to videotaping and interview definition] [CONCEPTS: consent, CCTV recording, meaning of interview, purposive interpretation, Kirby J dissent]

This chunk covers Carr and its approach to videotaping requirements.

*Carr v Western Australia* (2007) 239 ALR 415

**F**. Admissions made following a bank robbery. Banter outside of the lockup area was going on and the accused wasn't aware that CCTV was recording everything he was saying.

**H**. HCA took a purposive approach. Purpose was to ensure formality. Considered both the consent issue and meaning of interview raised in Browne:

\(1\) *Consent*: As long as the interview was still formal then it was ok. Majority believed that you did not have to give consent. Nothing in the wording of the legislation which imposes a consent requirement on the police. Note, Kirby J dissented and believed that consent was required.

\(2\) *Meaning of interview*: accused argued that the discussions were not an interview. Majority held however that the discussion did constitute an interview (note Kirby J's consent -- needs to be degree of formality to protect right to silence). There had been ***no trickery or collusion***. Majority recognised the purpose of the provisions (to protect the accused) and it is necessary to give it a wide interpretation in order to apply the protection of video recording. Thus, what has occurred here (information conversation outside of the interview room) also constituted an interview.

---

[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Crimes Act 1914 (Cth); Australian Security Intelligence Organisation Act 1979 (Cth)] [SECTION: Crimes Act 1914 (Cth) s 23C-W; ASIO Act s 34ZP] [CITATION: Cross-reference - Crimes Act s23C-W and ASIO Act s34ZP] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: related Commonwealth provisions on questioning and intelligence exceptions] [CONCEPTS: Crimes Act s23C-W, ASIO exceptional powers, questioning exceptions, federal cross-reference, intelligence interviews]
