# Criminal law: Interpreter requirement under detention and interrogation
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Criminal Law (Detention & Interrogation) Act 1995 (Tas)] [SECTION: s 5] [CITATION: Criminal Law (Detention & Interrogation) Act 1995 (Tas) s 5] [TYPE: procedural rule] [TOPIC: right to interpreter in detention/interrogation] [CONCEPTS: interpreter right, procedural fairness, detained person, questioning, language assistance, summons interpreter, hearsay implications]

The Criminal Law (Detention & Interrogation) Act 1995 (Tas) s 5 provides for the provision of an interpreter where required during detention and interrogation. The statutory rule requires that, when a detained person lacks sufficient understanding of English, an interpreter must be made available to ensure that the person understands rights, questions, and the consequences of answers given during custodial interviews. This obligation promotes procedural fairness and the voluntariness and reliability of any admissions. Police interviewing guidelines in Tasmania also treat the provision of an interpreter as mandatory practice. Failure to obtain an interpreter can engage hearsay issues and may require the prosecution to call or summons the interpreter to give evidence about what was communicated; non-compliance can result in exclusion of evidence or adverse findings about voluntariness. Relevant case law in multiple jurisdictions interprets how strictly this requirement is applied and the consequences of failure to provide an interpreter, including the need to assess whether the accused truly understood the interview and whether subsequent statements are admissible.

---

### Authority - interpreter cases and related authorities
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: Tas v Stojakovic [2008] TASSC 48; DPP v Viel & Vocaj [2024] VCC 336; VtV v R [2012] VSCA 213] [TOPIC: consequences of failing to obtain interpreter; hearsay; summonsing interpreter] [CONCEPTS: interpreter case law, summons interpreter, hearsay, admissibility, detained person understanding, interview guidelines]

These authorities address legal consequences where an interpreter was not obtained or where issues arise about understanding during police interviews. Tas v Stojakovic [2008] TASSC 48 considered the statutory requirement under s 5 and local practice; VtV v R [2012] VSCA 213 discussed hearsay implications and the circumstances where an interpreter must be summoned to give evidence about the content and meaning of statements. DPP v Viel & Vocaj [2024] VCC 336 (21 March 2024) is a Victorian decision addressing practical and procedural consequences when interpretation issues affect the admissibility or reliability of evidence. Collectively these cases illustrate that failure to provide an interpreter can lead to exclusion of admissions, the need to call an interpreter at trial, and scrutiny of whether the accused understood their rights and the interview questions.

# Personal search — common law rule on searches of persons
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: limits on personal searches at common law] [CONCEPTS: right to privacy, search incident to arrest, warrant requirement, consent to search, scope of personal search, standing orders not sufficient, reasonableness]

The common law recognises a strong right to privacy and provides that personal searches and seizures may only be lawfully executed in limited circumstances: (1) pursuant to a warrant; (2) incident to a lawful arrest; or (3) with consent. This doctrine constrains police powers so that routine or generalised searches (including searches based on standing orders) are not lawful absent specific, reasonable grounds related to the person searched. The common-law test focuses on whether, in the particular circumstances, there are reasonable grounds to believe a search is necessary for a defined purpose (for example, to find evidence, prevent destruction of evidence, to locate weapons, to secure items facilitating escape, or to remove items that could be used for self-harm). Policies requiring blanket searches for categories of arrestees are insufficient unless they align with individualized reasonable grounds. The prescriptive purposes and individualized assessment limit invasions of bodily privacy and require context-specific justification.

---

### Authority - Lindley v R (search of female detainee; removal of underwear)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: Lindley v R [1981] QB 128] [TOPIC: limits on invasive personal searches; necessity and reasonable grounds] [CONCEPTS: invasive search, removal of underwear, reasonable grounds, standing orders, search purposes]

Lindley v R [1981] QB 128 established that extremely invasive searches (including removal of underwear) require specific lawful basis and cannot be justified solely by blanket policies. In that case the court held police had no lawful basis to remove underwear where the individual had given no indication of self-harm and there were no particularized grounds to suspect concealed evidence. The decision restated that searches incident to arrest are limited to purposes such as obtaining evidence, preventing destruction of evidence, locating items that could facilitate escape or commit further offences, and removing items that may cause self-harm. The ruling emphasised that "standing orders" requiring classes of arrestees to be stripped or have underwear removed are insufficient without case-specific reasonable grounds.

# Police Offences Act 1935 (Tas) s 58B — search of person in custody
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Police Offences Act 1935 (Tas)] [SECTION: s 58B] [CITATION: Police Offences Act 1935 (Tas) s 58B] [TYPE: statutory provision] [TOPIC: statutory search powers of person in custody] [CONCEPTS: search in custody, removal of clothing, seizure powers, reasonable grounds, use of force, retain property, evidence preservation]

Section 58B of the Police Offences Act 1935 (Tas) authorises a police officer who reasonably believes it is necessary to search a person in lawful custody to search that person for specified purposes and to take and retain certain articles. The statutory purposes include: (a) ascertaining whether the person has concealed a weapon or article capable of inflicting injury or assisting escape; (ab) removing into safekeeping other articles belonging to the person; and (b) obtaining evidence relating to the commission of an offence or preventing loss or destruction of such evidence. Subsection (2) limits subsection (1)(b) by providing that a police officer cannot require removal of any clothing unless there are reasonable grounds to believe that removal may provide evidence of the commission of an offence. Subsection (3) permits retention of weapons or articles found and clothing removed under subsection (2); subsection (4) permits the use of reasonable force to exercise powers under the section; subsection (5) preserves searches otherwise lawful. The section thus gives statutory force to many common-law constraints, while authorising targeted searches in custody on reasonable grounds.

---

### Authority - Nguyen v Elliot (search and prosecutions for resisting)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: Nguyen v Elliot (VicSC, 6 February 1995)] [TOPIC: unlawfulness of search; consequences for prosecutions arising from resistance] [CONCEPTS: reasonable grounds, unlawful search, resisting arrest, credibility findings, appellate deference, search for drugs]

Nguyen v Elliot (VicSC, 6 February 1995) involved police approaching a group they suspected of drug involvement, conducting a search over objections, and then charging an individual after a scuffle. The trial judge and appeal court held the police lacked reasonable grounds for the search and therefore the initial police actions were not in the execution of duty; this affected the lawfulness of subsequent actions and prosecutions for resisting police. The appellate court ultimately upheld the decision based on trial judge credibility findings but expressed reluctance to depart from police accounts. Nguyen illustrates that unlawful searches can vitiate subsequent prosecutions and underscores the evidentiary and credibility importance of demonstrating reasonable grounds for searches.

# Misuse of Drugs Act 2001 (Tas) — stop-and-search powers (ss 29–30 and s 32)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Misuse of Drugs Act 2001 (Tas)] [CITATION: Misuse of Drugs Act 2001 (Tas) ss 29–30; s 32] [TYPE: statutory provision] [TOPIC: stop-and-search and pursuit powers] [CONCEPTS: reasonable grounds to suspect, stop and search, controlled substance, search without arrest, pursuit onto private property, uninterrupted pursuit]

The Misuse of Drugs Act 2001 (Tas) contains express stop-and-search powers: ss 29–30 permit a police officer to stop and search a person where the officer has reasonable grounds for suspecting the person possesses a controlled substance; these powers operate independently of arrest and therefore represent a statutory exception to the common-law requirement of arrest or warrant. Section 32 provides a separate power allowing a police officer who reasonably believes a person has committed an offence under the Act and who immediately and continuously pursues that person (who then flees into a private residence or other non-public place) to enter the premises without warrant and using no more force than reasonably necessary to continue the arrest or exercise the power. Together, these provisions expand police search and entry powers in drug enforcement contexts but remain constrained by the statutory requirement of reasonable grounds and limits on the force used.

# Forensic Procedures Act 2000 (Tas) — medical/forensic procedures: intimate vs non-intimate
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Forensic Procedures Act 2000 (Tas)] [CITATION: Forensic Procedures Act 2000 (Tas)] [TYPE: statutory provision] [TOPIC: forensic sampling; intimate and non-intimate procedures] [CONCEPTS: forensic procedures, intimate procedures, non-intimate procedures, informed consent, judicial authorisation, blood sample, swabs, genital examinations]

The Forensic Procedures Act 2000 (Tas) differentiates forensic procedures into "non-intimate" and "intimate" categories and prescribes different consent and authorisation requirements. Non-intimate procedures (for example: taking blood, saliva swabs, non-pubic hair samples, nail clippings, fingernail scrapings, chemical washes, fingerprints, handprints, footprints, photographs) may be performed with the person's consent or, in some cases, on the authority of a senior police officer (sergeant or above). Intimate procedures (for example: examinations of genital areas, cavity searches, pubic hair samples, genital impressions) require informed consent or a judicial officer order. The Act therefore restricts intrusive bodily procedures, protecting bodily integrity by requiring higher safeguards for intimate procedures. Commonwealth analogues include Crimes Act 1914 (Cth) provisions (ss 3ZJ–3ZL) that deal with comparable forensic procedure authorisations.

# Search of premises — common law protection and statutory clarity requirement
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: search of premises; trespass and statutory construction] [CONCEPTS: trespass, property rights, search warrants, narrow statutory construction, unmistakable and unambiguous language, intrusion, judicial control]

At common law the right of property holders to exclude others from their premises is fundamental; every unauthorised entry is a trespass. Consequently, courts construe statutory powers to enter and search premises narrowly and require that any statutory authority be expressed in unmistakable and unambiguous language. Traditionally, warrantless searches of premises were confined to searches pursuant to a search warrant or incident to a lawful arrest. The presumption is that, absent clear statutory language authorising otherwise, legislatures did not intend to permit intrusions on property and privacy. This doctrine underpins rigorous judicial scrutiny of police entries and searches without consent and supports remedies for unlawful entries.

---

### Authority - Coco v R (search of premises; trespass principle)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: Coco v R (1994) 179 CLR 427] [TOPIC: trespass and narrow construction of statutory powers to enter premises] [CONCEPTS: trespass, narrow construction, statutory authority, property rights, entry without consent]

Coco v R (1994) 179 CLR 427 reaffirmed that every unauthorised entry to private property is trespass and emphasised that statutory powers to enter must be clearly and unambiguously expressed. The High Court held that courts will narrowly construe any statutory regime that authorises entry to private property so as to protect the fundamental right to exclude. This principle requires express legislative authority for searches and grounds courts to be vigilant against expansive readings of executive power to enter premises.

# Family Violence Act 2004 (Tas) s 10 — power to enter premises without warrant
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Family Violence Act 2004 (Tas)] [SECTION: s 10] [CITATION: Family Violence Act 2004 (Tas) s 10] [TYPE: statutory provision] [TOPIC: police powers to enter private premises in family violence context] [CONCEPTS: entry without warrant, prevent family violence, arrest without warrant, search persons and premises, seize objects, firearms seizure, risk screening]

Family Violence Act 2004 (Tas) s 10 authorises a police officer, without warrant and using necessary force, to enter and remain on premises for a reasonable period to prevent family violence when requested by a person apparently residing there or when the officer reasonably suspects family violence is being, has been, or is likely to be committed. The section authorises arrest without warrant to facilitate protection orders, risk screening and safety audits, searches of persons and premises for objects used or created in family violence, seizure and retention of such objects, and entry to search for firearms where an officer reasonably suspects a person has committed or is likely to commit family violence and is in possession or control of a firearm. The provision therefore creates a statutory exception to ordinary warrant requirements tailored to the exigencies of family violence situations, subject to the reasonableness and necessity constraints stated.

# Misuse of Drugs Act 2001 (Tas) s 32 — pursuit into private property
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Misuse of Drugs Act 2001 (Tas)] [SECTION: s 32] [CITATION: Misuse of Drugs Act 2001 (Tas) s 32] [TYPE: statutory provision] [TOPIC: pursuit onto private property without warrant] [CONCEPTS: pursuit power, immediate pursuit, uninterrupted pursuit, entry without warrant, reasonable belief, force reasonably necessary]

Misuse of Drugs Act 2001 (Tas) s 32 authorises a police officer who reasonably believes a person has committed an offence under the Act, and who immediately and continuously pursues that person into a private residence or other non-public place, to enter without warrant and using only force reasonably necessary to effect the purpose. The statutory prerequisites require the officer to have reasonable belief of the commission of an offence, that the person fled while the officer was attempting arrest or exercising a power under the Act, that the pursuit was immediate and uninterrupted, and that the person entered private premises during the pursuit. The provision narrows but authorises exigent-entry powers in drug enforcement while retaining proportionality and reasonable-force limits.

---

### Authority - New South Wales v Ibbett (remedies for unlawful entry)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: New South Wales v Ibbett (2006) 229 CLR 638] [TOPIC: protection of private premises; remedies for unlawful police entry] [CONCEPTS: aggravated damages, unlawful entry, trespass remedy, executive accountability, protection of property rights]

New South Wales v Ibbett (2006) 229 CLR 638 demonstrated judicial willingness to protect property rights by awarding aggravated damages where police entered private premises unlawfully. The case shows the practical consequences for the executive when police unlawfully intrude on private premises and confirms that the courts will not shy from providing significant remedies to deter unlawful executive action.

---

### Authority - Kuru v New South Wales (principles on entry to land)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: Kuru v New South Wales [2008] HCA 26] [TOPIC: principles governing lawful entry to premises] [CONCEPTS: consent to entry, lawful authority, revocation of authority, prevention of breach of the peace, limits on investigative entry]

Kuru v New South Wales [2008] HCA 26 restated key principles: (1) entry to another's land must be justified by consent or lawful authority; (2) police have no special rights of entry except those provided by common law or statute; (3) authority to enter may be revoked and entrant must leave once authority is revoked; and (4) police may enter premises to prevent a breach of the peace but not merely to investigate whether a breach has occurred. These principles reinforce property rights and constrain investigatory entries.

# Scope of searches incidental to arrest — Dillon v O'Brian and Jeffrey v Black
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: geographic and temporal scope of searches incidental to arrest] [CONCEPTS: immediate area search, search of vehicle, scope of arrest-related search, moving arrestee, search limits]

At common law the personal search incident to arrest extends to the immediate area surrounding the arrestee (for example, the table in front of them or the vehicle they occupy), allowing officers to search for weapons, evidence or items facilitating escape. Dillon v O'Brian illustrates this principle by confirming searches may include the immediate vicinity of the arrestee. However, Jeffrey v Black [1977] 3 WLR 895 clarified that police may not effect an arrest in one location and then move the person to another location solely to conduct searches there; that would not constitute a lawful search incident to arrest. The legality of such searches depends on contemporaneous necessity and reasonableness.

---

### Authority - Ghani v Jones ('high water mark' for search seizure on entry)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: Ghani v Jones [1970] 1 QB 693] [TOPIC: conditions for lawful seizure when entering premises on warrant/arrest] [CONCEPTS: search on warrant, seizure of goods, reasonable belief, serious offence, evidence seizure, conduct judged at time of seizure]

Ghani v Jones [1970] 1 QB 693 represents a "high water mark" in authority permitting police, when entering premises pursuant to a search warrant or power of arrest for a serious offence, to search for and seize goods relating to that offence and, if reasonable, seize other goods found during the search. Lord Denning articulated conditions supporting such seizures: reasonable belief that a serious offence occurred; belief that property was the fruit, means, or evidence of the crime; belief that the possessor was involved; requirement to retain property no longer than necessary; and that lawfulness is judged at the time of seizure, not by subsequent events.

# Search warrants — purpose and judicial control (Search Warrants Act 1997 (Tas))
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Search Warrants Act 1997 (Tas)] [CITATION: Search Warrants Act 1997 (Tas)] [TYPE: procedural rule] [TOPIC: search warrants; judicial oversight] [CONCEPTS: search warrant purpose, judicial interposition, invaded privacy, particularity, control of executive, execution of warrant, cessation]

The Search Warrants Act 1997 (Tas) establishes the statutory framework for judicial authorisation of premises searches, creating a judicial gatekeeper between executive search powers and private citizens. A search warrant authorises invasion of private premises without occupier consent and requires the issuing judicial officer to be satisfied there are grounds for the warrant. Historically, common law limited search warrants to stolen property with strict particularity (Entick v Carrington (1765)); modern statutory regimes permit broader warrants subject to judicial assessment of supporting facts and reasonable grounds. The Act also governs execution and cessation of warrants, providing controls against fishing expeditions and requiring that searches conform to the warrant’s scope.

---

### Authority - Police Offences Act 1935 (Tas) s 60 — Commissioner's warrant
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Police Offences Act 1935 (Tas)] [SECTION: s 60] [CITATION: Police Offences Act 1935 (Tas) s 60] [TYPE: statutory provision] [TOPIC: Commissioner's general warrant for stolen property] [CONCEPTS: general warrant, Commissioner's warrant, stolen property, six-month validity, revocation]

Section 60 of the Police Offences Act 1935 (Tas) authorises the Commissioner to issue a general warrant to a police officer to search for stolen property. The warrant must be personally signed by the Commissioner, remains in force for up to six months (unless revoked sooner), authorises specified officers to exercise the powers in the warrant, and may be revoked by the Commissioner at any time. This statutory mechanism departs from the strict common-law particularity requirement by permitting a form of general warrant for stolen property, subject to executive regulation and internal controls.

# Duties of applicant and issuing officer for search warrants — George v Rockett and related authorities
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: issuing officer's duty to be satisfied and need for supporting facts] [CONCEPTS: reasonable grounds, issuing officer state of mind, supporting information, particularity, rubber-stamping prohibited, enquiry duty]

An applicant for a search warrant must provide information capable of persuading the issuing judicial officer that there are reasonable grounds for the warrant; the issuing officer must actually bring their mind to the question rather than mechanically rubber-stamping applications. George v Rockett (1990) 170 CLR 104 established that the issuing officer must be satisfied there are grounds for issuing the warrant and that mere assertion or the informant's suspicion is insufficient — supporting facts that provide reasonable grounds must be presented. The issuing officer's state of mind is determinative; where the officer has concerns or ambiguities, an obligation exists to make further enquiries to resolve those concerns. While Beneficial Finance Corp v AFP (1991) 31 FCR 523 emphasised the issuing officer need not investigate every deficiency behind the warrant, it confirmed a duty to address obvious ambiguities or gaps before issuing.

---

### Authority - George v Rockett (issuing officer satisfaction; meaning of suspicion)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: George v Rockett (1990) 170 CLR 104] [TOPIC: issuing officer's satisfaction and required supporting facts] [CONCEPTS: satisfaction, reasonable grounds, informant suspicion, judicial consideration, particularity requirement]

George v Rockett (1990) 170 CLR 104 held that a warrant will not be lawfully issued unless the judicial officer is satisfied there are grounds for issuing it; this requires the applicant to present information capable of producing reasonable grounds. The High Court explained that "suspicion" is more than idle wondering — it is a positive apprehension amounting to a slight opinion — and that issuing officers must exercise independent judgment rather than simply adopting an informant’s assertion.

# Degree of precision required in warrants — NSW v Corbett
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: New South Wales v Corbett [2007] HCA 32] [TOPIC: precision of offence description in search warrants] [CONCEPTS: statement of offence, nature of offence sufficient, indictment precision not required, boundaries of search, material misapprehension]

New South Wales v Corbett [2007] HCA 32 clarified that the description of the offence in a search warrant need not meet the precision required of an indictment; it is sufficient to state the nature of the offence so that the purpose and boundaries of the search are clear. A misdescription that does not materially mislead as to the nature of the offence will not necessarily invalidate a warrant. The decision emphasises functional adequacy over formalistic precision in warrant descriptions, provided the misstatement does not create a material misapprehension about the search's scope.

# Duty of disclosure and limits on fishing expeditions — Karina Fisheries, Lego, Crowley v Murphy
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: disclosure duties in warrant applications and prohibition on fishing searches] [CONCEPTS: duty of disclosure, non-disclosure, fraud or misrepresentation, fishing expedition, particularity of documents, best practice]

There is conflicting authority on the extent of an applicant's duty to disclose matters that could affect the issuing officer's decision. Karina Fisheries v Mitson (1990) 26 FCR 473 suggests a broad duty to disclose all matters affecting issuance, whereas Lego Australia v Paraggio (25/8/94) and other authorities indicate that only fraud or misrepresentation will invalidate a warrant for non-disclosure. Nevertheless, best practice for prosecutors and applicants is full disclosure of relevant material. Crowley v Murphy (1981) 34 ALR 496 emphasises that police must not use warrants as a "fishing exercise" to search for unspecified or unknown documents; the warrant must be directed by particularity and reasonable grounds.

---

### Authority - Baker v Campbell (legal professional privilege and search warrants)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: Baker v Campbell (1983) 153 CLR 52] [TOPIC: legal professional privilege as fundamental right; limits on search warrants for privileged documents] [CONCEPTS: legal professional privilege, fundamental right, exclusion from warrants, privileged documents, procedural protection]

Baker v Campbell (1983) 153 CLR 52 established that legal professional privilege is a fundamental common-law right, not merely an evidentiary rule; accordingly, search warrants cannot lawfully be applied to documents protected by privilege. The High Court treated privileged documents as deserving special protection and required mechanisms to ensure privilege claims are respected during warrant execution.

# Practical handling of privileged documents and related authority — AFP procedure and Oke v Commr AFP
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: Oke v Commr Australian Federal Police [2007] FCA 27] [TOPIC: execution of warrants and privileged documents; AFP handling protocol] [CONCEPTS: privileged documents procedure, independent reviewer, return of documents, warrant execution, invalid warrant, negotiation during search]

Following Baker, the AFP and law reform bodies adopted a practice whereby seized documents are quarantined and submitted to an independent reviewer while the person claiming privilege is given an opportunity (usually four days) to establish privilege. Oke v Commr Australian Federal Police [2007] FCA 27 illustrates problems that arise when the warrant is exercised improperly and negotiations over privileged materials fail; in Oke the court found the warrant invalid given the AFP's conduct and handling of privileged materials. The decision underscores the importance of strict adherence to procedures designed to protect privilege during searches.

# Execution and cessation of warrants — Larsson and statutory cessation (Search Warrants Act s 9)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Search Warrants Act 1997 (Tas)] [SECTION: s 9] [CITATION: Search Warrants Act 1997 (Tas) s 9] [TYPE: procedural rule] [TOPIC: when a warrant is executed and cessation of search] [CONCEPTS: execution of warrant, everything lawfully done, cessation, completion of lawful acts, return of warrant, limits on execution]

A warrant is executed when everything that can lawfully be done under it has been done; the cessation of a search occurs once all authorised acts have been completed. Larsson v Commr of Police for NSW (1988) 16 NSWLR 173 articulates the point that a warrant's execution occurs at completion of lawful acts. Search Warrants Act 1997 (Tas) s 9 contains statutory provisions dealing with cessation and procedural consequences when lawful actions under the warrant have concluded. Execution and cessation rules protect against prolonged or unnecessary invasions beyond what the warrant authorises.

---

[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: ICCPR Article 17 - Police Powers of Search note] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: International human rights principle relevant to search and privacy] [CONCEPTS: ICCPR, Article 17, privacy, search, international law]

Police Powers of Search

### Chunk 3 — Personal Search: common law privacy; Lindley v Rutter [1981] QB 128
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Lindley v Rutter [1981] QB 128] [CITATION: Lindley v Rutter [1981] QB 128 - Personal Search authority] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Personal search, scope of lawful search, removal of underwear, common law limitations] [CONCEPTS: personal search, privacy, underwear removal, standing orders, reasonable grounds, Lindley v Rutter, common law]

Covers common law recognition of right to privacy and the Lindley v Rutter decision regarding limits on personal searches.

The common law recognises the right to privacy should not be interfered with except for in extreme circumstances.

Thus, at common law, a lawful search and seizure can only

- occur pursuant to a warrant;

- incident to a lawful arrest; or

- with consent.

*Lindley v Rutter* [1981] QB 128 (**CR**)

**F**. Involved a woman being searched including the removal of underwear etc.

**H**. Held that the police had not basis for a lawful search, particularly to remove underwear. Appeal thus allowed and convictions quashed. Must not simply act on orders to search or seize, must consider the circumstances of the actual case to determine if the accused is doing anything that indicates a search may be necessary. Police argued that 'standing orders' required all females in custody had to remove bra. But she had done nothing to indicate she may self harm.

Restated the common law position (above). Further, they held that the powers of search in relation to arrest only extend to:

(1) obtaining evidence

(2) Prevent the destruction of evidence

(3) to locate items that may assist the accused to escape or commit further crime (i.e. to find a weapon that may help them escape)

(4) to search for items that may be used to inflict self harm.

So police powers of search are restricted to situations where they have a specific reasons to search a particular individual. Policies or 'standing orders' for particular classes of arrestee will not be a sufficient reason.

---

### Chunk 4 — Police Offences Act 1935 (Tas) s58B — Search of accused person in custody
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Police Offences Act 1935 (Tas)] [SECTION: s58B] [CITATION: Police Offences Act 1935 (Tas) s58B - Search of accused person in custody statutory text] [CATEGORY: legal doctrine] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Statutory powers to search person in custody and limits on removal of clothing] [CONCEPTS: search in custody, s58B, weapons, clothing removal, retention, reasonable grounds, police powers, force]

Covers statutory text of Police Offences Act 1935 (Tas) s58B concerning search of a person in lawful custody and related powers.

Note that we these principles now have statutory force:

*Police Offences Act* 1935 (Tas.), s 58B.

***Search of accused person in custody***

***(1)** If a police officer believes on reasonable grounds that it is necessary to search a person who is in lawful custody, a police officer may search that person --*

***(a)** for the purpose of ascertaining whether there is concealed on that person or in that person's*

*clothing a weapon or other article capable of being used to inflict injury or to assist that person to*

*escape from custody; or*

***(ab)** for the purpose of removing into safe keeping any other articles belonging to, or in the possession*

*of, the person; or*

***(b)** for the purpose of obtaining evidence relating to the commission of the offence or preventing the*

*loss or destruction of such evidence.*

***(2)** [[Subsection (1)(b)]{.underline}](http://www.thelaw.tas.gov.au/tocview/content.w3p;cond=;doc_id=44%2B%2B1935%2BGS58B%2FGs1%2FHpb%2FEN%2B20081016000000;histon=;prompt=;rec=;term=#GS58B@Gs1@Hpb@EN) does not authorise a police officer to require a person to remove any clothing unless there are reasonable grounds for believing that the removal may provide evidence of the commission of an offence.*

***(3)** A police officer may take and retain --*

***(a)** any weapon or article found as a result of the search under [[subsection (1)(a)]{.underline}](http://www.thelaw.tas.gov.au/tocview/content.w3p;cond=;doc_id=44%2B%2B1935%2BGS58B%2FGs1%2FHpa%2FEN%2B20081016000000;histon=;prompt=;rec=;term=#GS58B@Gs1@Hpa@EN); and*

***(b)** any article of clothing removed under [[subsection (2)]{.underline}](http://www.thelaw.tas.gov.au/tocview/content.w3p;cond=;doc_id=44%2B%2B1935%2BGS58B%2FGs2%2FEN%2B20081016000000;histon=;prompt=;rec=;term=#GS58B@Gs2@EN).*

***(4)** A police officer may use such force as is reasonably necessary for the purposes of exercising powers under this section.*

***(5)** Nothing in this section prevents a search of a person in lawful custody in any circumstances where it is otherwise lawful to search the person.*

### Chunk 5 — Nguyen v Elliot (6 February 1995 VicSC)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Nguyen v Elliot 6 February 1995 VicSC] [CITATION: Nguyen v Elliot - Unlawful search and resistance offences] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Search of group in park, lack of reasonable grounds, evidence, overturning charges] [CONCEPTS: stop and search, reasonable grounds, youth detention, unlawful search, resistance, VicSC, Nguyen v Elliot]

Covers facts and holding in Nguyen v Elliot about police lacking reasonable grounds for search and resultant acquittal of resistance offences.

*Nguyen v Elliot* 6th February 1995 VicSC

**F**. Police watching group of Asian youths in park (were known to have been involved in other offences). When they saw the police they behaved suspiciously (i.e. looking around etc). Eventually approached one of the boys and said they wanted to search. He objected. Police persisted and eventually he kicked a police officer (bit one during scuffle). Continued to resist and was then handcuffed and charged him with numerous offences relating to the resistance. No drugs found on him.

**H**. Accused defended the charges on the grounds that the police were not in the execution of their duty. They had no reasonable basis for the search. TJ and appeal judge agreed. Held that the police had no right to search the young men as there was no reasonable basis for the belief that the youths were in possession of drugs. Accused could thus not be prosecuted for the offences.

But, the only reason the Court of Criminal Appeal upheld the decision was that they said the TJ had a better basis to judge the demeanour of the witnesses. Clear they were reluctant to do this. The whole tenor of the decision is that they wanted to agree with the police.

---

### Chunk 6 — Inroads into personal search: Misuse of Drugs Act 2001 (Tas) ss29 & 30; Crimes Act 1914 (Cth) ss3ZE-3ZI
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Misuse of Drugs Act 2001 (Tas); Crimes Act 1914 (Cth)] [SECTION: Misuse of Drugs Act ss29-30; Crimes Act ss3ZE-3ZI] [CITATION: Misuse of Drugs Act 2001 (Tas) ss29-30 & Crimes Act 1914 (Cth) ss3ZE-3ZI - Inroads into search principles] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Statutory exceptions/expansions to common law search limitations (stop and search without arrest, frisk/strip search provisions)] [CONCEPTS: stop and search, reasonable suspicion, Misuse of Drugs Act, ss29, ss30, strip search, Crimes Act, ss3ZE-3ZI]

Covers statutory inroads into common law personal search principles by Misuse of Drugs Act and Crimes Act provisions.

Note the inroads into these principles; for example the *Misuse of Drugs Act* 2001 (Tas) ss29 & 30 provides that police can stop and search a person provided they have reasonable grounds for suspecting someone possesses a controlled substance. Search is thus made permissible without an arrest.

Also note the *Crimes Act* 1914 (Cth) ss3ZE-3ZI (covers frisk searches, ordinary searches and strip searches).

---

### Chunk 7 — Medical Examinations, Fingerprinting and Photographs (Forensic Procedures Act 2000 (Tas))
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Forensic Procedures Act 2000 (Tas)] [SECTION: general overview] [CITATION: Forensic Procedures Act 2000 (Tas) - Medical examinations, fingerprints, photographs (intimate vs non-intimate)] [CATEGORY: legal doctrine] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Forensic procedures classification; intimate vs non-intimate; consent and authorisation requirements] [CONCEPTS: forensic procedures, intimate, non-intimate, consent, senior police officer, judicial officer, fingerprints, swabs, pubic hair, genital examination, Forensic Procedures Act]

Covers classification of forensic procedures into intimate and non-intimate under the Forensic Procedures Act 2000 (Tas) and consent/order requirements, with lists of procedures.

Uniform legislation (*Forensic Procedures Act 2000* (Tas)) in this area gives the police very wide powers to obtain forensic samples from suspects and people charged with offences. Deals with two kinds of procedures; ***intimate*** and ***non-intimate***.

Non-intimate procedures can be performed with consent or on order of senior police officer (i.e. Sergeant or above).

Intimate procedures require informed consent or order of judicial officer.

***Non-intimate:***

- Taking blood

- Swabs (saliva)

- Hair samples (non-pubic)

- Nail clippings

- Scrapings from under nails

- Chemical washes

- Finger prints

- Hand prints

- Foot prints

- Photographs

- Etc

***Intimate:***

- Examinations of genital areas

- Cavity searches

- Pubic hair samples

- Genital impressions.

See also *Crimes Act* 1914 (Cth) ss.3ZJ-3ZL.

---

### Chunk 8 — Search of Premises: Coco v R (1994) 179 CLR 427 and common law protection of property
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Coco v R (1994) 179 CLR 427] [CITATION: Coco v R (1994) 179 CLR 427 - Common law protection of property and entry/search construal] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Common law protection of property; narrow construction of statutory authority to enter premises; requirement of clear language] [CONCEPTS: search of premises, trespass, Coco v R, private property, narrow statutory construction, warrant, incidental to arrest]

Covers common law protection of property and Coco v R holding about unauthorised entry being trespass; courts construe statutory authority narrowly.

The common law protection of property is extensive. For example held in *Coco v R* (1994) 179 CLR 427 at 435-6 the court held that every unauthorised entry to private property is a trespass and it is the right of property owners to exclude (this is a fundamental common law right).

Courts will therefore narrowly construe statutory authority to enter premises. Also, any statutory authority in that regard must be clearly expressed (*unmistakable and unambiguous language*). The presumption is that in the absence of express provision to the contrary the legislature did not intend to permit otherwise tortuous conduct. Therefore at common law, search of premises without consent is usually confined to searches pursuant to a ***search warrant*** or ***incidental to arrest***.

---

### Chunk 9 — Family Violence Act 2004 (Tas) s10 — Power of police to enter certain premises
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Family Violence Act 2004 (Tas)] [SECTION: s10] [CITATION: Family Violence Act 2004 (Tas) s10 - Power of police to enter certain premises statutory text] [CATEGORY: legal doctrine] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Police powers to enter premises without warrant in family violence situations; search/seizure/arrest powers] [CONCEPTS: family violence, entry without warrant, search, seizure, PFVO, FVO, s10, risk screening, forensic examination, firearms]

Covers statutory text of Family Violence Act 2004 (Tas) s10 concerning police powers to enter premises without warrant for family violence prevention and related powers.

*Family Violence Act 2004* (Tas), s 10

**Power of police to enter certain premises**

**(1)** A police officer may, without warrant, and using such force as is necessary, enter and remain on premises for such period as he or she considers reasonably necessary to prevent family violence --

**(a)** at the request of a person who apparently resides on the premises; or

**(b)** if the officer reasonably suspects that family violence is being, has been or is likely to be committed

on those premises.

**(2)** A police officer who enters premises under [[subsection (1)]{.underline}](http://www.thelaw.tas.gov.au/tocview/content.w3p;cond=;doc_id=67%2B%2B2004%2BGS10%2FGs1%2FEN%2B20081016000000;histon=;prompt=;rec=;term=#GS10@Gs1@EN) may --

**(a)** arrest, without warrant, any person on those premises to facilitate the issue of a PFVO, or the

making of an application for an FVO, in respect of that person; and

**(b)** remain on those premises in order to conduct a risk screening, safety audit or forensic examination

with such assistance as is necessary and reasonable in the circumstances.

**(3)** A police officer who enters premises under [[subsection (1)]{.underline}](http://www.thelaw.tas.gov.au/tocview/content.w3p;cond=;doc_id=67%2B%2B2004%2BGS10%2FGs1%2FEN%2B20081016000000;histon=;prompt=;rec=;term=#GS10@Gs1@EN) may, without warrant --

**(a)** search any person on those premises whom the officer reasonably suspects of having in his or her

possession any object which the police officer reasonably suspects has been used, or may be used, to

commit a family violence offence or has been created in the commission of a family violence offence;

and

**(b)** search those premises for the presence of any such object; and

**(c)** seize and retain any such object.

**(4)** If a police officer reasonably suspects that a person has committed family violence and in so acting has used or created an object, the officer may, without warrant and using such force as is necessary --

**(a)** enter any premises on which the officer reasonably suspects that the object may be found; and

**(b)** search those premises for the object; and

**(c)** search any person who the officer reasonably suspects has possession of the object; and

**(d)** seize and retain the object; and

**(e)** arrest, without warrant, any person on those premises to facilitate the issue of a PFVO, or the

making of an application for an FVO, in respect of that person.

**(5)** Where a police officer reasonably suspects that --

**(a)** a person has committed, or is likely to commit, family violence; and

**(b)** the person is in possession or control of a firearm --

the police officer may enter premises, without warrant and using such force as is reasonably necessary

in the circumstances, in order to search for and seize the firearm.

**(6)** On an application made by a police officer or by any person who claims to be the owner of an object seized and detained under [[subsection (3)]{.underline}](http://www.thelaw.tas.gov.au/tocview/content.w3p;cond=;doc_id=67%2B%2B2004%2BGS10%2FGs3%2FEN%2B20081016000000;histon=;prompt=;rec=;term=#GS10@Gs3@EN), [[(4)]{.underline}](http://www.thelaw.tas.gov.au/tocview/content.w3p;cond=;doc_id=67%2B%2B2004%2BGS10%2FGs4%2FEN%2B20081016000000;histon=;prompt=;rec=;term=#GS10@Gs4@EN) or [[(5)]{.underline}](http://www.thelaw.tas.gov.au/tocview/content.w3p;cond=;doc_id=67%2B%2B2004%2BGS10%2FGs5%2FEN%2B20081016000000;histon=;prompt=;rec=;term=#GS10@Gs5@EN), a court may order that the object --

**(a)** be forfeited to the Crown; or

**(b)** be destroyed; or

**(c)** be returned to the owner; or

**(d)** be otherwise disposed of in such manner as the court thinks fit.

**(7)** If a police officer reasonably suspects that a person has committed family violence, the officer, without warrant and using such force as is reasonably necessary in the circumstances, may --

**(a)** enter any premises on which the officer reasonably suspects the person may be found; and

**(b)** search those premises for the person; and

**(c)** arrest the person.

**(8)** In this section --

**"object"** means any matter, material or thing and includes firearms.

### Chunk 10 — Misuse of Drugs Act 2001 (Tas) s32 — Power to pursue suspect onto private property
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Misuse of Drugs Act 2001 (Tas)] [SECTION: s32] [CITATION: Misuse of Drugs Act 2001 (Tas) s32 - Power to pursue suspect onto private property statutory text] [CATEGORY: legal doctrine] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Pursuit power allowing entry to private residence during uninterrupted pursuit after flee] [CONCEPTS: pursuit, private property, s32, flee, immediate pursuit, without warrant, controlled substances, arrest]

Covers statutory text of Misuse of Drugs Act 2001 (Tas) s32 allowing pursuit onto private property under specified conditions.

*Misuse of Drugs Act 2001* (Tas), s32

**Power to pursue suspect onto private property**

Where:

**(a)** a police officer reasonably believes that a person has committed an offence under this Act; and

**(b)** the person flees from the police officer's presence while the police officer, or another police officer,

is trying to arrest the person or exercise another power under this Act in relation to the person; and

**(c)** the police officer, or another police officer, immediately pursues the person and the pursuit

continues without interruption; and

**(d)** during the pursuit the person enters a private residence or another place that is not a public place

the pursuing police officer may, ***without warrant*** and using no more force than is reasonably necessary for the purpose, enter the private residence or other place notwithstanding that it is not a public place.

---

### Chunk 11 — New South Wales v Ibbett (2006) 229 CLR 638 — courts protect private premises
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: New South Wales v Ibbett (2006) 229 CLR 638] [CITATION: New South Wales v Ibbett (2006) 229 CLR 638 - Protection of private premises and damages] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Protection of private premises from unauthorised entry; aggravated damages for unlawful entry] [CONCEPTS: trespass, aggravated damages, unlawful entry, private premises, New South Wales v Ibbett, CLR]

Covers the noted significance of New South Wales v Ibbett in protecting private premises and potential for aggravated damages.

*New South Wales v Ibbett* (2006) 229 CLR 638 (above)

**H**. This case demonstrated the lengths the court will go to in order to protect the above principles. If the police enter private premises without consent the courts will not be reluctant to award aggravated damages to send a clear message to the executive.

---

### Chunk 12 — Kuru v New South Wales [2008] HCA 26 — High Court principles on entry to land
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Kuru v New South Wales [2008] HCA 26] [CITATION: Kuru v New South Wales [2008] HCA 26 - Entry to land principles] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Principles on authority to enter land, consent, revocation, breach of the peace limitations] [CONCEPTS: entry to land, consent, lawful authority, breach of the peace, Kuru v NSW, HCA, occupier rights]

Covers High Court restatement of key principles about entry to land in Kuru v New South Wales.

*Kuru v New South Wales* [2008] HCA 26 at [40]-[55]

**H**. High Court re-stated a number of the key principles in this area.

(1) A person who enters the land of another must justify that entry by showing either that the entry was with the consent of the occupier or that the entrant had lawful authority to enter.

(2) Except in cases provided for by the common law and by statute, police officers have no special rights to enter land.

(3) Authority to enter land may be revoked and that, if the authority is revoked, the entrant no longer has authority to remain on the land but must leave as soon as is reasonably practicable.

(4) A police officer may enter premises to *prevent* a breach of the peace, but not to investigate whether a breach of the peace has or will occur.

---

### Chunk 13 — Dillon v O'Brian — extent of personal search incidental to arrest
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Dillon v O'Brian - Extent of personal search incidental to arrest] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Extent of personal search incidental to arrest and immediate surroundings] [CONCEPTS: incidental search, arrestee, immediate area, vehicle search, Dillon v O'Brian, reach of search]

Covers note that personal search incidental to arrest extends to immediate area around arrestee, with question of how far that can extend.

The common law originally confined search of premises (without consent) to searches pursuant to warrant or incidental to lawful arrest. *Dillon v O'Brian* shows that the personal search of an arrestee extends to the immediate area around the person (table in front of them or car they are in). The question is; how far can that search extend?

---

### Chunk 14 — Ghani v Jones [1970] 1 QB 693 — 'high water mark' principles
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Ghani v Jones [1970] 1 QB 693] [CITATION: Ghani v Jones [1970] 1 QB 693 - High water mark for search/seizure on entry pursuant to warrant or arrest] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Police entitlement on entry under warrant or arrest for taking goods; conditions and limits] [CONCEPTS: Ghani v Jones, search on entry, Lord Denning, property seizure, reasonable belief, serious offence, fruits of crime, lawfulness at time, seizure duration]

Covers Lord Denning's principles in Ghani v Jones about police entitlement when entering premises pursuant to warrant or arrest for serious offences.

*Ghani v Jones* [1970] 1 QB 693

*This case represents the 'high water mark'*

**H**. Per Lord Denning: it is settled law that where the police enter premises pursuant to search warrant or pursuant to power of arrest in relation to serious offence; they are entitled to search for and take any goods in relation to that offence. If during the search they find other goods relating to other events they are entitled to seize them as well provided they behave reasonably and detain the goods for no longer than is necessary.

Police can gain lawful entry, so long as they reasonable believe:

(1) that a serious offence has been committed;

(2) that the property was the fruit of the crime or the means by which it was committed, or material evidence of its commission;

(3) that the person in possession was involved in its commission either as a principal or accessory;

Further:

(4) the police must keep it for no longer than necessary to complete their investigations or preserve it for evidence;

(5) The lawfulness of the police conduct must be judged at the time of the seizure, not by what happens later (i.e. hindsight and subsequent events cannot justify the seizure).

To *some* extent *Ghani* has been enacted in Tasmania.

---

### Chunk 15 — Jeffrey v Black [1977] 3 WLR 895 — moving arrestee to search elsewhere
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Jeffrey v Black [1977] 3 WLR 895] [CITATION: Jeffrey v Black [1977] 3 WLR 895 - Arrest location and search limits] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Arrest location and impermissibility of moving arrestee to search elsewhere] [CONCEPTS: arrest, search incidental to arrest, removal to another location, Jeffrey v Black, unlawful search]

Covers the holding that police cannot arrest someone in one location and then move them to another location to search that location.

**H**. police cannot arrest someone in one location and then trot them off to another location to search that location. This is not a search incidental to arrest.

---

### Chunk 16 — Search Warrants Act 1997 (Tas) — overview and historical context
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Search Warrants Act 1997 (Tas)] [SECTION: general overview] [CITATION: Search Warrants Act 1997 (Tas) - Overview and historical context] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Purpose of search warrants, historical common law restrictions (Entick v Carrington), shift post-Ghani] [CONCEPTS: search warrant, Search Warrants Act, Entick v Carrington, general warrants, judicial oversight, police justification]

Covers overview of purpose and historical development of search warrants and interplay with common law and Ghani v Jones.

Search Warrants Act 1997 (Tas)

A search warrant is a document which authorises an invasion of private premises without permission of the owner or occupier. Seeks to interpose a judicial officer between the executive officer who will conduct the search and the private citizen. So they act as a control against excessive executive action. The police must justify or provide a basis for the search.

Original common law approach was to only grant search warrants for stolen property. Judicial officer had to be satisfied that particular stolen property was on the premises; both had to be described with particularity. Also couldn't seize goods not covered by the warrant; i.e. the common law rejected 'general warrants': see *Entick v Carrington* (1765) 19 St.T 129.

However, this position has now been wound back (see *Ghani v Jones*) provided the five conditions are met (see *Search Warrants Act*). Also, s60 of the *Police Offences Act* abrogates the common law regarding general warrants.

---

### Chunk 17 — Police Offences Act 1935 (Tas) s60 — Commissioner's warrant
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Police Offences Act 1935 (Tas)] [SECTION: s60] [CITATION: Police Offences Act 1935 (Tas) s60 - Commissioner's warrant statutory text] [CATEGORY: legal doctrine] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Commissioner's power to issue general warrants for stolen property] [CONCEPTS: Commissioner's warrant, general warrant, stolen property, s60, Police Offences Act, six months]

Covers statutory provision authorising the Commissioner to issue general warrants to search for stolen property.

*Police Offences Act 1935* (Tas), s60

**Commissioner's warrant**

**(1)** It shall be lawful for the Commissioner to issue to any police officer a general warrant, in the appropriate form set forth in [[Schedule II]{.underline}](http://www.thelaw.tas.gov.au/tocview/content.w3p;cond=;doc_id=44%2B%2B1935%2BJSII%2FEN%2B20081016000000;histon=;prompt=;rec=;term=#JSII@EN), authorizing such officer to search for stolen property.

**(2)** Every such warrant shall be signed by the Commissioner personally, and shall remain in force, unless sooner revoked, for 6 months from the date thereof, or for such shorter period, if any, as may be specified therein, and shall authorize the officer therein named to exercise the powers therein set forth.

**(3)** The Commissioner, at any time, may revoke any general warrant issued by him.

---

### Chunk 18 — Duties of applicant and issuing officer: Spyke (Tas Unreported 2/1981) & George v Rockett (1990) 170 CLR 104
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: George v Rockett (1990) 170 CLR 104; Spyke Tas Unreported 2/1981] [CITATION: George v Rockett (1990) 170 CLR 104 - Duty of applicant/issuing officer for search warrants] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Need to show supporting facts to satisfy issuing officer; issuing officer must apply mind; reasonable grounds beyond informant suspicion] [CONCEPTS: warrant application, issuing officer's satisfaction, reasonable grounds, George v Rockett, Spyke, particularity, supporting facts, rubber stamp]

Covers authorities and principles about duties of applicants and issuing officers when seeking search warrants, including George v Rockett facts/holding.

*Spyke* Tas Unreported 2/1981

*George v Rockett* (1990) 170 CLR 104 (**CR**)

**F**. Rocket obtained a search warrant to search George's office (solicitor). Wanted documents (bundles of pages). Argued it was unlawful as the belief wasn't held by the justice at the time that any evidence was held which would actually assist at trial.

**H**. Need to show supporting facts that the evidence will assist at trial. The judicial officer cannot just 'rubber stamp it'. Need reasonable grounds to grant the warrant (not the justice himself though, just that it could be held on the facts).

So you have to make the search warrant mean something. If it is to interpose a judicial officer between police and individual then you will only achieve this if the Justice actually brings their mind to the question of whether or not to grant the warrant.

Won't be a lawfully issued warrant unless the judicial officer is ***satisfied that there are grounds for issuing the warrant***. This requires the police to produce information which is capable of satisfying the judicial officer.

HCA held that a suspicion is more than a mere idle wondering. It is a positive feeling or apprehension amounting to a slight opinion. So must be afforded evidence that provides reasonable grounds for giving this belief.

---

### Chunk 19 — MacLeod (1991) 61 A.Crim.R. 465 — application of George v Rockett and summary
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: MacLeod (1991) 61 A.Crim.R. 465] [CITATION: MacLeod (1991) 61 A.Crim.R. 465 - Application of George v Rockett and issuing officer obligations] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Application of George v Rockett; state of mind of issuing officer; need for particularity] [CONCEPTS: issuing officer, state of mind, particularity, George v Rockett, MacLeod, warrant requirements, informant suspicion]

Covers MacLeod applying George v Rockett and summarised points on issuing officer responsibilities.

**H**. *George v Rockett* was applied by Slicer J here. See para 8-9 for a summary of the law in this area:

- it is the state of mind of the issuing officer that is relevant.

- issuing officer must satisfy himself or herself that the necessary criteria are present

- issuing officer cannot rely merely on the suspicion of the informant

- need for particularity in relation to object and the offence; mere reference to the relevant section is not enough.

---

### Chunk 20 — Beneficial Finance Corp v AFP (1991) 31 FCR 523 — issuing officer duties and enquiries
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Beneficial Finance Corp v AFP (1991) 31 FCR 523] [CITATION: Beneficial Finance Corp v AFP (1991) 31 FCR 523 - Issuing officer's obligation to clear up ambiguities] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Issuing officer need not investigate fully but must clear up ambiguities; related authorities Jackson v Mijovich] [CONCEPTS: issuing officer, enquiries, ambiguities, Beneficial Finance, Jackson v Mijovich, warrant clarity]

Covers the holding that issuing officers are not obliged to investigate fully but must clear up ambiguities if they have concerns.

*Beneficial Finance Corp v AFP* (1991) 31 FCR 523

**H**. The issuing officer doesn't have an obligation to go behind the warrant and investigate further (even if incomplete). However, there is an obligation to clear up ambiguities or make enquiries if they have concerns. Also see *Jackson v Mijovich* (1991) 23 ALD 470.

---

### Chunk 21 — New South Wales v Corbett [2007] HCA 32 — degree of precision required in identifying the offence
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: New South Wales v Corbett [2007] HCA 32] [CITATION: New South Wales v Corbett [2007] HCA 32 - Precision required for statement of offence in warrant] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: How precise the offence description must be in a warrant; nature of the offence suffices] [CONCEPTS: warrant description, statement of offence, precision, New South Wales v Corbett, HCA, nature of offence]

Covers facts and holding on required precision in identifying the offence on a search warrant.

*NSW v Corbett* [2007] HCA 32

*Degree of precision required in identifying the offence*

**F**. Warrant issued to search for firearms. The particular provision relied upon in the warrant had been repealed though. At first instance it was held that this misstatement of the offence invalidated the warrant. Upheld by the Court of Appeal (NSW) then appealed to HCA.

**H**. Appeal allowed. Statement of offence in search warrants ***does not have to have the precision that is required in an indictment***. Only require a statement of the ***nature of the offence*** so that you can clearly see what the search is for and establish the boundaries of the search.

Here the warrant was clear in stating the nature of the offence and all other details. Repealed provision had been replaced by a very similar provision so you couldn't say that this statement could possibly have given rise to any misapprehension of the police power to search for firearms. So here the similarity of the provisions was crucial.

---

### Chunk 22 — Disclosure duties: Karina Fisheries v Mitson (1990) 26 FCR 473; Lego Australia v Paraggio (25/8/94)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Karina Fisheries v Mitson (1990) 26 FCR 473; Lego Australia v Paraggio 25/8/94] [CITATION: Karina Fisheries v Mitson (1990) 26 FCR 473 & Lego Australia v Paraggio 25/8/94 - Disclosure duties in warrant applications] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Conflicting authority on duty of disclosure by warrant applicant; best practice note] [CONCEPTS: disclosure duty, non-disclosure, fraud, misrepresentation, Karina Fisheries, Lego Australia, warrant invalidity, best practice]

Covers conflicting authorities on disclosure obligations in warrant applications and best practice recommendation to disclose all relevant matters.

There is conflicting authority as to the duty of disclosure on the part of the applicant for the search warrant. *Karina Fisheries v Mitson* (1990) 26 FCR 473 held that ***all matters must be disclosed*** that would affect the issuing of the warrant. However, in *Lego Australia v Paraggio* 25/8/94 it was held that only where there has been fraud or misrepresentation will a warrant be struck out for non-disclosure. Most prosecutors believe though that everything relevant should be disclosed in the interests of best practice and conservativeness.

---

### Chunk 23 — Crowley v Murphy (1981) 34 ALR 496 — no fishing exercises
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Crowley v Murphy (1981) 34 ALR 496] [CITATION: Crowley v Murphy (1981) 34 ALR 496 - No fishing exercises by police] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Police cannot conduct general fishing exercises when applying warrants for documents] [CONCEPTS: fishing exercise, search for documents, particularity, Crowley v Murphy, warrant limits]

Covers authority that police cannot undertake a "fishing exercise" when seeking documents under a warrant.

*Crowley v Murphy* (1981) 34 ALR 496

**H**. Police cannot go on a '***fishing exercise***' looking for documents when they don't really know what they are looking for.

---

### Chunk 24 — Baker v Campbell (1983) 153 CLR 52 — LPP and search warrants
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Baker v Campbell (1983) 153 CLR 52] [CITATION: Baker v Campbell (1983) 153 CLR 52 - Legal professional privilege and search warrants] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Legal professional privilege as fundamental common law right preventing seizure under warrant] [CONCEPTS: legal professional privilege, LPP, search warrant, fundamental right, Baker v Campbell, privileged documents]

Covers HCA holding that legal professional privilege is a fundamental common law right that limits search warrants' application to privileged documents.

*Search warrants and their application to documents protected by legal professional privilege*

**F**. D (police) attempted to seize documents protected by privilege.

**H**. HCA held that the search warrant did not cover the documents as they were covered by LPP. Legal professional privilege is not simply a rule of evidence; rather it is a ***fundamental common law human right***. So the HCA treat LPP in a very important fashion. Search warrants thus cannot apply to documents covered by LPP. This causes some significant problems in practice.

---

### Chunk 25 — Oke v Commr Australian Federal Police [2007] FCA 27 — difficulties with AFP approach to privileged documents
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Oke v Commr Australian Federal Police [2007] FCA 27] [CITATION: Oke v Commr AFP [2007] FCA 27 - Handling of privileged documents during warrant execution] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Practical difficulties in handling privileged documents, solicitor negotiations, invalid warrant finding] [CONCEPTS: AFP, privileged documents, warrant execution, solicitor negotiation, Oke v Commr, invalid warrant, Larsson reference]

Covers facts and holding in Oke v Commissioner AFP about handling privileged documents and invalid warrant/execution.

*Oke v Commr Australian Federal Police* [2007] FCA 27

*Demonstrates the difficulties with the above process.*

**F**. Oke contacted solicitor about police going through documents (including client/solicitor information). Solicitor then spent the rest of the day trying to negotiate with police to not look at documents he alleged were covered by privilege. Persuaded the police to desist in search and entered into written agreement. This solicitor really stood his ground. AFP hadn't exercised the warrant properly and tried to get out of the whole thing by returning the documents. He refused so they dumped them on his doorstep.

**H**. The warrant was invalid.

A warrant is executed when everything that can lawfully be done, has been done: *Larsson v Commr of Police for NSW* (1988) 16 NSWLR 173, Also see s9 of the *Search Warrants Act* which deals with cessations.

---

### Chunk 26 — Larsson v Commr of Police for NSW (1988) 16 NSWLR 173 and Search Warrants Act s9 — execution/cessation
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Larsson v Commr of Police for NSW (1988) 16 NSWLR 173] [ACT: Search Warrants Act 1997 (Tas)] [SECTION: s9] [CITATION: Larsson v Commr of Police for NSW (1988) 16 NSWLR 173 & Search Warrants Act s9 - Execution and cessation of warrants] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: When a warrant is executed and reference to statutory cessation provisions] [CONCEPTS: warrant execution, cessation, Larsson, Search Warrants Act s9, completion of lawful acts]

Covers principle on when a warrant is executed and reference to s9 of the Search Warrants Act concerning cessation.

A warrant is executed when everything that can lawfully be done, has been done: *Larsson v Commr of Police for NSW* (1988) 16 NSWLR 173, Also see s9 of the *Search Warrants Act* which deals with cessations.

Warrant in first instance as less persuasive effect to oppose bail then a warrant for failing to appear (fleeing the jurisdiction)

Bail question?

### Court notes for extradition couple — scripted in-court identification and application
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Service and Execution of Process Act 1992 (Cth)] [SECTION: s83(14)(a)] [CITATION: Service and Execution Act 1992 (Cth) s83(14)(a) - Court Script: Identify Parties & Adjournment Application] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: script] [TOPIC: scripted courtroom identification, tendering warrants, adjournment and remand application for extradition couple] [CONCEPTS: identify parties, tender warrants, adjournment application, remand, extradition hearings, itinerary, interstate officers]

Script for court identification of parties and application to adjourn/remand.

Tactical workflow for proceeding in court when dealing with two subjects of interstate warrants.

IDENTIFY THE PARTIES

Your honour both parties were apprehended today by officers of Tasmania Police. During their investigation, it was revealed that the parties are both the subject of interstate arrest warrants:

Mr. Clayton Tierny having failed to appear at the Local Court of NSW at Sutherland on the 8^th^ of May,

Ms. Jedde Maloney, having failed to appear at the Local Court of NSW at Parramater on the 4^th^ of April. Addtionally, Ms. Maloney was on Parole with a balance of 3 months and 2 days remaining of her sentence, at the time, and I understand, that her failing to appear amounted to an offence in that State, and a subsequent breach of her parole order . As a consequence, there was a revocation order made, and a warrant for apprehensions and detention was issued on the 18^th^ of April by the NSW Parole Authority

Pursuant to the Commonwealth ***Service and Execution of Process Act 1992*** I tender copies of those warrants, executed earlier this evening.

> **TENDER COPIES**

You honour, the exaction of interstate warrants is provided for under **PART 5** of that same Act.

Section 83, sub-s (14), paragraph (a) empowers a justice of the peace to adjourn these proceedings and remand the defendants on bail, or in such custody as the justice of the peace specifies, for the period of the adjournment.

It is my application that these proceedings be adjourned until 14:15 tomorrow, that the defendants be held in remand, to appear by video link at that time.

The defendant's will be the subject of extradition hearings

Ensure: There needs to be a full detailed itinerary of movements from within Tas, out of Tas, and every step until in another jurisdictions court. Flight details and names of interstate officers etc**.\**

---

### Service and Execution Act 1992 (Cth) — Section 3: Interpretation excerpt
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Service and Execution of Process Act 1992 (Cth)] [SECTION: 3] [CITATION: Service and Execution Act 1992 (Cth) s3 - Interpretation Definitions] [CATEGORY: legal doctrine] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: definitions of 'magistrate' and 'person under restraint' under Service and Execution Act] [CONCEPTS: magistrate definition, person under restraint, parole, bail, interpretation, interstate warrants, Service and Execution Act]

This chunk covers the interpretation definitions in s 3 of the Service and Execution of Process Act 1992 (Cth).

Section 3: Interpretation

***magistrate***,

except in sections 57 and 67, includes:

\(a\) a justice of the peace who has power to issue warrants under a law of the State in which the justice holds that office; and

\(b\) a person who is appointed under section 120 of the Magistrates' Court Act 1989 of Victoria as a bail justice or is a bail justice because of holding a prescribed office within the meaning of section 121 of that Act.

***person under restraint*** means a person who:

\(a\) is on bail; or

\(b\) has been conditionally released from prison (whether on parole, licence, work release, home detention or otherwise) before the end of a term of imprisonment to which he or she has been sentenced; or

\(c\) is subject to the supervision of another person under a probation order; or

\(d\) is serving a period of home detention or a term of imprisonment by way of periodic detention; or

\(e\) is subject to:

\(i\) a community service order; or

\(ii\) a community based order; or

\(iii\) an attendance order; or

\(iv\) a work and development order; or

\(v\) any other restriction on his or her movements, imposed by law or by order of a court, that is inconsistent with the person complying with a subpoena served on the person under this Act;

but does not include a person who is in prison.

### Documents to provide court — Memorandum of Order (sample extradition order)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Service and Execution of Process Act 1992 (Cth)] [SECTION: s83(8)(b)] [CITATION: Service and Execution Act 1992 (Cth) s83(8)(b) - Memorandum of Order Sample] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: script] [TOPIC: sample written order to present to court for extradition transfer] [CONCEPTS: memorandum of order, extradition order, sample order, s83(8)(b), transfer custody, Tasmania Police, forms]

This chunk covers the documents and the sample memorandum of order to provide to the court in extradition applications.

The attached is self-explanatory and Jodie sets it out below. I think it is a good idea which will make extraditions that little easier. I have saved it to the Forms folder, please use it for future extraditions.

I have attached a copy of the Memorandum of Order used for applications for Extradition that may be of use to your Staff bringing these types of matters to court. In the past Prosecutors have handed up a typed copy of the order component of this document which has been of great benefit in expediting the matter in court. Just thought it might help, give me a call if I can help with anything else.

**[Below is the written order you should prepare and give to the court]{.underline}**

**[MEMORANDUM OF ORDER.]{.underline}**

In the Court of Petty Sessions held at Devonport.

DEFENDANT: JOHN BOB SMITH

**That** John Bob SMITH (DOB ...........), a prisoner in your custody and upon an application for Extradition to the State of Queensland

**[ORDER OF THE COURT]{.underline}**

Under Section 83(8)(b) of the *Service and Execution of Process Act,* IT IS ORDERED

That the said John Bob Smith is to be taken into the Custody of Tasmania Police and Detective Sergeant Mark John Corry, Criminal Investigation, and conveyed from the State of Tasmania on 25^th^ June, 2008 to the State of Queensland, to appear at Court at Caboolture Magistrates Court, 30 King Street Caboolture at 2:15pm on Friday the 27^th^ June, 2008.

[MAGISTRATE]{.underline}

### Police as Prosecutors — Justices Act s38
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Justices Act 1959 (Tas)] [SECTION: 38] [CITATION: Justices Act 1959 (Tas) s38 - Police as Prosecutors Text] [CATEGORY: legal doctrine] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: representation of parties by counsel and police conducting prosecutions under Justices Act s38] [CONCEPTS: police prosecutor, justices act, s38, representation by counsel, conducting case]

This chunk covers the text of s 38 of the Justices Act regarding representation and police conduct of prosecutions.

POLICE AS PROSECUTORS 

**JUSTICES ACT**

**38.**   **Representation of parties by counsel, &c.**

(1)  A complainant may, in person or by the complainant\'s lawyer, conduct his or her case and examine and cross-examine witnesses.

(2)  A defendant must, in person or by the defendant\'s lawyer, be admitted to make his or her full answer and defence to the complaint and to examine and cross-examine witnesses.

(3)  If the complainant is a public officer, the complainant may, in person or by the complainant\'s lawyer or another public officer, conduct his or her case and examine and cross-examine witnesses.

(4)  With the consent of the justices, a police officer may conduct the case of any complainant, and examine and cross-examine witnesses.
