### Court Procedure — Challenging Adjournments (Dietrich & Robinson v Lane [2011] TASSC 70)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: n/a] [CITATION: Robinson v Lane [2011] TASSC 70 - Challenging Adjournments & Dietrich Principle] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Dietrich principle and Robinson v Lane on adjournments and unrepresented litigants] [CONCEPTS: adjournments, Dietrich, unrepresented litigant, miscarriage of justice, Robinson v Lane, legal aid, TASSC]

This chunk covers the legal approach to adjournments when a defendant lacks legal representation, referencing Dietrich and Robinson v Lane [2011] TASSC 70.

Where defendant doesn't have a lawyer and keeps seeking an adj

Dietrich is the HC case about adjournments and miscarriage of justice if serious/ complicated matters proceed without legal counsel through no fault of the accused. Its been considered in a number of by TASSC. There is a good line in Robinson v Lane \[2011\] TASSC 70 "would result in the legal system grinding to a halt". Robinson v Lane 2011 TASSC 70 has a good summary of what accused would have to show and in that case Magistrate was not held to have made appealable error.

***Robinson v Lane 2011 TASSC 70***

Crazy woman insisted on a hearing despite a precious contest mention saying shed just get a fine. Unable to get a lawyer after that as LA funding didn't extend to fine only likely outcomes. There were a number of adjournments. She commenced the hearing and then found some challenges in the course of CX and EIC but Magi Mollard said no adj, crack on. Sha appealed because representing herself and claimed unable to run a case to same standard as a lawyer and she had mental health issues. TASSC ruled the magi was ok in doing what he did.

\[22\] *"It is not incumbent on a magistrate, in my view, where there is no apparent impediment to an unrepresented litigant conducting their case, to make exhaustive enquiries as to possible impediments before a hearing is even embarked upon. If such an impediment becomes obvious during the course of a hearing, the magistrate might then adjourn to address it. In this case, that is what the learned magistrate said he would do if necessary."*

\[24\] *"As to her suggestion that a normal lay person would have difficulty presenting the same sort of defence as a lawyer, were any court to decline to hear a matter simply because a litigant was unrepresented and might not present a case in the same manner as a lawyer, would result in the legal system grinding to a halt."*

---

### Adjournments — Hamilton v Litteljohn [2006] TASSC 109 and related principles
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: n/a] [CITATION: Hamilton v Litteljohn [2006] TASSC 109 - Adjournments Principles] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: legal principles for granting or refusing adjournments and appellate review] [CONCEPTS: adjournments, discretionary power, public interest, James v Williams, Cropper v Smith, appellate review]

This chunk covers appellate authority and principles on adjournments, referencing Hamilton v Litteljohn and related authorities.

Adjournments

Basic principle is that where a party seeks an adjournment which will create no injustice to the other side and which can adequately be remedied by an appropriate order for costs, then it seems to me that unless there are compelling reasons, such as manoeuvring or deliberate dilatoriness or inaction, ordinarily the discretion of justices to grant an adjournment ought not be exercised adversely to the party applying for it.

***Hammilton v Litteljohn \[2006\] TASSC 109***

*21 James v Williams; Ex parte James \[1967\] Qd R 496 was a case of appellate review of a refusal to grant an adjournment of the hearing of a complaint in circumstances very similar to the present matter, except that in James, the defendant sought an order that the case be dismissed when the prosecution was not ready to proceed. Hart J said, at 501 - 502:*

*\"If a defendant who has been served with a summons to appear on a certain day attends at Court on that day ready to answer the complaint, and the complainant is not then ready to proceed, then, if nothing else appears, the prima facie order to be made would be to adjourn the hearing and to require the complainant to pay the expenses of the defendant - the costs thrown away. If justice can be done by adjourning the case and ordering payment of costs, there is no justification for dismissing the complaint where the bona fides of the complainant is not challenged.\"*

*22 As Bowen LJ observed in Cropper v Smith (1884) 26 Ch D 700 at 710, \"Courts do not exist for the sake of discipline, but for the sake of deciding matters in controversy \...\". It is apparent that in the present case that is just what the learned magistrate was attempting to do by refusing the application for an adjournment and by dismissing the complaint, namely, discipline the prosecution. It is clear that his discretion miscarried when he refused to grant the application for an adjournment and this miscarriage resulted in the order of dismissal being made. He fell into error in that he failed to take into account, or adequately take into account:*

*(i) the public interest in the prosecution and trial of charges such as those brought against the respondent;*

*(ii) the reason for Mr Miller\'s inability to proceed with the hearing was due to inadvertent error;*

*(iii) the application for an adjournment was not opposed by the respondent;*

*(iv) the respondent did not claim that he would suffer any prejudice if an order of adjournment was made, nor was any such prejudice apparent;*

*(v) the order of dismissal was not sought by either of the parties to the litigation before the learned magistrate.*

*23 The learned magistrate also erred in placing too much weight upon:*

*(i) what he described as \"case management\";*

*(ii) the right of the courts to \"properly run\" the business in the courts; and*

*(iii) the need to achieve timely disposition of the complaint*.

---

### Costs — Costs in Criminal Cases Act 1976 (Tas) s4 & s5 and R v Freshney commentary
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Costs in Criminal Cases Act 1976 (Tas)] [SECTION: 4, 5] [CITATION: Costs in Criminal Cases Act 1976 (Tas) ss4-5 - Costs Principles & R v Freshney] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: legal doctrine / case authority] [TOPIC: awarding costs to defendants in criminal proceedings and related principles] [CONCEPTS: costs in criminal cases, s4 costs of successful defendant, s5 convicted defendant costs, criteria for costs, R v Freshney, judicial discretion]

This chunk covers the statutory provisions for costs in criminal cases and an authoritative statement of principles from R v Freshney.

Costs

[See **Department of Police and Emergency Management v Jones \[2007\] TASSC 107**]{.mark}

Cost orders in criminal matters

***Costs in Criminal Cases Act 1976, Section 4***

**4. Costs of successful defendant**

      **(1)** Subject to this Act, where a person having been charged with an offence is discharged from the proceedings in respect thereof, that is to say, where --

**(a)** he is acquitted of the offence;

**(b)** the complaint charging him with the offence is dismissed or withdrawn; or

**(c)** he is discharged upon an indictment for the offence --

the court having the conduct of the proceedings may, upon the application of the defendant, order that he be paid in respect of his defence such costs as it thinks just and reasonable.

      **(2)** The court, in deciding whether to grant costs and the amount of any costs granted, shall have regard to all relevant circumstances and in particular to the following:

**(a)** Whether the proceedings were brought and continued in good faith;

**(b)** Whether proper steps were taken to investigate any matter coming to, or within, the knowledge of any person responsible for bringing or continuing the proceedings;

**(c)** Whether the investigation into the offence was conducted in a reasonable and proper manner;

**(d)** Whether the evidence as a whole would support a finding of guilt but the defendant is discharged from the proceedings on a technical point;

**(e)** Whether the defendant is discharged from the proceedings because he established (either by the evidence of witnesses called by him or by cross examination of witnesses for the prosecution or otherwise) that he was not guilty.

      **(3)** No defendant shall be granted costs by reason only of the fact that he is acquitted of an offence, the complaint charging him with an offence is dismissed or withdrawn, or he is discharged upon an indictment.

      **(4)** No defendant shall be refused costs by reason only of the fact that the proceedings were properly brought and continued.

      **(5)** No defendant shall be refused costs by reason only of the fact that in the investigation of the offence with which he had been charged he remained silent or refused to assist in respect thereof.

Costs in Criminal Cases Act 1976, Section 5

**5. Costs of convicted defendant**

Where a defendant is convicted but the court is of the opinion that the charge involved a difficult or important point of law and that in the special circumstances of the case it is proper that he should receive costs in respect of the arguing of that point of law, the court may order that he be paid such costs as it thinks just and reasonable.

***R v Freshney* TSR 126 \[1977\]**

Cost in Criminal Cases

The *Cost in Criminal Cases Act 1976* by Section 4 provides for coast to be awarded in the courts discretion to defendants not prosecuted to conviction and for some matters to be considered and others not, to be bars in exercising that discretion

- The discretion is not to be fettered

- None of the matters considered is to be conclusive

- The court should refrain as far as possible from expressing or hinting at any view as to the guilt or innocence of the applicant

- The grant of power to award costs is to be construed in the context of the enactment against the background of the prior law

- The rules that criminal trials are for many reasons not to be equiparated to civil trials and that acquitted persons are not entitled to costs remain subject only to the act

- To award cost a positive reason must be shown, other than an acquittal, whether or not based on an error, inefficiency or irregularity, to give an applicant a just claim on the public for his costs

---

### Course of hearings (course of proceedings) — accused evidence timing (RPS v The Queen [2000] HCA 3)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: n/a] [CITATION: RPS v The Queen [2000] HCA 3 - Accused Evidence Timing] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: order of calling accused to give evidence and appellate guidance] [CONCEPTS: accused evidence, RPS v The Queen, order of witnesses, customary practice, trial procedure]

This chunk covers the rule that an accused need not give evidence first and the relevant High Court authority.

Defendant need not give evidence first. ***RPS v The Queen \[2000\] HCA 3 3 February 2000 S116/1998***

*\[84\] Hunt CJ at CL in the Court of Criminal Appeal stated what the true position was in these terms: \"Before turning to the appellant\'s argument ... it should be pointed out that the law does not say that the accused person must be called to give evidence before any other witnesses. It has often been said that it is desirable that he be called first, as he perforce will be present when other witnesses give evidence before him and the comment may be made that he has tailored his own evidence to fit in with theirs. The judge was correct when he said that it is customary to call the accused first (if he is to be Callinan J 32. called at all), but he was wrong to say that he must be.\" (emphasis in original*

### Mental states / intent — categories and s13 of the Code
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Criminal Code (Tas) (assumed) / Code referenced] [SECTION: 13] [CITATION: Criminal Code s13 - Intention and Motive; Mental States Overview] [CATEGORY: legal doctrine] [TYPE: legal doctrine / annotation] [TOPIC: classification of mental elements in offences, R v Hodgson quote, and s13 of the Code on intention and motive] [CONCEPTS: mens rea, strict liability, absolute liability, intention, s13, motive immaterial, R v Hodgson]

This chunk covers the mental element classifications for offences, the Hodgson authority, and the statutory text of s 13 of the Code on intention and motive.

This chunk covers mental states/intent classification and the statutory test for intention and motive.

12.5 In Australia, criminal offences are generally characterised in one of three ways:

• mens rea offences---the prosecution must prove a physical element (actus reus) and a mental element (mens rea);

• strict liability offences---the prosecution is not required to prove fault, but there is a defence of reasonable mistake available;5 and

• absolute liability offences---proof of fault is not required and no defences are available.6

*R v Hodgson* \[1985\] TASSC 40

***\"\... in law it is clear that the word \'intention\' like the word \'malice\' covers all consequences whatever which the doer of the act foresees as likely to result from it, whether he does the act with an actual desire of producing them or only in recklessness as to whether they ensue or not.\"***

### Voluntary and Intentional:

Intention -- Relates to the **act** not the outcome of the act. the act must exist in idea before it exists in fact.

Prescribed in s 13 of the Code

**13. Intention and motive**

\(1\) No person shall be criminally responsible for an act, unless it is voluntary and intentional; nor, except as hereinafter expressly provided, for an event which occurs by chance.

\(2\) Except as otherwise expressly provided, no person shall be criminally responsible for an omission, unless it is intentional.

\(3\) Any person who with intent to commit an offence does any act or makes any omission which brings about an unforeseen result which, if he had intended it, would have constituted his act or omission some other offence, shall, except as otherwise provided, incur the same criminal responsibility as if he had effected his original purpose.

\(4\) Except where it is otherwise expressly provided, the motive by which a person is induced to do any act or make any omission is immaterial.

### Chunk 2 — Strict Liability (doctrine and examples)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: legal doctrine] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Definition and features of strict liability offences] [CONCEPTS: strict liability, mens rea presumption, honest and reasonable mistake, elements, driving without licence, statutory defence] [CITATION: Strict Liability - doctrinal note]

This chunk covers the definition and key features of strict liability offences.

There is a common law presumption that knowledge of an unlawful act is an ingredient in every offence.

A strict liability offence is one where "intent" does not need to be proved. However, the defence of honest and reasonable mistake is still available. Need only prove that the defendant performed the prohibited act.

Driving without a licence is a strict liability offence. There is a defence if the driver has no notice of the fact that their licence has been suspended (case law and in defence prescribed in the Act).

This chunk covers the definition of absolute liability offences.

Need not prove that the defendant knew the action was an offence, only that he committed it.

### Chunk 6 — Subjective recklessness (with B v Visser)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: B v Visser [2000] TASSC 141] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Subjective recklessness and its application in arson] [CONCEPTS: recklessness, subjective foresight, wilfully includes recklessness, arson, Criminal Code, s267, s268, case excerpt] [CITATION: B v Visser [2000] TASSC 141 - Subjective recklessness]

This chunk covers subjective recklessness, including the B v Visser extract.

Subjective recklessness:

Foreseeing the possibility of the application of force but was heedless of such consequences when they acted.

Wilfully in the charge of arson (imported by section 267(2)) includes recklessness -- arson may be committed if D deliberately does a willed act aware that setting fire to a building is a likely consequence of his act .

*B v Visser* [2000] TASSC 141

*2 Under the Criminal Code, s268, any person who unlawfully sets fire to any building is guilty of a crime, which is called arson. By virtue of the Code, s267(3), an act causing injury to property cannot constitute the crime of arson "unless it is done wilfully". The word "wilfully" includes "recklessly", so that arson may be committed if the accused deliberately does a willed act, aware that setting fire to a particular building is a likely consequence of that act, and if he or she recklessly does that act, regardless of that risk: R v Hodgson [\[1985\] TASSC 40](http://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/viewdoc/au/cases/tas/TASSC/1985/40.html); [\[1985\] Tas R 75](http://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/LawCite?cit=%5b1985%5d%20Tas%20R%2075); R v Gardenal-Williams [\[1989\] TASSC 29](http://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/viewdoc/au/cases/tas/TASSC/1989/29.html); [\[1989\] Tas R 62.](http://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/LawCite?cit=%5b1989%5d%20Tas%20R%2062) *

### Chunk 7 — Strict Vs Absolute liability (doctrinal exposition with He Kaw Teh & Proudman)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: He Kaw Teh v R [1985] HCA 43; Proudman v Dayman [1941] HCA 28] [CATEGORY: legal doctrine] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Detailed exposition on strict vs absolute liability and presumption of mens rea] [CONCEPTS: mens rea presumption, strict liability, absolute liability, He Kaw Teh, Proudman v Dayman, statutory interpretation, honest and reasonable mistake] [CITATION: He Kaw Teh v R (1985) - Strict Vs Absolute liability exposition]

This chunk covers the broader doctrinal exposition distinguishing strict and absolute liability with case references.

The law recognises three categories of statutory offences -- (1) those in which the prosecution must prove some state of mind, often referred to as mens rea, as an essential ingredient; (2) offences of "**strict liability**", as to which the prosecution need not prove any mental element unless the defendant adduces or elicits evidence suggesting an honest and reasonable mistaken belief in a state of facts that, if true, would render his or her acts or omissions innocent; and (3) offences of "**absolute liability**", as to which the prosecution does not ever need to prove any mental element, but need only prove the objective ingredients of the offences: He Kaw Teh v R [1985] HCA 43; (1985) 157 CLR 523.

In *Proudman v Dayman* [1941] HCA 28; (1941) 67 CLR 536, the High Court was concerned with a charge of permitting an unlicensed person to drive a motor vehicle on a road. It was argued that the prosecutor needed to prove either knowledge that the driver was unlicensed or indifference. It was held that proof of knowledge or indifference was unnecessary. Rich ACJ and Dixon J (as he then was) held, on the facts, that no question of an honest and reasonable mistaken belief arose in that case. Dixon J suggested that that would have been a defence. At 541 he said:

"The burden of establishing honest and reasonable mistake is in the first place upon the defendant and he must make it appear that he had reasonable grounds for believing in the existence of a state of facts, which, if true, would take his act outside the operation of the enactment and that on those grounds he did so believe. The burden possibly may not finally rest upon him of satisfying the tribunal in case of doubt."

The question of what the Crown needs to prove as to the state of mind of a defendant in prosecutions under ss7, 8(1) and 9 has to be determined in accordance with the ordinary principles of statutory interpretation. There is a common law presumption that mens rea is an essential ingredient in every offence: Sherras v De Rutzen [1985] 1 QB 918 at 921; He Kaw Teh v R (above) per Gibbs CJ at 528. Mens rea was described in Sherras v De Rutzen by Wright J at 921 as "an evil intention, or a knowledge of the wrongfulness of the act". As Gibbs CJ made clear in He Kaw Teh at 529, one must consider the words of the statute creating the offence and the subject matter of the statute in order to determine whether the common law presumption has been displaced.

---

### Chunk 9 — Statutory offence categorisation (three types)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: legal doctrine] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Three broad categories of statutory offences by mental element] [CONCEPTS: offence categories, mens rea offences, strict liability, absolute liability, statutory interpretation] [CITATION: Statutory offences categories - doctrinal list]

This chunk covers categorisation of statutory offences into three types by reference to mental element.

More:

In broad terms, statutory offences fall into three categories if distinguished on the basis of the mental element involved in them.

(1) Offences which include as an element that the accused had guilty knowledge or intent.

(2) Offences which include as an element that the accused did not have an honest belief on reasonable grounds that the circumstances were such as to make his or her behaviour innocent. These are offences of strict liability.

(3) Offences which do not include either of the above elements. These are offences of absolute liability.

---

### Chunk 10 — Implied mental element in criminal offending (Cannell v G; G v Cannell)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Cannell v G; G v Cannell [2018] TASSC 55] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Implied mental element and mens rea presumption under common law in summary offences] [CONCEPTS: implied mental element, summary offences, Criminal Code s4(3), Acts Interpretation Act s36, mens rea presumption, Cannell, Gow v Davies] [CITATION: Cannell v G; G v Cannell [2018] TASSC 55 - Implied mental element]

This chunk covers implied mental element principles drawn from Cannell v G; G v Cannell.

56. The general principles relating to the state of mind implied as an element of an offence such as this are well settled. Having regard to s 4(3) of the *Criminal Code*, the principles of criminal responsibility under the Code are not applicable to an offence punishable summarily, except as provided by [s 36](http://www8.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/viewdoc/au/legis/tas/consol_act/aia1931230/s36.html) of the [*Acts Interpretation Act* 1931](http://www8.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/viewdoc/au/legis/tas/consol_act/aia1931230/), that is, where the act or omission constituting the summary offence also constitutes a crime punishable on indictment. In the case of an offence under [s 35](http://www8.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/viewdoc/au/legis/tas/consol_act/aia1931230/s35.html) of the Act,* *there is no cognate indictable crime. Accordingly, the principles of criminal responsibility will be determined in accordance with the common law: *Gow v Davies* [\[1992\] TASSC 8](http://www8.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/viewdoc/au/cases/tas/TASSC/1992/8.html); [\[1992\] 1 Tas R 1.](http://www8.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/LawCite?cit=%5b1992%5d%201%20Tas%20R%201)

57. Under the common law, it is presumed that mens rea is required before a person can be held guilty of an offence: *He Kaw Teh v The Queen* [\[1985\] HCA 43](http://www8.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/viewdoc/au/cases/cth/HCA/1985/43.html); [(1985) 157 CLR 523.](http://www8.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/LawCite?cit=%281985%29%20157%20CLR%20523) However, this presumption can be rebutted by express provision or necessary implication under the terms of the statute creating the offence. In *He Kaw Teh, *Gibbs CJ noted three considerations relevant to the question of whether the presumption has been displaced. They are the express words of the statute, the subject matter with which the statute deals and the question of whether displacement of the presumption will assist in the enforcement of the relevant provision.

58. In the same case, Brennan J cited the following propositions from the judgment of the Privy Council in *Gammon Ltd v A-G of Hong Kong *[\[1984\] UKPC 17](http://www.bailii.org/uk/cases/UKPC/1984/1984_17.html); [\[1985\] 1 AC 1](http://www8.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/LawCite?cit=%5b1985%5d%201%20AC%201) at 14:

"(1) there is a presumption of law that mens rea is required before a person can be held guilty of a criminal offence;

(2) the presumption is particularly strong where the offence is 'truly criminal' in character;

(3) the presumption applies to statutory offences, and can be displaced only if this is clearly or by necessary implication the effect of the statute;

(4) he only situation in which the presumption can be displaced is where the statute is concerned with an issue of social concern, and public safety is such an issue;

(5) even where a statute is concerned with such an issue, the presumption of mens rea stands unless it can also be shown that the creation of strict liability will be effective to promote the objects of the statute by encouraging greater vigilance to prevent the commission of the prohibited act."

His Honour went on further to comment on these propositions as follows:

"The first three propositions correctly emphasise the strength which contemporary authority gives to the presumption that mens rea is an essential element of an offence. The fourth proposition, if I may say so with respect, seems to be too categorical an approach to what is, after all, a question of statutory interpretation. It is not possible to decide that mens rea can be excluded only where the subject matter answers a given description (even so general a description as 'an issue of social concern'), without regard to the whole of the statutory context."

---

### Chunk 11 — Defence of involuntary action (R v Falconer principles)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: R v Falconer [1990] HCA 49] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Defence of involuntary action — principles and burden of proof] [CONCEPTS: involuntary act, presumption of voluntariness, burden on prosecution, inability to control actions, Falconer principles] [CITATION: R v Falconer [1990] HCA 49 - Defence of involuntary action]

This chunk covers the principles applicable to the defence of involuntary action with Falconer excerpt.

1. The principles applicable to the sort of defence raised by the applicant were set out by Mason CJ, Brennan and McHugh JJ in *R v Falconer *[1990] HCA 49; (1990) 171 CLR 30 at 40-41, as follows:

"\"The presumption that the acts of a person, apparently conscious, are willed or voluntary is an inference of fact and, as a matter of fact, there must be good grounds for refusing to draw the inference. Generally speaking, grounds for refusing to draw the inference appear only when there are grounds for believing that the actor is unable to control his actions. Although the prosecution bears the ultimate onus of proving beyond reasonable doubt that an act which is an element of an offence charged was a willed act or, at common law, was done voluntarily, \... the prosecution may rely on the inference that an act done by an apparently conscious actor is willed or voluntary to discharge that onus unless there are grounds for believing that the accused was unable to control that act.\" [Case reference omitted.]"

---

### Chunk 12 — Subjective knowledge as a mental element (stolen property, intoxication relevance)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Subjective knowledge required for receiving/possessing stolen property and intoxication evidence] [CONCEPTS: subjective knowledge, belief, stolen property, intoxication, Weiderman, Quillerat, Martin, mental element] [CITATION: Subjective knowledge - annotation and case references]

This chunk covers subjective knowledge as a mental element in offences such as receiving or possessing stolen property and the role of intoxication evidence.

I.e. knowing or believing property to be stolen for receive or possess. Belief is enough, need not be proven fact.

Voluntary intoxication can undermine and is relevant to the question of subjective knowledge.

Under the code the court refers to knowledge in two ways: Knowledge is referred to knowledge of an actual fact. Other times it refers to a belief. This belief dimension that is considered in stolen property as the mental element (subjective knowledge -any evidence of intoxication can be relied upon to deny subjective knowledge (*Weiderman*)). Certainty is not needed, just a real belief *Quillerat* & *Martin*

### Chunk 13 — Quillerat [1962] Tas SR (NC 16)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Quillerat [1962] Tas SR (NC 16)] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Quillerat on subjective knowledge and belief standard] [CONCEPTS: Quillerat, subjective test, belief standard, closing mind, evidence of knowledge] [CITATION: Quillerat [1962] Tas SR (NC 16) - case note]

This chunk covers the Quillerat authority and its teaching on subjective knowledge.

***Quillerat* [1962] Tas SR (NC 16)**

This case makes it clear that specific knowledge is a subjective test. One need only believe, knowledge to the extent of certainty is not necessary. Closing ones mind to knowledge is not counted as knowledge, but it may be evidence of it.

### Chunk 14 — Martin (Serial No 58/1980)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Martin on belief as sufficient for stolen property offences] [CONCEPTS: Martin, belief sufficient, stolen goods, subjective belief, case note] [CITATION: Martin Serial No 58/1980 - case note]

This chunk covers the Martin authority on belief sufficiency.

***Martin* Serial No 58/1980**

Belief is enough to constitute. It need not be knowledge of an actual certainty, but one needs a real belief that the goods are stolen

---

### Chunk 15 — Intoxication evidence and Weiderman reference
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Attorney General's Reference No 1 of 1996 (Weiderman)] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Use of intoxication evidence to deny subjective knowledge] [CONCEPTS: intoxication, subjective knowledge, Weiderman, mental element, evidence reliance] [CITATION: Weiderman - intoxication evidence to deny subjective knowledge]

This chunk covers the principle that evidence of intoxication can be relied upon to deny subjective knowledge.

Any evidence of intoxication can be relied upon to deny subjective knowledge (*in Attorney General's Reference No 1 of 1996(Weiderman)* )).

# Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999 (Tas)

<!-- block_008 procedure -->

### Chunk 1 — Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999 s 8 - Statutory definition of unfitness to stand trial
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999] [SECTION: 8] [CITATION: Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999 s 8 - Unfitness to stand trial (statute)] [CATEGORY: legal doctrine] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: statutory test for unfit to stand trial] [CONCEPTS: fitness to plead, unfit to stand trial, Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act, s8, inability to plead, inability to make defence]

Covers the statutory definition of unfitness to stand trial under the Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999 s 8.

8. Unfitness to stand trial

(1) A person is unfit to stand trial for an offence if, because the person's mental processes are disordered or impaired or for any other reason, the person is --

(a) unable to understand the nature of the charge; or

(b) unable to plead to the charge or to exercise the right of challenge; or

(c) unable to understand the nature of the proceedings; or

(d) unable to follow the course of the proceedings; or

(e) unable to make a defence or answer the charge.

(2) Notwithstanding subsection (1)(e), a person is not unfit to be tried if the only reason that the person is unable to make a defence or answer a charge is that he or she is suffering from memory loss.

---

### Chunk 2 — DPP Guidelines: Fitness to stand trial (presumption, investigation initiation, prosecutor obligations)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: DPP Guidelines - Fitness to stand trial - presumption and initiation] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: prosecutor obligations and threshold for fitness inquiry] [CONCEPTS: fitness to stand trial, presumption of fitness, prosecutor obligations, real and substantial question, expert report, Kesavarajah] [CASE: Kesavarajah v R (1994) 181 CLR 230]

Covers DPP Guidelines on presumption of fitness and when to commence investigation into fitness to stand trial.

Fitness to stand trial

A person is presumed fit to stand trial unless upon investigation it is established on the balance of probabilities that he or she is unfit.

Such investigation can be commenced by the court, of its own initiative, by the defendant or by the prosecutor at any time after a person is charged. Before such investigation is undertaken it must appear to the court that there is a real and substantial question as to fitness to stand trial.

Therefore, if a prosecutor becomes aware of matters that would raise a question as to fitness to stand trial, there lies an obligation to raise it with the defendant's counsel or, if unrepresented, with the court. Ordinarily, the fitness issue will be raised by the defendant's counsel.

In these circumstances, a prosecutor should consider the following:

• whether there is a reasonable prospect of conviction on each or any of the charges

• whether a report has been obtained from a suitably qualified expert providing an opinion on the defendant's fitness to stand trial which addresses the criteria set out in s8

• whether the report addresses the likelihood of the defendant becoming fit to stand trial during the next 12 months

The real and substantial question as to a defendant's fitness to stand trial only arises if one of the five criteria laid out in s8 is reasonably open. The issue of whether a defendant is fit to stand trial should be left to a jury unless no reasonable jury, properly instructed, could find the accused was not fit to be tried. (Kesavarajah v R (1994) 181 CLR 230 per Mason CJ, Toohey & Gaudron JJ at 244)

In some circumstances it will be readily apparent that the defendant is unfit to stand trial. However, where the issue of fitness is unclear an independent assessment may be required.

---

### Chunk 3 — DPP Guidelines: Independent assessment and procedural steps (memorial, consent, Presser/Ngatayi)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: DPP Guidelines - Independent assessment & procedural steps - Presser/Ngatayi] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: when to seek independent expert assessment and internal approvals] [CONCEPTS: independent assessment, memorandum, Director consent, R v Presser, Ngatayi] [CASE: R v Presser [1958] VR 45; Ngatayi v R (1980) 147 CLR 1]

Covers DPP Guidelines on obtaining independent assessments and the legal principles to be considered.

In determining whether it is appropriate to obtain an independent assessment of the defendant, a prosecutor should prepare a memorandum outlining the reasons for such a course of action. The consent of the Director or Deputy Director is required before an independent expert opinion is sought for that purpose.

Where fitness to stand trial is an issue requiring investigation, the principles discussed by Smith J in R v Presser [1958] VR 45 at 48; [1958] ALR 248 cited with approval in Ngatayi v R (1980) 147 CLR 1 at 8, are of consequence when considering the criteria laid out in s8

---

### Chunk 4 — Special hearing (s16/s15) — conduct and jury determination
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999] [SECTION: 15, 16] [CITATION: Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999 s15-s16 - Special hearing procedure] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: procedure] [TOPIC: special hearing following finding of unfitness to stand trial] [CONCEPTS: special hearing, s16, s15, not guilty plea, jury determination]

Covers the requirement to hold a special hearing when a defendant is found not fit and the manner of conducting that hearing.

Special hearing 

In circumstances where a defendant is found not fit to stand trial and after the court has determined whether the defendant is likely to become fit to stand trial in the next 12 months, it must proceed to hold a special hearing.

A special hearing is to be conducted in the same manner as a criminal trial, as the defendant is taken to have pleaded not guilty to the offence (s16).

In criminal proceedings the determination will be made by a jury (s15)

---

### Chunk 5 — Leave to withdraw plea of guilty — Tasmania authorities (Chatters) and summary of Wilkinson principles
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Leave to withdraw plea of guilty - Tasmania - Chatters & Wilkinson] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Tasmanian authorities and principles for withdrawing guilty plea] [CONCEPTS: withdraw plea, guilty plea, Tasmania v Chatters, R v Wilkinson, onus on applicant, miscarriage of justice] [CASE: Tasmania v Chatters [2017] TASSC 73; Tasmania v Chatters (No 2) [2017] TASSC 74; R v Wilkinson [2009] NSWSC 323; Day v Police [2005] SASC 200]

Covers Tasmanian authority references (Chatters) and the summarized principles from R v Wilkinson on applications to withdraw guilty pleas.

**Tasmania**

**ALSO**

**these are most recent Tas authority~!~!!**

*Tasmania v Chatters [2017] TASSC 73 (3 April 2017)*

**And**

*[Tasmania v Chatters (No 2) [2017] TASSC 74 (16 August 2017)]* per Bret J

**Withdrawing guilty plea**

The principles applicable when leave is sought to withdraw a plea of guilty were summarised in *R v Wilkinson* [2009] NSWSC 323; [2009] 195 A Crim R 20. Those principles include the following:

- (a) The court has a discretion to allow a person to withdraw a plea of guilty prior to conviction;

- (b) The onus lies on the applicant to demonstrate that leave should be granted. An application to withdraw a plea of guilty is to be approached with caution, bordering on circumspection;

- (c) A plea of guilty itself is a cogent admission of the ingredients of the offence; and

- (d) On an application for leave to withdraw a plea of guilty, the question is not guilt or innocence as such, but the integrity of the plea of guilty. A plea of guilty constitutes an admission of all of the elements of the offence. A conviction entered on the basis of such a plea will not be set aside unless it can be shown that a miscarriage of justice has occurred, and this will normally only arise when the accused person does not understand the nature of the charge, or did not intend by his plea to admit his guilt to it.

In *Day v Police* [2005] SASC 200, Gray J noted that where a plea of guilty has been entered following legal advice, a withdrawal will only be warranted "where it has been established that there has been a mistake, misunderstanding or improper inducement and there exists a substantial issue to be tried". The mistake or misunderstanding must relate to a lack of understanding by the defendant of his legal rights.

---

### Chunk 6 — Howe v S [2013] TASMC 33 — decision text and application to plea withdrawal
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Howe v S [2013] TASMC 33 - Magistrate M Brett decision extract] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Howe v S decision on withdrawing guilty plea and application of Wilkinson/Day] [CONCEPTS: Howe v S, withdrawal of plea, R v Wilkinson, Day v Police, affidavit, factual matters] [CASE: Howe v S [2013] TASMC 33]

Covers the excerpts from Howe v S [2013] TASMC 33 relating to principles for withdrawing guilty pleas and the court's reasoning in that case.

Magistrate M Brett Delivered on: 29 July 2013

PARA [4]. The principles applicable when leave is sought to withdraw a plea of guilty were summarised in R v Wilkinson [2009] 195 A Crim R 20. Those principles include the following:

(a) The court has a discretion to allow a person to withdraw a plea of guilty prior to conviction;

(b) The onus lies on the applicant to demonstrate that leave should be granted. An application to withdraw a plea of guilty is to be approached with caution, bordering on circumspection;

(c) A plea of guilty itself is a cogent admission of the ingredients of the offence; and

(d) On an application for leave to withdraw a plea of guilty, the question is not guilt or innocence as such, but the integrity of the plea of guilty.

A plea of guilty constitutes an admission of all of the elements of the offence. A conviction entered on the basis of such a plea will not be set aside unless it can be shown that a miscarriage of justice has occurred, and this will normally only arise when the accused person does not understand the nature of the charge, or did not intend by his plea to admit his guilt to it.

PARA [5]. In Day v Police [2005] SASC 200, Gray J noted that where a plea of guilty has been entered following legal advice, a withdrawal will only be warranted "where it has been established that there has been a mistake, misunderstanding or improper inducement and there exists a substantial issue to be tried". The mistake or misunderstanding must relate to a lack of understanding by the defendant of his legal rights.

PARA [6]. On behalf of the defendant, Mr Doyle submits that the basis upon which leave ought be granted to withdraw the pleas of guilty ought be that, in respect of each charge, the particulars alleged as to the matter of complaint are insufficient as a matter of law to support the allegation contained in the charge that the defendant has committed a breach of the relevant order. Accordingly, he submits, it would amount to a miscarriage of justice to leave in place pleas of guilty in circumstances where it cannot be said that the defendant has by his plea of guilty admitted allegations which amount in law to an offence.

PARA [7]. If he is right about this in respect of each matter, then that would, in my view, be a proper basis upon which to set aside the plea of guilty and substitute a plea of not guilty. It would amount to a clear miscarriage of justice to leave a plea of guilty in place in such circumstances.

PARA [8]. However, I would also make the point that if the particulars alleged in the charge are sufficient in law to support the allegation that there has been a breach of the relevant Police Family Violence Order, then in my view, there is, in this case, no other proper basis upon which to allow the plea of guilty to be withdrawn. In his affidavit, the defendant does not dispute that the basic factual matters contained in the particulars alleged to support each charge are, in fact, true. Furthermore, in circumstances where he entered the pleas after having been legally represented in respect of the charges for some months, and had deliberately instructed his counsel to seek leave to change pleas from a plea of not guilty to a plea of guilty after receiving advice, it would be impossible to say that the plea of guilty was not intended to be an unequivocal admission of the facts alleged in each charge. It can be inferred that he would have understood the effect of changing his plea from one of not guilty to guilty. In those circumstances, if the facts alleged in the charge amount to the offence alleged, then the pleas of guilty should stand

---

### Chunk 7 — Historical authorities on withdrawal of guilty pleas
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Historical authorities - De Kruiff, Maxwell, Clouter, Griffiths, Drew, Webb & Hay] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: historical authorities permitting withdrawal of guilty pleas and post-verdict change of plea] [CONCEPTS: historical authorities, withdrawal after plea, De Kruiff v Smith, Maxwell, Clouter, Webb & Hay]

Covers historical authorities on withdrawal of guilty pleas and when courts have allowed changes post-verdict.

Historical:

[Once an unequivocal guilty plea is entered at trial, it may only be withdrawn by leave of the court. Leave may be granted at any time before the matter is disposed of by sentence or otherwise: *De Kruiff v Smith* [1971] VR 761; *Maxwell* (1996) 184 CLR 501.]

If a jury verdict of guilty has been consequent on a change of plea to guilty during trial, the trial judge may allow a further change of plea after verdict and set aside the verdict: *Clouter* (1859) 8 Cox CC 237; *Griffiths* (1977) 137 CLR 293; *Drew* [1985] 2 All ER 1061; *Webb & Hay* (1992) 64 A Crim R 38 (SC SA).

---

### Chunk 8 — Circumstances where court will grant leave to withdraw plea (cases & examples)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Circumstances granting leave - McNally, Middap, Boag, Webb & Hay] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: situations where refusal to allow withdrawal would cause miscarriage of justice] [CONCEPTS: miscarriage of justice, basis for withdrawal, Boag, Webb & Hay, overborne, threats] [CASE: Boag (1994) 73 A Crim R 35; Webb & Hay (1992) 64 A Crim R 38]

Covers authorities setting out circumstances where a court will grant leave to withdraw a guilty plea, including examples of coercion or lack of evidence.

An application for leave to withdraw a guilty plea should be granted if its refusal may result in a miscarriage of justice: *McNally* [1954] 1 WLR 933; Collins 26/11/1984 CCA Vic; *Middap* (1989) 43 A Crim R 362.

In *Boag* (1994) 73 A Crim R 35 (CCA NSW), Hunt CJ at CL recognised at 37 the following situations where a miscarriage may occur if an offender was not permitted to withdraw his or her guilty plea:

> A miscarriage of justice may occur in many different situations if a prisoner is not permitted to withdraw his plea of guilty. Such a miscarriage will be established not only where the applicant did not appreciate the nature of the plea which he had entered but also, for example, if there was no evidence upon which he could have been convicted, or if he had not intended to admit that he was guilty or if his plea had been induced by fraud or threats or other impropriety, when he would not otherwise have pleaded guilty. ... there must be shown to be some circumstance which indicates that the plea of guilty was not really attributable to a genuine consciousness of guilt.

In *Webb & Hay* (1992) 64 A Crim R 38 (SC SA), the Court allowed an offender to withdraw his guilty plea after finding that the offender had always intended to plead not guilty to a murder charge. He did not enter a guilty plea until after the trial began and contrary to legal advice. The offender had been overborne by threats while in custody and an indirect reference had been made regarding that threat the day before he pleaded guilty. The Court regarded those matters, together with the very serious nature of the charge, as very compelling reasons for granting the offender leave to withdraw his guilty plea.

[The onus is on the offender to demonstrate the existence of a miscarriage of justice: *Boag* (1994) 73 A Crim R 35 (CCA NSW).]

The considerations the court should take into account will vary with the circumstances of a case: *Dodd* (1981) 74 Cr App R 50; *Ex parte Rowland* [1983] 3 All ER 689; *Clayton* (1984) 35 SASR 232; *Drew* [1985] 2 All ER 1061; *Sagiv* (1986) 22 A Crim R 73 (CCA NSW); *Roach* (1990) 54 SASR 491; *Webb & Hay* (1992) 64 A Crim R 38 (SC SA); *Boag* (1994) 73 A Crim R 35 (CCA NSW).

---

### Chunk 9 — Circumstances where court will refuse leave to withdraw plea (authority summary)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Circumstances refusing leave - Sagiv, Boag, Maxwell] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: when courts will refuse withdrawal of guilty pleas] [CONCEPTS: refusal to withdraw, deliberate plea, informed choice, Maxwell, Sagiv, Boag] [CASE: Sagiv (1986) 22 A Crim R 73; Boag (1994) 73 A Crim R 35; Maxwell v The Queen (1996) 184 CLR 501]

Covers authorities and principle that deliberate and informed guilty pleas will generally not be allowed to be withdrawn.

[Where an offender has made a deliberate and fully informed choice to plead guilty and has recognised his or her own guilt, the court will generally refuse leave to withdraw a guilty plea: *Sagiv* (1986) 22 A Crim R 73 (CCA NSW) at 80; *Boag* (1994) 73 A Crim R 35 (CCA NSW) at 39.]

It is also difficult to withdraw a guilty plea after it has been entered; [generally, a plea entered deliberately and on an informed basis must be considered final. *Maxwell v The Queen* (1996) 184 CLR 501.]

### Chunk 10 — Complaint on oath — Justices Act s32(b)(iii) and Justices Rules Form 2 r.15
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Justices Act 1959 (Tas)] [SECTION: s32(b)(iii); Justices Rules r.15 (Form 2)] [CITATION: Justices Act s32(b)(iii) & Justices Rules r.15 - Complaint on oath and Form 2] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: checklist] [TOPIC: complaint on oath — warrant power and required form] [CONCEPTS: complaint on oath, warrant, s32, Justices Rules, Form 2, r.15]

### Chunk 11 — Indictable and summary family violence offences — DPP guidance on single complaint and adjournment
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: DPP Guidelines - Indictable and summary FV offences - single complaint policy] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: combining indictable and summary offences on one complaint and adjournment practice] [CONCEPTS: family violence, indictable and summary charges, single complaint, adjourn sine die, Police Prosecution Services]

Covers DPP guidance on including indictable and summary family violence charges on one complaint and adjournment practice when pleas entered.

It is not uncommon for an accused person to be charged with an indictable crime and a number of related summary offences. Where possible, all charges should be included on the one complaint. Police Prosecution Services should be directed that at the time of a plea being entered to any indictable matter all other associated charges are to be adjourned sine die to avoid separation of conduct.

### Chunk 12 — Interstate connected offending — Territorial Application Act and DVO national recognition
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Criminal Law (Territorial Application) Act 1995] [CITATION: Criminal Law (Territorial Application) Act 1995 - interstate offending nexus; Domestic Violence Orders (National Recognition) Act 2016 s17] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: interstate connected offending — nexus, timing, DVO recognition] [CONCEPTS: territorial application, nexus with Tasmania, time of offence, voicemail example, Tas time, DVO national recognition, s17]

Needs to be a nexus with Tas.

Offence occurs when "act" is committed, not when injury occurs (i.e. when the call is made in breach of an order, not when the voicemail is heard).

Use Tas time, not interstate time.

For DVO's see s. 17 Domestic Violence Orders (National Recognition) Act 2016

### Chunk 14 — Difference between particulars and evidence; s31 Justices Act; Cannell v G; Irons v Moore [2019] TASSC 22
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Justices Act 1959 (Tas)] [SECTION: s31(4)] [CITATION: Justices Act s31(4) - Variance between complaint particulars and evidence; Cannell; Irons v Moore] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: variance between particulars and evidence; court power to delete particulars; prejudice test] [CONCEPTS: particulars, variance, s31, Cannell v G, Irons v Moore, prejudice, delete paragraphs] [CASE: Cannell v G; G v Cannell [2018] TASSC 55; Irons v Moore [2019] TASSC 22]

Covers the court's power to delete particulars that are not proved and discussion of variance between complaint particulars and evidence.

Court has power to delete paragraphs or additional acts of more than one are specified in a complaint. An example being a complaint nominating a number of different types of actions that amounted to Harass for the purposes of a breach condition charge. One of the particulars was accepted the others were not and the court used s 31 to simply delete the others and find the charge proven on the single proved example. Cannell v G; G v Cannell [2018] TASSC 55 (para 53)

Irons v Moore [2019] TASSC 22 Brett J

[25] The applicant also argued that there was a variance between the particulars of the assault found by the magistrate and those specified in the complaint. It is fair comment that there is a variance. The complaint alleges that the assault took place by grabbing and punching, whereas the evidence permitted a finding that the assault had taken place by hitting and/or pulling the complainant's hair.

[26] This was not a variance of significance. There is no suggestion that the particulars mis-informed the applicant of the nature of the allegation against him by diverting his attention, for example, to a different incident, or a different offence. The incident in question was clearly identified by the complainant, and addressed by the evidence. The applicant could have been under no misapprehension that what was being alleged against him was that during the course of an argument in the car, he unlawfully assaulted the complainant. The provisions of s 31(4) of the Justices Act 1959 are applicable, that is, that an objection shall not be taken to a summons in respect of a variance between it and the evidence in support of the complaint, although the justices may adjourn the hearing if the defendant has been prejudiced by the defect or variance. In my view, there was no prejudice to the applicant in this case, and the argument is without merit.

---

### Chunk 15 — Counter practical note — court unlikely to amend particulars under s31 where unfairness would result
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Justices Act 1959 (Tas)] [SECTION: s31] [CITATION: Practical counter - amendment under s31 and incurable unfairness] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: practical limits on amendment under s31 at end of hearing] [CONCEPTS: amendment, s31, incurable unfairness, notice to defendant, end of hearing, prejudice]

Covers a practitioner's practical note cautioning against late amendments under s31 that would create unfairness.

Court unlikely to amend the particulars under s 31 of the Justices Act where such an amendment without notice to the defendant would create an incurable unfairness at this point in the proceedings given the manner in which this case has been run. That is, at the end of the hearing where there is a significant difference.

### Chunk 16 — Amendments to complaints — Malahoff v White and Constable Julie McCullough v Auksorius (2025) — pith and substance & out-of-time amendments
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Amendments to complaints - Malahoff v White; Constable Julie McCullough v Auksorius [2025] TASMC 4] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: when amendments allowed despite referencing different statutory provisions or being out of time] [CONCEPTS: amendment, pith and substance, out of time, Malahoff v White, Constable Julie McCullough v Bronwyn Marie Auksorius] [CASE: Malahoff v White (TAS SC 37/1991); Constable Julie McCullough v Bronwyn Marie Auksorius [2025] TASMC 4]

Covers authority permitting amendments to complaints where the pith and substance remains the same and discussion of amendments even if statute-barred.

*Malahoff v White* Cox J. TAS SC 37/1991

In my opinion the offence charged in the amended complaint was of the same 'pith and substance' as that in the original complaint. The actus reus was the same act of driving on the same occasion, coupled with the fact of disqualification, pursuant to a court order. The fact that that disqualification was imposed under different Acts or that the offence might attract different sanctions, did not in my view effect any change in the quality of effect of the essential elements of the charge.

A justice or a magistrate is entitled to allow an amendment to a complaint even if it may be outside the statute of limitations from the time the offence was committed, for the following reasons;

- Justices/magistrates are not required to dismiss complaints by reason of unmeritorious technical reasons

- As long as there is no injustice to or prejudice against the defendant

- Complaint can be changed to a different offence as long as the pith and substance of the charge remains constant

- As long as the complaint is not defective to point of not disclosing an offence, or there being a variance between the complaint and the evidence to support it.

(Referencing Constable Julie McCullough v Bronwyn Marie Auksorius [2025] TASMC 4 below)

---

### Chunk 17 — Can amend even if out of time — Barnes v Hovington & Carlson v Wilkie
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Barnes v Hovington [2015] TASSC; Carlson v Wilkie [2018] TASSC 1 - amendment even if out of time] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: amendments out of time and pith and substance principle] [CONCEPTS: out of time amendment, pith and substance, Barnes v Hovington, Carlson v Wilkie, victim name not element]

Covers case law supporting amendments even when late and notes on victim name not being element of assault.

***Barnes v Hovington* [2015] TASSC**

Pith and Substance -- Porter J, "No injustice is done where the amendment results in a defendant facing a charge which is the same in pith and substance to that originally faced or is a cognate one; that is allied in nature or akin in quality. Injustice or unfairness may nonetheless arise, depending on the stage of the proceedings at which the application to amend is made."

*Carlson v Wilkie* [2018] TASSC 1

The victim's name is not an element of the crime of assault.

---

### Chunk 18 — Cannot amend a bad complaint — Constable Julie McCullough v Bronwyn Marie Auksorius [2025] TASMC 4
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Constable Julie McCullough v Bronwyn Marie Auksorius [2025] TASMC 4 - bad complaint vs good complaint] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: when amendment cannot salvage a complaint that references non-existent law] [CONCEPTS: bad complaint, repealed regulations, defective complaint, cannot amend, Constable Julie McCullough v Auksorius] [CASE: Constable Julie McCullough v Bronwyn Marie Auksorius [2025] TASMC 4]

Covers the example where a complaint referenced repealed regulations and was characterized as a bad complaint that cannot be amended.

**Constable Julie McCullough v Bronwyn Marie Auksorius [2025] TASMC 4**

Complaint referenced repealed regulations instead of current ones. As the complaint referenced law that did not exist at the time was generated, it was a "bad complaint" as opposed to a "good complaint" under Malahoff as the particularised offence was still an existing offence.
