# Amendments to complaints at close of case — Rule on prejudice
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: amendment of complaint at close of case] [CONCEPTS: amendment of complaint, prejudice, late amendment, cross-examination, recall witnesses, adjournment, natural justice, variance, particulars, summary hearing]

The court will permit an amendment to the particulars of a complaint at or near the close of the prosecution case only where the amendment does not cause prejudice to the defendant or where prejudice can be adequately remedied. An amendment that changes the basis or particulars of the charge but is consistent with the evidence already led, and where the defence has had notice through cross‑examination and the conduct of the hearing, is less likely to be regarded as prejudicial. Remedies to alleviate prejudice include permitting further cross‑examination, recalling witnesses, adjourning for additional evidence, and allowing submissions in reply. If the defence can show that the amendment deprives it of the opportunity to meet a materially different case (for example, by identifying different incidents or requiring different evidence or witnesses), the court may refuse the amendment or grant an adjournment; a failure to provide such remedial measures where necessary can amount to want of natural justice and may vitiate a conviction.

# Authority - Nilsson v McDonald [2009] TASSC 1
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: Nilsson v McDonald [2009] TASSC 1] [TOPIC: permitting late amendment where no prejudice] [CONCEPTS: late amendment, magistrate discretion, prejudice test, recall witnesses, cross-examination, particulars, Police Offences Act, natural justice]

Nilsson v McDonald [2009] TASSC 1 confirms that an amendment to the particulars of a complaint made during the hearing may be permitted where the magistrate is satisfied there will be no prejudice to the accused. In that case the particulars were amended to add an evidentiary basis for a direction given by police. The magistrate queried defence counsel about any prejudice; the evidence led demonstrated the police believed a breach of the peace had occurred or was likely, and cross-examination had been directed at those issues. The defence could not identify any witness they might have called or any specific prejudice, and the magistrate offered measures such as further questioning of witnesses. The Supreme Court concluded the magistrate did not err in permitting the amendment where the alteration was consistent with the evidence and did not deprive the defendant of the opportunity to meet the case.

# Authority - Ford v Tasmania [2012] TASSC 34
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: Ford v Tasmania [2012] TASSC 34] [TOPIC: remedial measures when enlargement of case occurs] [CONCEPTS: recall witnesses, adjournment, submissions in reply, natural justice, enlarged case, complaint amendment, magistrate error, conviction vitiation]

Ford v Tasmania [2012] TASSC 34 illustrates that where the evidence adduced departs materially from the particulars on which the defence has conducted its case, the magistrate should take active steps to avoid prejudice. The Court identified that the magistrate ought to have: (a) invited submissions in reply; (b) permitted the complainant to be recalled for further cross‑examination; or (c) otherwise allowed the defence an opportunity to address the enlarged case. Failure to do so amounted to denial of natural justice and required the conviction to be set aside. The decision highlights that remedial procedures are essential when an enlargement of the Crown case occurs at or near the close of the prosecution evidence.

# Authority - Irons v Moore [2019] TASSC 22
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: Irons v Moore [2019] TASSC 22] [TOPIC: variance between complaint particulars and evidence] [CONCEPTS: variance, Justices Act s 31(4), prejudice, adjournment, summary hearing, particulars v evidence, assault particulars, fair notice]

Irons v Moore [2019] TASSC 22 confirms that a variance between the particulars specified in a complaint and the factual particulars established by evidence will not necessarily invalidate a conviction where the variance is not of significance and the defendant was not prejudiced. The Court applied s 31(4) of the Justices Act 1959 (Tas), which prevents objections to a summons for a variance between it and the evidence unless the defendant has been prejudiced. In Irons, the complaint alleged grabbing and punching while the evidence supported hitting and hair‑pulling; the Court held the incident and identity were clear and the defendant could not show he was misinformed or prejudiced.

# Authority - Jerrim v Smith [2020] TASMC 6
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: Jerrim v Smith [2020] TASMC 6] [TOPIC: refusal to allow late amendment where element omitted] [CONCEPTS: omitted element, no offence known to law, late amendment refused, high threshold, summary hearing, drafting error, indictment validity]

Jerrim v Smith [2020] TASMC 6 demonstrates that where an initial complaint omits an essential element of the offence such that, as drafted, there is no offence known to law, courts may refuse a late amendment that would be tantamount to creating an offence at hearing. The case emphasises the high threshold for permitting amendments at the hearing stage: allowing an amendment that supplies an essential element may be impermissible where it would mislead or deprive the defendant of notice of the essential legal ingredients of the charge.

# Means of ameliorating prejudice when complaints are amended at hearing
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: remedies for prejudice from late amendments] [CONCEPTS: recall witnesses, adjournment, further cross-examination, submissions in reply, remedial measures, natural justice, trial fairness, reopening prosecution case]

Where an amendment to the complaint at or near the close of the prosecution case risks prejudice to the defendant, courts commonly employ specific remedial measures to cure prejudice rather than refuse the amendment outright. Effective measures include: (1) recalling the complainant or other prosecution witnesses for further cross‑examination on the new particulars; (2) granting an adjournment to allow the defence to obtain and call additional witnesses; (3) permitting further cross‑examination of prosecution witnesses on the new matters; and (4) allowing submissions in reply addressing the enlarged case. The availability of these measures is a key factor in determining whether the amendment will be permitted; their absence may constitute denial of natural justice as held in Ford v Tasmania [2012] TASSC 34.

# Justices Act 1959 (Tas) s 27 — Proceedings may be commenced by complaint
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Justices Act 1959 (Tas)] [SECTION: s 27] [CITATION: Justices Act 1959 (Tas) s 27] [TYPE: statutory provision] [TOPIC: commencement of proceedings by complaint] [CONCEPTS: complaint, oral information, arrested without warrant, commencement of proceedings, plea stage, Form 1, timing of complaint]

Section 27 of the Justices Act 1959 (Tas) provides that proceedings before justices shall be commenced by a complaint, which may be made by the complainant or a person authorised, subject to subsections (2) and (3) and other enactments. Critically, s 27(2) provides that where a person has been arrested without a warrant, oral information of the substance of the charge may take the place of a complaint up to the stage of proceedings at which a complaint is needed for the person charged to plead to it. This statutory exception allows temporary substitution of a written complaint by oral information until formal charging is required for a plea. The section governs when a written complaint becomes necessary in the flow from arrest to first appearance.

# Justices Rules 2003 (Tas) reg 17 — Lodgment of complaint
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Justices Rules 2003 (Tas)] [SECTION: reg 17] [CITATION: Justices Rules 2003 (Tas) reg 17] [TYPE: procedural rule] [TOPIC: lodging complaints with court] [CONCEPTS: lodging complaint, 7 day time limit, clerk, complaint made, procedural timing, summary prosecution, afterhours complaints]

Justices Rules 2003 (Tas) reg 17(1) requires that a complainant may lodge the complaint with a clerk and must lodge the complaint within 7 days after the complaint is made. The rule imposes a procedural time limit for lodging the written complaint following the act of "making" the complaint. The regulation operates in practice to require that written documentation of a complaint be filed promptly after the complaint is made, but its interaction with s 27(2) of the Justices Act—where oral information may substitute for a complaint after arrest without warrant—raises questions about the precise point by reference to which the seven‑day clock runs.

# Justices Rules 2003 (Tas) reg 6 — Form and making of complaint
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Justices Rules 2003 (Tas)] [SECTION: reg 6] [CITATION: Justices Rules 2003 (Tas) reg 6] [TYPE: procedural rule] [TOPIC: form and formality of complaints] [CONCEPTS: Form 1, sworn complaint, public officer exception, complaint formalities, summary jurisdiction, deposition, signing, Justices Rules]

Justices Rules 2003 (Tas) reg 6 prescribes that a complaint must be in accordance with Form 1 and must be sworn before a justice, except that a complaint made by a public officer does not need to be sworn before a justice. The rule fixes the formal requirements for a written complaint and thereby identifies what must be filed when a written complaint replaces an oral information. Compliance with Form 1 and the swearing requirement (where applicable) is necessary for a valid written complaint to commence proceedings where a written complaint is required.

# Interaction of s 27(2) and reg 17 — interpretive issue on timing of written complaint [REVIEW]
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: interpretive principle] [TOPIC: timing for lodging written complaint after oral complaint] [CONCEPTS: oral complaint, after‑hours court, first appearance, 7 day rule, lodgement timing, practitioner disagreement, procedural requirement, Form 1, [REVIEW]]

When an oral complaint is accepted at an after‑hours lock‑up and the accused is released or held pending a first appearance, the interplay between Justices Act 1959 (Tas) s 27(2) (which permits oral information to replace a complaint until a plea is required) and Justices Rules 2003 (Tas) reg 17 (which requires lodgment of a complaint within 7 days after it is made) gives rise to the question: when must the written complaint be lodged? One reasonable interpretation advanced by practitioners is that a written complaint needs only be lodged before the defendant’s first appearance to answer the charge, because s 27(2) allows oral information to stand until a plea is necessary; others disagree. This interpretive point is unresolved in the materials provided here and should be treated as a live question of procedure requiring local practice confirmation or authority. [REVIEW]

# Criminal Code Act 1924 (Tas) s 311 — Joinder of charges
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Criminal Code Act 1924 (Tas)] [SECTION: s 311] [CITATION: Criminal Code Act 1924 (Tas) s 311] [TYPE: statutory provision] [TOPIC: joinder of charges in same indictment] [CONCEPTS: joinder, same facts, closely related facts, series of crimes, nexus, time and place test, similar character, indictment drafting]

Criminal Code Act 1924 (Tas) s 311 permits charges of more than one crime to be joined in the same indictment if those charges arise substantially out of the same facts or closely related facts, or are, or form part of, a series of crimes of the same or a similar character. The provision contemplates two primary bases for joinder: (a) factual unity (same or closely related facts); or (b) a series of similar crimes where a sufficient nexus or feature of similarity exists. The statute does not permit joinder merely because offences are of the same legal category if they are factually disparate; a practical test looking at time, place, participants, and common purpose is applied to determine whether offences form a series.

# Criminal Code Act 1924 (Tas) s 363 — Separate trials for multiple accused
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Criminal Code Act 1924 (Tas)] [SECTION: s 363] [CITATION: Criminal Code Act 1924 (Tas) s 363] [TYPE: statutory provision] [TOPIC: separate trials, joint indictments] [CONCEPTS: separate trials, judge direction, joint trial prima facie, fair trial, positive injustice, severance, multiplicity of accused]

Criminal Code Act 1924 (Tas) s 363 provides that where two or more persons are charged in the same indictment, the judge may at any time during the trial direct that the trial of any of them be had separately from the trial of the others. The statutory provision gives the court power to sever trials where joint trial would risk unfairness; the common law presumption is that co‑accused charged with the same offence should be tried together, but that presumption yields where there is a real risk of positive injustice (for example, where evidence admissible against one is highly prejudicial and not admissible against another).

# Authority - Norman Brinkman and Fishpak Pty Ltd v Martin Stephen Dix and P J A Wright [1997] TASSC 50
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: Norman Brinkman and Fishpak Pty Ltd v Martin Stephen Dix and P J A Wright [1997] TASSC 50] [TOPIC: joinder / multiple defendants in summary jurisdiction] [CONCEPTS: joinder, multiple defendants, consent not required, summary hearing, practical joinder, aggregate hearing, magistrates' discretion]

Norman Brinkman and Fishpak Pty Ltd v Martin Stephen Dix and P J A Wright [1997] TASSC 50 held that there is no absolute statutory bar to different defendants being heard together in summary proceedings when they are not simply accessories; lack of consent alone does not mandate separate hearings. The Full Court explained that joint hearing of multiple defendants is a matter of discretion and practical considerations rather than a statutory prohibition, and counsel can argue against joinder where appropriate but absence of consent does not automatically preclude a joint hearing.

# Authority - Reg. v. Merriman [1973] A.C. 584 (Lord Diplock)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: Reg. v. Merriman [1973] A.C. 584] [TOPIC: duplicity and single-transaction rule] [CONCEPTS: duplicity, single count, single transaction, common purpose, one offence per count, multiple acts, indictment drafting, practical approach]

Reg. v. Merriman [1973] A.C. 584 (Lord Diplock) articulates the longstanding practical approach to the rule against duplicity — that a single count should not charge multiple distinct offences — explaining that where a number of similar acts committed by one or more defendants are connected by time, place or common purpose so as to form part of the same transaction or enterprise, they may appropriately be charged in a single count. The statement supports a pragmatic test for what constitutes "one offence" for charging purposes.

# Authority - Tasmania v Oakley [2015] TASSC 12
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: Tasmania v Oakley [2015] TASSC 12] [TOPIC: joinder — series of offences limb] [CONCEPTS: series of offences, nexus, time place circumstances, features of similarity, Criminal Code s 311, not unduly restrictive, factual correlation]

Tasmania v Oakley [2015] TASSC 12 explains the second limb of Criminal Code s 311: for offences to be joined as a "series of crimes of the same or similar character" there must be a nexus — a feature of similarity or factual correlation — enabling the offences to be described as a series. Porter J emphasised application of tests involving time, place and other circumstances as well as legal character, and cautioned against an unduly restrictive interpretation of the provision. Joinder requires sufficient correlation without straining the language.

# Authority - Tasmania v Martin (No 2) [2011] TASSC 36
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: Tasmania v Martin (No 2) [2011] TASSC 36] [TOPIC: improper joinder where offences lack nexus] [CONCEPTS: joinder improper, sexual offences v child pornography, lack of factual nexus, admissibility of same evidence, counts separation, R v May guidance]

Tasmania v Martin (No 2) [2011] TASSC 36 considered whether counts of different character could properly be joined. The Court, referencing R v May [2007] QCA 333, noted that even if offences are broadly of a similar category (e.g. sexual offences), there still must be a factual or evidentiary nexus — common participants, victims, timing, methodology or admissibility of the same evidence — to justify joinder. Where such nexus is absent, joinder may be improper.

# Authority - R v Cranston [1988] 1 Qd R 159
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: R v Cranston [1988] 1 Qd R 159] [TOPIC: factors for determining similarity and connection for joinder] [CONCEPTS: time place circumstances, legal character, features of similarity, joinder test, series of offences]

R v Cranston [1988] 1 Qd R 159 establishes that determining whether offences form a "series" for joinder purposes requires assessment of time, place and other circumstances of the offences in addition to their legal character. The decision supports a multi‑factor inquiry to ensure features of similarity and connection exist to a sufficient degree before permitting joinder.

# Authority - Ludlow v Metropolitan Police Commissioner [1971] AC 29
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: Ludlow v Metropolitan Police Commissioner [1971] AC 29] [TOPIC: nexus and series test in joinder] [CONCEPTS: series, nexus, factual similarity, joint offence, indictment practice, historical practice]

Ludlow v Metropolitan Police Commissioner [1971] AC 29 is cited for the proposition that to treat multiple offences as a "series" there must be a nexus or feature of similarity enabling collective description as a series; the decision is often referenced in subsequent joinder jurisprudence for guidance on the need for factual correlation.

# Authority - R v Carr [2003] TASSC 123
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: R v Carr [2003] TASSC 123] [TOPIC: joinder and series of offences] [CONCEPTS: series, nexus, similar character, factual correlation, joinder authority]

R v Carr [2003] TASSC 123 is cited as an authority addressing the requirement that offences forming a "series" must display a sufficient nexus; the case is used to illustrate application of the Cranston test in Tasmanian appellate decisions.

# Authority - De Jesus v The Queen (1986) 61 ALJR 1
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: De Jesus v The Queen (1986) 61 ALJR 1] [TOPIC: approach to interpretation of "series"] [CONCEPTS: series not unduly restricted, purposive interpretation, joinder liberal approach, appellate guidance]

De Jesus v The Queen (1986) 61 ALJR 1 emphasises that the word "series" in joinder provisions should not be given an unduly restrictive meaning; this supports a purposive approach favoring practical considerations of similarity and connection.

# Authority - R v Kray [1970] 1 QB 125
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: R v Kray [1970] 1 QB 125] [TOPIC: joinder jurisprudence historical support] [CONCEPTS: joinder, series, practicality, common enterprise, legal precedent]

R v Kray [1970] 1 QB 125 has been relied upon in joinder analysis as part of the common law background affirming that practical connection and common purpose can justify combining multiple acts in a single charging apparatus.

# Authority - R v May [2007] QCA 333
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: R v May [2007] QCA 333] [TOPIC: improper joinder where offences differ in legal character or lack nexus] [CONCEPTS: improper joinder, sexual offences, child pornography, lack of factual nexus, count separation, appellate guidance]

R v May [2007] QCA 333 is authority for the proposition that counts involving offences of different character (for example, sexual offences against a daughter versus possession of child pornography) may not properly be joined absent a factual or evidentiary nexus; appellate courts should scrutinise whether the counts share sufficient similarity or shared evidence to constitute a series.

# Authority - Webb and Hay v R (1994) 181 CLR 41
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: Webb and Hay v R (1994) 181 CLR 41] [TOPIC: prima facie joint trial rule] [CONCEPTS: joint trial, prima facie, same offence, co-accused, trial efficiency, fair trial proviso]

Webb and Hay v R (1994) 181 CLR 41 confirms the prima facie rule that accused persons jointly charged with committing the same offence should be tried jointly. This presumption is grounded in efficiency and coherence of proof but is subject to the proviso that joint trial must not create a real risk of injustice to any accused.

# Authority - R v Demirok [1976] VR 244
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: R v Demirok [1976] VR 244] [TOPIC: joint trial presumptions] [CONCEPTS: joint trial, presumptive joinder, severance grounds, fairness, trial risk]

R v Demirok [1976] VR 244 is authority supporting the principle that joint trials are prima facie appropriate for co‑accused but that severance may be required where joint trial would unfairly prejudice an accused's case.

# Authority - Leaman v R [1987] TASSC 21
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: Leaman v R [1987] TASSC 21] [TOPIC: joint trial principle in Tasmanian context] [CONCEPTS: joint trial, Tasmanian authority, severance, fair trial, co-accused]

Leaman v R [1987] TASSC 21 is a Tasmanian authority reiterating the general rule favouring joint trials of co‑accused and the exception where fairness requires separate trials.

# Authority - Tasmania v Smart [2014] TASSC 52
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: Tasmania v Smart [2014] TASSC 52] [TOPIC: prima facie joint trial and fair trial proviso] [CONCEPTS: joint trial presumption, fair trial, severance, Tasmanian jurisprudence]

Tasmania v Smart [2014] TASSC 52 applies the prima facie rule favouring joint trial and stresses that any departure must be justified by a real risk of positive injustice to an accused.

# Authority - R v Alexander and McKenzie (2002) 6 VR 53
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: R v Alexander and McKenzie (2002) 6 VR 53] [TOPIC: real risk of positive injustice as ground for separate trial] [CONCEPTS: positive injustice, severance, joint trial exception, risk assessment]

R v Alexander and McKenzie (2002) 6 VR 53 holds that the prima facie rule for joint trials is displaced when there is a real risk of positive injustice to any accused; that risk justifies severance and separate trials.

# Authority - R v Collins [1996] 1 Qd R 631
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: R v Collins [1996] 1 Qd R 631] [TOPIC: liberal approach to joinder] [CONCEPTS: liberal joinder, similar character offences, statutory comparison, practical approach]

R v Collins [1996] 1 Qd R 631 endorses a liberal approach to joinder under statutory provisions analogous to Criminal Code s 311, subject to the necessity for sufficient factual connection.

# Statutory exceptions and drafting of family violence complaints — Evidence Act s 142A [REVIEW]
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Evidence Act] [SECTION: s 142A] [CITATION: Evidence Act s 142A] [TYPE: statutory interpretation / practice note] [TOPIC: drafting family violence complaints and exceptions] [CONCEPTS: Evidence Act, s 142A, family violence orders, drafting complaints, exception wording, protective orders, Form drafting, practice note, Magistrate Jago trialled approach, [REVIEW]]

Evidence Act s 142A (as cited in practitioner notes) has been relied upon in practice to support drafting family‑violence related complaints without setting out every evidentiary exception in the body of the complaint; instead practitioners state the protective order (for example, "you are not to contact X directly or indirectly") and rely on the statutory regime to supply the necessary evidentiary context. This practice has been trialled in at least one magistrate's court (Magistrate Jago) without apparent difficulty. The citation and interpretation of s 142A in the source material is a practitioner note and is marked [REVIEW] for confirmation against the precise statutory text and authority.

# Justices Act 1959 (Tas) s 72B — Application of alternative convictions to summary hearing
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Justices Act 1959 (Tas)] [SECTION: s 72B] [CITATION: Justices Act 1959 (Tas) s 72B] [TYPE: statutory provision] [TOPIC: alternative convictions in summary jurisdiction] [CONCEPTS: alternative conviction, indictable code offence heard summarily, section 72B, Justices Act, summary hearing, alternative verdict, factual insufficiency for primary charge]

Justices Act 1959 (Tas) s 72B applies where an indictable code offence is dealt with summarily under the Justices Act: if the substantive code provides for an alternative conviction where the facts do not establish the primary offence, section 72B ensures that the alternative conviction is available in the summary hearing context as well. The provision prevents a defendant from escaping liability where the facts support a lesser included or alternative offence when a matter is heard summarily.

# Prosecutorial duties — minister of justice principle and DPP guideline
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: prosecutorial duties and limits on uses of evidence assertions] [CONCEPTS: prosecutor duty, minister of justice, fairness, not merely to convict, DPP guidelines, disclosure, reasonable grounds, avoid misconduct, informing court about evidence, fairness to accused]

Prosecutors carry an overarching duty to ensure the fair administration of criminal justice and to act as "ministers of justice" rather than mere advocates seeking convictions. This duty includes refraining from misconduct, avoiding statements or tactics calculated to produce wrongful convictions, and ensuring that all available legal proof is presented fairly. A specific DPP guideline reflected in the source material requires that prosecutors must not inform the court or the defence that the prosecution has evidence supporting an aspect of its case unless the prosecutor reasonably believes that such evidence will be available from material already in the prosecution's possession and available to the defence. This rule is designed to prevent misleading or unfair representations and to protect the accused's right to a fair trial.

### Chunk 2 — Nilsson v McDonald [2009] TASSC 1 — Magistrate allowed amendment; appeal denied
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Police Offences Act 1935] [SECTION: s 15B(2) (as referenced in complaint)] [CASE: Nilsson v McDonald [2009] TASSC 1 (16 January 2009)] [CITATION: Nilsson v McDonald [2009] TASSC 1 - Amendment allowed at hearing] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Magistrate permitted amendment of particulars at hearing; no demonstrated prejudice] [CONCEPTS: amendment at hearing, particulars, prejudice, cross-examination, magistrate discretion, Police Offences Act, section 15B, Nilsson]

Tactical workflow for assessing prejudice when a prosecutor seeks to amend particulars at the close of the case.

Need to show prejudice.

**Nilsson v McDonald [2009] TASSC 1 (16 January 2009)**

SC appeal of prosecutor being granted leave to amend at end of trial -- denied. Magi was fine to allow pross amendment. Def could not demonstrate any prejudice that might follow if not allowed, was gven option to ask further questions and it was clear from the case and from the way it unfilded and even the way def cx that the case was run on the basis of the amendment sought. It was a minor amendment tho

barne

**[(The amendment was allowed in the Magistrate court at the close of the case and was upheld by SC)]{.underline}**

[5] *The complaint in respect of the applicant identified the charge laid against her, namely, "Fail to comply with a direction of a police officer". It also identified that charge as being a breach of "Section 15B(2) [Police Offences Act 1935](http://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/viewdoc/au/legis/tas/consol_act/poa1935140/)". The particulars of the charge were amended during the course of the hearing. The particulars (with the words added by amendment at the hearing underlined) were:*

> *"You are charged with on the 15th June, 2007 at the Royal Hobart Hospital a public place at Hobart in Tasmania having been directed under the provisions of [subsection 1](http://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/viewdoc/au/legis/tas/consol_act/poa1935140/s1.html), to leave that place and not return for a specified period of not less than 4 hours, by Constable PAJAK a police officer who had reasonable grounds for believing you had committed or was likely to commit an offence, [and/or has committed or is likely to commit a breach of the peace]{.underline} and you unlawfully failed to comply with that direction."*

...

[36] *The learned magistrate permitted the amendment as sought by the prosecutor, expressing himself satisfied that there would be no prejudice to the applicant were the amendment allowed. The learned magistrate specifically queried counsel for the applicant in relation to the nature of any prejudice which might accrue to her client. His Honour pointed out that the evidence led at the hearing demonstrated very clearly that the police officers believed, rightly or wrongly, that there had either already been a breach of the peace or there was likely to be one, having regard to the applicant's behaviour as asserted by them. He also pointed out that the cross-examination by counsel had been directed to those issues. His Honour enquired what prejudice the applicant would suffer if, in particular, he permitted her to question witnesses further about any matters that she believed had not been covered already. While admittedly, counsel for the applicant was apparently taken by surprise by the application, she was unable to identify any particular prejudice which might flow to her client. In those circumstances his Honour permitted the amendment.*

[37] The applicant's written submissions again highlighted a misconception as to what a complaint needed to contain. It was not necessary for the complaint to identify the nature of the offence which might have been said to have been committed. The charge against the applicant was, both prior to and following the amendment, that of failing to obey the direction of a police officer. What was altered by the amendment was the addition of a basis upon which a police officer may have given that direction. The alteration was entirely consistent with the evidence, the applicant's counsel and the applicant had heard all the evidence, and the applicant's counsel had cross-examined in detail and at length in respect of the reasons the police officer gave for giving a direction to the applicant. There was no suggestion by the applicant's counsel that she could have or would have called other witnesses as to the nature of the applicant's conduct. In fact, that suggestion was only raised in submissions on the hearing of the review. The learned magistrate heard evidence from other witnesses present at the time of the incidents involving the applicant. There was no application from the applicant's counsel for an adjournment to enable her to present evidence from any other persons who might have been in the vicinity and, indeed, the applicant did not identify any specific witnesses who might have been able to give relevant and cogent evidence.

[38] I am not satisfied the applicant was in any way prejudiced by the amendment permitted by the learned magistrate. I am satisfied the learned magistrate did not make an error in permitting the amendment and this ground of review should fail.

### Chunk 4 — Ford v Tasmania [2012] TASSC 34 — Magistrate failed to afford natural justice by not recalling witness / inviting submissions
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Ford v Tasmania [2012] TASSC 34 (8 June 2012)] [CITATION: Ford v Tasmania [2012] TASSC 34 - Failure to recall and invite submissions] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Need to recall witnesses/allow further submissions where amendment/enlargement of case occurs] [CONCEPTS: natural justice, recall, adjournment, closing submissions, amendment of complaint, magistrate error, evidence]

Tactical note on remedies when an enlarged case emerges and the court's duties to afford natural justice.

**Ford v Tasmania [2012] TASSC 34 (8 June 2012)**

[14] *Counsel for the applicant did not suggest that the applicant was taken by surprise by the evidence led. However, he submitted that it was clear from the applicant's counsel's closing submissions that he was proceeding on the basis that this was a case of deprivation of liberty by a grabbing of the wrists, and he was never given an opportunity to address the enlarged case. [The learned magistrate did not invite submissions in reply in relation to the issue. The learned magistrate should have, of his own motion, amended the complaint. The only basis upon which he could have done this was to allow the complainant to be recalled for further cross-examination, and to allow counsel for the applicant to make further submissions]{.mark}. None of this was done. As a consequence the applicant was not afforded natural justice, and the learned magistrate should not have proceeded to conviction.*

### Chunk 5 — Example of successful application: Irons v Moore [2019] TASSC 22 — Variance not prejudicial
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Justices Act 1959] [SECTION: s 31(4)] [CASE: Irons v Moore [2019] TASSC 22 (22 May 2019)] [CITATION: Irons v Moore [2019] TASSC 22 - Variance and s31(4) Justices Act] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Variance between particulars and evidence; no prejudice; s31(4) Justices Act applicable] [CONCEPTS: variance, particulars, s31(4), Justices Act, prejudice, assault particulars, example success]

Tactical anchor: assessing whether a variance between particulars and evidence causes prejudice under s 31(4) Justices Act.

Example of successful application:

**Irons v Moore [2019] TASSC 22 (22 May 2019)**

[25] The applicant also argued that there was a variance between the particulars of the assault found by the magistrate and those specified in the complaint. It is fair comment that there is a variance. The complaint alleges that the assault took place by grabbing and punching, whereas the evidence permitted a finding that the assault had taken place by hitting and/or pulling the complainant's hair.

26. This was not a variance of significance. There is no suggestion that the particulars mis-informed the applicant of the nature of the allegation against him by diverting his attention, for example, to a different incident, or a different offence. The incident in question was clearly identified by the complainant, and addressed by the evidence. The applicant could have been under no misapprehension that what was being alleged against him was that during the course of an argument in the car, he unlawfully assaulted the complainant. The provisions of [s 31(4)](http://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/viewdoc/au/legis/tas/consol_act/ja1959119/s31.html) of the *Justices Act *1959 are applicable, that is, that an objection shall not be taken to a summons in respect of a variance between it and the evidence in support of the complaint, although the justices may adjourn the hearing if the defendant has been prejudiced by the defect or variance. In my view, there was no prejudice to the applicant in this case, and the argument is without merit.

---

### Chunk 6 — Example of failure: Jerrim v Smith [2020] TASMC 6 — High threshold for amendment at hearing; omission made draft no offence
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Jerrim v Smith [2020] TASMC 6 (7 May 2020)] [CITATION: Jerrim v Smith [2020] TASMC 6 - Amendment refused; element omitted] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Amendment refused at hearing where initial drafting omitted an element and left no known offence] [CONCEPTS: amendment refusal, high threshold, omitted element, no offence known to law, prejudice, procedural difficulty]

Tactical warning regarding the difficulty of obtaining leave to amend at hearing.

Example of failure:

**Jerrim v Smith [2020] TASMC 6 (7 May 2020)**

It's a fucking high threshold to get to be allowed to do so. Particularly when at the hearing stage.

IN the case an element of the offence was omitted in the initial drafting and the court refused to allow the amendment because the failure to include the element had the effect of aveing to no offence known to law.

It was a bloody harsh outcome. But there is some excellent discussion about grounds upon which an amendment can be made.

Take home though is that once at hearing its bloody hard.

### Chunk 7 — Oral complaints following afterhours lock-ups — Issue, Relevant Legislation, Argument
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Justices Act 1959] [SECTION: s 27] [CITATION: Oral complaints - lodgement timing and rules Reg 17/Reg 6] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Whether 7 day lodging rule applies to a written complaint following an oral one made afterhours; relevant rules and arguments] [CONCEPTS: oral complaint, written complaint, afterhours court, 7 day rule, Justices Rules reg 17, reg 6, s27, lodgement timing]

Tactical issue: whether a 7 day time limit applies to the lodgement of a written complaint following an oral one made in an afterhours court.

Issue:

Whether a 7 day time limit applies to the lodgement of a written complaint following an oral one made in an afterhours court? I'd argue that the written complaint need only be lodged at some point prior to the defendant's first appearance to answer the charge. Cathryn McCaffrey and Gavin disagree with me. Your thoughts?

Relevant Legislation:

JUSTICES ACT 1959 - SECT 27

27. Proceedings may be commenced by complaint

(1) Subject to subsections (2) and (3), and except as otherwise enacted or prescribed under any enactment, proceedings before justices shall be commenced by a complaint, which may be made or laid by the complainant in person, or by his or her lawyer or other person authorized in that behalf.

(2) Where a person has been arrested without a warrant, oral information of the substance of the charge may take the place of a complaint up to the stage of the proceedings at which a complaint is needed for the person charged to plead to it.

JUSTICES RULES 2003 - REG 17

17. Lodgment of complaint

(1) The complainant --

(a) may lodge the complaint with a clerk; and

(b) must lodge the complaint within 7 days after the complaint is made.

JUSTICES RULES 2003 - REG 6

PART 2 - Complaint and summons Division 1 - Complaint

6. Form and making of complaint

(1) A complaint is to be in accordance with Form 1.

(2) A complaint must be sworn before a justice.

(3) Despite subrule (2), a complaint made by a public officer does not need to be sworn before a justice.

Argument:

Regulation 17 of the ***Justices Rules*** states that a complaint may be lodged with a clerk. Further, that a complaint must be lodged within 7 days after the complaint is "*made*"

Regulation 6 prescribes the form and making of a complaint. A complaint is to be made in accordance with "Form 1", and except in the case of Police complaints, must be sworn before a justice.

Section 27(1) of the *Justices Act* states that proceedings cannot be commenced without a complaint. Section 27(2) provides an exception to 27(1) in circumstances where a person has been arrested without a warrant. Here, *oral information of the substance of the charge may temporally substitute a complaint*.

A complaint is not yet "made"

It need not be made until a stage of the proceedings at which a complaint is needed for the person charged to plead to it. At which time it then must conform to the requirements of "Form 1", per Reg 6 of the *Justices Rules*.

---

### Chunk 8 — Joinder — One matter of complaint (joinder of individual counts in summary complaints) — Norman Brinkman case and Lord Diplock quote
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Norman Brinkman and Fishpak Pty Ltd v Martin Stephen Dix and P J A Wright [1997] TASSC 50; (1997) 6 Tas R 460 (12 May 1997)] [CITATION: Norman Brinkman v Dix [1997] TASSC 50 - Joinder of defendants not barred by statute] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Joinder of multiple defendants and duplicity rule; lack of statutory bar to hearing together] [CONCEPTS: joinder, duplicity, defendants tried together, consent, practical approach, Lord Diplock, indictments]

Tactical note on joinder of defendants and counts in summary matters.

Joinder -

### One matter of complaint (joinder of individual counts in summary complaints)

***Norman Brinkman and Fishpak Pty Ltd v Martin Stephen Dix and P J A Wright [1997] TASSC 50; (1997) 6 Tas R 460 (12 May 1997)***

Full Court of the Tas Supreme court held there is no staturotrey bar to different defendatns being heard together where not "accersories." Lack of consnt itself is not a bar. It is a matter of argument.

***Lord Diplock in Reg. v. Merriman*** [1973J A.C. 584, at p.607, as follows:

"The rule against duplicity, viz. that only one offence should be charged in any count of an indictment, which is now incorporated in rule 4(1) of Schedule 1 to the Indictments Act 234 TASMANIAN REPORTS [1989 1915, has always been applied in a practical, rather than in a strictly analytical, way for the purpose of determining what constituted one offence. Where a number of acts of· a similar nature committed by one or more defendants were connected with one another, in the time and place of their commission or by their common purpose, in such a way that they could fairly be regarded as forming part of the same transaction or criminal enterprise, it was the practice, as early as the eighteenth century, to charge them in a single count of an indictment:'

TH 7-9-18

### Chunk 9 — Presumptions for and against JOINDER — Criminal Code s311 and doctrinal discussion
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Criminal Code (Tasmania)] [SECTION: s 311] [CITATION: Criminal Code (Tas) s311 - Joinder doctrine and cases] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Statutory test for joinder of charges; series of offences; jurisprudence and considerations for severance] [CONCEPTS: joinder, s311, series of offences, nexus, time/place/circumstances, Tasmania v Oakley, severance, fair trial]

Tactical doctrinal note on joinder under the Criminal Code and the presumptions for and against joinder.

JOINDER

The relevant section of the *Criminal Code* is section 311, which says (relevantly):

'...charges of more than one crime may be joined in the same indictment, if those charges arise substantially out of the same facts or closely related facts, or are, or form part of, a series of crimes of the same or a similar character. In any other case an indictment shall charge one crime only...'

This section has been considered in many cases, including recently *Tasmania v Oakley* [2015] TASSC 12. In that case, Porter J considered the second limb of the section and said:

[17]  Under the second limb, not only do the charges need to be ones of crimes of the same or similar character, the charges must be or form part of a series of such crimes. To be, or be part of, a "series", there needs to be a nexus between the offence; a feature of similarity which enables them to be described as a series: Ludlow v Metropolitan Police Commissioner[1971 AC 29] at 38; R v Cranston[1986] 1 Qd R 159 at 164; R v Carr [2003 TASSC 123] at [6]. The section does not countenance the joinder of counts charging offences which are legally the same or similar in character but which in their factual setting are disparate. What is required is a sufficient correlation to enable the offences to be described as a "series" without straining the word beyond its reasonable meaning: De Jesus v The Queen (1986) 61 ALJR 1 per Dawson J at 9.

[18]  The second limb calls for the application of a test involving time, place and the other circumstances of the offences, as well as their legal character or category in order to determine whether features of similarity and connection are present to a sufficient degree: R v Cranston at 164. **Generally, the provision should not be given an unduly restrictive meaning**: De Jesus (above) at 9; R v Kray[1970 1 QB 125] at 131.

(emphasis added).

Also relevant is the following:

[32]  Section 363 of the Code provides that where two or more persons are charged in the same indictment, the judge may at any time during the trial, direct that the trial of any of them shall be had separately from the trial of the other or others. The law is clear that the prima facie rule is that accused persons jointly charged with committing the same offence should be tried jointly: Webb and Hay v R (1994) 181 CLR 41; R v Demirok [1976] VR 244; Leaman v R 9/1987, [1987] TASSC 21; Tasmania v Smart [2014] TASSC 52 at [8]. The proviso to the rule is the right to a fair trial. The prima facie rule will be departed from where there is a real risk of positive injustice to the accused were there to be a joint trial: R v Alexander and McKenzie (2002) 6 VR 53 at 67 [31].

The other recent Tasmanian case which considered section 311 is *Tasmania v Martin (No 2)* [2011] TASSC 36, where Porter J noted

[23]  Of particular relevance to this case is R v May [2007] QCA 333 in which the Court of Appeal considered the joinder of a number of counts relating to the possession of child pornography (counts 16--22) in the indictment, with a number of counts of sexual offences in respect of which the complainant was the accused's daughter (counts 1--15). The statutory provisions under consideration were similar to those in the Code. Holmes JA, with whom Wilson and Philippides JJ agreed, said:

[34] Notwithstanding the liberal approach to be taken to joinder under s 567(2), [R v Collins [1996] 1 Qd R 631] I do not consider that these offences were properly joined. In the first instance, I do not think that counts 1--15 involved offences of the same or similar character as those entailed in counts 16--22. Consideration of whether charges entail a "series of offences of the same or similar character":

"call[s] for the administration of a test in which time, place and the other circumstances of the offences as well as their legal character or category are all factors which are considered for the purpose of seeing whether the necessary features of similarity and connection are present": R v Cranston [1988] 1 Qd R 159 at 164.

[35] These were not offences of the same legal character; nor could they be said to be of similar legal character, in the way that indecent assault and rape might be. But even if one were to interpret the term so broadly as to say that because they were all offences involving sexual abuse of children, they were of similar legal character, it was still necessary that there be some nexus or connection between them in order to establish a series: De Jesus v R (1986) 61 ALJR 1 at 9 per Dawson J. That nexus may be constituted by factual similarity or may be demonstrated by the admissibility of the same evidence in respect of the different offences.

[36] As to the first, there was no factual or temporal nexus between these offences, nor was there any practical aspect of their commission which connected them. They did not involve common participants or a common victim or a similar methodology ....'.

---

### Chunk 10 — Statutory exceptions — Drafting of complaints in FV matters and Evidence Act s142A
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Evidence Act 2001] [SECTION: s 142A] [CITATION: Evidence Act s142A - Drafting complaints in family violence matters] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Use of Evidence Act s142A to simplify drafting of FV complaints; practical note re Magistrate Jago] [CONCEPTS: Evidence Act, s142A, family violence, drafting complaints, exceptions, magistrate practice, FV orders]

Tactical drafting note: statutory exceptions and drafting of family violence related complaints.

Statutory exceptions 

Drafting of complaints (specifically FV matters) Tom Hogan has found a section of the Evidence Act, 142A which implies that we do not need to include all the exceptions in the body of the complaint. We just need to state what the order was, i.e, you are not to contact ???? directly or indirectly, etc. This has been tried with Magistrate Jago and it did not appear to cause any problems. Please remember this when checking FV files.

### Chunk 11 — Alternative convictions — Justices Act s72B applies to summary hearing of indictable code offences
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Justices Act 1959] [SECTION: s 72B] [CITATION: Justices Act 1959 s72B - Alternative convictions in summary hearing] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Application of alternative convictions where indictable code offences heard summarily under Justices Act] [CONCEPTS: alternative conviction, s72B, Justices Act, indictable heard summarily, lesser included offence, summary jurisdiction]

Tactical note: alternative convictions at summary hearing when indictable code offence is heard summarily.

# Alternative convictions 

Section 72B of the Justices Act applies where an indictable code offence is heard summarily under the Justices act (i.e. s. 72 etc.).

If the code provides an alternative conviction where facts do not substantiate the primary charge, s. 72B of the Justices act holds that it applies here it is heard summarily too.

### Chunk 12 — Prosecutorial Duties — quotations and DPP Guidelines
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Prosecutorial Duties - quotations and DPP guideline note] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Ethical duties of prosecutors; minister of justice principle; DPP guideline on disclosure statements] [CONCEPTS: prosecutor duty, minister of justice, fairness, disclosure, DPP Guidelines, refrain from misconduct, government imprimatur]

Tactical ethical reminder and relevant guideline for prosecutors.

> [M]ore than any other lawyer, [the prosecutor] must be especially careful to avoid misconduct because as a prosecutor well knows, her comments carry the imprimatur of the government ...[^1]
> 
> It is true prosecuting counsel ought not to press for a conviction. ... They should 'regard themselves' rather as 'ministers of justice' assisting in its administration than as advocates.[^2]
> 
> The United States Attorney is the representative not of an ordinary party to a controversy, but of a sovereignty whose obligation to govern impartially is as compelling as its obligation to govern at all, and whose interest, therefore, in a criminal prosecution is not that it shall win a case, but that justice shall be done. As such, he is in a peculiar and very definite sense the servant of the law, the twofold aim of which is that guilt shall not escape or innocence suffer. He may prosecute with earnestness and vigor -- indeed, he should do so. But, while he may strike hard blows, he is not at liberty to strike foul ones. It is as much his duty to refrain from improper methods calculated to produce a wrongful conviction as it is to use every legitimate means to bring about a just one.[^3]
> 
> It cannot be over-emphasized that the purpose of a criminal prosecution is not to obtain a conviction, it is to lay before a jury what the Crown considers to be credible evidence relevant to what is alleged to be a crime. Counsel have a duty to see that all available legal proof of the facts is presented: it should be done firmly and pressed to its legitimate strength but it must also be done fairly. The role of prosecutor excludes any notion of winning or losing ...[^4]
> 
> Prosecuting counsel in a criminal trial represents the State. The accused, the court and the community are entitled to expect that, in performing his function of presenting the case against an accused, he will act with fairness and detachment and always with the objectives of establishing the whole truth in accordance with the procedures and standards which the law requires to be observed and of helping to ensure that the accused's trial is a fair one.[^5]
> 
> Prosecutors are the most powerful players in the criminal justice system, capable of determining who should be charged and with what crimes. The duty to serve as a minister of justice is designed to limit abuse of this power and to compensate for the imbalance of resources that so often places the defense at a disadvantage. Demanding more of prosecutors than of other lawyers also fosters greater confidence in the legitimacy and accuracy of the criminal justice system.[^6]

Dpp Guidelines:

Prosecutors must not inform the court or the defence that the prosecution has evidence supporting an aspect of its case unless they believe on reasonable grounds that such evidence will be available from material already available to them

# Prosecutorial Discretion — Tasmanian Criminal Law

### Chunk 1
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Summary Prosecutions Manual - Case evaluation and non-prosecution extracts] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: excerpts re: decision to prosecute and non-prosecution] [CONCEPTS: decision to prosecute, discontinuance, case evaluation, public interest, prosecutorial discretion]

Tactical coverage: Extracts from the Summary Prosecutions Manual on case evaluation and reasons not to proceed.

The following are extracts from the Summary Prosecutions Manual, <http://intranet.police.tas.gov.au:8888/dpem/index.php?id=2158>

- Case evaluation must be made at each stage of bringing an action. ***(p. 50)***

- A decision not to proceed may be pragmatic, compassionate or recognise that the allegation is not born out by the evidence , or is countered by a well substantiated defence - if the pursuit of justice is the goal, then a decision to discontinue a prosecution is not necessarily a failure. ***(p. 51)***

- Having satisfied himself or herself that the evidence is sufficient to justify the institution or continuation of a prosecution, the prosecutor must then consider whether, in light of the provable facts and the whole of the surrounding circumstances, the public interest requires a prosecution to be pursued. It is not the rule that all offence brought tot the attention of the authorities must be prosecuted. ***(p. 51)***

### Chunk 2
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Tasmania Police Manual Part 8.10 - Decisions to Prosecute/Not to Prosecute] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: procedure] [TOPIC: Police guidelines on whether to prosecute] [CONCEPTS: reasonable prospect of conviction, not prosecute criteria, technical breaches, trivial contraventions, mental health]

Tactical coverage: Part 8.10 of the Tasmania Police Manual setting out criteria for deciding to prosecute or not.

Within Part 8.10 of the Police Manual (<http://hbtapp9v.police.tas.gov.au:81/TPM/>) there are guidelines as to the decision of whether to procrute/ not prosecute:

**8.10 Decisions to Prosecute/Not to Prosecute**

(1)    Tasmania Police will prosecute offenders where there exists a [reasonable prospect of conviction]{.mark}.

(2)    The criteria for not prosecuting, although there exists a reasonable prospect of conviction include:

(a)     technical breaches of the law;

(b)     trivial contraventions of the law;

(c)     vexatious, oppressive and malicious prosecutions;

(d)     prosecutions that would attract ridicule, or bring the law into contempt;

(e)     unreasonable delay has occurred in bringing a prosecution;

(f)      where the mental condition of the offender suggests treatment rather than prosecution; and

(g)     any other proper consideration.

### Chunk 3
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Criminal Code] [SECTION: s310(4)] [ACT: Evidence Act 2001 (Tas)] [SECTION: s18] [CITATION: DPP Guidelines - Reasonable prospect and evidence evaluation checklist] [CATEGORY: checklist] [TYPE: checklist] [TOPIC: reasonable prospect of conviction and evidence evaluation] [CONCEPTS: reasonable prospect, admissibility, witness reliability, identity evidence, s18 competence]

Tactical coverage: DPP GUIDELINES initial consideration — sufficiency of evidence and checklist of matters to evaluate when applying reasonable prospect of conviction.

The initial consideration in the exercise of the discretion to prosecute is whether the evidence is sufficient to justify the institution or continuation of a prosecution (s310(4) of the Criminal Code). A prosecution should not be instituted or continued unless there is admissible, substantial and reliable evidence that a criminal offence known to the law has been committed by an identifiable person.

However, application of the reasonable prospect of conviction test dispassionately, after due deliberation by a person experienced in weighing the available evidence, is the best way of seeking to avoid the risk of prosecuting an innocent person and the useless expenditure of public funds. When evaluating the evidence, regard should be had to the following matters:

- Are there grounds for believing the evidence may be excluded bearing in mind the principles of admissibility at common law and under statute?

- If the case depends in part on admissions by the accused, are there any grounds for believing that they are of doubtful reliability having regard to the age, intelligence and apparent understanding of the accused?

- Does it appear that a witness is exaggerating, or that his or her memory is faulty or contaminated, or that the witness is either hostile or friendly to the accused, or may be otherwise unreliable?

- Has a witness any apparent motive for telling less than the whole truth?

- Are there matters which might properly be put to a witness by the defence to attack his or her credibility? • If identity is likely to be an issue, how cogent and reliable is the evidence of those who purport to identify the accused?

- Is there is anything which causes suspicion that a false story may have been concocted?

- Are all the necessary witnesses available and competent to give evidence pursuant to s18 of the Evidence Act 2001?

- Where child witnesses are involved, are they competent to give evidence pursuant to the Evidence Act 2001?

---

### Chunk 4
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: DPP Guidelines - Public interest factors and prosecutorial practical reality] [CATEGORY: checklist] [TYPE: checklist] [TOPIC: public interest considerations for prosecution decisions] [CONCEPTS: public interest, seriousness of offence, deterrence, victim attitude, alternatives to prosecution, sentencing outcomes]

Tactical coverage: DPP Guidelines public interest test and the list of factors to consider when deciding whether the public interest requires prosecution.

Public interest

Having satisfied himself or herself that the evidence is sufficient to justify the institution or continuation of a prosecution, the prosecutor must then consider whether, in the light of the provable facts and the whole of the surrounding circumstances, the public interest requires a prosecution to be pursued. It is not the rule that all offences brought to the attention of the authorities must be prosecuted. Public interest does not equate to the level of public interest in an individual person or a particular matter.

The factors which can properly be taken into account in deciding whether the public interest requires a prosecution will vary from case to case. While many public interest factors militate against a decision to proceed with a prosecution, there are public interest factors which operate in favour of proceeding with a prosecution (e.g. the seriousness of the offence, the need for deterrence). In this regard, generally speaking, the more serious the offence the less likely it will be that the public interest will not require that a prosecution be pursued.

Factors which may arise for consideration in determining whether the public interest requires a prosecution include:

- the seriousness or, conversely, the triviality of the alleged offence or that it is of a "technical" nature only

- any mitigating or aggravating circumstances

- the youth, age, intelligence, physical health, mental health or special infirmity of the alleged offender, a witness or victim

- the alleged offender's antecedents and background

- the staleness of the alleged offence

- the degree of culpability of the alleged offender in connection with the offence

- the obsolescence or obscurity of the law

- whether the prosecution would be perceived as counter-productive, for example, by bringing the law into disrepute

- the availability and efficacy of any alternatives to prosecution

- the prevalence of the alleged offence and the need for deterrence, both personal and general

- whether the consequences of any resulting conviction would be unduly harsh and oppressive

- whether the alleged offence is of considerable public concern

- the risk to the public in not proceeding

- any entitlement of the victim or other person or body to criminal compensation, reparation or forfeiture if prosecution action is taken

- the likelihood of a confiscation order being made against the alleged offender's property upon conviction

- the attitude of the victim of the alleged offence to a prosecution

- where the victim does not wish to proceed, the likely effect on the victim in forcing him or her to give evidence. Also, conversely, the future risk to the victim, family members and the public in not proceeding.

- the likely length and expense of a trial

- whether the alleged offender is willing to co-operate in the investigation or prosecution of others, or the extent to which the alleged offender has done so

- the likely outcome in the event of a finding of guilt having regard to the sentencing options available to the court

- whether the alleged offence is triable only on indictment

- whether the alleged offender has already been sentenced for other offences and the likelihood of the imposition of an additional penalty having regard to the totality principle

- The applicability of and weight to be given to these and other factors will depend on the particular circumstances of each case.

As a matter of practical reality, the proper decision in many cases will be to proceed with a prosecution if there is sufficient evidence available to justify a prosecution. Although there may be public interest factors present in a particular case, often the proper decision will be to proceed with a prosecution and for those factors to be put to the court at sentence in mitigation. Nevertheless, where the offence is not so serious as to plainly require prosecution, or where the victim does not wish to proceed, the prosecutor should always apply his or her mind to whether the public interest requires a prosecution to be pursued.

Vehicle recovered. No damage. Little inconvenience to victims. Victims required to travel from FOSTER. Content for matter to drop. FSST required from to attend in person from HOBART. Unwilling retired police witness. Not in public interest to proceed.

### Chunk 5
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Disclosure - Kirby J adopting Lord Hope; Lord Bingham on full disclosure] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: prosecution disclosure obligations and judicial statements] [CONCEPTS: disclosure, undermining evidence, fairness, prosecution obligations, R v Brown, R v H]

Tactical coverage: Statements on prosecution disclosure obligations including Kirby J's adoption of Lord Hope in R v Brown and Lord Bingham in R v H.

Kirby J adopted the following statement of Lord Hope in R v Brown 226 in 1998 as representing an accurate statement ofthe law in Australia:

*The prosecution is not obliged to lead evidence which may undermine the Crown case, but fairness requires that material in its possession which may undermine the Crown case is disclosed to the defence. \[T\]he prosecution is not obliged to lead the evidence of witnesses who are likely in its opinion to be regarded by the judge or jury as incredible or unreliable. Yet fairness requires that material in its possession which may cast doubt on the credibility or reliability of those witnesses whom it chooses to lead must be disclosed.*

\[1998\] AC 367 at 377 quoted by kirby J (2005) 224 CLR 125 at 153.

As was highlighted in 2004 in R v lf241 by Lord Bingham: Fairness ordinarily requires that any material held by the prosecution which weakens its case or strengthens that ofthe defendant, if not relied on as part of its formal case against the defendant, should be disclosed to the defence. Bitter experience has shown that miscarriages of justice may occur where such material is withheld from disclosure. The golden rule is that full disclosure ofsuch material should be made. R v H \[2004\] 1 All ER 1269 at 1277.

---

### Chunk 6
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Tas disclosure requirements - Robertson v Reardon example] [CASE: Robertson v Reardon [2025] TASSC 21] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Tasmanian disclosure practice and example case] [CONCEPTS: disclosure obligations, evidentiary certificate, alibi, identity, Tasmanian case, Robertson v Reardon]

Tactical coverage: Tasmanian practice note on disclosure possibly being higher and an example where more should have been done (Robertson v Reardon).

Tas case eon disclosure requirements:

Might be higher than currently practiced

**Example where held we should have done more:**

***Robertson v Reardon \[2025\] TASSC 21***

It was an evade and drug drive charge. ID was in issue as was a defence alabi.

"The evidence was necessary, in my view, to unfold the narrative and give a complete account of the events upon which the prosecution was based"

In this case there were issues about the defendant's account that were arguably supported by evidence not shown/ played in court or disclosed.

However there was no discussion about the issues concerning matters covered by a evidentiary certificate. Obviously they are different.

### Chunk 7
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Model Rules 20.7 and R v Apostilides - Prosecutor obligations and High Court propositions] [CASE: R v Apostilides 15 A Crim R (1984) 88] [CASE: Weissensteiner v R (1993) 178 CLR 217] [CATEGORY: legal doctrine] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: prosecutor duties to call witnesses and consequences of failing to do so] [CONCEPTS: Model Rules 20.7, call witnesses, prosecutorial duty, miscarriage of justice, High Court propositions]

Tactical coverage: Model Rules 20.7 on prosecutors' duty to call witnesses; R v Apostilides summary and High Court propositions; Weissensteiner v R quote.

Model Rules of Professional Conduct and Practice

20.7 A prosecutor must call as part of the prosecution\'s case all witnesses:

20.7.1 whose testimony is admissible and necessary for the presentation of the whole picture;

20.7.2 whose testimony provides reasonable grounds for the prosecutor to believe that it could provide admissible evidence relevant to any matter in issue;

20.7.3 whose testimony or statements were used in the course of any committal proceedings; and

20.7.4 from whom statements have been obtained in the preparation or conduct of the prosecution\'s case, unless:

(a) the opponent consents to the prosecutor not calling a particular witness;

(b) the only matter with respect to which the particular witness can give admissible evidence has been dealt with by an admission on behalf of the accused; or

(c) the prosecutor believes on reasonable grounds that the administration of justice in the case would be harmed by calling a particular witness or particular witnesses to establish a particular point already adequately established by another witness or other witnesses,

provided that:

the prosecutor is not obliged to call evidence from a particular witness, who would otherwise fall within Rules 20.7.1-20.7.4, if the prosecutor believes on reasonable grounds that the testimony of that witness is plainly unreliable by reason of the witness being in the camp of the accused;

the prosecutor must inform the opponent as soon as practicable of the identity of any witness whom the prosecutor intends not to call on any ground within (a), (b) and (c), together with the grounds on which the prosecutor has reached that decision; and

(iii) the prosecutor must call any witness whom the prosecutor intends not to call on the ground in (i) if the opponent requests the prosecutor to do so for the purpose of permitting the opponent to cross-examine that witness.

*R v Apostilides* 15 A Crim R (1984) 88

The Crown prosecutor had declined at trial to call two witnesses whose veracity he had cause to doubt. The Court of Appeal ordered a new trial finding that a substantial miscarriage of justice may have occurred.

The High Court set out \[*extracted from Head notes*\] a number of propositions which apply to the conduct of a criminal trial.

(i) The Crown Prosecutor alone bears the responsibility of deciding whether a person will be called as a witness for the Crown;

(ii) The Trial judge may, but is not obliged to question the prosecutor in order to discover the reasons which lead the prosecutor to decline to call a particular person. He is not called upon to adjudicate the sufficiency of those reasons.

(iii) Whilst at the close Crown case the trial judge may properly invite the prosecutor to reconsider such a decision and to have regard to the implications as then appear to the judge at that stage of the proceedings, he cannot direct the prosecutor to call a particular witness.

(iv) When charging the jury, the trial judge may make such comment as he then thinks to be appropriate with respect to the effect which the failure of the prosecutor to call a particular person as a witness would appear to have on the course of the trial. No doubt, that comment, if any, will be affected by such information as to the prosecutor's reasons for his decision as the prosecutor thinks proper to divulge.

(v) Save in the most exceptional circumstances, the trial judge should not himself call a person to give evidence.

(vi) A decision of the prosecutor not to call a particular witness will only constitute a ground for setting aside a conviction if, when viewed against the conduct of the trial taken as a whole, it is seen to give rise to a miscarriage of justice.

The High Court in ***Weissensteiner v R* (1993)** 178 CLR 217 ( cited with approval in *Howlett v Budd* \[2003\] TASSC 49) that:

\"*If the question concerns the calling by the defence of a witness other than the accused, it will also be necessary to recall that the prosecutor \'has the responsibility of ensuring that the Crown case is presented with fairness to the accused\' and in many cases would be expected to call the witness in question as part of the case for the prosecution. And, if the question concerns the failure of the prosecution to call a witness whom it might have been expected to call, the issue is not whether the jury may properly reach conclusions about issues of fact but whether, in the circumstances, the jury should entertain a reasonable doubt about the guilt of the accused*.\"

---

### Chunk 9
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Witnesses - prosecution obligation - Richardson and Apostilides] [CASE: Richardson v The Queen (1974) 131 CLR 116] [CASE: R v Apostilides 15 A Crim R (1984) 88] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: prosecution's responsibility to present the Crown case fairly and to call witnesses] [CONCEPTS: fairness, call witnesses, Crown case, Richardson, Apostilides, prosecutorial responsibility]

Tactical coverage: WITNESSES — PROSECUTION OBLIGATION paragraph emphasising the prosecutor's responsibility and authority to call witnesses.

The prosecutor \"has the responsibility of ensuring that the Crown case is presented with fairness to the accused" and in many cases would be expected to call the witness in question as part of the case for the prosecution*. Richardson v The Queen (1974) 131 CLR 116* at 119 per Barwick CJ, McTiernan and Mason JJ. See also *R v Apostilides* (1984) 154 CLR 563

***R v Apostilides* 15 A Crim R (1984) 88**

The Crown prosecutor had declined at trial to call two witnesses whose veracity he had cause to doubt. The Court of Appeal ordered a new trial finding that a substantial miscarriage of justice may have occurred.

The High Court set out \[*extracted from Head notes*\] a number of propositions which apply to the conduct of a criminal trial.

(vii) The Crown Prosecutor alone bears the responsibility of deciding whether a person will be called as a witness for the Crown;

(viii) The Trial judge may, but is not obliged to question the prosecutor in order to discover the reasons which lead the prosecutor to decline to call a particular person. He is not called upon to adjudicate the sufficiency of those reasons.

(ix) Whilst at the close Crown case the trial judge may properly invite the prosecutor to reconsider such a decision and to have regard to the implications as then appear to the judge at that stage of the proceedings, he cannot direct the prosecutor to call a particular witness.

(x) When charging the jury, the trial judge may make such comment as he then thinks to be appropriate with respect to the effect which the failure of the prosecutor to call a particular person as a witness would appear to have on the course of the trial. No doubt, that comment, if any, will be affected by such information as to the prosecutor's reasons for his decision as the prosecutor thinks proper to divulge.

(xi) Save in the most exceptional circumstances, the trial judge should not himself call a person to give evidence.

(xii) A decision of the prosecutor not to call a particular witness will only constitute a ground for setting aside a conviction if, when viewed against the conduct of the trial taken as a whole, it is seen to give rise to a miscarriage of justice.

### Chunk 10
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Tas DPP Guidelines - calling witnesses, recording reasons and availability] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: procedure] [TOPIC: guidance on when prosecutors should call witnesses and file recording] [CONCEPTS: call witnesses, reasons in writing, availability to defence, witness unavailability, health considerations]

Tactical coverage: Tasmanian DPP Guidelines on calling witnesses, recording decisions, and making witnesses available to defence.

Tas DPP Guidelines

Prosecutors have a duty to call all witnesses whose testimony is necessary for the presentation of the whole picture, to the extent that it can be presented by admissible and available evidence, unless they form the view that the interests of justice would be prejudiced rather than served by calling a witness. The fact that a witness will give an account inconsistent with the prosecution case is not sufficient reason for not calling that witness. In those circumstances, a witness should only not be called where there is clear, objective material that all admissible accounts by that witness lack any credibility (see *R v Ashton, Farmer & Randall* \[2003\] TASSC 140 at \[29\]).

Where a prosecutor decides not to call a witness in these circumstances, the reasons should be recorded in writing and placed on the file. Without limiting the circumstances where a witness will not be called, they may include where an issue is not in dispute and other witnesses have already given evidence regarding that issue, the failure to call a witness is consented to by counsel for the accused, or where a witness is unavailable. Other considerations may include the physical and mental health of a witness. Where possible when a decision has been made not to call a witness that witness should be made available to the defence.

---

### Chunk 11
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Tasmania v Farhat [2017] TASSC 42 - disclosure limits re investigating witness credibility] [CASE: Tasmania v Farhat [2017] TASSC 42] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: limits on prosecution's duty to investigate and disclose matters affecting witness credibility] [CONCEPTS: disclosure limits, prosecution investigatory duty, witness credibility, Tasmania v Farhat, prosecutorial scope]

Tactical coverage: Note on the prosecution's lack of obligation to investigate matters that might affect prosecution witness credibility (Tasmania v Farhat).

There is, however, no obligation upon the prosecution to go out into the world and investigate matters that might reflect upon the credibility of prosecution witnesses and to then disclose those matters (see *Tasmania v Farhat* \[2017\] TASSC 42 at \[16\]).
