### Chunk 12
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Failure to Call Witness - Burston v Brooks and Martin v Visser contrast] [CASE: Darren John Burston v David John Brooks [1996] TASSC 152] [CASE: Martin v Visser [2000] TASSC 173] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: material witnesses must be called; failure may indicate inappropriate prosecution conduct] [CONCEPTS: failure to call witnesses, summary prosecutions, Burston v Brook, Martin v Visser, miscarriage of justice, material witnesses]

Tactical coverage: Authorities and observations on failure to call material witnesses in prosecution cases.

Failure to Call a Witness

### Material witnesses need to be called:

***Darren John Burston v David John Brook*s \[1996\] TASSC 152 (2 December 1996)**

17\. *I have mentioned that a number of witnesses who might have been expected to have been called by the respondent, namely the manager of the Saloon Bar and some four police officers, were not called by him and no explanation was given for this failure. Counsel who appeared for the applicant before the learned magistrate raised this matter in his opening address but the learned magistrate made no mention of it in his reasons. When spoken to in the charge room, the applicant made it quite clear that he did not consider that he had done anything wrong. On the prosecution version of events, the applicant\'s altercation with the manager was the catalyst which led to subsequent events. The respondent\'s failure to call her is mystifying and on the face of it suggests a quite inappropriate conduct of the prosecution. I consider that in summary prosecutions the prosecutor has the same duties as to the calling of witnesses as that held to be appropriate in relation to Crown counsel in Richardson v R [\[1974\] HCA 19](http://www.austlii.edu.au/au/cases/cth/HCA/1974/19.html); [(1974) 131 CLR 116](http://classic.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/LawCite?cit=%281974%29%20131%20CLR%20116?stem=0&synonyms=0&query=poa1935140%20s13) and R v Apostolides [\[1984\] HCA 38](http://www.austlii.edu.au/au/cases/cth/HCA/1984/38.html); [(1984) 154 CLR 563.](http://classic.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/LawCite?cit=%281984%29%20154%20CLR%20563?stem=0&synonyms=0&query=poa1935140%20s13)*

[CONTRAST HOWEVER WITH]{.mark}: ***Martin v Visser* \[2000\] TASSC 173 (20 December 2000)**

Where it was not until the defence led there case where it became apparent that arguably material witnesses were not called.

\[23\] *The charge was of disorderly conduct in St John Street, out of sight of the hotel. The event which was the immediate precipitant of the applicant\'s arrest occurred out of sight of the hotel. In the course of receiving the submission of the applicant\'s counsel in this regard, the learned magistrate commented \"what, you think that a sledge hammer should be used to crack a nut, you\'re saying everybody in sight should be called as a witness\" and \"I think that\'s making a bit much of it really, to suggest that people like that should have been called\"*

# Prosecution Witness Obligations

<!-- block_011 procedure -->

### Chunk 1 — Unknown witnesses / Hrycyszyn v Bonde extract
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Hrycyszyn v Bonde (2003) TASSC 126 - Unknown witnesses] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Unknown witnesses — prosecutor duty to call unknown witness] [CONCEPTS: unknown witness, duty to call, prosecution obligations, Pross enquiries, Constable Steele]

Covers: Unknown witnesses — Hrycyszyn v Bonde extract.

### Unknown witnesses. 

No obligation to call an unknown witness where impractical to find them. Defence argued that Pross failed to make enquiries to identify a potential witness and their potential evidence.

***Hrycyszyn v Bonde* \[2003\] TASSC 126 (27 November 2003)**

*\[16\] Counsel for the applicant conceded that no duty rested on the prosecutor to call the unknown person, referring in that regard to Richardson v R [\[1974\] HCA 19](http://www.austlii.edu.au/au/cases/cth/HCA/1974/19.html); [(1974) 131 CLR 116](http://classic.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/LawCite?cit=%281974%29%20131%20CLR%20116?stem=0&synonyms=0&query=poa1935140%20s13) at 119 - 121, Whitehorn v R [\[1983\] HCA 42](http://www.austlii.edu.au/au/cases/cth/HCA/1983/42.html); [(1983) 152 CLR 657](http://classic.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/LawCite?cit=%281983%29%20152%20CLR%20657?stem=0&synonyms=0&query=poa1935140%20s13) at 663 - 665 and Taylor v Hayes [(1989) 46 A Crim R 457](http://classic.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/LawCite?cit=%281989%29%2046%20A%20Crim%20R%20457?stem=0&synonyms=0&query=poa1935140%20s13) at 461 - 463. No authority was cited in support of the ground of review. No attack was made on the fairness of the hearing because of the failure of the police to locate and call the unknown witness. The attack was made solely on the omission of the learned magistrate to deal in his reasons with that failure. I can find no basis for concluding that his Worship ought to have done so. The issue would only have been material if it had been open to the learned magistrate to draw an inference adverse to the prosecution by reason of Constable Steele\'s failure to ascertain who the witness was and what he might have said in evidence. On such skimpy material, no adverse inference could be drawn that might have assisted the applicant\'s case. There is no merit in the ground.*

---

### Chunk 2 — Obligation to Call Material Witnesses (doctrine)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Obligation to Call Material Witnesses - Doctrinal Summary] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Prosecution obligation to call material witnesses] [CONCEPTS: prosecution duty, material witnesses, Apostilides, Whitehorn, call all witnesses, fairness]

Covers: Prosecution obligation to call material witnesses — doctrinal overview and case authorities.

The prosecution alone bears the responsibility of deciding whether to call a person as a witness for the prosecution (*Apostilides v R* (1984) 154 CLR 563).

The prosecution must call all witnesses necessary to unfold the narrative of events unless there is a good reason not to do so (*Whitehorn v R* (1983) 152 CLR 657; *Dyers v R* (2002) 210 CLR 285*; Mahmood v Western Australia* (2008) 232 CLR 397).

As the prosecution seeks the truth, it must call evidence favourable and unfavourable to its case (*R v Soma* (2003) 212 CLR 299; *R v Shaw* (1991) 57 A Crim R 425; *R v Glennon (No. 2)* (2001) 7 VR 631).

However, the prosecution does not need to call a witness if his or her evidence:

- Is likely to be unreliable, untrustworthy or otherwise incapable of belief (*Whitehorn v R* (1983) 152 CLR 657; *R v Newland* (1997) 98 A Crim R 455). It is not enough for the prosecution to merely suspect the witness is unreliable. There must be identifiable circumstances which clearly establish unreliability (*Apostilides v R* (1984) 154 CLR 563).

> The mere fact that a potential witness has made contradictory statements in the past is not a sufficient reason for failing to call him or her (*R v Shaw* (1991) 57 A Crim R 425; *R v Armstrong* \[1998\] 4 VR 533; *R v Palmer* \[2000\] VSCA 236).
>
> The fact that the prosecution expects the witness to be unfavourable, or to give evidence that does not accord with the prosecution's case theory, is also not a sufficient reason for failing to call a witness (*Whitehorse v R* (1983) 152 CLR 657; *R v Newland* (1997) 98 A Crim R 455; *R v Heinze* \[2005\] VSCA 124; *Dyers v R* (2002) 210 CLR 285; *R v Kneebone* (1999) 47 NSWLR 450).

- Is likely to be unnecessarily repetitious, in light of the number of witnesses available for the proof of the matter on which they would give evidence (*Whitehorn v R* (1983) 152 CLR 657); or

- Relates to an issue which has been raised by the defence, and on which the defence bears the onus of proof (e.g., mental impairment) (*R v Fitchett* \[2009\] VSCA 150).

---

### Chunk 3 — Evidence Act s38 and cross-examination (doctrinal note)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Evidence Act 2008] [SECTION: s38] [CITATION: Evidence Act 2008 s38 - Cross-examination & duty to call] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: s38 effect on duty to call witnesses and cross-examination] [CONCEPTS: Evidence Act s38, cross-examine own witness, Santo, Kanaan, Kneebone, prosecution duty]

Covers: How Evidence Act 2008 s38 changes prosecution ability to cross-examine its own witnesses and implications for calling witnesses.

Previously, one of the factors the prosecution could consider when deciding whether to call a witness was whether the interests of justice required that it be able to cross-examine that witness (see, e.g., *Richardson v R* (1974) 131 CLR 116). This was due to the restrictions the common law placed on the prosecution's ability to cross-examine its own witnesses. As *Evidence Act 2008* s38 now provides the prosecution with a greater opportunity to cross-examine its own witnesses, this is no longer a reasonable basis for refusing to call a witness (*Santo v R* \[2009\] NSWCCA 269; *Kanaan v R* \[2006\] NSWCCA 109; *Kneebone v R* (1999) 47 NSWLR 450 per Smart AJ).

It will usually be unjust to refuse the prosecution leave to cross-examine a witness under *Evidence Act 2008* s38 where it has called that witness because of its duty to put all material evidence (including unfavourable evidence) before the jury (*Santo v R* \[2009\] NSWCCA 269; *Kanaan v R* \[2006\] NSWCCA 109; *Kneebone v R* (1999) 47 NSWLR 450 per Smart AJ).

---

### Chunk 4 — Other Explanations for not calling a witness (common law)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Other Explanations for not calling a witness - Common law list] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Acceptable reasons prosecution may not call witness] [CONCEPTS: police failure, no statement, deported witness, privilege, Gillan, Solis note]

Covers: Common law reasons accepted for prosecution not calling a witness.

At common law, the prosecution has been held to have a good reason for not calling a witness in the following circumstances:

- Where it did not know what evidence the witness would give (e.g., because the police did not take a witness statement) (*Gillan v Police (SA)* (2004) 149 A Crim R 354);

(**My note** *CF* with (*Solis v The Queen* \[2018\] VSCA 275, \[104\]-\[108\]) where it was said a failure by police to question a relevant person is not a satisfactory reason for not calling the person. A prosecutor cannot remain passive, leaving a failure by police to dictate who should be called at the trial).

- Where the witness could not have been compelled to give evidence (*R v Reardon (No 2)* (2004) 60 NSWLR 454);

- Where the witness had been deported and could not be subpoenaed (*Fonseka v R* (2003) 140 A Crim R 395);

- Where the witness had indicated that he or she would not give evidence due to a privilege under *Evidence Act 2008* (*R v Kessing* (2008) 73 NSWLR 22).

---

### Chunk 5 — Can a prosecutor select witnesses to suit the case? (annotation)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Prosecutor selection of witnesses - Jones v Dunkel and Kyriacou note] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: Whether prosecutor may cherry-pick witnesses & implications] [CONCEPTS: prosecutor discretion, Jones v Dunkel, Kyriacou, fair trial, s38 Evidence]

Covers: Whether a prosecutor can select witnesses to suit his/her case and Jones v Dunkel consequences.

No, Prosecutor's obligation is to call all material witnesses unless ...

The object is a fair trial and section 38 Evidence, 2001 offers the prosecutor some ability to deal with unfavourable witnesses. Failure to call an apparent witness will generally give rise to a *Jones v Dunkel* warning! Though there is a authority that says there is limited application of the principle to criminal proceedings as explained by the Full Court of the ***South Australian Supreme Court in Police v Kyriacou*** \[2009\] SASC 66; (2009) 103 SASR 243; (2009) 193 A Crim R 490 at \[62\]- \[63\].

### Chunk 6 — Witnesses - Prosecution obligation (Whitehorn v R extract)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Whitehorn v R [1983] HCA 42; (1983) 152 CLR 657] [CITATION: Whitehorn v R - Prosecution obligation extract] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Whitehorn v R on prosecution duties and what constitutes material witness] [CONCEPTS: Whitehorn, Crown prosecutor, material witness, unfold the narrative, unreliable witness]

Covers: Whitehorn v R (Dawson J) on prosecution obligation and material witnesses.

In *Whitehorn v R* [\[1983\] HCA 42](https://mail.connect.tas.gov.au/owa/redir.aspx?C=2fUbEkXTpFQxCgwlL939wml8ZWeeqWYOzU8XMiVqCgxfZ4btcrPUCA..&URL=http%3a%2f%2fwww.austlii.edu.au%2fau%2fcases%2fcth%2fHCA%2f1983%2f42.html); [(1983) 152 CLR 657](https://mail.connect.tas.gov.au/owa/redir.aspx?C=96fI2NG7sD3bU6Vund976ve4uzKA7Nd0AdisaDqOSZ9fZ4btcrPUCA..&URL=http%3a%2f%2fwww.austlii.edu.au%2fcgi-bin%2fLawCite%3fcit%3d%25281983%2529%2520152%2520CLR%2520657) at 16, Dawson J said:

\[15\] *In Richardson v. The Queen [\[1974\] HCA 19](https://mail.connect.tas.gov.au/owa/redir.aspx?C=j5Sqf9udDEz-UTEH7ULB39z8Ox8pLI9QJtkZQaO2KqdfZ4btcrPUCA..&URL=http%3a%2f%2fwww.austlii.edu.au%2fau%2fcases%2fcth%2fHCA%2f1974%2f19.html); [(1974) 131 CLR 116](https://mail.connect.tas.gov.au/owa/redir.aspx?C=FEIeysrjjc8Slx3qQEsbZE7objG2_qPKO18ZzY3SC8RfZ4btcrPUCA..&URL=http%3a%2f%2fwww.austlii.edu.au%2fcgi-bin%2fLawCite%3fcit%3d%25281974%2529%2520131%2520CLR%2520116) this Court pointed out that although the choice made by a Crown Prosecutor of the witnesses to be called in support of the Crown case may be said to involve the exercise of a discretion, that means no more than that he is called upon to make a personal judgment bearing in mind the responsibilities of his office. It is not a discretion which he can be compelled to exercise in a particular manner, although his failure to call witnesses who ought to be called may constitute misconduct and may result in a miscarriage of justice which will constitute a ground for setting aside a conviction and granting a new trial. It is in this context that it is possible to speak of a Crown Prosecutor being bound, or under a duty, to call all available material witnesses. It is not a duty owed by the prosecutor to the accused which is imposed by some rule of law; rather it forms part of a description of the functions of a Crown Prosecutor. *

 

\[16\] *Nevertheless, there is good guidance in the cases for what constitutes a material witness. All available witnesses should be called whose evidence is necessary to unfold the narrative and give a complete account of the events upon which the prosecution is based. In general, these witnesses will include the eye-witnesses of any events which go to prove the elements of the crime charged and will include witnesses notwithstanding that they give accounts inconsistent with the Crown case. However, a prosecutor is not bound to call a witness, even an eye-witness, whose evidence he judges to be unreliable, untrustworthy or otherwise incapable of belief. And if the number of witnesses available for the proof of some matter is such that in the circumstances it would be unnecessarily repetitious to call them all, then a selection may be made. All witnesses whose names are on the indictment, presentment or information should nevertheless be made available by the prosecution in order that they may be called by the defence and should, if practicable, be present at court.*

---

### Chunk 7 — Randall v R; Farmer v R [2004] TASSC 42 extract
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Randall v R; Farmer v R [2004] TASSC 42 - Jury direction re uncalled witnesses] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Jury directions and speculation about uncalled witnesses] [CONCEPTS: Randall, Farmer, Jones v Dunkel, jury directions, speculation]

Covers: Randall v R; Farmer v R — trial judge directions about prosecution's failure to call witnesses.

Final paragraph at \[31\] *Further, as a general rule, a trial judge should not direct the jury in a criminal trial that the prosecution would be expected to have called persons to give evidence other than those it did call as witnesses. It follows that, as a general rule, the judge should not direct the jury that they are entitled to infer that the evidence of those who were not called would not have assisted the prosecution. A direction not to speculate about what the person might have said should be given. Again, exceptions to these general rules will be rare and will arise only in cases where it is shown that the prosecution\'s failure to call the person in question was in breach of the prosecution\'s duty to call all material witnesses.*

*R v Apostilides* \[1984\] HCA 38,

\[13\] *We have come to the conclusion that the following general propositions are applicable to the conduct of criminal trials in Australia:*

> *1. The Crown prosecutor alone bears the responsibility of deciding whether a person will be called as a witness for the Crown.*
>
> *2. The trial judge may but is not obliged to question the prosecutor in order to discover the reasons which lead the prosecutor to decline to call a particular person. He is not called upon to adjudicate the sufficiency of those reasons.*
>
> *3. Whilst at the close of the Crown case the trial judge may properly invite the prosecutor to reconsider such a decision and to have regard to the implications as then appear to the judge at that stage of the proceedings, he cannot direct the prosecutor to call a particular witness.*
>
> *4. When charging the jury, the trial judge may make such comment as he then thinks to be appropriate with respect to the effect which the failure of the prosecutor to call a particular person as a witness would appear to have had on the course of the trial. No doubt that comment, if any, will be affected by such information as to the prosecutor\'s reasons for his decision as the prosecutor thinks it proper to divulge.*
>
> *5. Save in the most exceptional circumstances, the trial judge should not himself call a person to give evidence.*
>
> *6. A decision of the prosecutor not to call a particular person as a witness will only constitute a ground for setting aside a conviction if, when viewed against the conduct of the trial taken as a whole, it is seen to give rise to a miscarriage of justice.*

### Chunk 8 — WITNESS - COURT CALLING A WITNESS (Whitehorn quote on judge's role)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Whitehorn v R [1983] HCA 42; (1983) 152 CLR 657] [CITATION: Whitehorn v R - Court calling a witness / judge role] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Judge's role and limits in calling witnesses / ensuring fairness] [CONCEPTS: judge role, propriety, fairness, parties' responsibilities, Barwick CJ Ratten quote]

Covers: Court calling a witness — Whitehorn v R passage on judge's role and limits.

**Court Calling a Witness**

*In Whitehorn v R* \[1983\] HCA 42; (1983) 152 CLR 657 at 17 - 18, Dawson J said:

\[17\] *No doubt all of these observations are merely aspects of the general obligation which is imposed upon a Crown Prosecutor to act fairly in the discharge of the function which he performs in a criminal trial. That function is ultimately to assist in the attainment of justice between the Crown and the accused. In this respect the Crown Prosecutor may have added responsibilities in comparison with other counsel but it does not mean that his is a detached or disinterested role in the trial process. As Barwick C.J. pointed out in Ratten v. The Queen (1974) 131 CLR 510, at p 517 :*

* *

> *\"It is a trial, not an inquisition: a trial in which the protagonists are the Crown on the one hand and the accused on the other. Each is free to decide the ground on which it or he will contest the issue, the evidence which it or he will call, and what questions whether in chief or in cross-examination shall be asked; always, of course, subject to the rules of evidence, fairness and admissibility. The judge is to take no part in that contest, having his own role to perform in ensuring the propriety and fairness of the trial and in instructing the jury in the relevant law. Upon the evidence and under the judge\'s directions, the jury is to decide whether the accused is guilty or not.\" (at p675)*

\[18\] The means by which a trial judge may ensure the propriety and fairness of a trial do not, however, extend to the assumption of responsibilities which are properly those of the parties. The decision whether to call or not to call witnesses in a criminal trial is a decision for the parties. If the Crown fails to call a witness whom the trial judge considers ought to be called, no doubt he may invite the Crown to reconsider whether the witness ought to be called and if the invitation is declined, and the judge remains of the same view, he may comment upon the failure of the Crown to call the witness. of usurping the 

---

### Chunk 9 — Darren John Burston v David John Brooks [1996] TASSC 152
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Darren John Burston v David John Brooks [1996] TASSC 152] [CITATION: Burston v Brooks [1996] TASSC 152 - Failure to call witnesses] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Failure to call expected witnesses; requirement to disclose reasons in reasons for judgment] [CONCEPTS: Burston, magistrate reasons, manager of Saloon Bar, Jones v Dunkel, miscarriage of justice]

Covers: Burston v Brooks — prosecutor failed to call expected witnesses; requirement for reasons.

***Darren John Burston v David John Brooks \[1996\] TASSC 152 (2 December 1996)***

\[17\] *I have mentioned that a number of witnesses who might have been expected to have been called by the respondent, namely the manager of the Saloon Bar and some four police officers, were not called by him and no explanation was given for this failure. Counsel who appeared for the applicant before the learned magistrate raised this matter in his opening address but the learned magistrate made no mention of it in his reasons. When spoken to in the charge room, the applicant made it quite clear that he did not consider that he had done anything wrong. On the prosecution version of events, the applicant\'s altercation with the manager was the catalyst which led to subsequent events. The respondent\'s failure to call her is mystifying and on the face of it suggests a quite inappropriate conduct of the prosecution. I consider that in summary prosecutions the prosecutor has the same duties as to the calling of witnesses as that held to be appropriate in relation to Crown counsel in Richardson v R [\[1974\] HCA 19](http://www.austlii.edu.au/au/cases/cth/HCA/1974/19.html); [(1974) 131 CLR 116](http://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/LawCite?cit=%281974%29%20131%20CLR%20116?stem=0&synonyms=0&query=poa1935140%20s13) and R v Apostolides [\[1984\] HCA 38](http://www.austlii.edu.au/au/cases/cth/HCA/1984/38.html); [(1984) 154 CLR 563.](http://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/LawCite?cit=%281984%29%20154%20CLR%20563?stem=0&synonyms=0&query=poa1935140%20s13)*

\[18\] *The effect of what was said by counsel in the course of opening of the defence case before the learned magistrate was that he invited the learned magistrate to apply the rule in Jones v Dunkel [\[1959\] HCA 8](http://www.austlii.edu.au/au/cases/cth/HCA/1959/8.html); [(1959) 101 CLR 298.](http://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/LawCite?cit=%281959%29%20101%20CLR%20298?stem=0&synonyms=0&query=poa1935140%20s13) Whether the rule strictly applies to the prosecution in a criminal case may be doubted having regard to the special nature of the duties imposed upon a prosecutor, but where there is a failure on the part of the prosecution \"to call a witness whose evidence is essential to the unfolding\" of its case \"the central question is \... whether in all the circumstances the verdict is unsafe or unsatisfactory\" (R v Apostolides (supra) at 577 - 578). The manager\'s evidence was essential to the unfolding of the respondent\'s case. The absence of her evidence was relevant to the issues which the learned magistrate was required to determine and had been the subject of a specific submission. What weight, if any, the learned magistrate attached to the failure of the respondent to call the manager cannot be gleaned from his reasons. His consideration of this question, and his conclusions concerning it, were required to be disclosed by his reasons.*

---

### Chunk 10 — Model Rules of Professional Conduct and Practice (Rule 20.7)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Model Rules of Professional Conduct and Practice - Rule 20.7] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: checklist] [TOPIC: Prosecutor obligations to call witnesses and exceptions (Rule 20.7)] [CONCEPTS: Model Rules, Rule 20.7, call witnesses, notification, unreliable witness, cross-examination request, exceptions]

Covers: Model Rules of Professional Conduct and Practice — Rule 20.7 obligations and exceptions.

20.7 A prosecutor must call as part of the prosecution\'s case all witnesses:

20.7.1 whose testimony is admissible and necessary for the presentation of the whole picture;

20.7.2 whose testimony provides reasonable grounds for the prosecutor to believe that it could provide admissible evidence relevant to any matter in issue;

20.7.3 whose testimony or statements were used in the course of any committal proceedings; and

20.7.4 from whom statements have been obtained in the preparation or conduct of the prosecution\'s case, unless:

\(a\) the opponent consents to the prosecutor not calling a particular witness;

\(b\) the only matter with respect to which the particular witness can give admissible evidence has been dealt with by an admission on behalf of the accused; or

\(c\) the prosecutor believes on reasonable grounds that the administration of justice in the case would be harmed by calling a particular witness or particular witnesses to establish a particular point already adequately established by another witness or other witnesses, provided that:

- the prosecutor is not obliged to call evidence from a particular witness, who would otherwise fall within Rules 20.7.1-20.7.4, if the prosecutor believes on reasonable grounds that the testimony of that witness is plainly unreliable by reason of the witness being in the camp of the accused;

- the prosecutor must inform the opponent as soon as practicable of the identity of any witness whom the prosecutor intends not to call on any ground within (a), (b) and (c), together with the grounds on which the prosecutor has reached that decision; and

- the prosecutor must call any witness whom the prosecutor intends not to call on the ground in (i) if the opponent requests the prosecutor to do so for the purpose of permitting the opponent to cross-examine that witness.

---

<!-- block_012 master -->

# Legal professional privilege
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: legal professional privilege — scope and sources] [CONCEPTS: legal professional privilege, common law privilege, statutory privilege, legal advice privilege, litigation privilege, dominant purpose, confidentiality, Evidence Acts, waiver, exceptions]

Legal professional privilege is a doctrine that protects certain confidential communications from compelled disclosure and is sourced in both common law and statute. Both the common law and statutory regimes in Australia recognise two principal species of privilege: legal advice privilege (protecting confidential communications made for the dominant purpose of obtaining legal advice) and litigation privilege (protecting communications and documents prepared for the dominant purpose of actual or reasonably anticipated litigation). Several Australian jurisdictions, including the Commonwealth and five states/territories, have enacted largely identical statutory provisions reflecting the common law protections; additionally, the Evidence Acts govern admissibility of purportedly privileged material in proceedings. Privilege is not confined to documents that pass between lawyer and client: it can attach to client notes recording communications with lawyers and to internal lawyer work-product (memos, chronologies) prepared for the client’s benefit. However, documents that already existed before being provided to a lawyer and were not created for the dominant purpose of legal advice will not attract privilege. The doctrine is subject to recognized exceptions, including waiver, statutory abrogation, and the illegal-purpose exception.

# Legal professional privilege — Dominant purpose test and onus
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: evidentiary rule] [TOPIC: dominant purpose test for privilege] [CONCEPTS: dominant purpose, onus of proof, purpose of document, legal advice, anticipated litigation, burden of proof, mixed-purpose documents, prospective litigation, preparatory documents]

The dominant purpose test requires that a communication or document must have been created or produced for the dominant purpose of obtaining legal advice or for use in actual or reasonably anticipated litigation to attract privilege. The onus of proving that dominant purpose rests on the party asserting privilege. If a document would have been created regardless of an intention to seek legal advice or to conduct litigation, it will not be privileged. Where a document contains both legal and non-legal content, only the portion produced for the dominant purpose of legal advice (or litigation) is privileged; commercial, public relations, or purely strategic material is not privileged. Dominant purpose is assessed objectively according to the document’s genesis and contemporaneous circumstances, not merely by later assertions.

# Legal advice privilege — definition and scope
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: statutory definition] [TOPIC: legal advice privilege] [CONCEPTS: legal advice privilege, confidential communications, client-lawyer communications, confidential documents, dominant purpose, legal advice, solicitor-client, privilege scope, work product]

Legal advice privilege protects confidential communications between a client and a lawyer when those communications or documents were made or prepared for the dominant purpose of providing legal advice. Confidential documents made by the client, lawyer, or a third party may be privileged if their dominant purpose was to facilitate legal advice. Privilege extends beyond formal written advice to include oral communications and internal lawyer work product prepared to provide legal advice. The protection is subject to confidentiality requirements and exceptions such as waiver, statutory abrogation, and the illegal-purpose exception. Documents that existed prior to disclosure to a lawyer and were not created for the dominant purpose of legal advice do not attract legal advice privilege.

# Litigation privilege — definition, scope and third-party communications
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: litigation privilege — scope and third-party communications] [CONCEPTS: litigation privilege, anticipated litigation, dominant purpose, expert communications, third party privilege, communications with experts, privilege waiver, litigation preparation]

Litigation privilege protects confidential communications and documents created predominantly for the dominant purpose of actual or reasonably anticipated proceedings. Unlike legal advice privilege, litigation privilege may extend to communications between a lawyer and certain third parties (for example, experts) where those communications are made confidentially and for the dominant purpose of litigation preparation. Communications briefing an expert confidentially for anticipated litigation will generally attract litigation privilege. However, if a party later relies on the expert’s report in litigation, privilege over the instructions to the expert and documents referred to or relied on within the report will typically be waived.

# Confidentiality requirement for privilege
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: evidentiary rule] [TOPIC: confidentiality requirement for privilege] [CONCEPTS: confidentiality, express obligation, implied obligation, third parties present, privileged communication, Evidence Acts definition, secrecy, disclosure, privilege loss]

A communication must be made confidentially to attract privilege: the Evidence Acts define a confidential communication as one made under an express or implied obligation not to disclose its contents. Where a communication occurs in the presence of a third party without a confidentiality obligation, privilege will likely not apply. Confidentiality may be express (written or spoken undertaking) or implied from the context (limited attendance at a meeting, expectation of privacy). The presence of unnecessary persons, public disclosures, or wide dissemination of the communication can undermine confidentiality and thus negate privilege.

# Privilege attaching to client notes and lawyer work-product; exclusion for pre-existing documents
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: work-product and pre-existing documents] [CONCEPTS: client notes, lawyer memos, work product doctrine, privilege attachment, pre-existing documents, contemporaneous creation, dominant purpose, preparation for advice]

Privilege attaches to some documents that do not pass between a lawyer and client, including notes made by a client recording communications with a lawyer and internal lawyer work-product (memos, chronologies) prepared for the client’s benefit. The critical factor is whether those materials were created for the dominant purpose of legal advice or litigation. By contrast, documents already in existence before being provided to a legal adviser, which were not created for the dominant purpose of advice or litigation, will not attract privilege merely by later submission to counsel.

# Evidence Acts versus common law protections
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Evidence Acts vs common law privilege] [CONCEPTS: Evidence Acts, admissibility, common law privilege, compulsory production, discovery, regulators, broader protection, pre-trial processes, statutory regime]

The Evidence Acts regulate whether evidence claimed to be privileged can be adduced or admitted in proceedings; they codify and limit certain privileges for admissibility purposes. The common law, however, provides overlapping but sometimes broader protection, particularly against compulsory processes beyond admissibility at trial — for example, discovery, subpoenas, and certain regulatory compulsion. In some circumstances, common law privilege can be relied upon to resist production in pre-trial discovery or regulatory production processes even when statutory rules do not directly apply. The precise interplay depends on the statute governing the proceeding and the process used to compel documents.

# In-house lawyers — independence and privilege risk
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: in-house counsel privilege] [CONCEPTS: in-house counsel, professional capacity, independence, non-legal role, mixed role risk, employer communications, confidentiality, corporate counsel, privileged status]

Communications between an in-house lawyer and their employer can attract legal professional privilege provided the communication is confidential and the lawyer is acting in their professional legal capacity. There is a heightened risk that privilege will not apply where the in-house lawyer performs substantial non-legal or operational roles, or where the lawyer lacks independence from management. Documents that are merely partisan company positions or routine operational communications without legal analysis will generally not be privileged. To preserve privilege, in-house lawyers should clearly identify when they act in a legal capacity and maintain confidentiality protocols.

# Third parties and experts — litigation privilege only; waiver on reliance
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: evidentiary rule] [TOPIC: experts and third-party communications] [CONCEPTS: expert privilege, third-party communications, litigation privilege only, briefing experts, confidential expert instructions, waiver by reliance, expert reports, disclosure obligations]

Communications with third parties, including experts, are generally only covered by litigation privilege (not legal advice privilege) and then only when made confidentially for the dominant purpose of litigation. Confidentially briefing an expert for an anticipated dispute will typically attract litigation privilege. However, if a party relies on an expert’s report in litigation, the act of reliance ordinarily waives privilege regarding the instructions to the expert and documents referred to or used in forming the expert’s opinion. Parties should engage experts through counsel and clearly frame the engagement to preserve as much privilege as possible, recognising that disclosure of expert reports may effect waiver.

# Waiver of privilege — general principles (express and implied)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: waiver of privilege] [CONCEPTS: waiver, express waiver, implied waiver, inconsistent conduct, fairness, misleading conduct, partial waiver, intentional disclosure, confidentiality breach]

Waiver of legal professional privilege occurs when the holder of the privilege acts in a way inconsistent with the maintenance of confidentiality; waiver can be express or implied. Implied waiver is assessed by whether it would be unfair or misleading to allow a party to maintain privilege despite conduct inconsistent with confidentiality — the party’s intention to waive is not determinative. Simple assertions that legal advice exists do not necessarily waive privilege, but voluntary production of privileged material, selective disclosure, or conduct intended to derive advantage from a document without protecting its confidential status may amount to waiver.

# Waiver by putting state of mind in issue
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: waiver by putting state of mind in issue] [CONCEPTS: state of mind, reliance on legal advice, waiver, estoppel, defence strategy, disclosure of advice, litigation tactic, fairness, putative reliance]

A party may waive privilege by putting their state of mind in issue in proceedings, for example by asserting that they acted on legal advice. When a party relies on legal advice as a basis for conduct relied upon in litigation, it becomes relevant to examine the information and advice known to the party at the time; privilege over such advice may be waived or limited so that fairness to the opposing party is preserved. Courts balance relevance against privilege; putting legal advice into issue can result in disclosure of the advice or aspects of it.

# Inadvertent disclosure — remedial action to preserve privilege
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: evidentiary rule] [TOPIC: inadvertent disclosure and waiver] [CONCEPTS: inadvertent disclosure, prompt action, remedial steps, clawback, privilege preservation, reasonable steps, error correction, disclosure mitigation, privilege protection]

Inadvertent disclosure of privileged material will not necessarily result in waiver if the disclosing party acts promptly and appropriately upon becoming aware of the error. Courts consider whether reasonable remedial steps (notification, return or destruction requests, seeking injunctions) were taken and whether the recipient acted in good faith. Prompt and proportionate actions to rectify accidental disclosure support arguments that privilege has not been waived.

# Common interest privilege / joint privilege
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: common interest / joint privilege] [CONCEPTS: common interest privilege, joint legal advisor, insurer and insured, shared confidentiality, joint defence, privileged communications, community of interest, third-party disclosure]

Communications shared between parties who have a common legal interest — for example, co-defendants, or insurer and insured — may attract a common interest or joint privilege. Where parties share the same lawyer or have sufficiently aligned legal interests, communications exchanged in confidence for the common legal purpose can remain privileged as between them. The protective scope depends on the closeness of interests and whether the third party is bound by confidentiality relating to the joint purpose.

# Authority — Lamers v Lamers [2017] VSC 165
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: Lamers v Lamers [2017] VSC 165] [TOPIC: waiver by inconsistent disclosure] [CONCEPTS: waiver, inconsistent document production, supplementary affidavit, inspection order, Victorian Evidence Act, voluntary production, cross-interpretation]

Lamers v Lamers [2017] VSC 165 confirms that inconsistent production of documents can cause waiver of privilege and permit inspection of otherwise privileged material. In that dispute the defendants produced certain documents voluntarily in an initial affidavit but then claimed privilege over related documents in a subsequent affidavit; the court found it necessary to inspect the claimed privileged documents to understand those voluntarily produced. The decision demonstrates that selective disclosure can render privileged material inspectable where the produced material cannot be properly understood without access to the remaining documents.

# Illegal or improper purpose exception
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: illegal-purpose exception to privilege] [CONCEPTS: illegal purpose, improper purpose, crime-fraud exception, no privilege for facilitating wrongdoing, independent of solicitor knowledge, exception scope, fraudulent purpose, public policy]

Communications made for the purpose of facilitating illegal or improper conduct are not protected by legal professional privilege, irrespective of whether the lawyer knew of or participated in the improper purpose. The illegal-purpose exception (sometimes called the crime-fraud exception) operates to deny privilege where there is a strong case that communications were used to further wrongdoing. The policy rationale is that privilege should not shield communications that are instruments of fraud or other illegal schemes.

# Authority — Aucare Dairy Pty Ltd v Huang [2017] FCA 746
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: Aucare Dairy Pty Ltd v Huang [2017] FCA 746] [TOPIC: illegal-purpose exception applied] [CONCEPTS: fraud, crime-fraud exception, production order, prima facie wrongdoing, privileged correspondence, fraudulent conduct, court-ordered production]

Aucare Dairy Pty Ltd v Huang [2017] FCA 746 applied the illegal-purpose exception to deny privilege where the court found a strong prima facie case of fraudulent conduct and steps taken to place assets beyond the reach of creditors. The Federal Court ordered production of documents claimed to be privileged, finding that communications made in the course of the alleged fraud were not protected even though the solicitors were not shown to have participated in or known of the fraud.

# Statutory abrogation example — s 192 Australian Securities and Investments Commission Act 2001 (Cth)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Australian Securities and Investments Commission Act 2001 (Cth)] [SECTION: s 192] [CITATION: Australian Securities and Investments Commission Act 2001 (Cth) s 192] [TYPE: statutory definition] [TOPIC: statutory abrogation of privilege] [CONCEPTS: statutory abrogation, ASIC, s192, privilege removed, regulators, production powers, legislative exclusion]

Section 192 of the Australian Securities and Investments Commission Act 2001 (Cth) abrogates legal professional privilege in specified contexts, enabling ASIC to require production of certain documents notwithstanding privilege. Statutory abrogation operates subject to the text and limits of the statute; where legislation removes privilege for a particular regulator or process, the common law claim to privilege is displaced to the extent the statute applies. Practitioners must identify any statutory power that overrides privilege when responding to compulsory notices.

# Remedies and enforcement — injunctions and litigation reliance
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: procedural rule] [TOPIC: enforcement of privilege and remedial measures] [CONCEPTS: injunction, restrain disclosure, pre-trial processes, discovery refusal, trial exclusion, clawback, protective orders, confidentiality undertakings, court process]

Privilege may be enforced by injunctions to restrain unauthorised disclosure of privileged communications and by refusing to produce privileged documents in pre-trial processes and at trial. During litigation, parties can resist production requests on privilege grounds, seek protective orders, or oppose admission of privileged communications obtained without consent. Injunctions may be available where threatened disclosure would cause irreparable harm to confidentiality; courts also supervise procedures for inspection and handling of privileged materials to preserve fairness.

# Privilege and regulators — responding to notices and raids
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: procedural rule] [TOPIC: privilege in regulatory investigations] [CONCEPTS: regulators, ASIC, ACCC, raids, compelled production, raids and seizure, withholding documents, confidentiality statement, clawback agreement, regulatory powers]

Privilege may provide a basis for withholding information from regulators in some situations, but whether it applies depends on each regulator’s statutory powers and the circumstances surrounding the request or seizure. When facing compulsory notices or raids (for example by ASIC or the Australian Competition and Consumer Commission), practitioners should assess statutory abrogation, seek protective arrangements where possible, and, if producing documents, accompany production with an express confidentiality statement or agreement asserting privilege and reserving rights. During raids, regulators may or may not be permitted to seize privileged documents; the outcome depends on the statutory powers and judicial oversight.

# Practical tips for practitioners to preserve privilege (selection)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: procedural rule] [TOPIC: practical steps to preserve privilege] [CONCEPTS: confidentiality protocols, limit attendance, in-house counsel practices, board papers, expert engagement, data room cautions, express confidentiality, remedial steps, privilege waiver avoidance]

Practical steps to preserve privilege include: (1) when giving verbal advice, ensure only necessary persons are present to maintain confidentiality; (2) avoid advising third parties about privileged advice as this may waive privilege; (3) in-house counsel should keep legal advice separate from strategic/operational commentary and act independently; (4) legal advice appended to board papers may lose privilege — consider lawyer attendance and preparing a separate record; (5) engage experts through counsel and ensure expert briefings are expressly for legal advice or litigation; (6) recognise reliance on an expert’s report in proceedings will likely waive privilege over instructions and materials; (7) if compelled to produce documents to a regulator, accompany production with an express statement reserving privilege and confidentiality; (8) be cautious with large document productions (data rooms) as wide dissemination can amount to waiver; (9) avoid producing partial material that refers to earlier privileged correspondence (phrases like “as set out in my previous letter”) without protecting the earlier letter.

# Charge negotiation (plea bargaining) — DPP Guidelines and criteria
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: procedural rule] [TOPIC: charge negotiation / plea bargaining — DPP Guidelines] [CONCEPTS: plea bargaining, DPP Guidelines, suitability criteria, proportional charges, sentence adequacy, evidence sufficiency, co-operation, victim views, inadmissible if claim of innocence, negotiation factors]

Charge negotiation is governed by prosecutorial guidelines that require proposed plea bargains to satisfy specified criteria. A proposal should not be entertained unless: (a) the charges bear a reasonable relationship to the accused’s criminal conduct; (b) those charges provide an adequate basis for an appropriate sentence in all circumstances; and (c) there is evidence to support the charges. Additional considerations include whether the accused has cooperated with investigations, likely sentence appropriateness (including existing imprisonment), the desirability of prompt and certain resolution, the accused’s antecedents, the strength of the prosecution case, likely adverse consequences to witnesses, restitution for financial loss, avoidance of delay in other cases, time and expense of trial/appeal, and views of investigating officers and victims. The defendant’s maintained plea of innocence to the charges proposed for guilty plea bars the prosecution from entertaining the proposal.

# Judicial bias — apprehension of bias principle (Livesey)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: apprehension of bias principle] [CONCEPTS: apprehension of bias, reasonable apprehension, impartial tribunal, Livesey v NSW Bar Association, natural justice, disqualification, fair-minded observer, perceived bias]

A judicial officer should not sit to hear a case if, in all the circumstances, parties or the public might entertain a reasonable apprehension that the judge might not bring an impartial and unprejudiced mind to resolve the matter. Livesey v NSW Bar Association (1983) 151 CLR 288 at 293–294 articulates that standard: the test is whether a reasonable observer might conclude that the decision-maker’s impartiality could be compromised. An application to disqualify a judge on grounds of perceived bias asks whether a fair-minded lay observer, informed of the circumstances, would reasonably apprehend a lack of impartiality.

# Pre-judgment versus predisposition — test (Baker v Barratt; Jia Legeng)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: pre-judgment vs predisposition test] [CONCEPTS: pre-judgment, predisposition, Baker v Barratt, Jia Legeng, incapacity to be swayed, fair-minded observer, decision-making standard, evidence vs non-evidence influence]

The test for judicial bias distinguishes pre-judgment from mere predisposition: pre-judgment describes a state of mind so committed to a conclusion as to be incapable of alteration by evidence or argument and will amount to actual bias. Baker v Barratt [2019] TASSC 28 applies the High Court reasoning in Minister for Immigration & Multicultural Affairs v Jia Legeng [2001] HCA 17 (205 CLR 507) that natural justice does not require the absence of any inclination; rather, it requires absence of a settled determination incapable of being swayed by evidence. For apprehended bias, the applicant must show that the fair-minded observer would perceive such an appearance of pre-judgment.

# Test for perceived bias — Kirkland v Tippett [2000] TASSC 94
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: perceived bias test (Kirkland v Tippett)] [CONCEPTS: perceived bias, Kirkland v Tippett, fair-minded observer, prior representation, magistrate impartiality, hypothetical observer, reasonable apprehension, disclosure of communications]

The perceived-bias test asks whether an informed and fair-minded observer, aware that the magistrate had previously represented the defendant, would reasonably apprehend that the magistrate might not bring an impartial and unprejudiced mind to determine the same matter. In Kirkland v Tippett [2000] TASSC 94 the court observed that where the observer lacks knowledge of the content of private communications between judge and party, the observer may reasonably apprehend partiality; selective undisclosed prior representation can lead to reasonable apprehension that impartiality is compromised.

# Apparent bias — definition and statutory error (Schreuder)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: apparent bias and denial of natural justice] [CONCEPTS: apparent bias, seeming partiality, Schreuder v Australian Securities Commission, Justices Act s107(4), error of law, denial of natural justice, public confidence, tribunal disqualification]

Apparent bias — the appearance of partiality — can constitute a denial of natural justice and an error of law under statutory provisions such as the Justices Act 1959 s 107(4). Schreuder v Australian Securities Commission (1996) 6 TAS R 223 at 233 (as cited in Kirkland) supports that denial of natural justice by apparent bias is a reviewable error. The dictionary meaning of “apparent” (seeming real or true, but not necessarily so) captures that the relevant test is the perception of bias by a fair-minded observer rather than proof of actual partiality.

### Chunk 1 — Legal professional privilege — Newsletter header and quick links
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Legal professional privilege - Newsletter TerraLex 13 Dec 2017 - Header & Quick links] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: newsletter header and quick links for legal professional privilege] [CONCEPTS: legal professional privilege, TerraLex, quick links, resources, newsletter]
Tactical anchor: This chunk covers the newsletter header and quick links for legal professional privilege.

Legal professional privilege 

**Newsletter - TerraLex Connections - 13 December 2017**

Legal professional privilege can protect against disclosure of documents.

 

**Quick links**

- https://www.landers.com.au/insights/publications/international/legal-professional-privilege-under-australian-law/#scope
- https://www.landers.com.au/insights/publications/international/legal-professional-privilege-under-australian-law/#application
- https://www.landers.com.au/insights/publications/international/legal-professional-privilege-under-australian-law/#exception
- https://www.landers.com.au/insights/publications/international/legal-professional-privilege-under-australian-law/#enforcing
- https://www.landers.com.au/insights/publications/international/legal-professional-privilege-under-australian-law/#Tips
