### Chunk 2 — General explanation of privilege and enforcement
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Legal professional privilege - Overview & Enforcement] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: overview of legal professional privilege and enforcement mechanisms] [CONCEPTS: privilege overview, enforcement, injunction, litigation, disclosure]
Tactical anchor: This chunk covers a general explanation of legal professional privilege and enforcement options.

For privilege to apply it is necessary to show a communication was confidential, and that its dominant purpose was for the purpose of obtaining legal advice or for actual or anticipated litigation.

Privilege may be able to be enforced via an injunction to restrain unauthorised use of privileged communications or via court processes during litigation.

There are a number of exceptions to legal professional privilege, including waiver, statutory exclusions and improper or illegal conduct. Clients and practitioners should be aware of these exceptions to dispute claims of privilege by opposing parties, and to ensure that their own privileged communications do not fall within an exception.

Parties should be particularly careful in relation to waiver of privilege as this can be implied where a party acts inconsistently with the maintenance of confidentiality.

---

### Chunk 3 — Types of privilege, scope beyond documents, and examples
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Legal professional privilege - Types & Scope] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: legal advice privilege vs litigation privilege; scope beyond documents] [CONCEPTS: legal advice privilege, litigation privilege, Evidence Acts, dominant purpose, confidentiality, client notes, lawyer work product]
Tactical anchor: This chunk covers distinctions between legal advice privilege and litigation privilege and the scope of privilege beyond documents.

The Commonwealth of Australia and five of the country's eight states and territories have enacted legislation relating to legal professional privilege, all of which are largely identical and reflect common law protections.^1^

Both common law and statute recognise two types of privilege: legal advice privilege and litigation privilege.

Legal advice privilege protects "confidential communications" between a client and a lawyer where confidential documents prepared by a client, lawyer or a third party are made or prepared for the dominant purpose of providing legal advice.

Litigation privilege protects confidential communications and documents prepared for the dominant purpose of actual or anticipated proceedings.

The litigation protection applies to communications between a client and their lawyer and may also apply to certain categories of communications between a lawyer and a third party.

The Evidence Acts govern whether evidence can be adduced or admitted into evidence in a proceeding where client legal privilege may apply to that evidence. The common law provides the same protection but also protects against a broader range of compulsory actions to produce documents, including in pre-trial processes such as discovery and, in certain circumstances, claims for production by regulators.

Privilege is not limited to documents, but applies to all communications between lawyers and clients which meet the dominant purpose test and confidentiality requirements set out below. Furthermore, privilege will attach to some documents which do not pass between a lawyer and client. This includes notes made by the client which record communications with the lawyer. Similarly it attaches to work by a lawyer for the benefit of the client, such as memos and chronologies, even if not given to the client.

---

### Chunk 4 — Existing documents provided to advisor do not attract privilege
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Legal professional privilege - Existing documents provided to advisor] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: existing documents provided to legal advisor do not attract privilege] [CONCEPTS: pre-existing documents, privilege exclusion, dominant purpose, confidentiality, court evidence]
Tactical anchor: This chunk covers the rule that existing documents provided to a legal advisor after creation do not attract privilege.

However, documents already in existence which are later provided to a legal advisor for the purpose of providing legal advice will not attract privilege.

Both the common law and the Evidence Acts in Australia require that a communication or document be made or produced for the "dominant purpose" of providing legal advice or actual or anticipated legal proceedings in order to attract privilege.^2^

The onus of proving the dominant purpose of a document or communication lies with the party claiming privilege. If a document would have been brought into existence regardless of an intention to seek legal advice, it will not be privileged.

Where a document or communication contains both legal and non-legal advice, the portion containing legal advice will be privileged and the non-legal advice will not be. Non-legal advice includes advice that is purely commercial or of a public relations character.^3^

A communication must have been made confidentially to attract privilege. The Evidence Acts define a confidential communication as one in which there was an "express or implied obligation not to disclose its contents". This means that where a communication is made in front of a third party, privilege will likely not apply.

---

### Chunk 5 — In-house lawyers
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Legal professional privilege - In-house lawyers] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: privilege for communications with in-house lawyers] [CONCEPTS: in-house counsel, confidentiality, professional capacity, independence, non-legal role]
Tactical anchor: This chunk covers privilege considerations for communications with in-house lawyers.

Privilege can attach to communications between an in-house lawyer and their employer, provided that the communication is made in confidence and the lawyer is acting in their professional capacity.^4^ There is a risk that privilege may not apply to communications with an in-house lawyer who is not sufficiently independent or who also has a non-legal role closely linked with their legal role.

---

### Chunk 6 — Third parties (including experts)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Legal professional privilege - Third parties & experts] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: litigation privilege and third-party communications including experts] [CONCEPTS: litigation privilege, experts, third parties, expert reports, waiver, instructions]
Tactical anchor: This chunk covers how litigation privilege applies to communications with third parties and experts.

Only litigation privilege (not legal advice privilege) can attach to communications with third parties, such as experts.

Generally when an expert is confidentially briefed for an opinion in relation to anticipated litigation this will attract privilege. However, where a party seeks to rely on an expert report in litigation, this will waive privilege over the instructions given and the documents referred to or relied upon within the expert's report.

---

### Chunk 7 — Waiver of privilege and Lamers v Lamers [2017] VSC 165 example
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Legal professional privilege - Waiver & Lamers v Lamers 2017 VSC 165] [CASE: Lamers v Lamers [2017] VSC 165] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: waiver of privilege; Lamers v Lamers example and principles] [CONCEPTS: waiver, implied waiver, supplementary affidavit, inspection, inconsistency, voluntary production, Victorian Evidence Act]
Tactical anchor: This chunk covers waiver of privilege, implied waiver principles, and the Lamers v Lamers example.

There are a number of exceptions to privilege, including waiver, statutory exclusions and illegal or improper conduct.

Waiver of privilege occurs when the party claiming privilege acts inconsistently with the maintenance of the confidentiality of the communication.^5^

Waiver can be express or implied. Where waiver is implied it will not be relevant whether the party intended to waive privilege. Instead the test is whether it would be unfair or misleading to allow a party to maintain privilege despite the inconsistent action.^6^Simply stating that legal advice exists will not be sufficient to waive privilege.

Waiver may also occur where the holder of the privilege puts his or her state of mind in issue. For example, where a party alleges in a proceeding that they undertook a particular action based on legal advice provided, they have put their state of mind in issue in the proceeding. It will then be relevant to consider the factors that the party was aware of at the time of the representation, including any legal advice which had been received.

Inadvertent disclosure may not result in a waiver of privilege as long as the party who committed the error acts promptly upon realising their error.^7^

In some circumstances, disclosure to a third party will not waive privilege if that party has a sufficiently close interest in the litigation or advice. Where parties share the same lawyer or have sufficiently close interests they will have joint privilege or common interest privilege. For example communications between an insurer and an insured may have common interest privilege.

A recent restatement by the Victorian Supreme Court of principles relating to waiver is found in Lamers v Lamers [2017] VSC 165.

This case concerned a dispute between the plaintiff and his uncle in relation to a farming property and promises about the business that the uncle allegedly made to his nephew, including that he would be made the appointer of a trust and have shares in a trustee company transferred to him on his uncle's death . The defendants filed an affidavit of documents and then a later supplementary affidavit.

A claim for privilege was made with respect to the supplementary affidavit. It became apparent that some documents over which privilege had not been sought in the first affidavit were the same documents that had been the subject of claims for privilege in the second affidavit.

The defendants tried to address this question by saying select documents produced voluntarily in the first affidavit were not really privileged. Unfortunately the court went further and said that, it would be necessary to inspect documents listed in the second affidavit which would otherwise be privileged in order to properly understand the documents voluntarily produced. Waiver had occurred and the plaintiff was entitled by the Victorian Evidence Act to inspect otherwise privileged documents or parts of documents relating to a disposal of shares and ownership of the disputed assets to enable a proper understanding of other documents that had already been given to the plaintiff.

---

### Chunk 8 — Privilege excluded for illegal or improper purposes and Aucare Dairy v Huang [2017] FCA 746
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Legal professional privilege - Illegal conduct exception & Aucare Dairy v Huang 2017 FCA 746] [CASE: Aucare Dairy Pty Ltd v Huang [2017] FCA 746] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: privilege exclusion for illegal or improper conduct; Aucare Dairy example] [CONCEPTS: illegal purpose, crime-fraud exception, privilege excluded, fraudulent conduct, Federal Court]
Tactical anchor: This chunk covers the exception for illegal or improper purposes and the Aucare Dairy Pty Ltd v Huang example.

Privilege does not apply to communications made for the purpose of facilitating illegal or improper purposes. This applies regardless of whether or not the lawyer was a party to, or even aware of, the improper purpose.

A recent example of where privilege was excluded in such circumstances is found in Aucare Dairy Pty Ltd v Huang [2017] FCA 746. The Federal Court found that, as there was a strong case that the respondents had engaged in fraudulent conduct, communications with their lawyers in the course of that conduct were not privileged. The applicants did not contend that the respondent's solicitors had knowledge of or participated in the fraud and did not need to do so to succeed in their application.

In this case two companies, Aucare and Noyier were parties to a joint venture agreement pursuant to which a third company, APD was incorporated as the JV vehicle and each of Aucare and Noyier had to contribute to 50% to the project. A fourth company, Great Vision was to supply equipment to APD for the ventures proposed baby milk factory.

Ms Huang was a director of APD, Noiyer and Great Vision. It was alleged that, when it became apparent that APD was insolvent, Great Vision had removed equipment from APD's premises and sold those assets to another company at under value.

The court found a strong prima facie case that the respondents took steps to place legal ownership and control of assets outside the reach of APD. It also found a strong prima facie case that wrongful and fraudulent conduct took place with the assistance of the respondents' lawyers.

For these reasons the court ordered that a number of documents be produced for which privilege was claimed, largely correspondence between Ms. Huang (or her de facto) and her lawyers.

---

### Chunk 9 — Statutory abrogation of privilege (ASIC Act s 192)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Australian Securities and Investments Act 2001 (Cth)] [SECTION: 192] [CITATION: Legal professional privilege - ASIC Act s192 abrogation] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: statutory abrogation of privilege under ASIC Act s192] [CONCEPTS: statutory abrogation, s192, ASIC Act, legal professional privilege, statute override]
Tactical anchor: This chunk covers statutory abrogation of privilege under the Australian Securities and Investments Act 2001 (Cth) s 192.

Privilege can be removed or altered by legislation. For example section 192 of the Australian Securities and Investments Act 2001 (Cth) abrogates legal professional privilege.^8^

---

### Chunk 10 — Injunctions, pre-trial use of privilege, and regulators
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Legal professional privilege - Injunctions & regulators] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: injunctions to restrain disclosure, withholding from regulators, and raids] [CONCEPTS: injunctions, pre-trial procedures, regulators, ASIC, ACCC, raids, seizure, production, privilege claims]
Tactical anchor: This chunk covers using privilege to prevent disclosure, withholding from regulators, and handling raids.

Where a party is threatening the disclosure of privileged information it may be possible to obtain an injunction seeking to restrain the disclosure.

Additionally, during the course of litigation, privilege can be used as a basis for refusing requests from another party to provide privileged documents during pre-trial procedures or during a trial. Privilege can also be used to combat attempts by other parties to have a court admit privileged communications which have been obtained despite the privileged status of those documents.

Privilege also provides a basis for withholding information from regulators in some situations. Whether this is possible and the necessary steps will depend on the specific regulator and the circumstances in which the information has been requested. For example, privilege can in some instances be used as a method for withholding privileged documents from for production for some compulsory notices issued by Australia's corporate regulator, the Australian Securities and Investments Commission (ASIC).

Where a regulator such as the Australian Competition and Consumer Commission or ASIC conduct a raid to seize documents, they may or may not be permitted to take privileged documents.

---

### Chunk 11 — Practical tips for practitioners (1–9)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Legal professional privilege - Practical tips 1-9] [CATEGORY: checklist] [TYPE: checklist] [TOPIC: practical tips to preserve privilege and avoid waiver] [CONCEPTS: confidentiality, waiver prevention, in-house counsel, experts, regulators, data rooms, production, privilege protection]
Tactical anchor: This chunk covers a practitioner's checklist of practical tips to preserve privilege and avoid waiver.

1.  When giving verbal advice, make sure that only those people who are necessary are present to ensure that there is the necessary confidentiality for privilege to apply.

2.  Advising a third party about the contents of advice may waive privilege.

3.  When acting as in-house counsel, legal advice should not be mixed with comments about strategic or operational matters. All advice given by in-house counsel must be independent of their employer to be privileged. Documents merely stating a partisan company position without independent thought will not be privileged.

4.  Legal advice which forms part of or an attachment to Board papers may not be privileged and nor will notes made about what non-lawyers have said at a board meeting when they discuss legal advice received. To get around this issue, a legal advisor should attend in person at any board meeting to advise its members and then prepare a record of that meeting to give directly to the board members to confirm the advice provided.

5.  Unless an expert is formally retained by a lawyer for the express purpose of assisting a lawyer to give advice and has all communications necessary for their work with the lawyer, it should be assumed that any communications between clients and experts, and any documents created by them may not be privileged. For this reason it is good to get lawyers involved early on during any investigations. Where a lawyer is engaged to investigate for the purpose of potential litigation the investigation will be privileged.

6.  If an expert's advice is relied on in any proceeding the communications with them or to them will likely not be privileged, nor will the expert's notes and draft documents.

7.  It is often not possible to refuse to provide documents to a regulator. If documents are produced, it should be done together with an express statement (or if possible an agreement) that the documents are confidential and privileged and the provision of the documents does not amount to any waiver.

8.  Be wary of provision of large volumes of documents in commercial transactions such as via data rooms and the like. Provision of documents in such circumstances can amount to a waiver of privilege.

9.  Be careful when producing documents to avoid a waiver of privilege in other documents. If a party produces a letter from a lawyer about a transaction which says "as set out in my previous letter" there may well be a waiver of privilege in that previous letter even if it is privileged.

---

### Chunk 12 — Charge negotiation (plea bargaining) — DPP Guidelines
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Charge negotiation - DPP Guidelines - Plea bargaining] [CATEGORY: checklist] [TYPE: checklist] [TOPIC: DPP guidelines for charge negotiation and considerations] [CONCEPTS: plea bargaining, DPP guidelines, charges, sentence, evidence, co-operation, restitution, victim views]
Tactical anchor: This chunk covers DPP Guideline criteria and considerations for charge negotiation (plea bargaining).

DPP Guidelines:

A proposal should not be entertained by the prosecution unless:

• the charges to be proceeded with bear a reasonable relationship to the nature of the criminal conduct of the accused

• those charges provide an adequate basis for an appropriate sentence in all the circumstances of the case

• there is evidence to support the charges

Any decision whether or not to agree to a proposal advanced by the defence, or to put a counter-proposal to the defence, must take into account all the circumstances of the case and other relevant considerations including:

• whether the accused is willing to co-operate in the investigation or prosecution of others, or the extent to which the accused has done so

• whether the sentence that is likely to be imposed if the charges are varied as proposed would be appropriate for the criminal conduct involved (taking into account such matters as whether the accused is already serving a term of imprisonment)

• the desirability of prompt and certain resolution of the case

• the accused's antecedents

• the strength of the prosecution case

• the likelihood of adverse consequences to witnesses

• in cases where there has been a financial loss to the State or any person, whether the accused has made restitution or arrangements for restitution

• the need to avoid delay in the resolution of other pending cases • the time and expense involved in a trial and any appeal proceeding

• the views of the investigating police officers

• the views of the victim or others significantly affected

In no circumstances should the prosecution entertain a proposal if the accused maintains his or her innocence **with respect to a charge or charges to which the accused has offered to plead guilty**

---

### Chunk 13 — Judicial Bias — Apprehension of bias (procedure)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Judicial bias - Apprehension of bias - procedure] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: procedure] [TOPIC: making an application to disqualify a judicial officer for apprehended bias] [CONCEPTS: apprehension of bias, disqualification application, Livesey, fair-minded observer, pre-judgment, natural justice]
Tactical anchor: This chunk covers the procedure and immediate steps for apprehension of bias (application to disqualify).

Make an application to disqualify themselves from the hearing upon the grounds of perceived bias

The learned magistrate's view of the defendant might have been affected as a result of the private communications she had from him

Livesey v NSW Bar Association 1983 151 CLR 288 at 293-294

*A judicial officer should not sit to hear a case if in all the circumstances the parties or the public might entertain a reasonable apprehension that he might not bring an impartial and unprejudiced mind to the resolution of the question involved in it*

---

### Chunk 14 — Baker v Barratt [2019] TASSC 28 (case note)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Judicial bias - Baker v Barratt 2019 TASSC 28] [CASE: Baker v Barratt [2019] TASSC 28 (4 July 2019)] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Baker v Barratt - standard for apprehended bias and pre-judgment] [CONCEPTS: apprehended bias, pre-judgment, fair-minded lay observer, familial relationship, evidence, Gummow J, Gleeson CJ, Jia Legeng]
Tactical anchor: This chunk covers the Baker v Barratt decision and its statement of the law on apprehended bias and pre-judgment.

Baker v Barratt [2019] TASSC 28 (4 July 2019)

The requisite standard required of a judicial officer is breached if, in the eyes of that fair minded lay observer, a decision-maker proceeds to determination of a question by reference to something other than the evidence which is led. Such pre-judgment will amount to actual bias. In the context of *apprehended *bias, what is required to be shown is that in the eyes of the fair minded lay observer there is the appearance of such a pre-judgment because of the identified matter, in this case the familial relationship with the complainant's employer. It must be demonstrated by an applicant that not only is there the appearance of that pre-judgment, but circumstances which suggest that it is incapable of being swayed by evidence or argument. The test is concerned with pre-judgment, not pre-disposition. The matter is expressed succinctly in *Minister for Immigration and Multi-cultural Affairs v Jia Legeng* [\[2001\] HCA 17](http://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/viewdoc/au/cases/cth/HCA/2001/17.html), [205 CLR 507](http://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/LawCite?cit=205%20CLR%20507) at [\[71\]](http://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/viewdoc/au/cases/cth/HCA/2001/17.html#para71)- [\[72\]](http://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/viewdoc/au/cases/cth/HCA/2001/17.html#para72) by Gleeson CJ and Gummow J:

\... The state of mind described as bias in the form of prejudgment is one so committed to a conclusion already formed as to be incapable of alteration, whatever evidence or arguments may be presented. Natural justice does not require the absence of any predisposition or inclination for or against an argument or conclusion \...\".

---

### Chunk 15 — Pre-judgment vs pre-disposition note (Jia Legeng citation)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Judicial bias - Pre-judgment vs pre-disposition note - Jia Legeng] [CASE: Minister for Immigration and Multi-cultural Affairs v Jia Legeng [2001] HCA 17; 205 CLR 507] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: distinction between pre-judgment and pre-disposition; Jia Legeng quote] [CONCEPTS: pre-judgment, pre-disposition, natural justice, Jia Legeng, predisposition]
Tactical anchor: This chunk covers the distinction between pre-judgment and pre-disposition as expressed in Jia Legeng.

The test is concerned with pre-judgment, not pre-disposition 

**Baker v Barratt \[2019\] TASSC 28 (4 July 2019)**

The test is concerned with pre-judgment, not pre-disposition. ### Chunk 16 — The test for perceived bias (Kirkland v Tippett excerpt)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Judicial bias - Kirkland v Tippett para 21-22 - perceived bias test] [CASE: Kirkland v Tippett [2000] TASSC 94] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: perceived bias test as stated in Kirkland v Tippett para 21-22] [CONCEPTS: perceived bias, fair-minded observer, prior representation, magistrate, apprehended bias, Kirkland v Tippett]
Tactical anchor: This chunk covers the test for perceived bias from Kirkland v Tippett [2000] TASSC 94 (paras 21–22).

### The test for perceived bias:
\[para 21 -- 22 Kirkland v Tippett \[2000\] TASSC 94\]

Whether an informed and fair minded observer, armed with the knowledge that the magistrate had previously represented the defendant, would reasonably apprehend that the leaned magistrate might not bring an impartial and unprejudicial mind to bear on the determination of the very same matter in which her honor represented the def

The hypothetical fair minded observer would have no idea what communications took place between her honor and the defendant

The observer would have little choice but to reasonably apprehend that the learned magistrate might not bring an impartial and unprejudiced mind to bear.

---

Dimech v Tasmania [2016] TASCCA 3 is cited in the source material as a relevant Tasmanian appellate authority but the source part does not record the legal principle drawn from the judgment. The present chunk therefore records the citation as a case authority for retrieval and flags the citation for review and integration: practitioners should obtain and read the full Tasmanian Court of Criminal Appeal judgment to extract the precise evidentiary or interpretive proposition relied upon (for example, any holdings on admissibility, hearsay, appeals from magistrates, or interaction with statutory evidence provisions). Because the uploaded part did not summarise Dimech’s ratio or holdings, this chunk does not attribute a substantive legal rule to the case and is marked [REVIEW] to indicate that the primary judgment must be consulted before relying on it in argument or import into the master file.

### Chunk 1 — One sided phone recordings: Listening Devices Act Tas and Sepulveda summary
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Listening Devices Act 1991 (Tas)] [SECTION: s5] [CITATION: Listening Devices Act 1991 (Tas) s5 - One sided phone recordings & Sepulveda] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: one-sided phone recordings and lawful interest defence] [CONCEPTS: one-sided recording, lawful interest, Sepulveda, s5 LDA, clandestine recording, victim recordings, admissibility]

One sided phone recordings:

Listening devices act tas

### Chunk 3 — Listening Devices Act 1991 - Legal Argument (s5(1)(b), s5(3), s14(1))

***Listening Devices Act 1991***

Section 5(1)(b) -- cannot record a conversation to which you are a party

If caught under 5(1)(b) it is inadmissible under s 14(1)

Exception to Section 5(1)(b) is contained in s 5(3)

5(3)(a) -- implied consent; or

**5(3)(b)(i) -- reasonably necessary for the protection of the lawful interests of that principal party**

### Chunk 4 — Moore v Harrison [2018] TASMC — written decision reference and factual background

### Chunk 5 — s38 application to cross-examine victim based on PIS recorded by sister — submissions and Moore paragraph references
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Evidence Act 2001 (Tas)] [SECTION: s38] [CITATION: Evidence Act 2001 (Tas) s38 - Application to CX on PIS recorded by third party (Moore submissions)] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: script] [TOPIC: s38 application and submissions on admissibility of PIS recorded by sister] [CONCEPTS: s38, PIS, cross-examination, third-party recording, Moore submissions, protection from family violence, para references, balancing necessary]

Covers: submissions in support of admitting a PIS recorded by the victim's sister for use in a s38 cross-examination.

IN an application under s38, to CX the victim, based on a PIS recorded by the sister, evidence was given that the reason for making the recording was because she knew, from experience, that the victim would not give an honest account about what happened, to protect the defendant from being punished.

His honour canvased the authorities as to

- *Lawful interests:*

- *Reasonably necessary to protect:*

**I Respectfully refer your honour to Para 20**- where it is seen that protecting the interests of a victim of a serous crime, including family violence, has been found to be a sufficient interest -

**And in fact at At para 29** The HH identified "the relevant lawful interest" in that case as protection from

family violence.

**And again at para 31** -- there was lawful interest in recording the conversation as could not be disputed in a way that oral evidence alone might be

I submit, in principle it is the same interest here, that is, to be protected from family violence in circumstances where there are no witnesses to the violence being perpetrated.

Whether it was "***Reasonably necessary to protect***" that question is canvased from para 33 onwards, it is essentially a balancing act of factors within the circumstances of the particular case

Including the means by which an otherwise lawful recording might be made -- such as getting a warrant -- which I submit was not viable.

Where there is an immediate need to make a recording.

Whether there is an ability to make contemporaneous notes -- I would submit such a proposition in such circumstances is bordering on ludicrous.

And relevant is the nature of the interest being protected, and on page 11, obtaining evidence to protect from family violence is specifically identified.

Even if against me on that submission, I submit the evidence ought to be otherwise admitted under 138(3) of the evidence act And I rely again on the reasoning of HH Magistrate Stanton -- from para \[51\]

### Chunk 6 — Reliance on s138(3) of Evidence Act and note on s14 of Listening Devices Act; reference to Dimech v Tasmania
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Evidence Act 2001 (Tas); Listening Devices Act 1991 (Tas)] [SECTION: Evidence Act s138(3); Listening Devices Act s14] [CITATION: Evidence Act 2001 (Tas) s138(3) & Listening Devices Act 1991 s14 - Alternative admissibility arguments and Dimech] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: reliance on s138(3) and interaction with s14 of LDA; Dimech v Tasmania] [CONCEPTS: s138(3), probative vs prejudicial, s14 LDA, admissibility alternative, Dimech v Tasmania, exclusion of illegally obtained evidence]

Covers: alternative admissibility under Evidence Act s138(3) and interaction with Listening Devices Act s14, with reference to Dimech v Tasmania.

I submit, in principle it is the same interest here, that is, to be protected from family violence in circumstances where there are no witnesses to the violence being perpetrated.

Whether it was "***Reasonably necessary to protect***" that question is canvased from para 33 onwards, it is essentially a balancing act of factors within the circumstances of the particular case

Including the means by which an otherwise lawful recording might be made -- such as getting a warrant -- which I submit was not viable.

Where there is an immediate need to make a recording.

Whether there is an ability to make contemporaneous notes -- I would submit such a proposition in such circumstances is bordering on ludicrous.

And relevant is the nature of the interest being protected, and on page 11, obtaining evidence to protect from family violence is specifically identified.

Even if against me on that submission, I submit the evidence ought to be otherwise admitted under 138(3) of the evidence act And I rely again on the reasoning of HH Magistrate Stanton -- from para \[51\]

Section 14 Of the listening devices act does not exclude the evidence because the victim (recorder) who is to give evidence in proceedings did not become aware of the conversation because of the recording. They were a party to the conversation. That's how they became aware of it.

Also look at ***Dimech v Tasmania* \[2016\] TASCCA 3 (2 March 2016)**

---

### Chunk 7 — Absence of evidence not terminal — Shepperd v Cannell [2018] TASSC 12
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Shepperd v Cannell [2018] TASSC 12] [CITATION: Shepperd v Cannell [2018] TASSC 12 - Absence of evidence not fatal] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: absence of evidence not necessarily fatal; police memory of injuries] [CONCEPTS: Shepperd v Cannell, absence of evidence, police memory, corroboration, photographs, immediate complaint]

Covers: authority that absence of evidence (eg police not recalling injuries) is not necessarily fatal to the complainant's case.

# Absence of evidence not terminal 

***Shepperd v Cannell \[2018\] TASSC 12***

**Para 20 is a good rundown on how the failure of a police officer to have no memory of injuries is not extraordinary and not necessarily indicative that they did not exist!**

\[20\] However, there were also several aspects of the evidence upon which the magistrate was entitled to rely as supportive of the complainant\'s version. The existence of the damage within the house was important corroborative evidence. The immediate complaint made by the complainant to the police was also a matter which the magistrate was entitled to regard as supporting the consistency of the complainant\'s version. The complainant had taken photographs of injuries. While these may have provided only limited support, they were consistent with the complainant\'s version. I do not regard the evidence of the police officers, that they did not observe any injuries, as carrying significant probative weight. There was no examination of the police about the extent to which they were looking for injuries or had examined the complainant. There was no evidence of the state of the injuries, in particular the cut lip, at the time the police were present. I would not regard it as particularly exceptional that police may not have been concerned at the time of this visit with matters of forensic importance, such as making and recording observations for later use in court.

---

Covers: authority that omission of reasons in notes/statement can be excused when officers were busy and legality was not in question.

### Collecting DNA and Fingerprint evidence - general sampling process
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: procedure] [TOPIC: collection and labelling workflow for DNA and fingerprint samples] [CONCEPTS: DNA collection, barcode, sample numbering, Tas Forensic Services, TasPol, sample chain of custody, photograph, sample taker, analyst]

Tactical workflow for collecting and recording DNA and fingerprint samples.

heyoldpal

**DNA and Fingerprint Evidence**

- Sample site recorded by photograph or by seizing item.

- Item or location is given a barcode/ sample number.

- Sample details are recorded by sampling officer and a barcode is kept in their personal records.

- Sample is sent to Tas Forensic Services (DNA) or to Fingerprint section (TasPol)

### DNA:
- DNA will result in report making reference to specific barcode numbers

### DNA: expert witness handling, authentication, and tendering
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Evidence Act 2001 (Tas)] [SECTION: ss 170, 171-172 (service & business records referenced elsewhere)] [CITATION: Evidence Act 2001 (Tas) - DNA Evidence Handling and Expert Authentication] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: procedure] [TOPIC: steps for linking DNA sample barcode to item and tendering DNA analyst evidence] [CONCEPTS: DNA report, barcode linking, sample taker, Tas Forensic Services, expert witness, authentication, tendering report, degradation, cross-examination]

Tactical workflow for proceeding from barcode linkage to tendering a DNA analyst as an expert witness.

- Call sample taker -- ensure to ask if the sample/ item was given a barcode/ reference number (note, can seek leave to permit officer to refer to their notes to retrieve the number)

- Once link between item/ sample location and barcode reference number is made, analyst can be called.

- Treat as with all expert witnesses -- confirm details of expertise and submit to court that witness is qualified to present expert opinion

- Once expertise established ask them if the prepared a report -- authenticate -- and seek to tender.

- Generally this is enough -- can ask more questions like degradation periods of samples etc. if poignant

It was not possible to determine from DNA analysis alone how, and for how long, DNA has been deposited on an item.

### Bank records warrants for records affidavits

### ID Evidence — Dock Identification (Police v Murtagh [2009] TASMC 05)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Police v Murtagh [2009] TASMC 05] [CITATION: Police v Murtagh [2009] TASMC 05 - Dock Identification] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: principle on dock identifications and confirmatory prior identifications] [CONCEPTS: dock identification, out-of-court identification, Jamal v R, R v Gorham, confirmatory identification, mistaken ID, Tasmanian authority]

Covers dock identification admissibility and confirmatory prior identification requirement.

***Police v Murtagh* [2009] TASMC 05**

\[67\] \... dock identifications are not usually permitted, other than as confirmatory of an acceptable prior out-of-court identification: *Jamal v R [\[2000\] FCA 1195](http://www.austlii.edu.au/au/cases/cth/FCA/2000/1195.html); [(2000) 182 ALR 307](http://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/LawCite?cit=%282000%29%20182%20ALR%20307)*, *R v Gorham* [\[1997\] SASC 6218](http://www.austlii.edu.au/au/cases/sa/SASC/1997/6563.html); [(1997) 68 SASR 505).](http://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/LawCite?cit=%281997%29%2068%20SASR%20505)

"\[65\] The courts have long adopted the attitude that, in cases in which a witness has not become familiar with the appearance of an accused person by reason of previous knowledge or association and has not seen the accused since the events in question, evidence of identification will not normally be admitted where the purported identification has occurred in circumstances in which the accused is specifically presented to the witness as a person who is suspected of having committed the relevant crime.

### ID Evidence — Recognition evidence (Sundram v Bearman [2020] TASMC 18)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CASE: Sundram v Bearman [2020] TASMC 18] [CITATION: Sundram v Bearman [2020] TASMC 18 - Recognition Evidence Caution] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: cautionary note on conviction based on recognition or identification evidence] [CONCEPTS: recognition evidence, mistaken identification, caution, Sundram v Bearman, reliability, displacement effect, rogues gallery effect]

Covers cautionary judicial remarks on recognition evidence reliability.

***Sundram v Bearman \[2020\] TASMC 18 (3 August 2020)***

*137 I direct myself as follows. The Court should be specially cautious before convicting in a case depending on identification or recognition evidence even if there is other evidence:**[^\[110\]^](http://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/viewdoc/au/cases/tas/TASMC/2020/18.html#fn110). Mistakes in identification do occur**[^\[111\]^](http://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/viewdoc/au/cases/tas/TASMC/2020/18.html#fn111). As a result of honest but mistaken identification, innocent people have been convicted**[^\[112\]^](http://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/viewdoc/au/cases/tas/TASMC/2020/18.html#fn112). Even close friends and relatives can be mistaken**[^\[113\]^](http://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/viewdoc/au/cases/tas/TASMC/2020/18.html#fn113). Honest and convincing witnesses may be mistaken**[^\[114\]^](http://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/viewdoc/au/cases/tas/TASMC/2020/18.html#fn114). \"Recognition\" cases can involve as much danger of mistaken identification as cases involving persons first seen at the times of their alleged crimes**[^\[115\]^](http://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/viewdoc/au/cases/tas/TASMC/2020/18.html#fn115). However, recognition evidence will, generally speaking, be more reliable than identification evidence** **strictly so called because, for example, the displacement effect and the 'rogues gallery effect' would not appear to be material.**[^\[116\]^](http://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/viewdoc/au/cases/tas/TASMC/2020/18.html#fn116) Ms Paul's evidence was not directly challenged on the basis that she had made an error in her recognition of Mr Bearman, but his defence necessarily raised that issue.*

---

### ID Evidence — Police ID Evidence (YOUNG v LUSTED [2011] TASSC 22; note re Baker v Penneyston [2018] TASSC 8)

It was relevant to the probative value of the evidence. The magistrate was justified in having regard to the fact that a police officer who by reason of his rank had obviously had long experience as a police officer, would have greater training and experience for perceiving and remembering the features of a person under observation than many lay persons. The Inspector was likely to have been more aware than a lay person of the need to observe and retain a memory of his observations.

\[29\] For those reasons, I conclude that the magistrate correctly decided to admit the evidence.

That evidence included the following. When Inspector Hopkins first saw the Falcon on Hobart Road it was approaching him from the direction of Perth, where the applicant lived. It was not long after the Inspector lost sight of the vehicle that the applicant was found nearby and arrested by Constable Greenwood, following which he was placed in the police car. When Constable Greenwood found him he was walking from Hobart Road along Lockhart Street, the route the Falcon had followed not long before when it was followed by the Inspector. ### Voir Dire — Conduct and Management: overview and commencement rules
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Criminal Code (Tasmania); Evidence Act 2001 (Tas)] [SECTION: Criminal Code s 351(6); Evidence Act ss 142, 66(2), 137, 138, 90, 189(6), 361A referenced elsewhere] [CITATION: Voir Dire Conduct and Management - Chief Justice Blow & Daryl Coates SC Notes (Law Society Conference 26 Feb 2016)] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: procedure] [TOPIC: commencement of trial, judge discretion on order of evidence on voir dire, oral vs paper determinations] [CONCEPTS: voir dire, commencement of trial, call on to plead, judge discretion, R v Turner, oral evidence, on the papers, criminal trial commencement]

Tactical workflow and notes on conducting a voir dire in criminal trials.

The Conduct and Management of a Voir Dire

(*Notes for a presentation by the Hon Alan Blow OAM, Chief Justice of Tasmania and Daryl Coates SC, Director of Public Prosecutions, at the Law Society of Tasmania's Criminal Law Conference, 26 February 2016*)

A criminal trial is "deemed to begin when the accused is called upon to plead": Criminal Code , s 351(6).

The court has a discretion as to which party presents evidence first on the voir dire: *R vTurner (No 12)* \[2001\] TASSC 103. However the defence is rarely, if ever, compelled to go first.

Prima facie, all evidence on a voir dire is to be given orally. In practice, counsel very often seek to have questions of admissibility determined wholly or partly "on the papers".

---

### Voir Dire — Evidentiary thresholds: burden and standard (s 142 Evidence Act; Moore v Knowles)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Evidence Act 2001 (Tas)] [SECTION: s 142] [CITATION: Evidence Act 2001 (Tas) s 142 - Burden and Standard on Voir Dire] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: burden and standard of proof on voir dire and Moore v Knowles example] [CONCEPTS: burden of proof, balance of probabilities, s142, Moore v Knowles, voir dire standard, admissibility facts]

Covers the burden and standard of proof for facts determined on a voir dire.

Evidentiary thresholds:

In proceedings on the voir dire, the burden of proof of a fact is upon the party asserting that fact.

In proceedings on the voir dire, the standard of proof is proof on the balance of probabilities: *Evidence Act*, s 142. See also *Moore v Knowles \[2017\] TASMC* 12

*Moore v Knowles \[2017\] TASMC* 12

> *\[15\] The effect of [s 142](https://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/viewdoc/au/legis/tas/consol_act/ea200180/s142.html) of the [Evidence Act 2001](https://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/viewdoc/au/legis/tas/consol_act/ea200180/) is that I am to find the facts necessary for deciding a question about the admissibility of evidence, including the exercise of a discretion, on the balance of probabilities. The defendant is alleging impropriety or illegality in the conduct of the search by Constable Morrison in order to have the evidence of what was found on that search excluded pursuant to [s 138.](https://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/viewdoc/au/legis/tas/consol_act/ea200180/s138.html) If the evidence is excluded by [s 138(1)](https://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/viewdoc/au/legis/tas/consol_act/ea200180/s138.html) I then have to exercise a discretion as to whether to admit the evidence in any event. Those matters are clearly within the province of [s 142](https://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/viewdoc/au/legis/tas/consol_act/ea200180/s142.html) of the [Evidence Act 2001](https://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/viewdoc/au/legis/tas/consol_act/ea200180/). I must therefore determine the facts relevant to those issues on the balance of probabilities.*

---

### Voir Dire — Oaths, accused protections, and limits on cross-examination
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Evidence Act 2001 (Tas)] [SECTION: s 189(6)] [CITATION: Evidence Act 2001 (Tas) s 189(6) - Accused Protections on Voir Dire] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: checklist] [TOPIC: oaths for witnesses, accused cannot be asked incriminating questions, scope of cross-examination on voir dire] [CONCEPTS: voir dire oath, new oath, accused protection, incriminating questions, s189(6), cross-examination limits, abuse of process]

Covers procedural protections and oath requirements on the voir dire.

- If a witness has commenced giving evidence before a jury, and needs to give evidence on the voir dire, that witness must first swear another oath or make another affirmation. That is to say, the voir dire is treated as a separate proceeding, requiring afresh oath or affirmation. The form of oath or affirmation is the same as the form used for evidence before the jury.

- If the accused gives evidence on the voir dire, he or she should not be asked whether he or she committed the crime charged, or any question that might tend to in criminate him or her in the crime charged. Such questions do not have to be answered by the accused: *Evidence Act*, s 189(6).

- Cross-examination on the voir dire is permissible only in relation to issues that arise on the voir dire. Questions relating to issues that arise on the trial, but not on the voir dire, amount to an abuse of process, and should be disallowed as irrelevant.

---

### Voir Dire — Common matters: Danger of unfair prejudice (s 137 Evidence Act)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Evidence Act 2001 (Tas)] [SECTION: s 137] [CITATION: Evidence Act 2001 (Tas) s 137 - Danger of Unfair Prejudice on Voir Dire] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: s137 considerations and case authorities for unfair prejudice] [CONCEPTS: unfair prejudice, probative value, s137, KMJ v Tasmania, R v Shamouil, directions to jury, Dupas, IMM]

Covers the s 137 test and authorities on danger of unfair prejudice.

### Danger of unfair prejudice. 

Evidence may be objected to on the basis that "its probative value is outweighed by the danger of unfair prejudice to the defendant": *Evidence Act*, s 137. In assessing the probative value of the evidence, it must be assumed that the jury will accept the evidence as credible, and that the jury will draw from it every inference favourable to the Crown that is reasonably open: *KMJ v Tasmania* (2011) 20 Tas R 425; *R v Shamouil* (2006) 66 NSWLR 228; *Rv Sood* \[2007\] NSWCCA 214; *R v XY* (2013) 84 NSWLR 363. A different view has been taken in Victoria: *Dupas v The Queen* (2012) 40 VR 182. The High Court reserved a decision on the point: *IMM v The Queen* \[2016\] HCA Trans 8. In the meantime, judges in this State must follow *KMJ*. The reference to a "danger of unfair prejudice" relates to the effect that evidence can have "when the jury are likely to give the evidence more weight than it deserves or when the nature or content of the evidence may inflame the jury or divert the jurors from their task": *Festa v The Queen* (2001) 208 CLR 593, per McHugh J at \[51\]. Prosecutors commonly argue that appropriate directions by the judge to the jury will neutralise or substantially reduce any danger of unfair prejudice. Defence counsel commonly argue that, in the circumstances of the case, that is not likely.

---

### Voir Dire — Common matters: Tendency and coincidence evidence (ss 97, 98, 101(2))
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Evidence Act 2001 (Tas)] [SECTION: ss 97, 98, 101(2)] [CITATION: Evidence Act 2001 (Tas) ss 97-98 - Tendency & Coincidence Evidence Guidance] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: tendency and coincidence evidence considerations on voir dire, sexual cases, notice requirements] [CONCEPTS: tendency evidence, coincidence evidence, tendency notice, significant probative value, s97, s98, s101(2), Lockyer, Forward, PWD, BP]

Covers tendency and coincidence evidence issues commonly argued on voir dire.

### Tendency and coincidence evidence. 

Objections relating to these types of evidence are particularly common in sexual cases involving multiple complainants. The Crown's contentions as to tendency and/or coincidence evidence are restricted by the contents of the tendency notice or coincidence notice that has to be given in writing to the defence: *Evidence Act*, ss 97(1)(a),98(1)(a). Defence counsel commonly argue that evidence from two or more complainants is not cross-admissible. It may be argued that the evidence is not tendency evidence at all, or not coincidence evidence at all: *Evidence Act*, ss 97,98. Or that the evidence does not have "significant probative value": ss 97(1)(b),98(1)(b). Or that the probative value of the evidence does not substantially outweigh some prejudicial effect that it may have on the defendant: s 101(2). Tendency evidence and/or coincidence evidence has "significant probative value" if it has more than mere relevance and is "important "or "of consequence": *R v Lockyer* (1996) 89 A Crim R 457 at 459; *L v Tasmania* (2006) 15 Tas R 381 at \[31\]. For tendency evidence to be admissible under s 97, there does not have to be evidence of striking similarities between events or circumstances, or even similar behaviour on different occasions: *R v Forward* (2009) 273 ALR 286 at \[38\] and \[125\]; *R v PWD* \[2010\] NSWCCA 209 at \[79\]. Generally, the closer and more particular the similarities, the more likely it is that the evidence will have significant probative value: *BP v The Queen* \[2010\] NSWCCA 303 at \[108\].

---

### Voir Dire — Common matters: Admissions (s 90 Evidence Act)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Evidence Act 2001 (Tas)] [SECTION: s 90; s 137 referenced] [CITATION: Evidence Act 2001 (Tas) s 90 - Admissions Admissibility] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: s90 admission unfairness test and interplay with s137] [CONCEPTS: admissions, s90, unfair to defendant, s137, exclusion of evidence]

Covers admissibility of admissions and interaction with unfair prejudice.

### Admissions. 

A judge may refuse to admit evidence of an admission if "having regard to the circumstances in which the admission was made, it would be unfair to a defendant to use the evidence": *Evidence Act*, s 90(b). When s 90 is relied on, defence counsel usually also rely on s 137 (danger of unfair prejudice).

---

### Voir Dire — Common matters: Evidence improperly or illegally obtained (s 138 Evidence Act)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Evidence Act 2001 (Tas)] [SECTION: s 138; s 138(3) referenced] [CITATION: Evidence Act 2001 (Tas) s 138 - Improperly Obtained Evidence] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: s138 balancing test, burden allocations, and matters to consider per s138(3)] [CONCEPTS: s138, improperly obtained evidence, balancing test, burden of proof, Tasmania v Seabourne, Acheson v Hibble, Willis v The Queen, R v Brown]

Covers the s 138 exclusion test, burdens and authorities.

### Evidence improperly or illegally obtained. 

Evidence that was obtained improperly or in contravention of an Australian law, or in consequence of an impropriety or of such a contravention, is not to be admitted "unless the desirability of admitting the evidence outweighs the undesirability of admitting evidence that has been obtained in the way in which the evidence was obtained": *Evidence Act*, s138(1).

The defence bear the burden of proving impropriety or contravention of an Australian law, on the balance of probabilities ***Tasmania v Seabourne \[2010\]*** TASSC 35 per Wood J at \[27\]; ***Acheson v Hibble*** \[2008\] TASSC 42 per Blow J (as his Honour then was) at \[13\]; ***Willis v The Queen*** \[2016\] VSCA 176 at \[104\] and \[110\]-\[117\]

If one of those things is established, the Crown then bears the burden of proving facts warranting the admission of the evidence. When s 138(1) applies, there are eight matters that s138(3) requires the Court to take into account in deciding what is desirable or undesirable. The list is not an exhaustive list. See, for example, *R v Brown* \[2014\] TASSC 18, which concerned non-compliance with a provision of a Commonwealth statute relating to search warrants.

---

### Voir Dire — Common matters: Freshness of memory (s 66(2) Evidence Act)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Evidence Act 2001 (Tas)] [SECTION: s 66(2)] [CITATION: Evidence Act 2001 (Tas) s 66(2) - Freshness of Memory for First-hand Hearsay] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: s66(2) admissibility of first-hand hearsay where memory was fresh] [CONCEPTS: freshness of memory, s66(2), first-hand hearsay, recent complaint evidence, sexual cases, voir dire]

Covers s 66(2) freshness of memory considerations for first-hand hearsay.

### Freshness of memory. 

Under s 66(2) of the *Evidence Act*, first-hand hearsay evidence of an assertion by a

person who is available as a witness is prima facie admissible "if, when the representation was made, the occurrence of the asserted fact was fresh in the memory of the person who made the representation". Questions of freshness of memory therefore sometimes have to be determined on the voir dire, particularly in relation to "recent complaint "evidence in sexual cases.

---

### Voir Dire — Decisions to be made by counsel (timing, scope, oral vs papers, second voir dire)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Evidence Act 2001 (Tas)] [SECTION: s 361A referenced re timing; ss 97-98; s 142; s 138 referenced elsewhere] [CITATION: Voir Dire - Counsel Decisions, Timing and Tactical Considerations (s361A guidance)] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: procedure] [TOPIC: tactical decisions for counsel on whether to seek a voir dire, timing under s361A, scope and basis of objection, oral evidence vs decision on papers, second voir dire] [CONCEPTS: timing of voir dire, s361A, scope of objection, notice, oral evidence, papers, Director of Public Prosecutions v Lynch, second voir dire, P v The Queen]

Covers tactical decision-making points counsel must address when considering and conducting a voir dire.

Decisions to be made by counsel 

There are a number of decisions that need to be made by counsel in relation to a voir dire. *Voir dire or no voir dire?* Counsel should consider the likelihood or otherwise of the objection being successful. It may sometimes be preferable to concentrate on the weakness of the prosecution case, rather than on questions of admissibility.

***Timing of the voir dire*.**

Section 361A should be used for long voir dires in order not to inconvenience a jury. It sometimes happens that questions of admissibility are determined under s 361A by a judge in one sittings and, for convenience, the matter is continued as a second trial before another judge in another sittings. This runs the risk that the second judge might disagree with the first judge's determination -- something that seems not yet ever to have happened. A short voir dire between the empanelment and the opening speech can be appropriate, particularly when the outcome of the voir dire will determine whether or not the prosecutor may rely on a piece of evidence that he she would like to refer to in the opening speech. A voir dire that interrupts the proceedings before the jury can be very inconvenient, but there may occasionally be tactical reasons to take a witness by surprise at that stage. There is a risk that the judge and/or jury might be irritated by an avoidable interruption. If the Crown is taken by surprise and therefore seeks an adjournment, it is extremely likely that the judge will grant the adjournment.

***Scope and basis of the objection.***

The scope and basis of the objection need to be identified in order to define and limit the scope of the voir dire, and in order to identify the case that the other side has to meet. Counsel should identify with precision the evidence that is objected to. Usually the evidence will be set out in the Crown papers, and it will be possible to take the judge to the appropriate pages, paragraphs, sentences, and lines. Counsel should also articulate the basis or bases of the objection, and the sections in the *Evidence Act* that are relied upon. These things should be stated in open court: *Director of Public Prosecutions v Lynch* (2006) 16 Tas R 49 at\[16\].

***Oral evidence or a decision on the papers?***

Oral evidence from one or more witnesses is essential if it is not known what a witness will say about a particular point, or if there are facts that one party needs to establish, or to try to establish. Sometimes it is desirable to call undisputed oral evidence on the voir dire so that the evidence can be explained to the trial judge. If counsel for both sides are agreeable to the trial judge basing his or her decision wholly or partly on the papers, it is desirable to be specific as to what statements, proofs, or parts thereof are relied upon.

***Need for a second voir dire in relation to an objection already determined?***

This situation is unusual, but it can arise when a witness gives evidence before the jury that is not the same as the evidence given by that witness on a voir dire: *P v The Queen* \[2002\] TASSC 61; *Tasmania v L* \[2004\]TASSC 86; *L v Tasmania* (2006) 15 Tas R 381 at \[6\].

---

### Defences (Criminal Responsibility):
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: heading for defences (criminal responsibility)] [CONCEPTS: defences, criminal responsibility, placeholder, heading]

Covers the heading for defences.

# Defences (Criminal Responsibility):

# Doli incapax

Applies to youths aged 10-13. Note section 18 states UNDER 14 yo! Not 14 and under.

### Chunk 3 — 4.1 Statements/admissions made by the child
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: using child admissions to rebut doli incapax] [CONCEPTS: admissions, child interview, rebut doli incapax, Whitty, Crofts, NSW Police Code] [CASE: R (a child) v Whitty (1993) 66 A Crim R 462] [CITATION: Doli incapax - Admissions - 4.1 Statements/admissions made by the child]

Tactical workflow for assessing statements/admissions to rebut doli incapax.

4.1 Statements/admissions made by the child

*An admission made by a child will often be sufficient to rebut* doli incapax*. Thomas Crofts (at 187) argues:*

"It has been established that an important source of information for assessing a child's appreciation of the seriousness of the act is what the child says when interviewed by the police. This type of evidence is preferable in as far as it refers directly to a child's appreciation of the act itself and is not drawn from a general analysis of the behaviour and personality of the child."

The classic Australian case on point is the Victorian case of [R (a child) v Whitty]{.underline} (1993) 66 A Crim R 462. In [Whitty's]{.underline} case a child was arrested for shoplifting and when interviewed by police regarding the offence used the words, '*I stole'* (the goods). It was held that the use of these words was evidence of mischievous discretion. The child's language was interpreted to indicate knowledge that the act of stealing was wrong, perhaps in contrast to the words *'I took'*.

However, before an alleged admission could be used as evidence to rebut the presumption of *doli incapax* the usual questions relating to any alleged admission must be asked. Additionally, if the alleged admission was made, is it indicative of the child's understanding at the time of the offence.

---

### Chunk 4 — (i) Is a child required to make admissions?
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: child's right to silence and police obligations when interviewing under 14s] [CONCEPTS: right to silence, advice to child, record of interview, Code of Practice, police questioning]

Tactical note: what this chunk covers — whether a child must make admissions and police approach.

(i) Is a child required to make admissions?

Despite the additional obligations placed on the prosecution in order to rebut *doli incapax* a child is still entitled to rely on his or her right to silence.

A child under fourteen should be advised of the additional dangers of making a record of interview. If a child elects to make a record of interview the investigating police are likely to ask questions with the specific intention of rebutting *doli incapax*.

The [NSW Police Service 'Code of Practice']{.underline} at page 33 reminds officers:

*"Remember, when interviewing children between 10 and 14 you need to obtain evidence they knew what they were doing was wrong (as opposed to mischievous or naughty)."*

---

### Chunk 5 — (ii) Can otherwise inadmissible admissions be used to rebut doli incapax? (Checklist)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: checklist] [TYPE: checklist] [TOPIC: checklist for admissibility of child admissions rebutting doli incapax] [CONCEPTS: s13 Children Act, legal advice, Evidence Act Part 3.4, exclusion grounds, s281 Criminal Procedure Act] [ACT: Children (Criminal Proceedings) Act 1987] [SECTION: s13] [CITATION: Children (Criminal Proceedings) Act 1987 s13 - Admissions checklist]

Tactical checklist: admissibility issues to verify before relying on an alleged admission to rebut doli incapax.

(ii) Can otherwise inadmissible admissions be used to rebut *doli incapax*?

A child should not be put into any worse position than an adult offender and is entitled to attempt to exclude otherwise inadmissible admissions. A quick checklist includes:

(a) Are the admissions caught by s.13 of the [Children (Criminal Proceedings) Act (1987)?]{.underline}

(b) Did the child receive legal advice? See [R v ME , R v LT and R v CE]{.underline} (Unreported, Supreme Court Common Law Division, 3 October 2002)

(c) Are the admissions admissible under Part 3.4 of the [Evidence Act]{.underline}?

(d) Should the admission be excluded under s.90, s.135, s.137 or s.138?

(e) Is the admission caught by s281 of the [Criminal Procedure Act]{.underline}?

---

### Chunk 6 — (iii) Are the words attributed to the child 'clear beyond all doubt or contradiction'?
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: clarity of admissions and IPH v Chief Constable example] [CONCEPTS: equivocal admissions, mischievous vs seriously wrong, IPH v Chief Constable, interpretation of interview statements] [CASE: IPH v Chief Constable of South Wales [1987] Crim LR 42] [CITATION: Admissions clarity - IPH v Chief Constable example]

Tactical note: this chunk covers the requirement for clarity of alleged admissions and example case.

(iii) Are the words attributed to the child 'clear beyond all doubt or contradiction'?

The alleged admissions must show that the child understood that his actions were seriously wrong and not just naughty. If the admissions are equivocal, or ambiguous, then it can be argued that the prosecution has not successfully rebutted the presumption.

It has been suggested in [IPH v. Chief Constable of South Wales]{.underline} it was suggested that the most appropriate way to determine the *doli incapax* issue is for investigating police to ask the child 'did you appreciate that what you did was seriously wrong?' In the absence of such a direct question, the evidence will require the Court to interpret what was the understanding of the child.

Facts of IPH [v. Chief Constable of South Wales]{.underline} \[1987\] Crim LR 42.

An 11-year-old boy was convicted of criminal damage to a van. The van's windows were smashed, the paintwork was scratched and the van was pushed into a pole. The child was interviewed by police. During the interview the child said, "*Yeah, I knew I would damage the truck by pushing it into the pole*". On appeal the Divisional Court said that the admission proved that the child knew that damage would result from the action. The admission did not prove knowledge that the action was seriously wrong as opposed to mischievous or naughty.

---

### Chunk 7 — (iv) Do the admissions indicate the child's understanding at the time of the alleged offence?
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: annotation] [TYPE: annotation] [TOPIC: timing of admissions and potential tainting by arrest/interrogation processes] [CONCEPTS: contemporaneous understanding, post-offence statements, tainted admissions, appreciation of wrongness, arrested child]

Tactical note: this chunk covers assessing whether admissions reflect understanding at the time of offending.

(iv) Do the admissions indicate the child's understanding at the time of the alleged offence?

The child's intention must be assessed at the time of committing the offence. Any statements given by a child after the offence may have been tainted or affected by the process. It is arguable that since being arrested, taken to a police station and placed in a dock the child has come to an appreciation that he or she has done something wrong. This understanding may not have been consistent with the child's state of mind at the time of the alleged offence.

---
