### Chunk 9 — CASE NOTE: TPL v The State of WA [2025] WASCA 195 — Issue & Prosecution-relied indicia
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: TPL v WA - whether prosecution rebutted presumption of incapacity under s29 Criminal Code (WA)] [CONCEPTS: doli incapax, capacity to know serious wrongness, secrecy, deception, planning, sexual offending] [CASE: TPL v The State of Western Australia [2025] WASCA 195] [ACT: Criminal Code (WA)] [SECTION: s29] [CITATION: TPL v The State of Western Australia [2025] WASCA 195 - Case note Issue & Prosecution indicia]

Tactical summary: what this chunk covers — Issue and prosecution-relied indicia at trial in TPL v WA.

CASE NOTE -- Doli Incapax: Indicia For / Against Capacity

Case

TPL v The State of Western Australia \[2025\] WASCA 195

2025WASCA0195

Court of Appeal (WA)

Issue (relevant to you)

Whether the prosecution rebutted the **presumption of incapacity** under **s 29 Criminal Code (WA)** for conduct committed when the accused was aged **10--14**, by proving beyond reasonable doubt that he had the **capacity to know the conduct was seriously wrong by ordinary adult standards**.

1. Prosecution-relied indicia of capacity (trial level)

The trial judge relied heavily on **circumstances of offending**, treating them as "capacity circumstances". These included:

A. Secrecy / concealment

- Luring complainants away from others (shed, bins, tents)

- Moving behind objects for concealment

- Acting when others were asleep

- Pretending to be asleep when footsteps were heard

B. Ruse / deception

- "Smell my fingers" incident

- Pretending to show something

- Creating games with sexual "rewards"

- Blaming or attempting to shift responsibility if discovered

C. Warnings / threats

- Telling complainants not to tell

- Threatening to scream or get them into trouble

- Using force to compel compliance

D. Sexual knowledge

- Asking "can I put it in"

- Demonstrating masturbation

- Awareness of sexual mechanics

E. Planning

- Repeated offending

- Structured sequences (games, rewards)

- Escalation over time

Trial judge's inference:\
These factors showed foresight, awareness of consequences, and appreciation that the conduct was "seriously wrong".

---

### Chunk 10 — TPL v WA — Why the Court of Appeal rejected those indicia; core principle
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Court of Appeal reasoning that circumstances alone insufficient to rebut doli incapax] [CONCEPTS: secrecy not proof, capacity vs rule awareness, moral understanding, RP v The Queen, BDO v The Queen] [CASE: TPL v The State of Western Australia [2025] WASCA 195] [CITATION: TPL v WA - Court rejection and core principle]

Tactical note: what this chunk covers — Court of Appeal core reasoning rejecting reliance on circumstances.

2. Why the Court of Appeal rejected those indicia as insufficient

The Court's reasoning is extremely important for future cases.

Core principle (re-stated clearly)

Secrecy, force, deception or planning --- even in sexual offending --- do NOT, without more, rebut doli incapax.

The Court repeatedly emphasised that:

- The question is **capacity to know serious moral wrongness**, not:

  - legal wrongness

  - fear of getting in trouble

  - awareness of rules

  - "naughtiness"

  - ability to manipulate

---

### Chunk 11 — TPL v WA — Specific indicia rejected; absence of positive indicia; documentary records; expert evidence; net result; practical takeaway
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: TPL v WA - specifics on why indicia were ambiguous, missing positive indicia, evidentiary lessons and practical takeaways for Tasmanian practice] [CONCEPTS: secrecy ambiguous, use of force, deception not moral, sexual knowledge insufficient, need for positive indicia, developmental evidence, contemporaneous observations] [CASE: TPL v The State of Western Australia [2025] WASCA 195] [CITATION: TPL v WA - Specific indicia and practical takeaway]

Tactical note: what this chunk covers — specifics of indicia weakness and recommended evidence.

3. Specific indicia the Court said were ambiguous or weak

A. Secrecy ≠ moral understanding

The Court held that concealment may show:

- fear of punishment

- awareness of rules

- desire to avoid trouble

But **does not necessarily show appreciation of serious moral wrongness**.

This follows **RP v The Queen** and **BDO v The Queen**.

B. Use of force

The Court expressly rejected the idea that:

- using force against a younger child

- overcoming resistance

- causing distress

necessarily implies understanding of moral autonomy or serious wrongdoing.

Children may:

- know someone doesn't want something

- still not understand *why* it is morally wrong

C. Ruses and deception

Deception may show:

- cognitive functioning

- problem-solving ability

But **does not equate to moral development**.

The Court cautioned against equating "cleverness" with capacity.

D. Sexual knowledge

Awareness of sexual mechanics or terminology:

- does not prove appreciation of moral wrongness

- does not prove capacity to evaluate harm or ethical boundaries

This was particularly important given the **familial context**.

4. Absence of *positive* indicia of capacity (decisive)

What ultimately sank the prosecution case was **what was missing**, not what was present.

The Court emphasised the lack of:

- contemporaneous carers

- teachers

- counsellors

- psychologists

- caregivers

- evidence of moral instruction

- evidence of internalised moral framework

No witness could say:

"At that time, this child understood that this conduct was seriously wrong by adult moral standards."

5. Documentary records -- expressly limited probative value

School records

- Poor academic performance

- Behavioural issues

- Some sexualised language

The Court held:

- These **do not establish moral capacity**

- Lack of intellectual disability ≠ capacity

- "Intelligent" was a mischaracterisation

Child protection records

- Demonstrated chaos, neglect, trauma

- No evidence of moral guidance or internalised norms

- Actually **supported developmental delay**

6. Expert evidence -- key lesson

Although the trial judge rejected the defence psychologist's opinion, the Court made this critical point:

**Rejecting defence expert evidence does not supply affirmative proof of capacity.**

Once the expert was set aside:

- the State had nothing but circumstances of offending

- that was insufficient as a matter of law

7. Net result -- practical indicia framework

❌ Insufficient indicia (without more)

- Secrecy

- Deception

- Ruse

- Force

- Sexual knowledge

- Repetition

- Fear of getting caught

- Awareness of rules

✅ What would likely be required (not present here)

- Evidence of moral instruction

- Evidence of ethical reasoning

- Evidence of internalised norms

- Contemporaneous observations of moral understanding

- Evidence child distinguished "seriously wrong" from "naughty"

- Evidence child could appreciate harm in an adult moral sense

8. Practical takeaway for you (Tas context)

This case is **very strong authority** for the proposition that:

**You cannot rebut doli incapax by offence circumstances alone, even where the conduct is extremely serious.**

For prosecutors:

- This sharply limits reliance on "capacity circumstances"

- Requires early thinking about **developmental evidence**

For defence:

- This is a blueprint for attacking inferential reasoning

- Especially where there is trauma, neglect, or instability

---

### Chunk 12 — Defences: Illegally or improperly obtained s138 — principle and cases
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: legal doctrine] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: s138 Evidence Act and cases on exclusion for impropriety/illegality] [CONCEPTS: s138 Evidence Act, improper evidence, Tasmania v Bott, Bunning v Cross, Reg v Ireland, discretion to exclude] [CASE: Tasmania v Bott [2015] TASSC 13] [CASE: Bunning v Cross [1978] HCA 22] [CASE: Reg v Ireland [1970] HCA 21] [CITATION: Illegally or improperly obtained evidence - s138 principles and case law]

Tactical note: what this chunk covers — evidentiary exclusion principles and leading authority.

Defences:

Illegally or improperly obtained s138:

*Tasmania v Bott* \[2015\] TASSC 13

17\. The accused bears the onus of establishing that the evidence was obtained improperly or illegally. If the onus is discharged, it is for the Crown to establish that the desirability of admitting the evidence outweighs the undesirability of admitting it: *R v Salem *[[(1997) 96 A Crim R 421.]{.underline}](http://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/LawCite?cit=%281997%29%2096%20A%20Crim%20R%20421)

***Bunning v Cross* \[1978\] HCA 22; (1978) 141 CLR 54**

Case cites Chief Justice in Reg. v. Ireland [\[1970\] HCA 21](http://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/viewdoc/au/cases/cth/HCA/1970/21.html); (1970) 126 CLR. The Chief Justice there said (1970) 126 CLR at p 335 :\
\
*\"Whenever such unlawfulness or unfairness appears, the judge has a discretion to reject the evidence. He must consider its exercise. In the exercise of it, the competing public requirements must be considered and weighed against each other. On the one hand there is the public need to bring to conviction those who commit criminal offences. On the other hand is the public interest in the protection of the individual from unlawful and unfair treatment. Convictions obtained by the aid of unlawful or unfair acts may be obtained at too high a price. Hence the judicial discretion.\"*

*Ireland* \[1970\] HCA 21; (1970) 126 CLR 321

\- Evidence of relevant facts ascertained by unlawful or unfair acts is not for that reason

alone inadmissible

\- The judge will weigh up the value of the evidence against the unlawfulness of the method of obtaining it and decide admissibility

That statement represents the law in Australia; it was concurred in by all other members of the Court in Reg. v. Ireland and has since been applied in a number of Australian cases. Its concluding words echo the sentiments expressed long ago by Knight Bruce V.C. when, in a different yet relevant context, he said, (Pearse v. Pearse [\[1846\] EngR 1195](http://www.worldlii.org/int/cases/EngR/1846/1195.html); (1846) 1 De G & Sm 12 (63 ER 950, at p 957) ):

##### Indicia of admissibility or not

*Bunning v Cross* \[1978\] HCA 22; (1978) 141 CLR 54

\- was there a deliberate disregard of the law by police

\- does the nature of illegality affect the truthfulness of the evidence

\- the ease of which the law might have been complied with in obtaining the evidence

\- seriousness of offence **V** unlawful conduct - does the legislation deliberately intend to narrowly restrict Police powers

**See also s 138.** Evidence Act -  **Discretion to exclude evidence improperly or illegally obtained**

---

### Chunk 13 — Evidence Act 2001 (Tas) s138 (statutory text)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Evidence Act 2001 (Tas)] [SECTION: 138] [CATEGORY: legal doctrine] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: Evidence Act 2001 (Tas) s138 full text on exclusion of improperly/illegally obtained evidence] [CONCEPTS: Evidence Act 2001 (Tas), s138, improperly obtained evidence, exclusion discretion, probative value] [CITATION: Evidence Act 2001 (Tas) s138 - statutory text]

Tactical note: what this chunk covers — full statutory text for s138 Evidence Act 2001 (Tas).

138.   Discretion to exclude evidence improperly or illegally obtained

(1)  Evidence that was obtained --

(a) improperly or in contravention of an Australian law; or

(b) in consequence of an impropriety or of a contravention of an Australian law --

is not to be admitted unless the desirability of admitting the evidence outweighs the undesirability of admitting evidence that has been obtained in the way in which the evidence was obtained.

(2)  Without limiting [subsection (1)](https://www.legislation.tas.gov.au/view/html/inforce/current/act-2001-076?query=(((PrintType%3D%22act.reprint%22+AND+Amending%3C%3E%22pure%22+AND+PitValid%3D%40pointInTime(20170818000000))+OR+(PrintType%3D%22act.reprint%22+AND+Amending%3D%22pure%22+AND+PitValid%3D%40pointInTime(20170818000000))+OR+(PrintType%3D%22reprint%22+AND+Amending%3C%3E%22pure%22+AND+PitValid%3D%40pointInTime(20170818000000))+OR+(PrintType%3D%22reprint%22+AND+Amending%3D%22pure%22+AND+PitValid%3D%40pointInTime(20170818000000))))+AND+(Title%3D(%22Evidence%22+AND+%22Act%22+AND+%222001%22))&dQuery=Document+Types%3D%22%3Cspan+class%3D%27dq-highlight%27%3EActs%3C%2Fspan%3E%2C+%3Cspan+class%3D%27dq-highlight%27%3EAmending+Acts%3C%2Fspan%3E%2C+%3Cspan+class%3D%27dq-highlight%27%3ESRs%3C%2Fspan%3E%2C+%3Cspan+class%3D%27dq-highlight%27%3EAmending+SRs%3C%2Fspan%3E%22%2C+Search+In%3D%22%3Cspan+class%3D%27dq-highlight%27%3ETitle%3C%2Fspan%3E%22%2C+Where+All+Words%3D%22%3Cspan+class%3D%27dq-highlight%27%3EEvidence+Act+2001%22%3C%2Fspan%3E+%2C+Point+In+Time%3D%22%3Cspan+class%3D%27dq-highlight%27%3E18%2F08%2F2017%3C%2Fspan%3E%22#GS138@Gs1@EN) , evidence of an admission that was made during or in consequence of questioning, and evidence obtained in consequence of the admission, is taken to have been obtained improperly if the person conducting the questioning --

(a) did, or omitted to do, an act in the course of the questioning even though he or she knew or ought reasonably to have known that the act or omission was likely to impair substantially the ability of the person being questioned to respond rationally to the questioning; or

(b) made a false statement in the course of the questioning even though he or she knew or ought reasonably to have known that the statement was false and that making the false statement was likely to cause the person who was being questioned to make an admission.

(3)  Without limiting the matters that the court may take into account under [subsection (1)](https://www.legislation.tas.gov.au/view/html/inforce/current/act-2001-076?query=(((PrintType%3D%22act.reprint%22+AND+Amending%3C%3E%22pure%22+AND+PitValid%3D%40pointInTime(20170818000000))+OR+(PrintType%3D%22act.reprint%22+AND+Amending%3D%22pure%22+AND+PitValid%3D%40pointInTime(20170818000000))+OR+(PrintType%3D%22reprint%22+AND+Amending%3C%3E%22pure%22+AND+PitValid%3D%40pointInTime(20170818000000))+OR+(PrintType%3D%22reprint%22+AND+Amending%3D%22pure%22+AND+PitValid%3D%40pointInTime(20170818000000))))+AND+(Title%3D(%22Evidence%22+AND+%22Act%22+AND+%222001%22))&dQuery=Document+Types%3D%22%3Cspan+class%3D%27dq-highlight%27%3EActs%3C%2Fspan%3E%2C+%3Cspan+class%3D%27dq-highlight%27%3EAmending+Acts%3C%2Fspan%3E%2C+%3Cspan+class%3D%27dq-highlight%27%3ESRs%3C%2Fspan%3E%2C+%3Cspan+class%3D%27dq-highlight%27%3EAmending+SRs%3C%2Fspan%3E%22%2C+Search+In%3D%22%3Cspan+class%3D%27dq-highlight%27%3ETitle%3C%2Fspan%3E%22%2C+Where+All+Words%3D%22%3Cspan+class%3D%27dq-highlight%27%3EEvidence+Act+2001%22%3C%2Fspan%3E+%2C+Point+In+Time%3D%22%3Cspan+class%3D%27dq-highlight%27%3E18%2F08%2F2017%3C%2Fspan%3E%22#GS138@Gs1@EN) , it is to take into account --

(a) the probative value of the evidence; and

(b) the importance of the evidence in the proceeding; and

(c) the nature of the relevant offence, cause of action or defence and the nature of the subject matter of the proceeding; and

(d) the gravity of the impropriety or contravention; and

(e) whether the impropriety or contravention was deliberate or reckless; and

(f) whether the impropriety or contravention was contrary to or inconsistent with a right of a person recognised by the International Covenant on Civil and Political Rights; and

(g) whether any other proceeding, whether or not in a court, has been or is likely to be taken in relation to the impropriety or contravention; and

(h) the difficulty, if any, of obtaining the evidence without impropriety or contravention of an Australian law.

---

### Chunk 14 — Impropriety (FW: Tasmania v Thompson [2022] TASSC 27; Wilkie v Barnaba [2021] TASSC 21)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: what amounts to impropriety under s138; Ridgeway and Robinson principles applied locally] [CONCEPTS: impropriety test, minimum standards of police conduct, Ridgeway, Robinson, Kadir, Wilkie v Barnaba] [CASE: Wilkie v Barnaba [2021] TASSC 21] [CASE: Tasmania v Thompson [2022] TASSC 27] [CITATION: Impropriety under s138 - Tasmania v Thompson & Wilkie v Barnaba]

Tactical note: what this chunk covers — judicial framing of "impropriety" under s138 in Tasmanian authorities.

#### impropriety

**FW: Tasmania v Thompson \[2022\] TASSC 27 (28 April 2022)**

In *Wilkie v Barnaba *[\[2021\] TASSC 21](http://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/viewdoc/au/cases/tas/TASSC/2021/21.html), Wood J observed that what amounts to improper conduct under the section is assessed in accordance with the common law principles explained by the High Court in *Ridgeway v The Queen* [\[1995\] 184 CLR 19.](http://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/LawCite?cit=%5b1995%5d%20184%20CLR%2019) Her Honour noted that this was so held by the New South Wales Court of Criminal Appeal in *Robinson v Woolworths Ltd *[\[2005\] NSWCCA 426](http://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/viewdoc/au/cases/nsw/NSWCCA/2005/426.html), [158 A Crim R 546](http://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/LawCite?cit=158%20A%20Crim%20R%20546), and endorsed by the High Court in *Kadir v The Queen *[\[2020\] HCA 1](http://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/LawCite?cit=%5b2020%5d%20HCA%201), [267 CLR 109.](http://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/LawCite?cit=267%20CLR%20109) Her Honour encapsulated those principles as follows:

\"Improper conduct is \'not criminal but which is quite inconsistent with the minimum standards which a society such as ours should expect and require of those entrusted with powers of law enforcement\': Ridgeway per Mason CJ, Deane and Dawson JJ at 36; Robinson at \[102\]. The stage of impropriety will be reached in the case of conduct which is \'clearly inconsistent\' with minimum standards of acceptable police conduct in all the circumstances: Robinson at \[23\].

Under s 138, impropriety is not about identifying good policing, but identifying the minimum standards we should expect of police officers. Impropriety cannot be shown by demonstrating that the police search fell short of best practice.\"

---

### Chunk 15 — Police impropriety (section 138) — Barnes v Anderson [2020] TASMC 7
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Barnes v Anderson - blood test and police conduct under s138] [CONCEPTS: blood test, police misleading advice, unfairness, exclusion under s138, breath vs blood evidence] [CASE: Barnes v Anderson [2020] TASMC 7] [CITATION: Barnes v Anderson [2020] TASMC 7 - Police impropriety under s138]

Tactical note: what this chunk covers — example where police conduct led to exclusion.

***Barnes v Anderson* \[2020\] TASMC 7 (3 February 2020)**

Blood test

Cop said that blood test would possibly lead to higher reading -- court accepted that this would have impacted free decision of def to elect blood after breathalyser had positive result. Even though was clearly over on breath, may possibly have fallen below limit had blood been take. Court critical of cops giving legal type advice and excluded the breath results and dismissed the charge.

---

### Chunk 16 — Examples of success under s138 (case list)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Tasmanian cases where s138 led to exclusion or relevant outcomes] [CONCEPTS: s138 success cases, McLean v Wilkie, Tasmania v Hodges, Wilkie v Barnaba, Orr v Johnston, Moore v Knowles] [CASE: McLean v Wilkie [2022] TASSC 13] [CASE: Tasmania v Hodges [2022] TASSC 45] [CASE: Wilkie v Barnaba [2021] TASSC 21] [CASE: Orr v Johnston [2014] TASMC 5] [CASE: Moore v Knowles [2017] TASMC 12] [CITATION: s138 - Tas case examples]

Tactical note: what this chunk covers — brief notes on successful s138 outcomes.

Example of success under 138

**McLean v Wilkie \[2022\] TASSC 13**

Didn't state "requirement" still allowed under s. 138.

**Tasmania v Hodges \[2022\] TASSC 45 (26 July 2022)**

Illegal entry and search of motel room and def finding drugs

- High probative value -- was entire case

- Trafficking is a serious offence

- Officers were beat cops -- not experienced detectives who should have know better.

- It was a motel room -- not a private residence.

- Once cops realized they fucked up they sought advice and a warrant.

**Wilkie v Barnaba \[2021\] TASSC 21 **

Def spoke poor English and did not understand that electing blood wouldn't see him liable for 2 sperate drink drive charges, so decided not to. At summary hearing it was excluded as unfair. SC said no, it was fair. Not duty to rovide legal advice. Evidence of breath test should be admitted.

### Chunk 17 — Entrapment — Ridgeway v R [1995] HCA 66 excerpt
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: entrapment doctrine and High Court stance] [CONCEPTS: entrapment, substantive defence, Ridgeway, discretionary exclusion, unlawful police conduct] [CASE: Ridgeway v R [1995] HCA 66] [CITATION: Ridgeway v R [1995] HCA 66 - entrapment]

Tactical note: what this chunk covers — High Court positions on entrapment and discretionary exclusion.

Basically, the HCA says no!

***Ridgeway v R* \[1995\] HCA 66**

MASON CJ, DEANE AND DAWSON JJ

A defence of entrapment?

10\. The question whether the common law of this country recognizes a substantive defence of \"entrapment\" to a charge of a criminal offence has not been directly addressed in any case in this Court. It has, however, been considered on a number of occasions by State Supreme Courts which have consistently and emphatically answered it in the negative (2). Similarly, the courts of England (3), Canada (4) and New Zealand (5) have denied the existence of such a substantive common law defence. The decisions to that effect are not surprising since it is a central thesis of our criminal law that a person who voluntarily and with the necessary intent commits all the objective elements of a criminal offence is guilty of that offence regardless of whether he or she was induced to act by another, whether private citizen or law enforcement officer. And that is so even if the inducement involved criminal conduct on the part of a law enforcement officer. Where that is so, the result is not that the induced person is not guilty of the crime. It is that the law enforcement officer is also guilty of an offence.

Discretionary Exclusion of evidence

15\. At least since Bunning v. Cross (22), it has been \"the settled law in this country\" (23) that a trial judge has a discretion to exclude prosecution evidence on public policy grounds in circumstances where it has been obtained by unlawful conduct on the part of the police. That discretion is distinct from the discretion to exclude evidence of a confessional statement on the grounds that its reception would be unfair to the accused (24). The discretion extends to the exclusion of both \"real\" (or non-confessional) evidence (25) and confessional evidence (26). As Barwick CJ pointed out in Reg. v. Ireland (27), in a judgment with which the other four members of the Court agreed (28), the rationale of the discretion is that convictions obtained by means of unlawful conduct \"may be obtained at too high a price\". In its exercise, a trial judge must engage in a balancing process to resolve \"the apparent conflict between the desirable goal of bringing to conviction the wrongdoer and the undesirable effect of curial approval, or even encouragement, being given to the unlawful conduct of those whose task it is to enforce the law\" (29). The basis in principle of the discretion lies in the inherent or implied powers of our courts to protect the integrity of their processes. In cases where it is exercised to exclude evidence on public policy grounds, it is because, in all the circumstances of the particular case, applicable considerations of \"high public policy\" (30) relating to the administration of criminal justice outweigh the legitimate public interest in the conviction of the guilty.

---

### Chunk 18 — Tasmanian authority on entrapment — R v Osborne [2003] TASSC 17
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CATEGORY: case authority] [TYPE: case authority] [TOPIC: Tasmanian application of Ridgeway principles re entrapment] [CONCEPTS: entrapment Tas, R v Osborne, no substantive defence, exclusion of evidence] [CASE: R v Osborne [2003] TASSC 17] [CITATION: R v Osborne [2003] TASSC 17 - Tasmanian authority on entrapment]

Tactical note: what this chunk covers — how Ridgeway was applied in Tasmania.

#####  Tasmanian Authority:

*R v Osborne \[2003\] TASSC 17*

21 The decision in *Ridgeway* states three general principles, namely that:

(1) entrapment *per se* does not afford a substantive defence to criminal conduct;

(2) evidence obtained by means of \"entrapment\" procedures may be excluded where the commission of the crime was itself brought about by the conduct of officers of the state;

(3) evidence obtained during the course of \"entrapment\" procedures might be excluded when those procedures were themselves contrary to law or tainted by impropriety.

### No common-law right to a speedy trial — Lillico v McKenna (1995)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [CASE: Lillico v McKenna (1995) 5 Tas R 147] [TOPIC: speedy trial vs fair trial] [CONCEPTS: speedy trial, fair trial, Jago v District Court, delay prejudice, balancing interests]

The rule is that there is no standalone common-law right to a speedy trial; the fundamental right is to a fair trial, and delay is relevant only insofar as it renders a fair trial impossible. In Lillico v McKenna (1995) 5 Tas R 147 counsel relied on Aboud v Attorney-General (NSW) but the court observed that the High Court in Jago v District Court of New South Wales (1989) 168 CLR 23 rejected a separate common-law right to a speedy trial. Delay may be relevant if it produces prejudice that undermines fairness, but delay alone does not automatically entitle a defendant to dismissal. The court must assess whether the passage of time has so impaired the ability to obtain a fair trial (for example by loss of evidence or witness memory) that continued proceedings are unjust. This chunk captures the principle applied in Lillico that a remedy of dismissal for delay is extreme and appropriate only in limited circumstances where fairness cannot be restored.

---

### Test of unfairness from delay — balancing interests (Jago principle)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [CASE: Jago v District Court of New South Wales (1989) 168 CLR 23] [TOPIC: unfairness test for delay] [CONCEPTS: unfairness test, balancing test, community interest, defendant prejudice, remedy of dismissal]

The rule for assessing unfairness from delay is a balancing process between the interests of the accused and the interests of the community in prosecuting offences. Jago v District Court of New South Wales (1989) 168 CLR 23 explains that courts must weigh the prejudice caused to the accused (loss of memory, loss of evidence, impaired defence) against the public interest in bringing offenders to trial. Dismissal of charges because a subsequent trial would be unfair is an extreme remedy reserved for circumstances where prejudice cannot reasonably be ameliorated. Factors bearing on the balance include the length of delay, reasons for delay, the nature of prejudice to the accused, and whether the accused caused or contributed to delay (for example by evading process). The ultimate question is whether a fair trial remains possible.

---

### Insanity: diagnosis alone is insufficient — Tasmania v Scott [2025] TASSC 53
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [CASE: Tasmania v Scott [2025] TASSC 53] [TOPIC: insanity, mental disease, criminal responsibility] [CONCEPTS: insanity test, mental disease, Criminal responsibility, Radford, Falconer, balance of probabilities, Hitchens]

The rule is that the mere diagnosis of a mental illness does not automatically excuse criminal responsibility; additional statutory or common-law criteria must be satisfied before the defence of insanity applies. In Tasmania v Scott [2025] TASSC 53 the court held that bipolar disorder may constitute a "mental disease" within the meaning of the relevant provision (identified in the source as s 16), but emphasised that existence of disease alone does not establish non-responsibility. The court followed the approach in R v Radford (1985) 42 SASR 266 and R v Falconer (1990) 171 CLR 30, and applied the traditional requirement that the accused affirmatively prove, on the balance of probabilities, that the disease produced the particular cognitive or volitional defects required by law. The legal test requires evidence not only of diagnosis but that the disease produced the legally prescribed consequences (for example incapacity to know the nature or quality of the act, or inability to control conduct), as elaborated in precedent.

---

### Hitchens v The Queen (1962) — burden and test for insanity (authority)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: Hitchens v The Queen [1962] TASStRp 6; [1962] Tas SR 35] [TOPIC: burden of proof for insanity] [CONCEPTS: burden on accused, balance of probabilities, mental disease insufficient, legal tests, criminal responsibility]

The principle confirmed in Hitchens v The Queen is that the accused bears the affirmative burden of establishing insanity on the balance of probabilities, and that mere existence of a mental disease is insufficient to acquit. The Court of Criminal Appeal (Burbury CJ, Crisp and Cox JJ) explained that it is not enough to show that the crime would not have occurred but for the disease; the accused must establish the additional criteria prescribed by law as tests of criminal responsibility. The decision emphasises that proof must link the diagnosed disease to the specific legal consequences (e.g. incapacity to understand the nature or wrongness of the act) required to displace criminal responsibility.

---

[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: statutory definition] [TOPIC: statutory limbs of insanity — s 16(1)(a)(ii) and s 16(10)(b)] [CONCEPTS: s16, statutory limbs, insanity elements, mental impairment, statutory interpretation]

The source identifies particular statutory limbs relevant to the insanity/mental impairment inquiry — namely "s 16(1)(a)(ii)" and "s 16(10)(b)" — and indicates Tasmania v Scott [2025] TASSC 53 contains a critical examination of those provisions. The source text does not specify the Act containing those subsection references. Because the source part does not provide the full Act name or citation for s 16, this chunk records the presence of those statutory references and flags that the exact legislative source must be confirmed before applying them. (Marked [REVIEW] because the Act containing s 16 is not specified in the uploaded text.)

### Mental impairment (insanity) procedure and post-verdict reports — Bonde v Weate [2017] TASMC 11
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act] [TYPE: procedural rule] [CASE: Bonde v Weate [2017] TASMC 11] [TOPIC: post-verdict procedure for not-guilty by reason of mental impairment] [CONCEPTS: mental impairment procedure, s33 report, s34 report, forensic reports, dispositions, custody recommendations]

The rule is that where a verdict of not guilty by reason of mental impairment is made, statutory procedural reports under the Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act are required to inform dispositional decisions. In Bonde v Weate [2017] TASMC 11 the source indicates the need for s.33 and s.34 reports under the Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act to be prepared and considered. These statutory reports typically assess the accused's mental condition, risk to the community, treatment needs, and recommend custody, supervision, or treatment dispositions. The precise content and timing of s.33 and s.34 reports are governed by the Act; practitioners should consult the Act for detailed obligations and report format. (The source provided the Act name but did not supply a legislative citation.)

---

### Automatism defence — overview and threshold (Wilson v Judges [2025] TASSC 10)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [CASE: Wilson v Judges [2025] TASSC 10] [TOPIC: automatism, voluntariness, onus] [CONCEPTS: automatism, voluntariness presumption, medical evidence, onus on prosecution, self-induced intoxication, head injury, reasonable possibility]

The rule is that the defence of automatism has a high evidentiary threshold; the presumption that actions of an apparently conscious person are voluntary can be displaced by credible medical evidence raising a reasonable possibility of non-voluntariness, after which the prosecution must exclude that possibility beyond reasonable doubt. In Wilson v Judges [2025] TASSC 10 the court affirmed that while voluntariness and intentionality are presumed, opinion medical evidence that points to an alternative causal explanation (for example concussion from a head injury) can raise a reasonable possibility that the defendant's actions were not voluntary. Once such a reasonable possibility is raised, the prosecution bears the onus of disproving it (for example by calling contrary medical evidence or demonstrating that the alternative explanation is implausible), and failure to do so may leave automatism available. The source emphasises that the defence need not prove the precise underlying circumstances beyond raising a reasonable possibility.

---

### R v Falconer (1990 HCA) — automatism, insanity distinction and onus (authority)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: R v Falconer [1990] HCA 49; 171 CLR 30] [TOPIC: automatism versus insanity, onus of proof] [CONCEPTS: non-insane automatism, insanity, transient condition, causation, onus, voluntary act presumption]

The authoritative principle from R v Falconer is that non-insane automatism can, in appropriate circumstances, provide an acquittal, but where the automatism is caused by a mental disease it will amount to insanity rather than non-insane automatism. Falconer explains that to amount to non-insane automatism the malfunction must generally be transient, attributable to external physical or psychological trauma, and not prone to recurrence; otherwise the condition falls within insanity frameworks. Falconer also clarifies that while the ultimate legal onus of proving criminal culpability remains with the prosecution, the prosecution may rely on the factual presumption that an apparently conscious actor acted voluntarily unless there is credible evidence to the contrary.

---

### Presumption of voluntary action and evidentiary burden — Morrison v Bonde [2016] TASSC 7
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [CASE: Morrison v Bonde [2016] TASSC 7] [TOPIC: presumption of voluntariness, evidentiary displacement] [CONCEPTS: presumption of voluntariness, ability to control actions, prosecution onus, reasonable grounds to displace, Falconer citation]

The rule is that acts of an apparently conscious person are presumed to be willed or voluntary, and this presumption is an inference of fact that will be displaced only where there are good grounds to believe the actor was unable to control his actions. Morrison v Bonde [2016] TASSC 7 (citing R v Falconer) explains that the prosecution retains the ultimate onus to prove voluntariness beyond reasonable doubt, but it may discharge that onus by relying on the ordinary inference of voluntariness unless the defence adduces credible evidence raising a reasonable possibility of involuntariness (for example expert medical evidence). The existence of grounds for believing the defendant could not control his actions is typically necessary before the presumption will be refused.

---

### Automatism established for sleepwalking — Lusted v Kingston [2016] TASMC 1
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: Lusted v Kingston [2016] TASMC 1] [TOPIC: sleepwalking automatism] [CONCEPTS: sleepwalking, automatism defence, expert medical evidence, non-insane automatism, voluntariness displaced]

The principle from Lusted v Kingston is that non-insane automatism can be established where credible medical evidence supports that the defendant acted while sleepwalking, thereby lacking voluntary control. In Lusted the defence relied heavily on medical evidence of sleep disorder and respiratory factors from sleep experts; the court accepted that evidence as sufficient to displace the presumption of voluntariness. This authority demonstrates that carefully supported expert evidence can make a non-insane automatism defence viable, particularly in cases of parasomnia or sleepwalking with medical corroboration.

---

### Lack of memory does not displace presumption of voluntariness — Bonde v Morrison [2015] TASMC 9
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [CASE: Bonde v Morrison [2015] TASMC 9] [TOPIC: memory loss, voluntariness, reasonable possibility standard] [CONCEPTS: lack of memory, voluntariness presumption, reasonable possibility, Falconer, evidentiary threshold]

The rule is that an accused's inability to remember events does not by itself negate the presumption that the acts were voluntary and intentional; credible evidence must raise a reasonable possibility of involuntariness. Bonde v Morrison [2015] TASMC 9 reiterates that mere lack of recollection is distinct from evidence that the actor lacked volitional control. R v Falconer establishes that the presumption of voluntariness will only be displaced if there are grounds to believe the actor was unable to control his actions; without such evidence the jury/magistrate is entitled to infer voluntariness despite the defendant's memory loss.

---

### Unconsciousness and indicia of awareness — Bonde v Morrison [2015] TASMC 9
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [CASE: Bonde v Morrison [2015] TASMC 9] [TOPIC: unconsciousness, indicia of consciousness] [CONCEPTS: unconsciousness, Glasgow Coma Scale, contextual speech, awareness, voluntary acts]

The rule is that claims of unconsciousness will fail where there are indicia of cognitive awareness inconsistent with unconsciousness. In Bonde v Morrison [2015] TASMC 9 the defence's claim of unconsciousness was rejected because evidence showed the defendant talking coherently, responding on the Glasgow Coma Scale, and uttering context-dependent phrases (for example asserting refusal of medical treatment) that indicated awareness and purposeful action. Such evidentiary signs permit an inference of voluntariness and defeat an unconsciousness claim.

---

### de minimis non curat lex and criminal proceedings — Harrison v Moore [2018] TASSC 53
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [CASE: Harrison v Moore [2018] TASSC 53] [TOPIC: de minimis doctrine, criminal law inapplicability] [CONCEPTS: de minimis, triviality doctrine, civil law, criminal proceedings, dismissal of trivial matters]

The rule is that the civil-law maxim de minimis non curat lex ("law is not concerned with trifles") does not apply to criminal proceedings in the sense of permitting dismissal of offences as too trivial. In Harrison v Moore [2018] TASSC 53 the Tasmanian Supreme Court affirmed that criminal law is not constrained by de minimis in the same way as civil remedies; triviality alone will not automatically bar prosecution. Courts retain discretion, but the maxim is not a substantive defence to criminal charges.

---

### Self-defence applies to summary and non-Code matters — Tasmania v Milligan [2021] TASSC 59
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [CASE: Tasmania v Milligan [2021] TASSC 59] [TOPIC: scope of self-defence] [CONCEPTS: self-defence scope, summary matters, Code principles, Blow CJ, analogous offences]

The rule is that self-defence principles are not confined to Code or "like offences" and can apply in summary or non-Code proceedings. Tasmania v Milligan [2021] TASSC 59 (para 10) records Blow CJ's view that self-defence can be raised in summary hearings and in non-Code contexts; the underlying legal principles governing justification do not vanish merely because an offence is prosecuted summarily or under a different statutory regime. Practitioners should therefore consider self-defence issues across procedural contexts where the facts support it.

---

### Self-defence must be raised as a reasonable possibility — Irons v Moore [2019] TASSC 22
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [CASE: Irons v Moore [2019] TASSC 22] [TOPIC: raising self-defence, reasonable possibility threshold] [CONCEPTS: raise as reasonable possibility, onus on prosecution, Viro, Walsh, Hindrum, summary hearing]

The rule is that self-defence will only be left to the jury or considered by a magistrate where it is raised as a reasonable possibility on the evidence and the accused raises the question. Irons v Moore [2019] TASSC 22 reiterates established authorities (Viro v The Queen (1978); Walsh v The Queen [1993] TASSC 91) that the accused must show a reasonable possibility of self-defence; if so, the prosecution must exclude that possibility beyond reasonable doubt. The same standard applies in summary hearings: magistrates are not required to consider self-defence unless it is reasonably raised (Hindrum v Lane [2014] TASFC 5).

---

### Evidentiary onus where exculpatory facts lie solely within accused's knowledge — Irons v Moore [2019] & Weissensteiner principle
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: evidentiary rule] [CASE: Irons v Moore [2019] TASSC 22] [TOPIC: evidentiary onus, hypotheses consistent with innocence] [CONCEPTS: evidentiary onus, Weissensteiner, hypotheses consistent with innocence, accused-only knowledge, circumstantial cases]

The rule is that in circumstantial cases, hypotheses consistent with innocence may cease to be rational in the absence of evidence to support them when that evidence, if it exists, must be within the accused's knowledge. Irons v Moore [2019] TASSC 22 invoked the Weissensteiner v The Queen (1993) 178 CLR 217 principle: if the only person who can provide evidence explaining or contradicting the prosecution case is the accused, there is an evidentiary onus on the accused to provide that explanation; failure to do so can render self-defence not a reasonable possibility. The court applied this to a case where the complainant could not recall events and the accused offered no explanatory evidence.

---

### Raising self-defence and the prosecution onus once raised
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: evidentiary rule] [TOPIC: onus once self-defence raised] [CONCEPTS: prosecution onus, reasonable doubt, leave to jury, Walsh, Hindrum, Viro, summary proceedings]

The rule is that once self-defence is raised as a reasonable possibility on the evidence, the prosecution must negative that possibility beyond reasonable doubt. Authorities such as Walsh v The Queen [1993] TASSC 91 and Viro v The Queen (1978) establish that a trial judge (or magistrate in summary jurisdiction per Hindrum v Lane [2014] TASFC 5) must leave self-defence to the fact‑finder where it is reasonably raised, and thereafter the prosecution bears the burden of disproving the defence beyond reasonable doubt.

---

### Pre-emptive self-defence — Wright v Tasmania [2005] TASSC 113
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [CASE: Wright v Tasmania [2005] TASSC 113; BC200509811] [TOPIC: pre-emptive force, imminence, Beckford] [CONCEPTS: pre-emptive self-defence, imminence, Beckford v R, R v Lawrie, reasonable belief]

The rule is that a person who reasonably believes they are about to be attacked may use pre-emptive force and is not always required to wait for the assailant to strike first. Wright v Tasmania [2005] TASSC 113 (Blow J) affirms that circumstances may justify pre-emptive force; authorities such as Beckford v R and R v Lawrie support the proposition that the imminence of perceived danger and reasonableness of belief determine whether pre-emptive force is lawful. The defendant's belief must be genuinely held and assessed in its context.

---

### Original aggressor generally cannot claim self-defence — Viro v The Queen (1978)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: case authority] [CASE: Viro v The Queen (1978) 141 CLR 88] [TOPIC: aggressor and self-defence] [CONCEPTS: original aggressor, lawful force, retreat, breaking off attack, transmutation into self-defence]

The principle in Viro v The Queen is that an original aggressor cannot claim self-defence against lawful defensive force used by the victim, and only in narrow circumstances (for example where the aggressor clearly broke off his attack and thereafter reasonably believed he was still in danger) can self-defence become available. Gibbs J explained the difficulty in seeing how an original violent attack can instantly transmute into lawful self‑defence unless the attacker had clearly desisted, and noted the significance of failure to retreat where feasible.

---

### Self-defence test: subjective belief and objective reasonableness (Walsh; Cretu v Negri) [REVIEW re s46]
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [CASE: Cretu v Negri [2012] TASMC 12] [TOPIC: self-defence test, subjective and objective components] [CONCEPTS: subjective belief, genuine belief, objective reasonableness, s46, reasonable person test, stressful situation]

The legal test for self-defence comprises a subjective limb (whether the accused genuinely believed force was necessary in the circumstances as he perceived them) and an objective limb (whether the force used was reasonable judged by a reasonable person in those believed circumstances). Cretu v Negri [2012] TASMC 12 (paras 9–10) explains s46 involves these two questions: first, did the accused genuinely believe defensive force was necessary; second, was the amount of force used reasonable in the circumstances as the accused believed them to be. The court emphasized that genuine but mistaken belief is assessed for genuineness, while reasonableness of the response is judged objectively and accounts for stress and immediacy. The source refers to "s46" but does not specify the Act containing that section; that legislative source should be confirmed. (Marked [REVIEW] because the Act containing s46 is not specified in the uploaded text.)

---

### Defence of property — reference to "Section 39 of the CODE" and R v McKay (1957) [REVIEW]
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [TYPE: legal doctrine] [TOPIC: defence of property, statutory provision reference] [CONCEPTS: defence of property, force to protect property, s39, R v McKay, statutory interpretation]

The source notes the defence of property under "Section 39 of the CODE" and suggests R v McKay (1957) VR 560 as authority; however the uploaded text does not identify which Act or Code contains "Section 39." The rule typically permits reasonable force in defence of property, subject to necessity and proportionality constraints, but the precise statutory wording, thresholds, and qualifications depend on the specific Code provision. Practitioners must identify the exact legislative source of "s39" in the jurisdictional Code before applying it. (Marked [REVIEW] because the Act/citation for "the CODE" was not provided in the source part.)

### Chunk 1 — No Right to a speedy trial! (Lillico v McKenna)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Lillico v McKenna (1995) 5 Tas R 147 - No Right to a speedy trial!] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: annotation / case authority] [TOPIC: No common law right to a speedy trial; balancing test for unfairness] [CONCEPTS: speedy trial, fair trial, Jago v District Court, balancing test, delay prejudice]

Tactical workflow for assessing complaint of delay and unfairness in prosecution (Lillico v McKenna).

Para [18] ***Lillico v McKenna (1995) 5 Tas R 147***

Counsel for the applicant principally relied on the judgment of McHugh JA in Aboud v Attorney-General (NSW) (supra) but neither counsel adverted to the fact that the basic premise upon which his Honour proceeded, namely that there is a common law right to a speedy trial, was rejected by the High Court in Jago v District Court of New South Wales [\[1989\] HCA 46](https://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/viewdoc/au/cases/cth/HCA/1989/46.html); [(1989) 168 CLR 23.](https://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/LawCite?cit=%281989%29%20168%20CLR%2023) The right is to a fair trial as distinct from a speedy trial, although in particular circumstances a trial which is long delayed may cease to be capable of being a fair trial.

19. The test of unfairness which is relevant involves a balancing process between the interests of the applicant on the one hand and the interests of the community, which has an interest in seeing that those who commit offences are brought to trial, on the other hand (Jago v District Court of New South Wales (supra) at 33, 49-50). To dismiss a complaint because any subsequent trial would be unfair is to provide a defendant with an extreme remedy and one which should only be provided in very limited circumstances. I find it difficult to conceive of circumstances where that remedy ought to be provided in a case such as the present where, at least in relation to the complaints other than complaint 2181/1985, delay and prejudice have arisen in circumstances where they would not have arisen had the applicant not sought to evade the criminal process.

[CASE: Lillico v McKenna (1995) 5 Tas R 147]

### Chunk 2 — Insanity: Diagnosis alone not enough (Tasmania v Scott)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Tasmania v Scott [2025] TASSC 53 - Diagnosis alone not enough] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: case authority / annotation] [TOPIC: mental disease, insanity threshold, Hitchens quote; bipolar disorder as mental disease] [CONCEPTS: insanity, mental disease, s16, bipolar disorder, Hitchens v The Queen, Radford, Falconer]

Tactical workflow for applying s16 insanity principles in light of Tasmania v Scott.

**Tasmania v Scott [2025] TASSC 53**

**17:** I am satisfied that bipolar disorder is a mental disease within the meaning of s 16 and that Mr Scott was afflicted with that condition when he pointed the toy gun at the complainant. I direct myself in accordance with the comments of King CJ in *R v Radford *[(1985) 42 SASR 266](https://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/LawCite?cit=%281985%29%2042%20SASR%20266), as approved by Mason CJ, Brennan and McHugh JJ in *R v Falconer *[\[1990\] HCA 49](https://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/viewdoc/au/cases/cth/HCA/1990/49.html); 171 CLR 30 at 53. However, the existence of a mental disease by itself does not excuse an accused person. In *Hitchens* *v The Queen* [\[1962\] TASStRp 6](https://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/viewdoc/au/cases/tas/TASStRp/1962/6.html); [\[1962\] Tas SR 35](https://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/LawCite?cit=%5b1962%5d%20Tas%20SR%2035) , the Court of Criminal Appeal comprising Burbury CJ, Crisp and Cox JJ said at 49:

" It was for the accused affirmatively to establish to the satisfaction of the jury that upon the balance of probabilities he by reason of mental disease was not criminally responsible for his acts. For the mere existence of mental disease would not excuse him from criminal responsibility. Nor was it sufficient for him to prove that his alleged crime was the product of mental disease in the sense that it would not have occurred but for the existence of the disease. \...The law prescribes additional criteria to be established by evidence as a condition of criminal responsibility. An act may be a crime although the mind of a person who does it is affected by mental disease if such disease does not in fact produce upon his mind one of the results prescribed by the law as tests of criminal responsibility." (Citations removed)

[CASE: Tasmania v Scott [2025] TASSC 53] [CASE: R v Radford (1985) 42 SASR 266] [CASE: R v Falconer [1990] HCA 49; 171 CLR 30] [CASE: Hitchens v The Queen [1962] TASStRp 6; [1962] Tas SR 35]

### Chunk 3 — Insanity limbs; Mental Impairment (insanity) procedure (Bonde v Weate; CJ Act reports)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [ACT: Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999 (Tas)] [SECTION: ss 33, 34] [CITATION: Mental Impairment procedure - Bonde v Weate [2017] TASMC 11 - s33 s34 reports] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: procedure / annotation] [TOPIC: process on finding of not guilty on grounds of insanity; need for s33 and s34 reports] [CONCEPTS: mental impairment procedure, not guilty by reason of insanity, s33 report, s34 report, Bonde v Weate]

Tactical workflow for post-acquittal procedure on finding of not guilty by reason of insanity.

Mental Impairment (insanity) procedure

Process on finding of not guilty on grounds of insanity:

See *Bonde v Weate* [2017] TASMC 11

Need s. 33 and 34 reports under *Criminal Justice (mental impairment) Act*

[CASE: Bonde v Weate [2017] TASMC 11] [ACT: Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999 (Tas)] [SECTION: s33; s34]

### Chunk 4 — Automatism: Wilson v Judges [2025] TASSC 10 (summary)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Wilson v Judges [2025] TASSC 10 - Automatism summary] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: case authority / annotation] [TOPIC: automatism threshold, burden, medical evidence, concussion example] [CONCEPTS: automatism, voluntariness, medical evidence, concussion, burden of proof]

Tactical summary of Wilson v Judges and how to litigate automatism defences.

***Wilson v Judges [2025] TASSC 10***

Latest decision with best summary!

Bit rough:

Court affirms high threshold. Presumtion vol and intentional act but medical evidence can displace presumption. Not necessary for defence to prove underlying circumstances that caused the state, just the opinion that something else caused it. It is then for the prosecution to prove beyond reasonbale dount that whatever alt source there was didn't happen, or could only be attributed to vol intoxication -- which negates the defence.

Court said: "Having raised a reasonable possibility that the applicant's actions were not voluntary and intentional and that the state which led to that outcome was attributable to a concussion from a head injury, the prosecution had the onus of excluding that possibility, thereby leaving self-induced intoxication as the only reasonable explanation. The prosecution did not discharge that onus. For example, it did not call any contrary medical evidence, nor did it attempt to demonstrate the negative of the proposition concerning the head injury, that is that the bruised eye was not explained by her suffering a head injury prior to committing the relevant actions."

[CASE: Wilson v Judges [2025] TASSC 10]

---

### Chunk 5 — R v Falconer: Automatism as a Defence — Onus and Burden
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: R v Falconer [1990] HCA 49; 171 CLR 30 - Automatism doctrine] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: legal doctrine / case authority] [TOPIC: automatism vs insanity; criteria for transient automatism; burden and presumption of voluntariness] [CONCEPTS: automatism defense, insanity exemption, transient automatism, burden of proof, presumption of voluntariness]

Tactical notes on R v Falconer and how to approach automatism versus insanity in submissions.

***R v Falconer***

Automatism as a Defence -- Onus and Burdon of Proof

In this case the defendant killed her husband (separated) after being raped by him, she claimed at the time she was in a state of non-insane automatism, claiming to not remember the killing.

Automatism raises a defence of unsoundness of mind or insanity unless the malfunction was,

1) transient,

2) caused by physical or psychological trauma unlikely to have been withstood by

an ordinary person, and

3) not prone to reoccur

Proof of this exemption would entitle an acquittal under s23 of the Criminal Code (WA)

Whilst the ultimate burden lies with the Crown of proving every element essential in the crime, nevertheless in order to prove that the act was voluntary act, the Crown is entitled to rely on the *presumption* that every man has sufficient mental capacity to be responsible for his crimes, and if the defence wish to displace that presumption they must give some evidence from which the contrary may be reasonably inferred

- A defence of insanity or unsoundness of mind need only be proved on the balance of probabilities, but the accused bears the onus of proving it.

[CASE: R v Falconer [1990] HCA 49; 171 CLR 30]

---

### Chunk 6 — Morrison v Bonde [2016] TASSC 7: Falconer cited re presumption of voluntariness
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Morrison v Bonde [2016] TASSC 7 - Presumption of voluntariness quote] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: case authority / annotation] [TOPIC: presumption that acts of conscious person are willed; prosecution onus vs inference] [CONCEPTS: presumption of voluntariness, prosecution onus, Falconer quote, inability to control actions, reasonable grounds]

Tactical citation and quote for arguing the presumption that acts are willed and when it can be displaced.

1.  Mason CJ, Brennan and McHugh JJ in *R v Falconer *[\[1990\] HCA 49](http://www8.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/viewdoc/au/cases/cth/HCA/1990/49.html); [(1990) 171 CLR 30](http://www8.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/LawCite?cit=%281990%29%20171%20CLR%2030) at 40-41, as follows:

"The presumption that the acts of a person, apparently conscious, are willed or voluntary is an inference of fact and, as a matter of fact, there must be good grounds for refusing to draw the inference. Generally speaking, grounds for refusing to draw the inference appear only when there are grounds for believing that the actor is unable to control his actions. Although the prosecution bears the ultimate onus of proving beyond reasonable doubt that an act which is an element of an offence charged was a willed act or, at common law, was done voluntarily, \... the prosecution may rely on the inference that an act done by an apparently conscious actor is willed or voluntary to discharge that onus unless there are grounds for believing that the accused was unable to control that act.

[CASE: Morrison v Bonde [2016] TASSC 7] [CASE: R v Falconer [1990] HCA 49; 171 CLR 30]

---

### Chunk 8 — Not able to remember (automatism) — Bonde v Morrison [2015] TASMC 9
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Bonde v Morrison [2015] TASMC 9 - Memory and voluntariness] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: case authority / annotation] [TOPIC: claimed lack of memory; presumption of voluntariness; Falconer authority] [CONCEPTS: lack of memory, voluntariness presumption, R v Falconer, reasonable possibility, defence evidence]

Tactical script for addressing claimed lack of memory as basis for reasonable doubt on voluntariness.

**Voluntary and Intentional Act**

*Bonde v Morrison* [2015] TASMC 9

(the paragraphs are poorly numbered, this is paragraph 23 when you count them)

The defendant's claimed lack of memory of the events in question is also raised by Mr Wright as a reason for having a doubt that the defendant's actions were voluntary and intentional. However *R v Falconer* [[\[1990\] HCA 49]{.underline}](https://mail.connect.tas.gov.au/owa/redir.aspx?C=ccV0kIcp-jcUpktPqKpB9oilrF8J6D3Z-JK0XpqmR_Ltx6S37qLUCA..&URL=http%3a%2f%2fwww.austlii.edu.au%2fau%2fcases%2fcth%2fHCA%2f1990%2f49.html); [[(1990) 171 CLR 30]{.underline}](https://mail.connect.tas.gov.au/owa/redir.aspx?C=vIOQnfygRzbeMYQQm4lRIT8tvKG5Byh501FORFqHBgztx6S37qLUCA..&URL=http%3a%2f%2fwww.austlii.edu.au%2fcgi-bin%2fLawCite%3fcit%3d%25281990%2529%2520171%2520CLR%252030) is authority for the proposition that the defendant is presumed to have acted in a voluntary and intentional manner in the absence of evidence to the contrary, and that this presumption will only be displaced if there is credible evidence which raises a reasonable possibility that his actions were not so

[CASE: Bonde v Morrison [2015] TASMC 9] [CASE: R v Falconer [1990] HCA 49; 171 CLR 30]

### Chunk 9 — Unconsciousness (Bonde v Morrison [2015] TASMC 9)

### Chunk 10 — de minimis non curat lex (doctrine and limitation in criminal proceedings)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: de minimis non curat lex - Harrison v Moore [2018] TASSC 53] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: annotation / legal doctrine] [TOPIC: de minimis doctrine; not applicable to criminal proceedings per Harrison v Moore] [CONCEPTS: de minimis, trivial matters, dismissal, criminal proceedings, Harrison v Moore]

Tactical annotation on the Latin maxim and its applicability to criminal law.

"de minimis non curat lex"

***An abbreviated form of the Latin Maxim de minimis non curat lex, "law is not concerned with small things." A legal doctrine by which a court refuses to consider trifling things. In a lawsuit, the de minimis doctrine is applied by a court to avoid resolving trivial matters that are not worthy of judicial scrutiny. Its application sometimes leads to an action being dismissed, especially when the only redress being sought is for a nominal sum, such as a dollar. When appropriate, the appellate courts also use the de minimis doctrine.***

The maxim is not applicable to criminal proceedings. - ***Harrison v Moore* [\[2018\] TASSC 53 (19 October 2018)](http://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/viewdoc/au/cases/tas/TASSC/2018/53.html) -- Tasmanian Supreme Court**

[CASE: Harrison v Moore [2018] TASSC 53]

---

### Chunk 12 — Self defence: Must be raised (Irons v Moore)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Irons v Moore [2019] TASSC 22 - Self-defence must be raised] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: case authority / annotation] [TOPIC: requirement that self-defence be raised as reasonable possibility; onus shifts to prosecution] [CONCEPTS: self-defence must be raised, reasonable possibility, onus on prosecution, Viro, Walsh, Hindrum v Lane]

Tactical checklist for when self-defence must be left to jury/magistrate.

**Irons v Moore [2019] TASSC 22 (22 May 2019)**

1.  It is well established that in a case involving a jury, the issue of self-defence will only be left for the jury's consideration where [it is raised as a reasonable possibility on the evidence]{.mark} "and the accused raises the question": *Viro v The Queen *[(1978) 141 CLR 88](http://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/LawCite?cit=%281978%29%20141%20CLR%2088) at 95, per Barwick CJ. However, where the question is raised as an issue, then the onus will be on the prosecution to negative the reasonable possibility of lawful self-defence beyond reasonable doubt: *Walsh v The Queen* [\[1993\] TASSC 91.](http://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/viewdoc/au/cases/tas/TASSC/1993/91.html)

15. The same principles will apply in a summary hearing before a magistrate: *Hindrum v Lane* [\[2014\] TASFC 5.](http://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/viewdoc/au/cases/tas/TASFC/2014/5.html) In particular, the magistrate will not be required to consider the issue of self-defence unless it is raised as a reasonable possibility on the evidence, and in that event the prosecution will have the onus of excluding that possibility beyond reasonable doubt.

[CASE: Irons v Moore [2019] TASSC 22] [CASE: Viro v The Queen (1978) 141 CLR 88] [CASE: Walsh v The Queen [1993] TASSC 91] [CASE: Hindrum v Lane [2014] TASFC 5]

---

### Chunk 13 — Self defence: Higher burden on defendant when only defendant can give evidence (Irons v Moore detailed)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Irons v Moore [2019] TASSC 22 - Evidentiary onus when complainant absent] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: case authority / annotation] [TOPIC: evidentiary onus on accused where facts known only to accused; Weissensteiner principle] [CONCEPTS: evidentiary onus, Weissensteiner, circumstantial case, complainant absent, reasonable hypotheses consistent with innocence]

Tactical workflow and script for addressing evidentiary onus in circumstantial cases where only accused can explain facts.

In this case the victim was unwilling to give evidence, that is she was 38'ed and only stated she "could not remember" so that all that was in evdiecen was the physical injuries reported by witnesses and the recent complaint evidence to them.

On appeal the def said given it was a circumsytantial case, the magi could nto have rulled out self-defence as there was no evidence to exclude it and cirumatntial ases require not uity when there are reasonable hytpothes consistant with innocence.

Howerve, the appeal court said technically there was no one else OTHER than the defendant who could espouse on what had occurred. The vic did not remember, and as such there was an evidneitaryy onus on the def to provide evidence in support of self-fence. As a result the appeal court rulled that t

> "If there were facts which explained or contradicted the evidence against the accused, they were facts which were within the knowledge only of the accused and thus could not be the subject of evidence from any other person or source."

1.  The court relied upon the following passage from *Weissensteiner v The Queen *[\[1993\] HCA 65](http://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/viewdoc/au/cases/cth/HCA/1993/65.html); [(1993) 178 CLR 217](http://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/LawCite?cit=%281993%29%20178%20CLR%20217) at 227-228, per Mason CJ, Deane and Dawson JJ:

> "\... in a criminal trial, hypotheses consistent with innocence may cease to be rational or reasonable in the absence of evidence to support them when that evidence, if it exists at all, must be within the knowledge of the accused."

21. this is not a case where the complainant has not been heard from. The complainant was called by the prosecution to give evidence, and her sworn testimony was that she could not remember the relevant events. She maintained this version under cross-examination from the prosecutor. This evidence was not challenged by the applicant's counsel. In fact, the applicant's counsel did not cross-examine the complainant at all.

22. Further, the previous representations of the complainant were in evidence as part of the prosecution case and were admissible as proof of the truth of the facts asserted in them. It is true that the assertion of an assault was vague, but when taken together with the evidence of the injuries and the hair seen by Mr and Mrs Warner, the representations were capable of establishing the case that an assault had been perpetrated by the applicant. In the face of this evidence, the applicant offered no explanation at all for the manner in which the injuries had been caused, nor did his counsel cross-examine the complainant with respect to the matters contained in her out-of-court representations. It was not suggested to her that she did not say these things to Mr and Mrs Warner, or that the facts asserted were not true. It was not positively put to her that circumstances had occurred which were consistent with the lawful application of force.

23. It is true that the magistrate expressed the view that the complainant was "not making a genuine attempt to give evidence". However, the point is that the applicant did not challenge her assertion under oath that she could not recall the relevant events. In that circumstance, he was the only person able to explain the application of force, that was otherwise accepted as fact by the magistrate, on the basis of self-defence or some other circumstance that would render the force lawful. For this reason, the evidential onus remained on the applicant and had not been discharged by him.

24. In the light of the evidence that force had been applied by the applicant to the complainant and in the absence of any contradictory or explanatory evidence from the applicant, I am satisfied that the application of force in lawful self-defence was not available to the magistrate as a reasonable possibility or hypothesis consistent with innocence. It was not incumbent on the magistrate to consider and exclude self-defence or some other basis of lawful use of force. In the circumstances, it was reasonably open to the magistrate to conclude that the applicant had applied force to the complainant and that such force had been applied unlawfully.

[CASE: Irons v Moore [2019] TASSC 22] [CASE: Weissensteiner v The Queen [1993] HCA 65; 178 CLR 217]

---

### Chunk 14 — Self defence: Raising it (onus shifts when raised)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Self-defence raising and onus - Viro/Walsh/Hindrum synthesis] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: annotation / checklist] [TOPIC: how and when self-defence must be raised; prosecutor's burden thereafter] [CONCEPTS: raise self-defence, reasonable possibility, onus on prosecution, summary hearings, magistrate duties, Viro, Walsh]

Tactical checklist: how to ensure self-defence considered and who bears onus after it's raised.

Once raised, the Prosecution has to prove beyond reasonable doubt it was not self-defence.

It is well established that in a case involving a jury, the issue of self-defence will only be left for the jury's consideration where it is raised as a reasonable possibility on the evidence "and the accused raises the question": *Viro v The Queen* (1978) 141 CLR 88 at 95, per Barwick CJ. However, where the question is raised as an issue, then the onus will be on the prosecution to negative the reasonable possibility of lawful self-defence beyond reasonable doubt: *Walsh v The Queen* [\[1993\] TASSC 91.

The same principles will apply in a summary hearing before a magistrate: *Hindrum v Lane* [\[2014\] TASFC 5. In particular, the magistrate will not be required to consider the issue of self-defence unless it is raised as a reasonable possibility on the evidence, and in that event the prosecution will have the onus of excluding that possibility beyond reasonable doubt.

[CASE: Viro v The Queen (1978) 141 CLR 88] [CASE: Walsh v The Queen [1993] TASSC 91] [CASE: Hindrum v Lane [2014] TASFC 5]

---

### Chunk 15 — Self defence: Can pre-empt self-defence (Wright v Tasmania)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Wright v Tasmania [2005] TASSC 113 - Pre-emptive force in self-defence] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: case authority / annotation] [TOPIC: pre-emptive use of force; not required to wait for first blow] [CONCEPTS: pre-emptive force, belief of imminent attack, Beckford, R v Lawrie, Wright v Tasmania]

Tactical note: include submissions supporting pre-emptive self-defence where evidence shows belief of imminent attack.

A PRE-EMPTIVE RESORT TO SELF-DEFENCE

***Wright v Tasmania [2005] TASSC 113; BC200509811***

Blow, J:

[17] A person who believes he or she is about to be attacked does not necessarily have to wait for the assailant to strike the first blow or fire the first shot. Circumstances may justify the use of pre-emptive force in self-defence. See Beckford v R [1988] AC 130; R v Lawrie [1986] 2 Qd R 502 at 505

[CASE: Wright v Tasmania [2005] TASSC 113]

---

### Chunk 16 — Self defence: An attacker cannot rely upon claim of self-defence (Viro)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Viro v The Queen [1978] HCA 9; (1978) 141 CLR 88 - Original aggressor cannot claim self-defence] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: case authority / annotation] [TOPIC: self-defence unavailable to original aggressor; Gibbs J reasoning] [CONCEPTS: original aggressor, lawful resistance, transmutation to self-defence, Gibbs J, retreat significance]

Tactical extract for submissions where accused was original aggressor.

(SELF-DEFENCE IN RESPONSE TO A SITUATION OF LAWFULLY APPLIED FORCE)

***Viro v. The Queen [1978] HCA 9; (1978) 141 CLR 88***

Gibbs J. pointed out that self-defence is not a defence available to the original aggressor in a conflict either when the original victim uses lawful force to resist him or when the original victim uses unlawful force but the original aggressor does not believe on reasonable grounds that it is necessary to do what he did to defend himself.

He said (at pp.116-117):

" If, therefore, one man makes a violent attack upon another with intent to rob him, and the man attacked defends himself, using no more force than is reasonably necessary, the original assailant cannot be said to be acting in self-defence in trying to overcome the other's resistance, since that resistance was lawful. However, if the original assailant has desisted from his attack, and his intended victim no longer needs to defend himself, and can not reasonably believe that he is still in danger, but nevertheless takes the offensive and out of anger or revenge himself becomes the attacker, the original assailant is not obliged to let himself be killed or injured without any attempt at resistance. Nevertheless, in such a case it is difficult to see how, as a matter of fact, the conduct of the aggressor, which commences as a criminal assault with an intent to commit a serious crime, can become transmuted in split seconds into lawful self-defence, unless the aggressor has clearly broken off his attack. In such circumstances the fact that he did not retreat when he had the opportunity to do so assumes a special significance."

[CASE: Viro v The Queen [1978] HCA 9; (1978) 141 CLR 88]

---

### Chunk 17 — Self defence: TEST — Walsh v R and Cretu v Negri (subjective and objective elements)
[DOMAIN: Tasmanian Criminal Law] [CITATION: Walsh v R [1993] TASSC 91 & Cretu v Negri [2012] TASMC 12 - Self-defence test] [CATEGORY: procedure] [TYPE: case authority / annotation] [TOPIC: subjective belief and objective reasonableness tests under s46; how to assess genuine belief and reasonable force] [CONCEPTS: s46 test, subjective belief, objective reasonableness, genuine belief, reasonable person standard, Cretu v Negri]

Tactical script for applying the two-limb self-defence test and directions for magistrates/juries.

### TEST:

**(***Walsh v R* [\[1993\] TASSC 91.](https://www.austlii.edu.au/cgi-bin/viewdoc/au/cases/tas/TASSC/1993/91.html))

Subjective -- mind of the person claiming the defense

Objective -- in the subjective circumstances was the force used unreasonable

**Cretu v Negri [2012] TASMC 12 (26 April 2012)**

Para 9 and 10

9. S46 involves a subjective and an objective test. The subjective test involves two questions, whether the accused was acting in self-defence when he used force, and what were the circumstances as he believed them to be which might be taken into account. Thus the court is required to place itself in the position of the defendant and ascertain his belief as to the danger. The question is whether he genuinely and honestly believed that force was necessary. He may be mistaken but the test is genuineness of belief not reasonableness. Although the reasonableness or unreasonableness of the belief is material to the question of whether the belief was held at all. If the belief was in fact held its unreasonableness so far as guilt or innocence is concerned is neither here nor there. If I am satisfied beyond reasonable doubt that the defendant did not believe the circumstances required the use of force in defence of himself then the issue goes no further.

10. The second requirement of s46 is determined objectively, and that is whether the force used was in the circumstances as the defendant believed them to be reasonable to use. The prosecution must satisfy me beyond reasonable doubt that a reasonable person would consider the amount of force used was unreasonable judged against the circumstances as the defendant believed them to be. In determining whether the amount of force used in self-defence or defence of another was reasonable or excessive I must take into account the fact that a person may be in a stressful situation with little or no time to think. A defendant is not required to weigh the amount of force used nicely. A person who believes he or she is about to be attacked, or someone else is about to be attacked, does not necessarily have to wait for the assailant to strike the first blow. Circumstances may justify the use of pre-emptive force in self-defence .

[CASE: Walsh v R [1993] TASSC 91] [CASE: Cretu v Negri [2012] TASMC 12]

---
